# Candle & Crumb Christmas
## A Tinsel Town Novella: Wes & Sam

*A cozy MM holiday romance about privacy, consent, boundaries, and learning to be seen*

---


Wes realized, with the slow dread of a man who was absolutely going to do something stupid, that Sam Rook did not look at him like a bakery owner.

Sam Rook looked at him like a problem worth solving.

And Wes Candle was so tired of being the one who fixed everything.

Sam didn’t touch anything at first. He just stood there in the entryway, eyes sweeping the bakery in a slow, methodical pass. The display case gleamed under the dim lights. The counters were wiped clean. The chairs were stacked in a neat line like they were waiting for a lecture.

Wes leaned his shoulder into the wall and tried to look casual about having a cop in his bakery at… he glanced at the clock… 10:47 p.m.

“Are you going to… do cop things?” Wes asked.

Sam’s gaze flicked to him. “I’m doing them.”

Wes tilted his head. “Standing very still?”

Sam’s mouth twitched again, almost a smile. “Listening.”

Wes frowned. “For what?”

Sam held up one finger.

Wes shut his mouth.

For a few seconds, the bakery was quiet except for the soft ticking of the ovens cooling down. Wes had always liked that sound. It was a promise that the day was over, the work done, the world temporarily held at bay.

Then something scratched.

Wes’s blood went cold in a way that had nothing to do with winter.

The scratch came again, followed by a tiny thump.

Sam’s gaze moved, sharp and calm, toward the back hallway that led to the pantry and the utility closet.

Wes lifted his hands. “Okay. So. I would like to clarify that I did not invite anyone into my bakery. I barely invite people into my life.”

Sam’s eyes stayed on the hallway. “Stay here.”

Wes made a face. “I live here, emotionally. Of course I’m staying here.”

Sam’s head turned just enough for Wes to see the warning in his expression. Not anger. Not impatience. Just a firm line that suggested Sam was used to people doing dumb things and had run out of tolerance for it.

Wes held up a hand. “Fine. I’m a statue. A handsome, cinnamon-scented statue.”

Sam moved.

He didn’t rush. He didn’t stalk dramatically like he was in a crime show. He walked with quiet certainty, steps controlled, weight balanced like he’d learned to move through other people’s panic without adding to it.

Wes watched him go with a weird twist in his chest that he absolutely refused to label.

Sam reached the hallway, paused, then glanced back at Wes.

Wes froze in place and tried to look like a statue that wasn’t terrified of raccoons.

Sam’s gaze lingered for a second too long, and Wes’s pulse stuttered.

Then Sam turned and disappeared into the dark.

Wes swallowed and tried to breathe through his nose like a reasonable person. He failed. Everything smelled like sugar and spice and the cold clean edge of Sam’s coat.

A clatter sounded from the back.

Wes winced. “Please don’t shoot anything,” he called. “If it’s a raccoon, I can negotiate. I speak fluent trash panda.”

“Wes,” Sam called back, voice low.

Wes’s spine went rigid.

Sam had said his name like he already knew it belonged in his mouth.

Wes cleared his throat. “Yes?”

“Do you have cats?”

Wes blinked. “No.”

There was a brief pause.

Then Sam’s voice, still calm, said, “You do now.”

Wes shoved off the wall and started toward the hallway before he remembered he’d been told to stay.

Sam appeared at the end of the hall with something squirming in his hands.

It was small. Grey. Furious. Very much not a cat.

Wes stared. “That is not a cat.”

Sam’s expression was unchanged, but his eyes looked… amused. “Correct.”

The creature made an offended noise that sounded like it had swallowed a kazoo. It wriggled harder, tiny paws flailing.

Wes squinted. “Is that a possum?”

“It’s a baby raccoon,” Sam said.

Wes put a hand to his chest. “Oh, thank God. For a second I thought it was a demon.”

Sam walked toward the front counter, holding the raccoon with hands that were somehow both gentle and unyielding. The raccoon snapped its little teeth in the air like it wanted to sue for defamation.

Wes watched Sam’s fingers, steady and careful, and felt a ridiculous thought float through his brain.

*Those hands could do a lot of things.*

Wes immediately tried to drown that thought in cinnamon. It didn’t work.

“How did it get in here?” Wes asked, forcing his voice back into normal territory.

Sam nodded toward the back. “Window in the utility closet was unlatched.”

Wes frowned. “That window is always latched.”

Sam’s gaze flicked to him. “Always?”

Wes hesitated.

If Wes had a flaw, besides being emotionally constipated and willing to fight a man over the last cinnamon knot, it was that he assumed he could handle everything alone.

Wes sighed. “Okay. Fine. Sometimes I crack it for ventilation after I mop. Because the cleaner smells like chemical despair.”

Sam’s brow lifted a fraction. “And you forgot to latch it.”

Wes pointed at the raccoon. “I would like to file a complaint against this tiny criminal. He broke in. He trespassed. He is not wearing a hairnet.”

The raccoon made another furious kazoo noise.

Sam looked down at it. “He’s a baby.”

Wes stared. “That’s how they get you. It’s a con.”

Sam’s mouth tipped again. A real hint of a smile this time, brief and dangerous. “Where do you want him?”

Wes blinked at the question, then at Sam’s hands.

Wes was not thinking about raccoons anymore.

Wes snapped his gaze back up like he’d been caught stealing. “Outside. Preferably far away. With a tiny attorney present.”

Sam nodded once. “Okay.”

He carried the baby raccoon out the front door like it was a delicate package rather than a tiny felon. Wes followed, pulling the door shut behind them, and the cold hit him full in the face.

Snow dusted the sidewalks and made the streetlamps glow soft and golden. The town was quiet in that late-night way that made everything feel like a secret.

Sam walked to the edge of the alley beside the bakery and crouched near the fence, holding the raccoon low. The little creature wriggled, sniffed, then darted into the shadows with a final indignant squeak.

Wes stared into the alley. “Do you think it will tell its friends?”

Sam straightened. “Probably.”

Wes sighed. “Great. I’m going to get robbed by a raccoon gang.”

Sam’s eyes met his. “Latch the window.”

Wes lifted his hands. “Yes, Dad.”

Sam didn’t look offended by the word. If anything, he looked like he was trying not to laugh.

Wes’s chest did that weird tightening thing again. Sam laughing would be… a problem. A warm, dangerous problem.

Wes shoved his hands in his pockets and tried to shift the conversation into safer territory. “So who called you? Like, who’s out here panic-reporting my bakery at midnight?”

Sam’s gaze slid to the street as if checking for witnesses, then returned. “Mayor Kringle.”

Wes froze. “The mayor called in a break-in?”

Sam’s expression was neutral. “He said he saw a light on.”

Wes’s eyes narrowed. “The man lives for drama.”

Sam’s gaze sharpened with something like agreement. “He also said you’ve been working too much.”

Wes bristled. “I work a normal amount.”

Sam looked at him.

Wes sighed. “I work an emotionally unhealthy amount.”

Sam’s eyes held his for a beat too long. “Yes.”

Wes should’ve been annoyed. He should’ve cracked a joke and bounced right past the uncomfortable honesty.

Instead, he felt his shoulders drop, just a little, like his body trusted Sam’s steadiness more than Wes trusted himself.

Wes cleared his throat. “You can go now. The bakery is safe from the raccoon menace.”

Sam didn’t move.

The snow fell in slow quiet flakes around them, and Wes suddenly became acutely aware of how alone he was out here, standing under a streetlamp in front of his bakery, with a man who smelled like winter and looked at him like Wes mattered.

Sam’s voice was low. “Do you live above the shop?”

Wes blinked. “Yeah.”

Sam nodded once. “I’m going to walk you up.”

Wes’s pulse stumbled. “That’s… not necessary.”

Sam’s gaze didn’t waver. “It is.”

Wes opened his mouth to argue.

Then he remembered what he’d written on the employee board last week in a moment of sleep-deprived honesty:

**REMINDER: LET PEOPLE HELP YOU.**

Wes had circled it twice and underlined it three times, like he could bully himself into receiving care through sheer punctuation.

Wes exhaled. “Fine,” he muttered. “But I’m telling you right now, if you see flour in my hair and judge me, I will cry.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Noted.”

Wes unlocked the front door and held it open for Sam. Sam stepped inside and paused, scanning the bakery again like it was his job to protect it now.

Protect Wes.

Wes felt the thought land and settle, uncomfortable and warm.

He locked the door behind them and turned toward the stairs that led up to his apartment.

Sam followed.

Halfway up, Wes glanced back and found Sam watching him. Not in the casual way people watched to be polite. In the steady, attentive way that made Wes feel… visible.

Wes’s throat tightened. “What?”

Sam’s gaze didn’t move. “You’re shaking again.”

Wes swallowed. “I’m not.”

Sam stopped on the step below him, close enough that Wes could feel his body heat in the cold stairwell. “Wes.”

Wes froze.

Sam’s voice wasn’t sharp. It wasn’t scolding.

It was the same tone Sam had used when he said *stay here*.

Like Sam expected Wes to listen.

Wes stared at him, breath catching for no reason that made sense.

Sam’s eyes softened, barely. “Are you okay?”

Wes hated that the question did something to him.

Wes hated that he wanted to say no.

Wes forced air into his lungs and tried honesty on, just to see if it fit.

“I’m…” Wes swallowed. “Tired.”

Sam nodded once, like Wes had just confirmed a fact Sam already knew. “Yeah.”

Wes tried for a joke. “Good news is, I’m too tired to commit any crimes.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “You already did.”

Wes blinked. “Excuse me?”

Sam’s gaze flicked over Wes’s face, then held. “You worked all day. You cleaned. You stayed late. You forgot to latch the window. You invited a raccoon into your life.”

Wes huffed a laugh despite himself. “That’s not a crime.”

Sam’s voice dropped, quieter now. “It is if you do it alone.”

Wes went still.

There it was again. That straight-line honesty that made Wes feel like he’d been seen through, gently but completely.

Wes licked his lips, suddenly aware of the small space between them. The quiet. The fact that Sam hadn’t moved away.

Wes’s voice came out rough. “You always talk like this?”

Sam’s gaze stayed on him. “Only when I mean it.”

Wes’s pulse thudded once, hard.

Then he did the only sensible thing he could do in the face of sincerity.

He kept walking.

He unlocked his apartment door at the top of the stairs, stepped inside, and flicked on the light.

His place was small. Warm. Smelled like sugar and laundry detergent. A holiday throw blanket had been tossed over the couch like it had given up.

Wes turned and found Sam in the doorway, framed by the stairwell light, coat still on, eyes sweeping the room like it was another scene he needed to assess.

Sam’s gaze landed on the couch. The blanket. The single mug in the sink. The stack of festival flyers on the counter.

His eyes returned to Wes.

“You’re doing the Twelve Days baking,” Sam said, like it wasn’t a question.

Wes’s shoulders tensed. “Yeah.”

Sam nodded once. “Mayor Kringle should’ve assigned someone to check on you.”

Wes snorted. “He assigned you.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “Yeah.”

Wes stared back, heart doing something stupid. “So what now, Officer Rook? You going to arrest me for overworking?”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “No.”

Wes waited.

Sam’s voice went even lower. “I’m going to make sure you eat something.”

Wes blinked. “I eat.”

Sam looked at him.

Wes threw up his hands. “Fine. I eat pastry scraps and resentment.”

Sam stepped fully into the apartment, the door clicking shut behind him, and Wes suddenly couldn’t tell if the warmth in the room was from the heater or from the fact that Sam Rook was here, in his space, like he belonged.

Sam’s gaze dropped to Wes’s hands. Flour dusted under his nails. Small burn marks near his knuckles where the tray had slipped earlier.

Sam’s eyes lifted again, steady.

“Sit,” Sam said.

Wes stared.

His pulse jumped like a traitor.

Wes swallowed, then leaned back against his kitchen counter and tried to sound like he wasn’t suddenly in danger of feelings.

“You Christmas people,” Wes muttered. “Always with the orders.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Sit, Wes.”

Wes’s breath caught.

He sat.

And for the first time in weeks, the quiet didn’t feel lonely.

It felt like someone had shown up.

**[STOP WRITING]**


Wes sat on the edge of his couch like he was waiting for a verdict.

Sam stood in the middle of the room, coat still on, hands still bare, gaze steady as if Wes had just turned into another scene Sam needed to secure. Except this wasn’t a bakery. There were no raccoons to remove. No doors to lock.

Just Wes, caught in the beam of Sam’s attention like it was a spotlight he hadn’t agreed to step into.

Sam glanced toward the kitchenette. “Do you have food?”

Wes bristled automatically. “I have ingredients.”

Sam’s eyes returned to him. “That’s not what I asked.”

Wes’s mouth tightened. “Yes.”

Sam didn’t say *prove it.* He didn’t have to. He just walked to the fridge and opened it like he’d been invited.

Wes watched in frozen disbelief.

The fridge light revealed… exactly what Wes had expected. Half a carton of eggs. A block of cheese. Leftover soup in a container with a handwritten label that said **DO NOT EAT** which, in fairness, was mostly a note to Wes himself from three days ago. A tub of butter. Milk that was flirting with its expiration date.

Sam took it in, then closed the fridge with a quiet click.

Wes cleared his throat. “I’m not helpless.”

Sam’s gaze flicked over him. Flour on his knuckles. A faint red line across one finger. The tired slump he’d been pretending wasn’t there.

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “I didn’t say you were.”

Wes stared.

Sam’s eyes didn’t waver. “I said you’re not eating.”

Wes opened his mouth, then shut it again. Because… yeah. Okay. Fair.

Sam turned slightly and looked at the counter. There was a loaf of bread in a paper bag. A jar of jam. A butter knife sitting in the sink like it had given up halfway through a life.

Sam pointed at the bread. “That’s bread.”

Wes blinked. “Incredible detective work.”

Sam’s mouth tipped, almost. “Sit still.”

Wes’s pulse jumped again. He hated that his body reacted like it recognized the tone.

He shifted on the couch, trying to find a posture that didn’t scream *I am emotionally compromised by a man holding the concept of authority.* “If you’re about to make me a sandwich, I need you to know this is going to ruin my reputation.”

Sam didn’t look at him as he pulled out the bread. “Your reputation is already ruined.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “By whom?”

Sam glanced back. “By you.”

Wes’s throat tightened in the most irritating way. He tried to laugh it off. “I’m an upstanding citizen. I only commit crimes against butter.”

Sam set two slices of bread on a plate with quiet competence. “You work alone.”

Wes’s stomach dipped. He forced breeziness. “I run a bakery. Alone is kind of the job description.”

Sam’s gaze slid to him. “No. Alone is the way you do it.”

Wes went still.

Sam turned back to the counter. He opened a drawer, found a knife without asking, and began slicing the cheese. The domestic ease of the motion felt… illegal. Like Sam shouldn’t belong in Wes’s apartment this naturally.

Wes stared at his own hands in his lap. The flour that never fully washed out. The small burns he’d ignored. The way his fingers curled like they were always bracing to grip something.

“You’re not my dad,” Wes muttered.

Sam’s voice came back without pause. “I know.”

Wes swallowed. “Then stop acting like I’m twelve.”

Sam set the cheese down. His gaze lifted, steady and quiet.

“I’m not treating you like you’re twelve,” Sam said. “I’m treating you like you matter.”

Wes’s breath caught.

He hated that. He hated how simple it was. How it slipped past all his defenses because it wasn’t dramatic. It wasn’t a grand statement. It was just… true.

Wes forced air out of his lungs. “People treat me like I matter all the time.”

Sam raised his brows. “Do they?”

Wes flinched.

Sam didn’t pounce on it. He turned back to the counter and spread butter on the bread with slow, even strokes.

The quiet filled the room again, but it wasn’t lonely now. It was full. It had weight.

Wes’s voice came out smaller than he meant it to. “Mayor Kringle told you to check on me.”

Sam set the knife down. “He asked me to.”

Wes’s eyes narrowed. “That’s not an answer.”

Sam looked at Wes for a long beat. Then he reached into his pocket and pulled out his phone, thumb tapping once. He held it up so Wes could see the screen.

A text thread.

**MAYOR KRINGLE:** *Light on at Candle & Crumb again. He’s going to work himself into the ground.*
**MAYOR KRINGLE:** *Can you swing by?*
**MAYOR KRINGLE:** *As a friend.*
**SAM:** *On my way.*

Wes stared at the last line until it burned.

“As a friend,” Wes repeated, voice flat.

Sam’s gaze didn’t waver. “Yes.”

Wes tried to scoff, but it came out weak. “Since when are we friends?”

Sam set the phone away and picked up the plate. He walked over, stopped in front of Wes, and held it out.

On the plate: a grilled cheese that looked like it belonged in a brochure. Golden, crisp edges. Melted cheese pulling slightly at the seam.

Wes stared like he’d been handed a live grenade.

Sam’s voice was calm. “Eat.”

Wes looked up. “Is everyone in this town obsessed with telling people to sit and eat?”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “The people who care are.”

Wes took the plate because not taking it felt impossible. His fingers brushed Sam’s for half a second.

Sam didn’t pull away. He didn’t press closer. He just let the touch exist, like it mattered and like it wasn’t going to be used against Wes later.

Wes sat there holding a sandwich and trying not to feel like he’d been given something dangerous.

Sam took a seat in the chair across from the couch, posture easy but alert, like he was still working. Like Wes was still a scene worth protecting.

Wes picked up the sandwich and took a bite.

It was… really good.

Wes chewed slowly, glare fixed on Sam. “You did not have to make it this good.”

Sam’s eyes warmed faintly. “I did.”

Wes swallowed. “You’re annoying.”

Sam nodded. “I’ve heard.”

Wes took another bite. The warmth of the food hit his stomach like a quiet apology.

For a minute, they didn’t speak. Wes ate. Sam watched him with a steadiness that should’ve been invasive but somehow wasn’t. Like Sam was counting Wes’s bites the way Noel counted volunteer shifts. Like feeding Wes was simply another problem solved.

Wes finished half the sandwich before the words slipped out, uninvited.

“I hate it when people see me like this.”

Sam’s gaze sharpened, but his voice stayed soft. “Like what?”

Wes gestured vaguely at himself. At the flour. The tiredness. The fact that he was sitting down.

“Not in control,” Wes admitted. “Not… sparkling.”

Sam was quiet for a beat.

Then he said, “You don’t have to sparkle to be worth looking at.”

Wes’s throat tightened again. It was becoming a trend.

Wes swallowed hard and looked away toward the window, the soft fall of snow, the glow of a streetlamp.

“You’re too calm,” Wes said, because it was easier than saying *thank you.*

Sam’s voice came low. “You’re too alone.”

Wes’s hands clenched around the sandwich.

He tried to laugh. Tried to turn it into a joke. “Maybe I like it.”

Sam didn’t argue.

He just said, very evenly, “No.”

Wes’s head snapped up. “Excuse me?”

Sam’s gaze held his. Patient. Certain. “You like control,” Sam corrected. “You like knowing what happens next. You like being needed.”

Wes went still, like Sam had just read his diary.

Sam’s voice lowered, gentler without losing firmness. “You don’t like being lonely.”

Wes’s mouth opened.

No words came out.

Because Sam was right, and Wes hated him for it.

Wes looked down at the sandwich in his hands, now smaller, half gone. He realized he’d eaten without thinking, like his body had trusted Sam’s order more than Wes’s own denial.

Wes licked his lips, voice rough. “So what. You’re going to start showing up and… parenting me?”

Sam’s eyes softened, barely. “No.”

Wes’s pulse stumbled. “Then what?”

Sam leaned forward slightly, forearms on his knees. Not crowding. Not pushing. Just… there.

“I’m going to check on you,” Sam said. “During the Twelve Days.”

Wes stared. “Why?”

Sam’s gaze didn’t move. “Because the festival makes people stupid.”

Wes blinked. “That’s your official stance?”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “It’s my professional assessment.”

Wes tried to snort. It came out like a breath. “And that’s the only reason.”

Sam’s eyes warmed like he’d been expecting the challenge. “No.”

Wes’s chest tightened. “So what’s the other reason, Officer?”

Sam’s voice went lower, quiet enough that it felt like a secret in Wes’s apartment.

“Because I like you.”

Wes froze.

The words were simple. Clean. No theatrics. No flirtation to hide behind.

Just truth.

Wes’s heart hammered once, hard, like it wanted to escape.

He forced a laugh that sounded wrong in his own ears. “You barely know me.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “I know you stay late. I know you feed the whole town. I know you make yourself small when you’re tired so nobody notices.” Sam paused. “I know you let me in.”

Wes’s breath caught.

He looked down at the sandwich again like it might give him instructions.

It didn’t.

Wes scraped together the only defense he had left. “You’re going to ruin my ability to pretend I’m fine.”

Sam’s eyes softened. “Good.”

Wes stared at him. “That’s not a nice thing to say.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “It’s a necessary thing to say.”

Wes’s fingers tightened around the plate. He hated the heat creeping up his neck. Hated the way his body leaned toward Sam’s steadiness like it wanted to rest against it.

Wes swallowed. “Okay,” he said, too fast, because slowing down felt like falling. “Okay, you can check on me. But you don’t get to… make it weird.”

Sam’s mouth tipped, almost a smile. “Define weird.”

Wes glared. “No.”

Sam’s gaze warmed, amused now. “That’s not a definition.”

Wes groaned and pushed a hand through his hair, then immediately regretted it because flour probably exploded everywhere. “You know what I mean.”

Sam stood up.

Wes’s pulse jumped again because apparently Wes’s body had decided Sam moving was now a full event.

Sam walked to the counter, picked up a paper towel, and dampened it at the sink. Then he came back and stopped in front of Wes.

Wes stared up at him, suspicious. “What are you doing.”

Sam held up the paper towel. “You have flour on your cheek.”

Wes’s face went hot. “I do not.”

Sam didn’t argue.

He lifted the towel and touched Wes’s cheek with a gentleness that made Wes’s whole body go still.

Sam wiped once, slow.

Wes’s breath hitched.

Sam’s gaze flicked to Wes’s eyes. “There.”

Wes swallowed. “Thanks.”

Sam didn’t move away right away. He stayed there, close enough that Wes could feel his presence like a wall at his back.

Wes’s voice came out quiet. “Is this… weird?”

Sam’s mouth tipped, but his eyes were steady. “Not yet.”

Wes’s pulse thudded. “That’s ominous.”

Sam’s voice dropped, softer. “It’s honest.”

Wes stared up at him, caught.

Sam’s gaze dipped to Wes’s mouth for the briefest second.

Wes felt it like a touch.

Sam looked back up, like he’d decided something and then decided not to say it.

“I’m going to go,” Sam said.

Wes’s chest tightened, immediate and sharp. He hated that too.

Wes forced a shrug. “Sure. You did your civic duty. Saved me from raccoons and starvation.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “I’ll check the utility window on my way out.”

Wes nodded. “You do that.”

Sam turned toward the door, then paused, hand on the knob.

He didn’t look back right away.

When he did, his gaze met Wes’s like a tether.

“Lock the door behind me,” Sam said.

Wes blinked. “I can lock a door.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “I know.”

Wes frowned. “Then why are you telling me.”

Sam’s voice came low, steady. “Because I’m going to be here tomorrow morning.”

Wes froze. “What?”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “I’m walking the route for the Twelve Days tree delivery anyway. Noel and Eben are meeting at Merryweather Hall at seven. I’ll swing by the bakery at six-thirty.”

Wes stared, heart thudding. “Why do you know Noel’s schedule.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Because the whole town knows Noel’s schedule.”

Wes made a face. “Fair.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “Eat breakfast,” Sam said.

Wes pointed with the plate. “I just ate.”

Sam’s brows lifted. “Tomorrow.”

Wes groaned. “You’re all insane.”

Sam’s eyes warmed, amused. “Yes.”

Then Sam opened the door, let in a bite of cold air, and stepped into the stairwell.

Wes sat there holding an empty plate and realizing, with slow horror, that he was smiling.

And that the quiet didn’t feel lonely anymore.

It felt like anticipation.

**[STOP WRITING]**



Wes woke up to the smell of cinnamon and the faint, traitorous feeling of hope.

Which was stupid, because hope was how you ended up volunteering to bake six hundred cookies for a toy drive and then spending the night cleaning raccoon paw prints off your pantry shelves.

He rolled over, squinting at the clock.

**6:04 a.m.**

Wes made a sound that was half groan, half threat. “No.”

His apartment was still dim, the winter light barely creeping around the edges of the curtains. The bakery below him was quiet, which meant he was currently experiencing the rare and endangered state known as *not actively working.*

Wes stared at the ceiling for three long breaths.

Then, because he was apparently the kind of person who had *opinions* about being told what to do, he sat up and swung his legs over the side of the bed.

“Breakfast,” he muttered, to nobody. “Fine. Congratulations. You win.”

He shuffled into the kitchen, hair doing something unholy, and opened the fridge again as if the contents might have magically improved overnight.

They had not.

Wes stared at the eggs like they’d betrayed him.

A knock sounded from the stairwell door below.

Wes froze.

His heartbeat did that little lurch again, the one he’d been pretending wasn’t connected to Sam Rook.

The knock came again, steady and polite.

Wes stared at the clock.

**6:31 a.m.**

Wes’s throat tightened.

He was on time.

Wes hated that he noticed.

Wes hated that the noticing felt like a small gift.

Wes grabbed the first hoodie he saw, yanked it over his head, and stomped down the stairs barefoot like a man determined to look unimpressed by the concept of being cared about.

He unlocked the bakery door and yanked it open.

Sam stood on the doorstep with a paper bag in one hand and two coffees in the other, breath fogging in the cold like the morning itself belonged to him.

Wes’s brain stopped functioning for a full second.

Sam’s gaze swept over Wes in the doorway. Bare feet. Hoodie. Sleep-tousled hair. A faint line of flour still on his wrist because apparently Wes shed baking evidence like glitter.

Something softened in Sam’s eyes.

Wes immediately resented it.

“You’re early,” Wes said.

Sam’s mouth tipped, almost. “I’m on time.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “That’s the same thing.”

Sam held up the paper bag. “Breakfast.”

Wes stared at the bag like it might be a trap. “Did you… bring me food.”

Sam’s expression stayed neutral. “Yes.”

Wes’s voice went suspicious. “From where.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Garland Cup.”

Wes blinked. “The Garland Cup is… two blocks away.”

Sam nodded once. “It opens at six.”

Wes’s throat tightened for no reason that made sense. He stepped back automatically to let Sam in, because the alternative was standing here with the door open while Sam looked at him like that.

Sam stepped inside and paused, gaze flicking around the bakery like he couldn’t help himself. Checking corners. Checking locks. Checking that Wes was, in fact, still alive.

Wes shut the door behind him and tried to sound casual. “So you’re just… doing wellness checks now.”

Sam set the coffees on the counter. “Call it a community initiative.”

Wes scoffed. “You’re going to ruin my brand.”

Sam’s gaze slid to him. “Your brand is ‘I’m fine’ while you’re actively collapsing.”

Wes bristled. “I am not collapsing.”

Sam’s eyes held his.

Wes exhaled. “Okay. I’m collapsing aesthetically.”

Sam’s mouth tipped again, a real hint of amusement. “Sit.”

Wes stared at him.

His pulse jumped.

Wes hated his body.

“No,” Wes said on principle.

Sam didn’t argue. He just reached into the paper bag and pulled out a wrapped breakfast sandwich, then set it on a plate like he lived here.

Wes watched Sam’s hands, calm and competent, doing small domestic things in Wes’s space like it was the most natural thing in the world.

Wes’s voice came out quieter. “You really like telling people to sit.”

Sam didn’t look up. “You really like pretending you don’t need to.”

Wes’s throat tightened. Again. It was getting annoying.

Wes moved to the counter and leaned on it, arms crossed. “Fine. I’ll stand and eat. Like a rebellious teenager.”

Sam’s gaze flicked up, steady. “If you stand and eat, you’ll start working.”

Wes froze.

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “You’ll finish the sandwich in three bites. You’ll go check the ovens. You’ll tell yourself you’re just making sure everything’s fine. And then it’ll be noon and you’ll be running on pastry scraps and spite.”

Wes stared at him.

“How do you know that,” Wes demanded.

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Because I’ve been watching you do it for two years.”

Wes’s breath caught.

He tried to hide it by rolling his eyes. “That’s creepy.”

Sam’s expression didn’t change. “It’s concerned.”

Wes swallowed hard and glanced away, pretending to be offended by the display case rather than the fact that Sam had just admitted he’d been paying attention for *two years.*

Wes forced a laugh. “So what, you’ve been in love with my croissants this whole time?”

Sam’s gaze held his. “Among other things.”

Wes’s heart did something stupid and loud.

Wes stared at Sam like he was a new ingredient Wes wasn’t sure was safe to ingest.

“You can’t just say things like that,” Wes muttered.

Sam’s voice stayed low, steady. “Why not.”

Wes threw up his hands. “Because I’m not built for it.”

Sam’s eyes softened, just a fraction. “Then I’ll go slow.”

Wes’s chest tightened so sharply he almost flinched.

Sam stepped closer, stopping just on the other side of the counter. Not crowding. Not demanding. Just… present.

Sam’s gaze flicked to Wes’s bare feet. “You’re cold.”

Wes scoffed. “I’m always cold. It’s a bakery. It’s basically a sugar freezer until the ovens kick on.”

Sam reached out, slow, and didn’t touch Wes. His hand hovered near Wes’s wrist, giving Wes time to pull away.

“May I,” Sam asked.

Wes stared at the hand hovering like it was a live wire.

He should’ve said no. He should’ve made a joke. He should’ve done anything except let this turn into something real.

But Sam had asked like Wes’s yes mattered. Like Wes was allowed to choose.

Wes swallowed. “Yeah,” he said, voice rough. “Okay.”

Sam’s fingers closed gently around Wes’s wrist. Not tight. Not restraining. Just warm, steady contact that made Wes’s breath catch like he’d been punched.

Sam’s thumb brushed once over the inside of Wes’s wrist, right where Wes’s pulse hammered.

Wes’s brain went very quiet.

Sam’s eyes stayed on Wes’s face, watching his reaction like it mattered.

“Good,” Sam murmured.

Wes’s stomach flipped.

Wes forced air into his lungs. “Stop doing that.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “Doing what.”

Wes gestured vaguely at his entire body. “That. Whatever that is. The… calm voice. The ‘good.’ The… touching my pulse like you’re checking if I’m alive.”

Sam’s thumb stilled instantly. “Do you want me to stop touching you.”

Wes’s mouth opened.

No words came out.

Because the answer was no and Wes hated that.

Wes exhaled hard. “No,” he admitted, then immediately tried to recover with humor. “But don’t make it weird.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Define weird.”

Wes groaned. “You’re impossible.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “Sit,” Sam said again. Softer this time. “Please.”

The please landed like a second hand on Wes’s spine. Not pushing. Just… asking.

Wes hated that his eyes stung.

Wes turned away fast, grabbed the breakfast sandwich like it was an excuse, and sat on the stool behind the counter.

“There,” Wes muttered. “Happy?”

Sam’s eyes softened. “Yes.”

Wes took a bite of the sandwich and realized it was unfairly good. Egg, cheese, a little pepper, the bread toasted just enough to crunch.

Wes glared around the bakery as if someone could be blamed for how safe he suddenly felt.

Sam leaned his hip against the counter, close but not too close, and took a sip of his coffee.

Wes chewed and tried not to stare at Sam’s mouth.

He failed.

Sam’s gaze caught him.

Wes immediately looked down at his sandwich like it had committed a crime.

Sam’s voice was quiet. “I’m walking the tree delivery route.”

Wes swallowed. “Right. The thing you said you were doing.”

Sam nodded. “Noel is meeting Eben at Merryweather Hall.”

Wes snorted around a bite. “That poor man.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Noel or Eben.”

“Both,” Wes said promptly. “Noel needs to sit down and Eben needs to stop looking like he’s about to file everyone as a liability.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “Eben’s not wrong.”

Wes swallowed and took another bite, then paused. “So you’re… helping them.”

Sam’s voice stayed steady. “I’m helping the festival.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “That’s not an answer.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “I’m helping you.”

Wes’s throat tightened.

Wes stared at his sandwich because if he looked at Sam too long, something was going to crack and then Wes would have to deal with it, and Wes did not have time for emotional revelations before dawn.

Wes swallowed hard. “Why,” he asked again, quieter this time. Realer.

Sam didn’t answer right away.

When he did, his voice was low. “Because you take care of everyone.”

Wes’s fingers tightened around the wrapper.

Sam continued, calm as ever. “You feed the town. You show up for every committee event. You make extra trays when someone’s short. You stay late so other people can go home.” Sam paused. “And you don’t let anyone take care of you.”

Wes’s chest ached.

Wes tried to scoff. It came out weak. “I don’t need taking care of.”

Sam’s gaze didn’t waver. “Yes, you do.”

Wes’s breath hitched.

Sam’s voice softened, just a fraction. “That doesn’t make you weak.”

Wes’s throat tightened. His eyes burned.

Wes hated that the words were working.

Wes swallowed hard and took another bite, because chewing was safer than crying.

Sam watched him, steady.

Wes finished the sandwich more slowly this time. On purpose. Like he was proving something to himself. Like he could receive without immediately turning it into fuel for more work.

When he was done, Wes set the wrapper down and exhaled.

“Congratulations,” Wes muttered. “I have eaten. You can put a gold star on my forehead.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “I might.”

Wes’s pulse stumbled. “Don’t.”

Sam’s eyes warmed, amused. “Okay.”

Wes stared at him. “You say okay like you mean it.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “I do.”

Wes’s breath caught again.

He leaned his elbows on the counter and rubbed his face with both hands like he could physically wipe away his feelings.

When he lowered his hands, Sam was still watching him.

Wes’s voice came out quiet. “Are you going to do this every morning.”

Sam’s brows lifted slightly. “Do what.”

Wes gestured vaguely between them. “Show up. Feed me. Make me sit down. Ruin my ability to pretend I’m fine.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “If you want me to.”

Wes went still.

There it was again. The choice. The permission. The fact that Sam wasn’t forcing his way into Wes’s life. He was just… offering a hand and letting Wes decide whether to take it.

Wes stared at Sam for a long beat.

Then, because Wes Candle was apparently determined to terrify himself, he said, “Okay.”

Sam’s expression didn’t change much, but something in his eyes warmed like a light turning on.

“Okay,” Sam echoed.

Wes swallowed. “But we set rules.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Rules.”

Wes nodded, finding structure like it was a life raft. “Yes. You don’t get to tell anyone about the raccoon.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Agreed.”

“And you don’t get to rearrange my kitchen like you live there.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “I didn’t rearrange anything.”

Wes pointed. “You found a knife without asking. That’s basically moving in.”

Sam’s eyes stayed amused. “Noted.”

“And,” Wes added, voice going quieter, more serious despite himself, “you don’t get to… make fun of me if I’m not okay sometimes.”

Sam’s amusement faded into something steady and real. “I wouldn’t.”

Wes’s throat tightened again. “Promise.”

Sam’s gaze held his like it was a vow. “I promise.”

Wes exhaled, shaky, and looked away before he did something stupid like believe it too hard.

A sound came from downstairs. A faint click, like something shifting in the bakery.

Wes’s head snapped up.

Sam’s posture changed instantly, alert. “What was that.”

Wes frowned. “Probably the ovens settling.”

The click came again.

Wes’s stomach dropped. “Or.”

Sam moved without a word, stepping around the counter with quiet speed. His hand went to his belt out of habit, then stopped. No gun. Just a cop’s muscle memory.

Wes stared. “If that’s another raccoon, I’m moving.”

Sam shot him a look. “Stay here.”

Wes opened his mouth to argue.

Sam’s gaze cut to him, calm but firm, and Wes’s body did the same thing it had done last night.

It listened.

Wes froze, heart pounding.

Sam stepped toward the back hallway, silent as winter.

Wes clutched the coffee cup in both hands like it could be weaponized.

The click came again.

Then a familiar, irritated voice called from the pantry area, “Wes! Why is there a broom booby-trapped against the door like you’re defending a castle?”

Wes’s eyes widened.

“Oh no,” Wes whispered.

Sam paused mid-step, brows lifting.

Wes’s shoulders slumped with immediate dread. “That,” Wes said softly, “is Hugh Everhart.”

Sam’s gaze flicked back to Wes. “You have a man trapped in your pantry.”

Wes closed his eyes. “It’s worse. I have Hugh trapped in my pantry.”

Hugh’s voice rose, offended. “I heard that!”

Wes muttered, “Of course you did.”

Sam’s mouth tipped, almost. “Do you want me to arrest him.”

Wes opened his eyes and stared at Sam.

For one beautiful second, Wes imagined it.

Then Wes sighed. “No. But if he has glitter, you’re allowed to use force.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Noted.”

Wes watched Sam head down the hall, then, because his life was apparently a holiday sitcom now, Wes called, “Hugh! If you break anything, you’re replacing it with something that isn’t covered in rhinestones!”

Hugh’s voice came back immediately. “I would never! Also, why is Sam Rook in your bakery at dawn?”

Wes froze.

Sam froze.

The air went dangerously still.

Wes stared at the hallway, then down at his bare feet, then at the empty sandwich wrapper, like any of those things would help.

Sam’s voice, calm but edged with amusement, drifted back. “Good morning, Hugh.”

Hugh made a delighted sound. “Oh my God. It’s true.”

Wes put his head on the counter. “I hate this town.”

Sam’s voice came softer, closer now. “No, you don’t.”

Wes’s cheeks heated. He didn’t lift his head. “Shut up.”

Sam’s low chuckle warmed the air like cocoa.

Wes stayed very still, heart pounding, realizing two terrible things at once:

One: Sam Rook sounded *good* when he laughed.

Two: Wes Candle was going to let him keep doing it.

**[STOP WRITING]**



Wes kept his forehead on the counter like it could absorb humiliation through osmosis.

Behind him, footsteps approached with the calm inevitability of consequences.

Sam’s presence stopped just beside Wes, close enough that Wes could feel the warmth of him in the chilly bakery air. Sam didn’t touch him. He didn’t tease. He just stood there like a guardrail.

Wes muttered into the laminate, “If Hugh is holding glitter, I’m setting the building on fire and collecting the insurance.”

Sam’s voice was low. “Arson is not festive.”

Wes lifted his head an inch. “It is if it’s symbolic.”

Before Sam could respond, Hugh Everhart emerged from the hallway like a man making an entrance on a stage he fully believed belonged to him.

Hugh was bundled in a bright scarf that looked handmade and aggressively cheerful. His hair was a bit wild, his cheeks pink from the cold, and he was carrying… a box.

Wes stared. “Why are you here.”

Hugh blinked innocently. “Good morning to you too, sunshine.”

Wes pointed at the clock. “It is not morning. It is criminal o’clock.”

Hugh set the box on the counter with a reverent little thump. “I came to save you.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “From what.”

Hugh looked genuinely baffled. “From yourself.”

Sam made a small sound that might’ve been a cough. It also might’ve been a laugh he swallowed.

Wes’s cheeks went hot again. “Why were you in my pantry.”

Hugh waved a hand. “The window in the utility closet was open. I saw the light on. I assumed you’d died.”

Wes stared. “That is… not a normal assumption.”

Hugh shrugged. “This is Tinsel Town. Dramatic interpretations are a civic duty.”

Sam’s gaze slid to Wes. “You left the window unlatched.”

Wes glared at him. “Don’t start.”

Hugh’s eyes darted between them, interest sharpening like a cat spotting a laser pointer. “Okay,” Hugh said slowly. “So Sam is here because of the window. And you are wearing a hoodie with no shoes. And you are blushing.”

Wes lunged across the counter and slapped a hand down on the box. “What is this.”

Hugh smiled like he’d been waiting for the moment. “A care package.”

Wes stared. “A what.”

Hugh lifted the lid.

Inside were: a thermos, two neatly wrapped muffins, a jar of something that looked like homemade jam, and a small tin labeled in Hugh’s glittery handwriting:

**SLEEP TEA. DRINK IT OR I’LL CRY.**

Wes blinked.

Sam’s gaze flicked to the tin, then to Wes, something unreadable softening in his eyes.

Hugh leaned in. “I am conducting unofficial wellness checks on Noel’s entire committee because if you people collapse, the festival collapses, and if the festival collapses, I will have to emotionally process disappointment, which I refuse to do.”

Wes swallowed. “That’s… almost kind.”

Hugh beamed. “Thank you.”

Wes rubbed his face. “Why muffins.”

Hugh looked offended. “Because you cannot survive on cookie scraps and resentment.”

Sam’s mouth tipped.

Wes shot him a look. “Stop finding this funny.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “I’m not.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “You are.”

Sam didn’t deny it. Which was honestly worse.

Hugh’s gaze flicked again between them, bright with curiosity. “So,” Hugh said, too casually, “are we talking about you two, or are we still pretending Sam just wanders into bakeries at dawn for civic enrichment.”

Wes pointed toward the door. “Out.”

Hugh clutched his chest. “Excuse you.”

“Out,” Wes repeated. “Before I weaponize frosting.”

Sam stepped in smoothly, voice calm. “Hugh.”

Hugh turned to Sam like he’d been summoned by name. “Yes, Officer.”

Sam’s expression was neutral. “Go help Noel.”

Hugh blinked. “Why.”

Sam didn’t raise his voice. He didn’t threaten. He just looked at Hugh with the calm authority of a man who could end a parade with a single sentence.

Hugh sighed dramatically. “Fine. I will go rescue Noel from his martyr complex.”

Wes blinked. “He’s meeting Eben at seven.”

Hugh pointed, delighted. “SEE? You know Noel’s schedule too!”

Wes hissed, “I do not.”

Hugh bounced on his toes. “You do. You’re caring.”

Wes’s cheeks burned. “I’m… adjacent to caring.”

Hugh waved his scarf like it was a flag. “Anyway! I will leave you to your… *breakfast situation.*”

Wes’s glare sharpened. “Hugh.”

Hugh paused at the door, hand on the knob, eyes sparkling with mischief. “Yes?”

Wes pointed at the tin. “No glitter.”

Hugh gasped. “I would never glitter your tea.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “Hugh.”

Hugh held up two fingers. “Scout’s honor.”

Hugh slipped out into the cold with a last little grin at Sam, then disappeared down the stairs like a rumor escaping.

The bakery fell quiet again.

Wes stared at the closed door, then exhaled. “That man is going to get me killed.”

Sam’s voice was low, amused. “Probably.”

Wes turned back to the counter and stared at the care package like it was evidence in a case titled *Why Are People Being Nice To Me, This Is Suspicious.*

Sam picked up his coffee and took a sip, gaze still on Wes.

Wes shifted, uncomfortable under the attention. “You didn’t have to bring me breakfast,” he muttered.

Sam’s eyes held his. “I wanted to.”

Wes’s throat tightened again. He hated how easy Sam made that sound. Like wanting to take care of Wes wasn’t something Sam had to justify.

Wes cleared his throat, reaching for the thermos in Hugh’s box like it was safer than looking at Sam. “So. Tree route.”

Sam nodded once. “I’m going to walk past Evergreen Square, then up to Merryweather Hall. Check the streets, check the curb clearance, see where the truck can turn.”

Wes made a face. “You really do plan for everything.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “It’s my job.”

Wes hesitated. “And you… you’re going to keep checking on me.”

Sam didn’t flinch. “If you want me to.”

Wes stared at him for a long beat.

He hated that the answer came so fast.

“Yeah,” Wes said quietly. “I want you to.”

Sam’s eyes warmed in a way that made Wes’s ribs feel too small for his heart.

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “Okay.”

Wes swallowed. “But we need more rules.”

Sam’s brows lifted. “More.”

“Yes,” Wes insisted, because structure was how you survived feelings. “Rule one: you don’t get to tell anyone you found me barefoot and pathetic.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “You’re not pathetic.”

Wes pointed. “Rule one.”

Sam nodded. “Okay. I won’t tell anyone.”

Wes nodded sharply, like he’d secured a major victory. “Rule two: you don’t get to stare at me like that.”

Sam’s gaze sharpened with quiet amusement. “Like what.”

Wes gestured at his own face. “Like I’m… like I’m the most important thing in the room.”

Sam didn’t answer right away.

Then, very evenly, he said, “You are.”

Wes froze.

The room went still in a way that wasn’t awkward. It was heavy. It was warm. It was dangerous.

Wes’s voice came out rough. “You can’t just say that.”

Sam’s gaze didn’t move. “Why not.”

Wes swallowed hard. “Because then I might believe you.”

Sam’s expression softened, just barely. “Good.”

Wes stared at him, heart pounding, and for a second he couldn’t find a joke. Couldn’t find a deflection. Couldn’t find anything except the truth sitting in his chest like a fresh loaf rising.

Wes’s fingers curled against the counter edge. “Sam.”

Sam’s eyes held his. “Yeah.”

Wes didn’t know what he was going to say.

He just knew he wanted to say *something*.

Sam stepped closer, slow, deliberate, giving Wes time to back away. Time to make it a joke. Time to pull the usual stunt of turning tenderness into sarcasm.

Wes didn’t move.

Sam stopped within arm’s reach. Close enough that Wes could smell coffee and winter. Close enough that Wes’s body leaned forward on its own, traitor that it was.

Sam’s voice went lower. “Do you want me to go.”

Wes blinked. “What.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “Do you want space.”

Wes’s throat tightened. He forced honesty because Sam seemed to deserve it.

“No,” Wes whispered.

Sam’s eyes warmed, but his posture stayed careful. “Okay.”

Wes swallowed. The air between them felt charged, like the bakery lights were suddenly too bright.

Wes’s voice came out small. “Do you… do you kiss people you’re doing wellness checks on.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. A real smile this time, brief and soft. “Not usually.”

Wes’s pulse jumped. “But.”

Sam’s gaze flicked to Wes’s mouth, then back to his eyes. “But I’m not here because of the mayor.”

Wes’s breath caught. “Then why.”

Sam’s voice was steady, like a promise he’d decided to keep. “Because I kept noticing you. And I didn’t want to keep pretending I didn’t.”

Wes stared at him, throat tight, and realized he was standing on the edge of something.

Something he wanted.

Something that scared the hell out of him.

Wes swallowed. “If you kiss me,” Wes said, voice shaking a little despite his best efforts, “I’m going to make it weird.”

Sam’s eyes softened with warmth. “I can handle weird.”

Wes exhaled, a shaky laugh. “You say that now.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “Wes.”

Wes went still at his name.

Sam didn’t touch him. He didn’t rush him. He just waited, giving Wes the last choice.

Wes stared at him for one long beat.

Then he whispered, “Okay.”

Sam’s expression didn’t change much, but something in his eyes warmed like a light turning on.

Sam leaned in slowly.

Wes met him halfway, because of course he did. Of course Wes Candle, who fed the entire town and pretended he didn’t need anyone, couldn’t resist closing the distance when someone finally asked properly.

Sam’s mouth brushed Wes’s, soft at first. Testing. Asking.

Wes made a small sound that was not dignified and tugged Sam closer by the front of his coat.

Sam’s hand lifted and settled gently at Wes’s jaw, thumb stroking once, grounding him.

The kiss deepened, still slow, still careful, like Sam was building something rather than taking it.

Wes’s knees went weak in the most humiliating way.

Sam pulled back just enough to breathe against Wes’s mouth. “Still okay,” Sam murmured.

Wes swallowed, eyes half-lidded, cheeks hot. “Yes.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “Good.”

Wes’s body shivered at the praise like it had been waiting for that exact word.

Wes exhaled, forehead tipping forward until it rested briefly against Sam’s. “This is,” Wes whispered, “going to ruin my life.”

Sam’s voice was quiet and steady. “No.”

Wes blinked. “No?”

Sam’s hand stayed at Wes’s jaw, thumb still, anchoring. “It’s going to change it,” Sam said. “That’s not the same thing.”

Wes’s chest tightened. He hated that his eyes stung again.

Wes forced a shaky breath. “Okay,” he whispered, because apparently that was his new thing.

Sam’s gaze softened. “Okay.”

Outside, the morning light brightened, slow and gold.

Somewhere across town, Noel Merrick was probably sprinting toward Merryweather Hall with a clipboard and a prayer. Eben Frost was probably arriving early with a contingency plan and a face that said *humanity is optional.*

And Wes Candle was standing in his bakery, kissed raw and warm, realizing that maybe the Twelve Days weren’t going to just be about sugar and sponsors and schedules.

Maybe they were going to be about letting someone stay.

Sam’s phone buzzed in his pocket.

Sam didn’t move away immediately. He kept his forehead against Wes’s for one more beat, like he was memorizing the moment.

Then he stepped back and checked the screen.

Sam’s expression shifted, subtle but immediate.

Wes’s stomach dipped. “What.”

Sam’s voice went calm, but there was a new edge under it. “Tree truck just called it in.”

Wes blinked. “Called what in.”

Sam looked at him.

“Delivery delay,” Sam said. “Big one.”

Wes stared.

Then, because the universe had timing issues, Wes laughed. “Of course.”

Sam’s gaze stayed on him, steady and practical. “Noel’s going to panic.”

Wes grabbed his apron from the hook like it was armor. “Noel panics professionally.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “You’re coming.”

Wes froze. “Excuse me?”

Sam’s gaze held his, and Wes hated that his body reacted to the tone again. “You’re on the committee,” Sam said. “And you’re the only person who can bribe a volunteer mob with baked goods.”

Wes bristled. “That is not true.”

Sam lifted a brow.

Wes sighed. “Fine. It’s true.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Put shoes on.”

Wes grabbed his boots and stomped them on too fast, muttering, “This is why I don’t kiss cops before sunrise. It always leads to labor.”

Sam’s mouth tipped as he pulled his gloves back on. “You kissed me too.”

Wes glared. “Allegedly.”

Sam’s gaze warmed over his shoulder. “Wes.”

Wes’s chest tightened.

“Yeah,” Wes muttered.

Sam’s voice softened, just a fraction. “After we help Noel, you’re eating again.”

Wes stared. “I just ate.”

Sam’s eyes held his. “Later.”

Wes opened his mouth, then closed it.

Because arguing felt pointless.

Because… weirdly… being told to eat sounded like care now, not control.

Wes exhaled. “Fine,” he muttered. “But you’re not allowed to look smug about it.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Okay.”

Wes grabbed the muffins from Hugh’s box and shoved them into a bag. “Let’s go save Christmas,” Wes said, resigned.

Sam opened the bakery door. Cold air swept in.

Sam paused on the threshold and glanced back at Wes, eyes steady. “You good.”

Wes’s pulse jumped. “That was not a question.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “It was.”

Wes swallowed, then forced honesty like a man walking into weather without a coat.

“I’m… good,” Wes said quietly. “Yeah.”

Sam nodded once, like he’d secured the answer he needed.

Then he stepped into the snow, and Wes followed.

**[STOP WRITING]**





Can I keep writing **this story**? Yes.
Can I keep writing it as **graphic erotica**? No. I’m not here to generate explicit porn on demand, even if the raccoon gang insists it’s “for the art.”

What I *can* do is keep it **steamy, romantic, consent-forward, and very suggestive**, with **fade-to-black** when things would go explicit. Which, frankly, usually sells better in cozy-romcom land anyway.

Picking up exactly where we left off:

---

Snow hit Wes’s face like a judgmental aunt.

He pulled his hood up and followed Sam down the sidewalk, clutching the bag of muffins like it was both a bribe and a weapon. The town was waking slowly, lights still glowing in windows, the streetlamps turning the falling snow into glittering confetti.

Wes squinted at Sam’s broad back, the way his coat moved with each step, the calm speed of him. Sam walked like he belonged in the morning. Like he’d never woken up with flour in his hair and dread in his chest.

Wes trotted to keep up, boots crunching over salted pavement. “So,” Wes said, because silence was how feelings got you, “how bad is the delay.”

Sam didn’t look back. “Bad enough Noel’s going to invent new forms of panic.”

Wes huffed. “Noel’s panic is performance art.”

Sam’s mouth tipped, barely. “Eben won’t tolerate it.”

Wes pictured Eben Frost and his clipboard aura. “Eben doesn’t tolerate oxygen.”

Sam glanced back then, eyes warmed with amusement. “You met him.”

“Twice,” Wes said. “Once with his face. Once with his vibe.”

Sam’s pace didn’t change, but his voice softened the tiniest bit. “Noel needs him.”

Wes’s chest tightened unexpectedly. He glanced at Sam’s profile as they crossed Evergreen Square, the big tree already lit and proud in the center like it was personally offended by the idea of another tree arriving late.

Wes muttered, “Noel needs everyone. Noel collects responsibility like it’s seasonal décor.”

Sam’s gaze flicked to him. “And you don’t.”

Wes rolled his eyes. “I don’t collect responsibility. I curate it.”

Sam’s mouth tipped again. “You also don’t sit down.”

Wes pointed at the snow. “I am literally walking. That’s basically sitting for my soul.”

Sam made a quiet sound that might have been a laugh. It warmed Wes more than it had any right to.

They reached Merryweather Hall and Wes immediately knew things were going sideways.

A cluster of volunteers stood outside the front steps, bundled in scarves, holding clipboards and coffee cups and expressions that said **I was promised a festive morning and instead I got logistical despair**.

Noel stood at the center of them like a very pretty storm cloud in a peacoat.

His cheeks were red from cold, his hair slightly wind-tousled, and he was gesturing with a clipboard like it was a weapon.

Across from him, Eben Frost stood unnervingly calm, hands in his coat pockets, watching Noel like Noel was a system failure he was about to troubleshoot.

Wes slowed. “Oh. There’s the tension.”

Sam’s gaze sharpened. “That’s normal tension.”

Wes nodded gravely. “This town runs on tension.”

Noel spotted them and his face lit up with relief so sharp it looked like a betrayal of his pride.

Wes felt something soften in his chest, because Noel was annoying and loud and emotionally allergic to rest, but Noel was also the kind of person who remembered everyone’s birthdays and made sure volunteers had gloves.

Noel hurried down the steps. “Thank God,” Noel said, breath puffing white. “Wes. Sam.”

Wes lifted the muffin bag. “I brought bribes.”

Noel’s eyes almost teared up. “I love you.”

Wes pointed with a muffin. “Don’t get weird.”

Noel snatched the bag like it contained the last surviving joy in town. “No promises.”

Eben approached with the controlled grace of a man who would alphabetize a fire.

He stopped beside Noel and nodded at Wes. “Candle.”

Wes nodded back. “Frost.”

Eben’s gaze flicked to Sam. “Rook.”

Sam’s voice stayed even. “Frost.”

Wes looked between them. “This is like a spreadsheet met a police report.”

Noel made a distressed sound. “Okay. Emergency. The tree truck called. It’s stuck on Ridge Road. There’s ice, and apparently the driver ‘doesn’t feel safe’ which is fair, but also, I am going to dissolve into glitter and scream.”

Eben’s eyes narrowed slightly. “We will not be screaming.”

Noel’s jaw clenched. “We might.”

Eben’s gaze stayed on him. “Noel.”

Noel’s shoulders twitched like he was about to argue purely out of muscle memory.

Then Noel did something surprising.

He exhaled.

“Fine,” Noel said, too sharp, but he said it anyway. “No screaming. Yet.”

Eben’s posture eased by half an inch like Noel had just complied with a safety protocol.

Wes watched it happen, a little fascinated. Noel had a type. Eben was that type. Noel was going to suffer.

Sam spoke up, calm as ever. “Truck is stuck where.”

Noel shoved his phone out. “Ridge Road. Past the old covered bridge.”

Eben nodded once, taking control without making it a fight. “We need to reroute. We need a smaller vehicle to bring the ornaments and base supplies. We need a tow option, or a tractor.”

Wes blinked. “A tractor.”

Noel pointed at Wes like he’d been waiting for this moment. “Harold Gibbons.”

Wes sighed. “Of course.”

Sam looked mildly impressed. “You have a tractor guy.”

Wes looked at Sam. “We have a guy for everything. This is a small town. People specialize.”

Eben’s gaze flicked to the volunteers. “We keep them here. We give them tasks that feel useful.”

Noel nodded fast. “Yes. Yes. Usefulness. The town’s love language.”

Wes held up the muffin bag Noel had stolen. “And muffins.”

Noel looked at the volunteers like he was about to deliver a sermon. “Okay, everyone! Slight delay! But we have muffins and an updated schedule and I’m not panicking!”

One volunteer squinted. “You look like you’re panicking.”

Noel smiled too brightly. “I’m radiating holiday spirit.”

Wes leaned in to Sam, voice low. “He’s lying.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “I noticed.”

Eben turned to Noel. “Assign them to indoor prep. Garland sorting. Stage setup. Cocoa station. Anything that moves the plan forward.”

Noel nodded, already shifting into command mode. “Right. Yes. Cocoa station. Glitter-free cocoa station.”

Wes raised a hand. “I can take two volunteers and start the cocoa station.”

Noel grabbed his arm. “Bless you.”

Wes stared at Noel’s hand on his sleeve. Then at Sam, whose gaze had sharpened in a way that made Wes’s stomach flip. Not angry. Not possessive. Just… attentive.

Noel let go quickly, oblivious. “Okay! Sam, Eben, you go do… man things. Tractor things. Ice things. Don’t die.”

Eben nodded once. “We won’t.”

Sam said, “We’ll handle it.”

Noel turned to Wes, lowering his voice like this was confidential. “Can you keep them occupied so they don’t riot.”

Wes glanced at the volunteers. One of them already had her arms crossed in the universal posture of *I woke up early for this.*

Wes sighed. “I’ll distract them with sugar and false purpose.”

Noel squeezed Wes’s shoulder like Wes was a life raft. “Thank you.”

Then Noel spun back to the group, voice bright. “Everyone inside! We’re making the cocoa station the cutest thing you’ve ever seen!”

The volunteers moved, grumbling but interested. Muffins were strong propaganda.

Wes started to follow, then felt Sam’s presence beside him.

Sam leaned in slightly, voice low enough that only Wes could hear. “You okay.”

Wes blinked. “You’re copying Eben now?”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Answer the question.”

Wes’s throat tightened. He glanced at Sam’s eyes, steady and warm, and felt his own defenses wobble.

“I’m… fine,” Wes started automatically.

Sam’s brow lifted a fraction.

Wes exhaled. “I’m good,” he corrected, quieter. “Just… not used to this.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “Being helped.”

Wes swallowed. “Being… seen.”

Sam didn’t move, didn’t push, just held Wes’s gaze like it was safe to do so in the middle of a chaotic Christmas crisis.

Sam’s voice was low. “I see you.”

Wes’s chest tightened so hard he almost laughed.

Wes muttered, “This is going to make me insufferable.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Too late.”

Wes snorted, grateful for the joke. “Go do your tractor ice thing.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “I’ll be back.”

Wes’s pulse jumped. “That’s not necessary.”

Sam’s gaze didn’t waver. “It is.”

Wes stared at him for a beat, then nodded once, because apparently that was his new thing too.

Sam started to turn, then paused. His gaze dipped briefly to Wes’s mouth and back up, quick as a thought.

“Later,” Sam said, voice quiet.

Wes’s breath caught. “Later,” he echoed, even quieter.

Sam left with Eben, their coats disappearing into the snow toward the parking lot.

Wes watched them go for one stupid second too long, then shook himself and marched inside to face the only thing scarier than feelings.

Volunteer management.

Inside, Merryweather Hall buzzed with that frantic pre-festival energy. Noel was already assigning tasks with the desperate cheer of a man holding a dam together with tape.

Wes grabbed two volunteers, pointed them toward the cocoa supplies, and started building a station that looked festive enough to distract people from the fact that Christmas itself was currently stuck on Ridge Road.

As he worked, Wes kept catching himself looking toward the door, listening for footsteps.

Waiting.

Because apparently Sam Rook had kissed him once and now Wes’s brain was doing the most embarrassing thing possible.

It was hoping.

**[STOP WRITING]**

---

If you want the next chunk, I’ll take it into: **Sam/Eben on the ice road** (competence + banter), then **Sam returns** (small moment), then **Wes’s first real “I want” admission** (still cozy, still low stakes, still delicious).




Cool. That’s the human equivalent of “the motor is running, I guess.” So I’ll keep rolling.

---

Sam’s truck smelled faintly of coffee and clean upholstery, like Sam had opinions about not living in chaos.

Eben sat in the passenger seat with a clipboard balanced on his knee like it was a weapon. He didn’t fidget. He didn’t sigh. He didn’t do anything except stare out the windshield at the snow-slick road like he was judging it personally.

Sam kept both hands on the wheel, calm and steady as the heater fought to turn winter into something survivable.

After three minutes of silence, Eben said, “Ridge Road is always a problem.”

Sam’s eyes stayed forward. “Because people drive it like they’re immortal.”

Eben nodded once. “Correct.”

Sam glanced sideways. “You’re not from here.”

Eben’s gaze didn’t move. “No.”

Sam waited.

Eben added, like it explained everything, “I worked municipal logistics in three cities that hated joy.”

Sam’s mouth tipped, barely. “This town loves joy.”

Eben’s tone was flat. “That is why it requires more containment.”

Sam snorted softly. “You and Noel are going to be a spectacle.”

Eben’s eyes flicked to him. “Noel panics loudly.”

Sam’s gaze stayed forward. “And you don’t panic at all.”

Eben’s jaw tightened slightly, like Sam had stepped near a boundary. “I plan.”

Sam didn’t push. He just nodded. “He needs that.”

Eben’s gaze drifted to the snow swirling past the headlights. “He needs rest.”

Sam’s hands tightened on the wheel for half a second. “Yeah.”

They drove in silence again, but it was different now. Not cold. Just… focused. Two men who understood responsibility like it was a second language.

The truck turned onto Ridge Road, and immediately the world shifted.

The snow was thicker here, the road narrower, the trees leaning in like they were listening. Ice glittered along the edges where the plows had missed. The kind of road that made you instinctively stop talking.

Sam slowed, tires crunching.

Ahead, hazard lights blinked in the white: the tree truck, angled slightly, half on the shoulder, like it had given up.

A man in a high-vis jacket stood beside it, waving his arms in the universal sign for *I regret all my decisions*.

Sam pulled in behind the truck and set the parking brake.

Eben was already unbuckling. “We need traction. Salt. Tow. Or a tractor.”

Sam opened the door and stepped out into the cold. The air hit like a slap.

The driver hurried over. “I’m not taking this thing another inch,” he said, voice tight. “It slid twice. The trailer’s swinging. If I lose it, it’s going in the ditch.”

Sam nodded once. “You did the right thing stopping.”

The man blinked, visibly startled by calm. “I did?”

Sam’s voice stayed steady. “Yeah. Nobody needs a headline about a Christmas tree dying on Ridge Road.”

The driver let out a shaky laugh. “Thank you.”

Eben stepped closer, gaze sweeping the scene. “What’s your last stable point.”

The driver frowned. “My what.”

Eben pointed with the clipboard like he was conducting. “Where the truck felt safe. Where you last had full control.”

The driver stared at him, then nodded slowly. “Back at the turn. Near the covered bridge.”

Eben’s jaw tightened. “So we’re stuck between two bad choices.”

Sam looked down the road at the glazed surface, then at the slope near the shoulder. “We need a pull from the front. Gentle. Controlled.”

The driver spread his hands. “I called dispatch. Tow is an hour out.”

Eben’s voice was flat. “That’s too long. Volunteers will freeze and riot.”

Sam’s phone buzzed again.

Sam checked it. A text from Noel:
**NOEL:** *I’m being brave. How long. I need facts before I invent doom.*
Sam typed with his thumb:
**SAM:** *We’re here. You’re fine. Start indoor tasks. Bribe with muffins. ETA unknown, working it.*

Eben watched Sam type, then looked at the driver. “Do you have chains.”

The driver grimaced. “On the truck. Trailer doesn’t.”

Eben nodded like this was the worst kind of answer. “Of course it doesn’t.”

Sam blew out a breath. “We need Harold Gibbons.”

Eben turned slightly. “The tractor.”

Sam nodded. “The tractor.”

Eben’s gaze sharpened. “Call him.”

Sam already had his phone out.

---

Harold Gibbons picked up on the third ring, sounding like he’d been awake since 4 a.m. out of pure stubbornness.

“Rook.”

Sam didn’t waste time. “Harold, we need the tractor on Ridge Road. Tree truck’s stuck.”

A pause. Then Harold’s voice, warm with righteous purpose. “Finally. I been waiting all year for someone to admit they need me.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Can you come now.”

Harold snorted. “Son, I’m already in my boots.”

---

Eben exhaled through his nose, a sound that was not quite relief and not quite annoyance. “This town is… strange.”

Sam glanced at him. “You’re not wrong.”

Eben’s gaze shifted back to the truck. “When Harold arrives, we need the tractor to pull the trailer straight. Minimal angle. No sudden torque.”

Sam’s brow lifted. “You know tractors.”

Eben’s expression didn’t change. “I know physics. Tractors are just stubborn physics.”

Sam huffed a quiet laugh.

Eben looked at him, faintly surprised.

Sam didn’t stop smiling. It was brief, but it happened.

Eben stared like he’d witnessed an eclipse.

Then Eben’s gaze slid away again, voice dry. “Don’t get comfortable. This is still a municipal disaster.”

Sam’s smile faded into something softer. “Yeah.”

---

Twenty minutes later, the tractor arrived like a myth.

Harold Gibbons rolled up in a battered machine that sounded like it had eaten a muffler and decided it liked the taste. He wore a thick coat, a plaid hat, and the satisfied expression of a man who’d been proven right by the universe.

He hopped down and stomped through the snow. “Which one of you is in charge.”

Eben stepped forward without hesitation. “Me.”

Harold squinted at him. “You look like a man who alphabetizes his socks.”

Eben’s tone was flat. “Do you want this tree in town or not.”

Harold grinned, delighted. “I like you.”

Sam watched, amused, as Eben explained the pull angle with crisp precision while Harold nodded like he’d found his people.

The tractor hooked up.

The pull began.

Slow. Controlled. The trailer eased forward like it didn’t want to admit it had been scared.

The driver exhaled so hard he practically deflated.

Sam watched the rig straighten, then checked his phone.

He texted Noel:
**SAM:** *Tree is moving. Tractor miracle. You can stop imagining doom (for now).*

Noel replied instantly:
**NOEL:** *I imagined doom for sport. Proud of you. Tell Eben he’s terrifying but useful.*

Sam pocketed the phone.

Eben glanced at him. “Update.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Noel says you’re terrifying but useful.”

Eben nodded once, like he’d been given a performance review. “Acceptable.”

Sam couldn’t help it. He laughed, quiet and real.

Eben looked mildly offended by the concept of laughter in a crisis.

Then Eben said, very evenly, “We should return before Noel starts making contingency banners.”

Sam nodded. “Yeah.”

But as Sam climbed back into the truck, he found his thoughts tugging toward Candle & Crumb.

Toward Wes.

Toward the way Wes had said “Okay” like it cost him something.

Sam started the engine and drove back toward town, steady hands on the wheel, calm face, heart doing something annoyingly unprofessional.

---

Back at Merryweather Hall, the doors opened to a blast of warmth and noise.

Volunteers were inside, sorting garlands, setting up tables, arguing about ribbon color like it was state policy. The cocoa station was half-built and already charming.

Wes Candle stood behind it in an apron, hair still chaotic, cheeks flushed from exertion and heat. He was gesturing with a ladle like he was conducting an orchestra.

Wes looked up.

He saw Sam.

And the smallest thing happened: Wes’s shoulders dropped.

Like his body had been waiting for Sam to come back before it believed the world was stable.

Sam felt it in his chest, sharp and quiet.

He crossed the room.

Wes tried to look unimpressed and failed. “So you didn’t die.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Disappointing, I know.”

Wes’s eyes warmed. “I had a whole speech ready.”

Sam stopped beside the cocoa station. “Tree’s moving. Harold did tractor magic.”

Wes exhaled hard. “Bless Harold Gibbons.”

Sam’s gaze held Wes’s for a beat too long. “You ate.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “That is not the first thing you say after a crisis.”

Sam’s voice stayed steady. “It is to you.”

Wes blinked, caught.

Wes cleared his throat, reaching for a mug like it was safer than the way Sam was looking at him. “So, um. Cocoa.”

Sam’s eyes flicked to the mug, then back. “Are you offering.”

Wes huffed. “I am bribing you. It’s different.”

Sam took the mug. Their fingers brushed.

Wes went still.

Sam didn’t move away.

Wes’s voice came out quieter. “You came back.”

Sam’s expression softened. “I said I would.”

Wes swallowed. Then, like it slipped out before he could stop it, he said, “Good.”

Sam’s pulse kicked.

Sam leaned in slightly, voice low enough that only Wes could hear. “You doing okay.”

Wes stared at him, cheeks warming, eyes steady in a way that looked like he was trying to be brave.

Then Wes said, quiet and honest, “I’m… better when you’re here.”

Sam held still. No jokes. No teasing. Just the truth hanging between them like a light.

Sam nodded once, slow. “Okay.”

Wes let out a shaky breath and looked away, muttering, “Don’t make it a thing.”

Sam took a sip of cocoa. “Too late.”

Wes’s mouth twitched, almost a smile.

Outside, a distant engine rumbled. The tree truck arriving, finally.

Inside, Noel’s voice rose in delighted relief, and the volunteers cheered like Christmas had been personally rescued.

Wes looked at Sam over the rim of his ladle, eyes soft.

Sam looked back, steady.

Low stakes, sure.

But Wes Candle’s heart was starting to feel like the biggest thing in the room.

**[STOP WRITING]**



Tree trucks have a special talent: arriving late and acting like they did you a favor.

The rig rolled into Evergreen Square with hazard lights blinking, Harold Gibbons trundling behind it like a triumphant chaperone. The crowd’s cheer rose up in a warm wave, volunteers clapping and laughing like they hadn’t just spent an hour fantasizing about arson and mutiny.

Noel appeared at the doors of Merryweather Hall like a man summoned by chaos.

He burst outside, clipboard in hand, scarf flung over one shoulder, and practically jogged down the steps toward the arriving truck.

“YOU’RE HERE!” Noel shouted.

The driver leaned out the window. “We’re here!”

Noel pointed at the tree like it was personally accountable. “You’re LATE!”

The driver shrugged helplessly. “We’re alive!”

Noel looked like he wanted to argue with mortality, then took a breath like Eben had trained him to.

“No screaming,” Noel muttered to himself.

Eben stepped up beside Noel, calm and unimpressed by everyone’s emotional volume. “We proceed,” Eben said, like it wasn’t a miracle and more of a scheduled occurrence. “Unload. Secure. Crane.”

Noel nodded fast. “Yes. Yes. Proceed. You heard the terrifying man, everybody. Proceed.”

Wes watched from inside the hall, ladle in hand, cocoa steam curling around his face like a warm excuse.

Sam stood beside the cocoa station with his mug, watching the square the way he watched everything. Calm. Protective. Like he could hold the whole morning steady with just his gaze.

Wes tried not to stare at Sam’s mouth while Sam sipped cocoa.

He failed, again.

Sam’s eyes flicked to him.

Wes immediately looked at the marshmallows like they were fascinating. “So,” Wes said, because silence was dangerous and also because he was apparently incapable of not poking at things, “you’re just… staying.”

Sam’s gaze stayed on the window, but his voice was low. “For now.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “For what.”

Sam finally looked at him, eyes steady. “For you.”

Wes blinked.

That landed too clean. Too direct. Like Sam was building a house out of honest statements and Wes was still standing there holding a match.

Wes scoffed to recover. “You’re going to get bored. I’m not that interesting. I’m mostly carbohydrates and coping mechanisms.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “I disagree.”

Wes’s cheeks warmed. He pretended to fuss with the cocoa cups. “Sure you do.”

Sam’s voice was quiet. “Wes.”

Wes froze at his name.

Sam didn’t touch him. He didn’t crowd him. He just waited like last time, like Wes got to choose what happened next.

Wes swallowed. “What.”

Sam nodded toward the door. “Come outside for a minute.”

Wes’s pulse jumped. “Why.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “Because you’ve been vibrating like you’re about to bolt.”

Wes opened his mouth to deny it, but his body betrayed him by… continuing to vibrate.

Wes huffed. “Fine. One minute. If anyone steals the cocoa, I’m pressing charges.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “I’ll guard it.”

Wes stared at him. “You’re literally a cop.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Exactly.”

Wes rolled his eyes and followed Sam through the hall and out the side door, stepping into the cold behind Merryweather Hall where it was quieter. Snow muffled everything. The sound of the crowd was distant, softened.

Wes crossed his arms, breath puffing white. “Okay. We’re outside. I’m outdoors-ing.”

Sam stood close but not too close, shoulders blocking some of the wind without making a big deal of it. He took a slow breath and watched Wes’s face.

“You’re doing a lot,” Sam said.

Wes scoffed automatically. “I’m doing normal.”

Sam didn’t blink. “You’re doing too much.”

Wes’s mouth tightened. “We’re all doing too much. It’s Christmas.”

Sam’s gaze sharpened gently. “You use that like an excuse.”

Wes opened his mouth.

Then closed it again.

Because yeah.

Wes exhaled, long and slow, trying to get his ribs to stop feeling tight. “I don’t know how to… not.”

Sam’s voice stayed steady. “You don’t have to know how. You just have to let someone help.”

Wes looked away, jaw clenched. “People helping is messy.”

Sam nodded once. “Sometimes.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “And then they leave.”

The words slipped out before Wes could stop them.

He hated that. He hated the silence that followed. He hated that he’d given away something soft like he was handing Sam a knife.

Sam didn’t rush. He didn’t try to fix it with a speech. He just stood there, eyes on Wes, and let the truth exist without punishing Wes for saying it.

Then Sam said, very quietly, “I’m not leaving.”

Wes’s breath caught.

Wes let out a short laugh that sounded wrong. “You don’t know that.”

Sam’s gaze didn’t waver. “I do.”

Wes stared at him, heart pounding like it wanted to fight for its life.

Wes whispered, “Why.”

Sam’s voice was calm, but there was something warmer under it now. “Because I like being around you.”

Wes’s cheeks burned. “That’s not a real reason.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “It’s a better reason than the mayor.”

Wes swallowed hard and looked down at the snow by his boots. His fingers curled tight inside his sleeves.

“This is going to get complicated,” Wes muttered.

Sam’s gaze softened. “Maybe.”

Wes’s voice went even quieter. “And I’m bad at complicated.”

Sam took a slow step closer, careful, giving Wes time to move back.

Wes didn’t.

Sam stopped within reach, eyes steady. “Then we don’t do complicated,” Sam said. “We do simple.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “Simple like… what.”

Sam’s gaze flicked to Wes’s mouth and back to his eyes. “Simple like I check on you. You let me. We take it one day at a time.”

Wes stared at him, chest aching in a way that felt unfair.

Then Wes said, quiet and stubborn, “And the rules.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Rules.”

Wes nodded fast, grateful for structure. “Yes. Rules. Like… no public chaos.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Hugh will violate that immediately.”

Wes huffed a laugh, relief cracking through. “Okay, fine. Minimal public chaos.”

Sam nodded. “Agreed.”

Wes swallowed. “And… you don’t get to make me promise things I can’t keep.”

Sam’s expression softened, serious now. “I won’t.”

Wes blinked, throat tight. “Promise.”

Sam’s gaze held his like a vow. “I promise.”

Wes exhaled, shaky.

Sam’s hand lifted, slow. He didn’t touch Wes. He hovered, waiting.

“May I,” Sam asked.

Wes’s pulse skittered. He nodded once. “Yeah.”

Sam’s fingers settled lightly at the back of Wes’s neck, warm through the hoodie. The touch was gentle and grounding, like Sam was anchoring Wes back into his own body.

Wes made a small sound in his throat that he would absolutely deny in court.

Sam leaned in and kissed him again, soft and brief. A promise, not a demand.

Wes’s hands fisted in Sam’s coat automatically, like his body was done pretending it didn’t want this.

Sam pulled back just enough to murmur, “Still okay.”

Wes’s voice came out wrecked. “Yes.”

Sam’s thumb stroked once at Wes’s neck, and Wes shivered.

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Good.”

Wes hated that word now. Hated how it lit him up and calmed him down at the same time.
