Wes swallowed, forehead tipping forward until it rested against Sam’s for a second. “If you keep doing that,” Wes whispered, “I’m going to start expecting it.”

Sam’s voice was quiet and steady. “Okay.”

Wes blinked. “That’s not…”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “It’s an answer.”

Wes stared at him, then let out a breath that turned into a reluctant laugh. “You’re ridiculous.”

Sam’s gaze stayed on him. “Yeah.”

Behind them, a cheer erupted as the crane finally lifted the massive tree upright in the square.

Wes turned his head slightly to watch, and Sam stayed close, hand still at Wes’s neck, like he wasn’t letting go just because the town needed its centerpiece.

Wes’s chest tightened with something that felt dangerously like happiness.

Then, of course, the universe ruined the moment.

A voice called from the side alley, loud and delighted: “WES CANDLE.”

Wes froze.

Sam’s hand stilled.

Wes closed his eyes. “No.”

Hugh Everhart appeared around the corner like a festive ghost, eyes shining, scarf flaring dramatically in the wind.

Hugh’s gaze snapped to Wes. Then to Sam. Then to the fact that Sam’s hand was at Wes’s neck.

Hugh gasped with the force of a carol finale. “OH MY GOD.”

Wes opened his eyes and glared. “Hugh.”

Hugh clasped his hands under his chin like he was witnessing sacred art. “This is the best day of my life.”

Wes hissed, “Go away.”

Hugh ignored him completely and looked at Sam. “Officer Rook,” Hugh said solemnly, “thank you for your service.”

Sam’s expression didn’t change, but his eyes warmed with amusement. “You’re welcome.”

Wes groaned. “I hate both of you.”

Hugh bounced closer, vibrating with gossip energy. “Is this a thing. Is it a *thing thing.*”

Wes pointed a finger at Hugh like it was a warning label. “If you say the words ‘thing thing’ again, I’m throwing you into the snow.”

Hugh blinked innocently. “You wouldn’t.”

Wes smiled sweetly. “Try me.”

Sam’s hand slipped away from Wes’s neck, not because Sam wanted distance, but because Hugh had the timing of a disaster.

Sam leaned in slightly and murmured near Wes’s ear, “We can continue this later.”

Wes’s pulse jumped. “Stop saying ‘later’ like it’s a guarantee.”

Sam’s voice was quiet. “It is.”

Wes swallowed hard and glared at Hugh to avoid melting on the spot.

Hugh pointed between them. “I knew it. I KNEW it.”

Wes grabbed Hugh by the scarf and started herding him back toward the hall. “You need a job,” Wes muttered.

Hugh perked up instantly. “I love jobs.”

Wes shoved him toward the supply closet door. “Good. You’re on glitter containment.”

Hugh gasped. “An honor.”

Sam’s low chuckle followed them, warm as cocoa.

Wes tried not to smile.

He failed.

And out in Evergreen Square, the tree stood tall at last, lights blinking on one by one, like the whole town had decided to keep going.

Wes Candle, against all his instincts, decided he might keep going too.

With help.

**[STOP WRITING]**


Wes shoved Hugh into the supply closet like he was filing a problem into a drawer.

“Glitter containment,” Wes said, deadly calm. “If I find a single sparkle particle outside this room, I’m billing you for emotional damages.”

Hugh clutched the box of ribbon like it was sacred. “I accept this responsibility with reverence.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “And silence.”

Hugh blinked. “I can’t promise silence.”

Wes pointed. “Try.”

Sam’s quiet laugh followed them as Wes slammed the closet door with the kind of finality that suggested Hugh might live there now.

Wes exhaled and turned back toward the hall.

The moment he stepped inside, Merryweather Hall hit him full force: warmth, noise, and the frantic hum of a hundred small tasks pretending to be a plan.

Noel was at the front with a clipboard and a megawatt smile, corralling volunteers like a man who’d decided chaos would not win today.

Eben stood two steps behind him like a composed shadow, arms crossed, gaze sweeping the room with the calm of a man who had already written three contingency plans in his head.

The tree lights in Evergreen Square blinked in through the big front windows, bright and triumphant now, like nothing had ever gone wrong.

Wes looked around at the cocoa station, the garlands being sorted, the folding tables being set, the volunteers laughing again, and felt a weird twist in his chest.

They’d done it.

Not perfectly. Not smoothly. But they’d done it.

Wes stepped back behind the cocoa station and grabbed the ladle again like it was his rightful scepter.

“Okay!” Wes called, voice carrying, because if you spoke like you belonged in charge, people tended to believe you. “Hot cocoa is now available for anyone who does not threaten to riot!”

A few volunteers cheered.

Noel turned toward Wes and mouthed, *thank you* with visible gratitude.

Wes waved him off like it was nothing, because Wes Candle could perform competence in his sleep.

Sam drifted back into the hall a moment later, shaking snow from his coat, looking composed like he hadn’t just dragged a Christmas tree delivery out of the jaws of icy doom.

Wes’s eyes found him without permission.

Sam’s gaze caught Wes’s for a beat, steady and warm.

Wes’s throat tightened.

Wes immediately turned to pour cocoa like it was life-or-death.

Because it kind of was.

Hugh’s muffled voice floated from the supply closet: “WHO WANTS TO HEAR A ROMANTIC DEVELOPMENT.”

Wes didn’t even look up. “NO ONE.”

A volunteer near the garlands perked up. “Wait, what romantic development?”

Wes slammed a mug onto the counter with a little too much force. “Cocoa. Drink. Forget everything.”

Sam stepped closer, close enough to be heard only by Wes. “He’s going to tell them.”

Wes hissed, “I will end him.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “He won’t if you give him a job.”

“I gave him a job,” Wes muttered. “Glitter prison.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “He’ll escape.”

Wes glared at him. “Stop being right.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes’s pulse jumped. Annoying.

Noel clapped his hands loudly at the front. “Okay! Attention everyone!”

The room quieted in messy increments.

Noel held up his clipboard like it was a flag. “First: the tree is up. We are not dying. That’s a win.”

A cheer went up.

Noel’s shoulders visibly loosened like he’d been holding his breath for hours.

Eben leaned in slightly and murmured something near Noel’s ear.

Noel nodded and continued, voice steadier now. “Second: we’re shifting the schedule by thirty minutes for set-up. It’s fine. You’re fine. I’m fine.”

Someone called, “You don’t look fine!”

Noel smiled too brightly. “I’m fine in a festive way.”

Wes snorted.

Sam’s shoulder brushed Wes’s as he leaned in. “He’s trying.”

Wes’s voice went quieter. “He’s always trying.”

Sam’s gaze stayed on Noel, but his voice softened. “Yeah.”

Noel pointed down the volunteer list. “I need two people on stage support, four on decoration, and one person who is willing to wrestle the inflatable snowman into submission.”

A hand shot up. “I’ll do it.”

Noel pointed like he’d just knighted someone. “Bless you. You are a hero.”

Eben stepped forward, calm and surgical. “Safety note. The snowman’s anchor points must be secured. If it becomes airborne, it is a hazard.”

The volunteer blinked. “It can become airborne?”

Eben’s gaze was flat. “Everything can become airborne.”

Wes muttered to Sam, “I love him.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “You don’t.”

Wes didn’t deny it fast enough.

Sam’s gaze slid to him.

Wes immediately coughed and busied himself with marshmallows.

Hugh’s voice rose again from the closet, muffled but determined: “I HAVE IMPORTANT NEWS.”

Wes snapped, “NO YOU DON’T.”

Hugh shouted, “I DO. IT’S ABOUT THE BAKER AND THE COP.”

The room went very still.

Wes froze mid-ladle.

Noel’s head snapped toward the closet, eyes widening with the force of a man who loved gossip but also loved not dying today.

Eben’s eyes narrowed like he was about to draft a policy against Hugh.

A volunteer whispered, delighted, “The baker and the cop?”

Wes’s face went nuclear.

Sam’s posture stayed calm, but his eyes flicked to Wes, checking.

Wes made a decision in a single furious heartbeat.

He marched to the closet door, yanked it open, and stared at Hugh with the dead-eyed patience of a man holding a knife made of cinnamon sticks.

“Hugh,” Wes said, voice low and dangerous, “if you say one more word, I will assign you to mop duty for the rest of the season.”

Hugh went pale. “You wouldn’t.”

Wes smiled sweetly. “Try me.”

Hugh swallowed. His eyes flicked past Wes to Sam, who was watching with quiet amusement.

Hugh raised one finger. “Okay. Fine. I won’t announce it.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “Good.”

Hugh brightened immediately. “I will imply it subtly through crafts.”

Wes shut his eyes. “No.”

Hugh clasped his hands. “It’s called foreshadowing.”

Wes pointed at a bin of plain brown paper. “You. Paper-only.”

Hugh gasped like he’d been stabbed. “That’s cruel.”

Wes’s voice stayed flat. “I am capable of cruelty.”

Eben’s voice cut in from behind them. “Everhart.”

Hugh turned slowly, like a man being addressed by the law itself.

Eben’s gaze was calm and terrifying. “You will not create rumors that destabilize the volunteer corps.”

Hugh blinked. “Rumors build community.”

Eben didn’t blink. “Rumors create inefficiency.”

Hugh looked horrified. “You’re allergic to joy.”

Eben’s gaze stayed steady. “Correct.”

Hugh clutched his scarf dramatically. “Fine. I will be… silent.”

Wes stared. “You can do that?”

Hugh looked insulted. “I can do anything. I’m just choosing not to most of the time.”

Wes pointed back into the closet. “Paper-only. Quietly. With your mouth closed.”

Hugh sighed. “I’m oppressed.”

Wes shut the closet door again and leaned his forehead against it for one second.

Sam stepped up beside him, voice low. “You handled that.”

Wes exhaled. “If I handled it any harder, he’d explode into glitter.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “You’re blushing.”

Wes jerked upright. “I am not.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “You are.”

Wes glared. “Stop looking at me.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “No.”

Wes’s pulse jumped again, traitor.

Noel popped up beside them with a grin like he’d been holding it in. “So,” Noel said brightly, “the baker and the cop.”

Wes’s eyes widened. “Noel.”

Noel held up both hands. “I’m not judging. I’m thriving.”

Wes hissed, “We are not making it a town thing.”

Noel’s grin softened into something kinder. “Okay,” Noel said, unexpectedly gentle. “Your thing.”

Wes blinked.

Noel leaned in slightly, voice dropping. “But also, I’m happy for you.”

Wes’s throat tightened, inconveniently. “Don’t.”

Noel smiled. “I won’t make it weird.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “You can’t promise that.”

Noel looked offended. “I absolutely can. I’m a professional.”

Eben appeared behind Noel like a consequence. “He is not.”

Noel pointed at Eben without looking. “Shh. I’m being supportive.”

Wes swallowed hard and nodded once, like that was the only form of acceptance he could tolerate.

Noel patted his arm quickly. “Okay. I have to go make sure the snowman doesn’t become airborne.”

Eben said, “It will.”

Noel looked over his shoulder, horrified. “Don’t manifest that!”

Eben’s tone was flat. “I’m predicting it.”

Noel sprinted away.

Eben lingered for a beat, gaze sliding to Sam, then to Wes.

“You,” Eben said to Sam, “keep him fed.”

Wes blinked. “Excuse me?”

Eben’s gaze didn’t move. “He forgets.”

Wes sputtered. “I do not forget. I… prioritize.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “He forgets.”

Wes glared at both of them. “Traitors.”

Eben nodded once, like he’d completed his duty, and moved away to inspect the stage setup with the solemnity of a man reviewing a bridge.

Wes stared after him, then turned back to Sam. “Did I just get assigned a caretaker.”

Sam’s voice was low, amused. “Sounds like it.”

Wes’s cheeks warmed again. “I hate this.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “No, you don’t.”

Wes opened his mouth to argue.

Then a volunteer stepped up, nervous smile in place. “Hi, Wes? The cocoa is… amazing. Also, can I ask you a question.”

Wes straightened instantly, competence snapping into place. “Yes.”

The volunteer hesitated, glancing at Sam like Sam was intimidating by default. “Is Officer Rook going to be… around more this season.”

Wes froze.

Sam stayed calm, eyes on Wes, silently letting Wes choose what to say.

Wes swallowed. His heart thudded like it was trying to sabotage him.

Wes forced a smile that didn’t feel like lying. “Sam’s helping with safety,” Wes said, firm and simple. “The festival needs him.”

Sam’s eyes warmed, approving.

The volunteer brightened. “Oh good. He makes everyone feel safe.”

Wes’s chest tightened at that. He nodded once. “Yeah,” Wes said quietly. “He does.”

The volunteer wandered off, satisfied.

Wes let out a breath he hadn’t realized he’d been holding.

Sam leaned in slightly, voice low. “You did good.”

Wes’s pulse jumped.

Wes muttered, “Stop saying that.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “No.”

Wes stared at him, then shook his head like he could shake off how much he liked it.

Wes grabbed a fresh mug and shoved it toward Sam like it was a peace offering and an accusation. “More cocoa.”

Sam took it. Their fingers brushed.

Wes’s breath caught.

Sam’s eyes flicked to his, steady. “Later,” Sam murmured again, like a promise he had every intention of keeping.

Wes swallowed, cheeks warm. “Stop.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Okay.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “That sounded fake.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “It was.”

Wes huffed a laugh despite himself, and the sound felt like something loosening inside him.

Outside, the tree lights glowed brighter as the square filled with people, music starting up somewhere near the stage. The festival was moving forward, messy and alive.

Wes looked out the window at the crowd gathering, then back at Sam.

Wes’s voice came out quiet, almost surprised by its own honesty. “If we’re doing this,” Wes said, low, “we’re doing it slow.”

Sam’s gaze softened, serious now. “Okay.”

Wes swallowed. “And private.”

Sam nodded once. “Okay.”

Wes hesitated, then forced the hardest part out. “And… you don’t leave without telling me.”

Sam’s expression didn’t change much, but something in his eyes warmed like a steady light. “I won’t.”

Wes blinked. “Promise.”

Sam’s voice was calm and absolute. “I promise.”

Wes exhaled, shaky, and looked away fast, because if he looked at Sam too long, he might do something reckless like trust him.

Sam’s voice came gentle, close. “After this,” Sam said, “I’m walking you home.”

Wes’s chest tightened.

Wes muttered, “I can walk.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “I know.”

Wes’s throat tightened at that, stupidly. He nodded once.

Outside, someone started a countdown for the official lighting moment, voices rising together.

Ten.

Nine.

Eight.

Wes stood behind the cocoa station with warm mugs lined up like offerings.

Sam stood beside him like a steady wall.

And when the crowd hit **one** and the square erupted in light, Wes felt Sam’s shoulder brush his.

Not accidental.

Not performative.

Just there.

Wes’s breath caught, and for a second, he let himself lean back into it.

Just a little.

Just enough.

Because maybe, for once, he didn’t have to hold everything alone.

**[STOP WRITING]**


Wes didn’t mean to lean.

It just… happened. Like his body recognized steadiness and took it without asking his pride first.

Sam didn’t react like it was a big deal. He didn’t stiffen. Didn’t turn it into a moment. He just stayed there, shoulder warm against Wes’s, while the square exploded into light and noise and applause.

Noel, somewhere near the steps, looked like he might cry with relief and fury at the same time.

Hugh, inevitably, was already trying to lead a chant that sounded suspiciously like it would become gossip later.

Wes focused on the cocoa. On the mugs. On the volunteers. On anything except the quiet satisfaction in his chest that felt like a dangerous new ingredient.

“Cocoa,” Wes announced, loud enough to carry. “Hot. Sweet. Available to anyone who doesn’t ask me personal questions.”

Someone in the crowd yelled, “TOO LATE.”

Wes grimaced. “I hate all of you.”

Sam’s voice, low near his ear: “They like you.”

Wes muttered, “They like free sugar.”

Sam took a sip from his mug. “They also like you.”

Wes’s face went warm. “Stop.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes made a strangled sound and aggressively sprinkled marshmallows like he could physically distract himself.

The first wave of townsfolk hit the cocoa station like it was a pilgrimage site. Wes went into muscle memory: pour, stir, smile, accept compliments, refuse tips, pretend he wasn’t exhausted.

Sam hovered just to the side, never in the way, but always present. He redirected a kid who tried to shove ahead, gently. He moved a wobbly table back into place with one hand. He caught a mug before it tipped and spilled.

Wes watched him do it and felt something in his chest squeeze, soft and stupid.

You could build a life around that kind of quiet competence.

Wes immediately told himself to shut up.

After the rush eased, Noel swept by, cheeks flushed, eyes bright, scarf askew.

He stopped at the station, leaned in, and whispered, “The tree is up. We didn’t die. Eben is smiling internally.”

Wes snorted. “Liar.”

Noel looked offended. “He blinked slower. That’s basically joy.”

Eben appeared behind Noel like he’d been summoned by the mention of his name. “We have a next task.”

Noel pivoted instantly. “Yes. We love tasks.”

Eben’s gaze slid to the schedule board they’d taped to the wall. “Parade staging begins in twenty minutes. We need barricades moved, and the stage sound check is behind.”

Noel nodded, then glanced at Wes, softer. “You okay.”

Wes opened his mouth to say *fine.*

Sam’s gaze landed on him, steady.

Wes exhaled. “I’m… good,” Wes said. “Just busy.”

Noel’s face softened, genuinely pleased, like Wes had just performed a miracle. “Okay. Good.”

Then Noel was gone again, swallowed by duty.

Eben lingered half a beat longer, eyes scanning the cocoa station with the severity of a man assessing structural integrity.

“Candle,” Eben said.

Wes braced. “Yes.”

Eben nodded once. “Adequate.”

Wes blinked. “Was that… praise.”

Eben’s eyes narrowed. “Don’t make it weird.”

Wes stared after him as he walked away. “I hate him,” Wes muttered.

Sam’s voice was low. “No, you don’t.”

Wes glared. “Stop doing that.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Okay.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “That one sounded real.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “It was.”

Wes’s stomach flipped.

The music shifted. Someone started a carol in the square. The crowd thinned into clusters, people drifting toward the stage, toward the lights, toward whatever part of the night felt most like a story.

Wes glanced at the clock above the hall entrance and felt his fatigue settle into his bones like flour dust after a long day.

He set the ladle down and wiped his hands on his apron. “Okay,” Wes announced to no one. “Cocoa station is now self-serve.”

A volunteer gasped. “That’s anarchy!”

Wes pointed at a sign. “There are instructions.”

The volunteer squinted. “Wes, this says ‘DON’T MAKE ME REGRET THIS.’”

Wes nodded. “Correct.”

Sam’s low chuckle warmed the air.

Wes turned to him, pretending annoyance. “Don’t laugh. You’re about to be demoted.”

Sam lifted his brows. “Demoted to what.”

Wes gestured at the mugs. “You’re on dish duty.”

Sam nodded like that was normal. “Okay.”

Wes froze. “Wait. You’re just… going to do it.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “Yes.”

Wes’s throat tightened, inconveniently. “You don’t have to.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “I want to.”

Wes swallowed hard and looked away like the snow outside was suddenly fascinating. “Fine,” he muttered. “But you’re doing it wrong.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Show me.”

Wes stared, then sighed. “You’re impossible.”

Sam started rinsing mugs at the back sink like he’d been doing it his whole life. Calm. Unbothered. Present.

Wes watched him for a beat too long, then busied himself wiping down the counter with way more intensity than necessary.

When the last mug was stacked, Sam dried his hands on a towel and glanced at Wes. “Ready.”

Wes blinked. “For what.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “To go home.”

Wes’s chest tightened.

Wes made a face. “I’m not a child.”

Sam nodded. “I know.”

Wes swallowed. “Then why are you… doing this.”

Sam didn’t flinch from the question. “Because it’s late. You’re tired. And I like walking with you.”

Wes’s breath caught at the last part.

Wes’s voice came out rough. “You can’t keep saying things like that.”

Sam’s eyes stayed steady. “You can keep telling me to stop. I won’t.”

Wes stared at him, pulse thudding, and realized Sam was doing something worse than flirting.

Sam was being consistent.

Wes grabbed his coat from behind the counter and shoved his arms into it like he was suiting up for battle. “Fine,” Wes muttered. “Walk me home. But if anyone sees us, I’m going to pretend you’re escorting me for… public safety.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Okay.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “Was that sarcastic.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “No.”

Wes huffed a laugh and headed for the side door.

Outside, the snow had softened into a fine drift, the air cold and clean. The square was still bright, music and laughter rolling through the night like a tide.

Wes and Sam moved along the edge of the crowd, not hiding exactly, but not advertising either. Wes kept his hands in his pockets. Sam kept his pace matched to Wes’s like it was instinct.

They passed the stage where Noel was speaking into a microphone with theatrical enthusiasm.

“Welcome to the Twelve Days of Tinsel Town!” Noel’s voice boomed. “If you are here tonight, you are now legally obligated to have fun!”

A cheer went up.

Wes muttered, “He’s going to get sued.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “He’ll enjoy it.”

Eben stood beside the stage with his clipboard, gaze scanning the crowd like a man preparing for a riot he expected to win.

Wes watched Noel talk and felt a sudden, quiet pang.

Noel looked happy. Not perfect. Not rested. But happy.

Wes thought of himself in his apartment last night, sitting for the first time in hours because someone had told him to and made it feel like care instead of criticism.

Wes swallowed.

Sam’s voice came low. “What.”

Wes blinked. “What.”

Sam’s gaze held him. “You went quiet.”

Wes shrugged, too fast. “I’m normal.”

Sam didn’t buy it. “Wes.”

Wes stopped walking.

Sam stopped too, turning slightly so he was facing Wes, blocking the wind again without making it a big deal.

Wes’s throat tightened. “This is not a great place for… feelings.”

Sam’s voice stayed steady. “Then keep it small.”

Wes stared at him. “You’re too good at this.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “I’m good at staying calm. You’re good at running yourself into the ground.”

Wes’s cheeks warmed. “Rude.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “True.”

Wes exhaled, then said the thing he’d been trying not to say all evening.

“It’s easier,” Wes admitted quietly, “when you’re around.”

Sam’s expression softened in a way that made Wes’s ribs feel too tight. “Okay.”

Wes blinked. “That’s all you have.”

Sam took a slow step closer, careful, voice low. “It’s not all I have,” Sam said. “It’s all I’m going to say right now. Because you asked for slow.”

Wes’s throat tightened. He swallowed. “Right.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “But I heard you.”

Wes stared at him, cheeks warm, eyes stinging in the most irritating way. He nodded once, because nodding was safer than anything else.

Sam’s hand lifted, hovering between them like a question.

“May I,” Sam asked softly.

Wes’s pulse skittered. He nodded. “Yeah.”

Sam’s fingers brushed Wes’s sleeve near the wrist, just a light touch, grounding. Not possessive. Not demanding. Just there.

Wes breathed out slowly. “Okay.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “Okay.”

They started walking again.

By the time they reached Candle & Crumb, the bakery windows glowed softly, warm in the snow like a promise.

Wes unlocked the door and hesitated, hand on the knob.

He didn’t invite Sam in.

He also didn’t say goodbye.

Wes stood there for a beat, caught between habit and want.

Sam didn’t push. He just waited, patient as ever.

Wes swallowed, voice rough. “Do you want to… come up. For a minute.”

Sam’s gaze softened, but his voice stayed careful. “For a minute.”

Wes nodded fast. “A minute.”

Sam stepped inside.

Wes locked the door behind them, heart pounding like he’d just committed a crime.

They climbed the stairs in silence, the kind that wasn’t empty. It was full of everything neither of them was saying.

At Wes’s apartment door, Wes fumbled the key once, cursed under his breath, and finally got it open.

Warmth spilled out.

Wes stepped aside, letting Sam in. Sam looked around like last night, taking in the small space, the evidence of Wes’s life.

Then Sam’s gaze returned to Wes, steady.

Wes’s throat tightened. “So,” Wes said, uselessly. “Minute.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Minute.”

Wes hovered near the kitchen counter, hands in his pockets like he didn’t know what to do with them.

Sam took off his coat, folding it neatly and setting it on the chair like it mattered. He moved with the calm competence of a man who could make even a cramped apartment feel ordered.

Wes watched him and felt something inside him ache.

Sam stepped closer.

He didn’t touch Wes. He just stopped within reach and looked at him like Wes was the only thing in the room.

Wes’s breath caught.

Sam’s voice was low. “You did good tonight.”

Wes flinched like the praise was too bright. “Stop.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “No.”

Wes swallowed hard, eyes stinging. “You’re going to make me… expect things.”

Sam’s voice softened. “Okay.”

Wes blinked. “Why do you keep saying okay like that.”

Sam’s expression softened, serious now. “Because you’re telling me what you’re afraid of,” Sam said. “And I’m not going to punish you for it.”

Wes’s throat tightened. He looked away fast, jaw clenched.

Sam’s hand lifted slowly, hovering. “May I.”

Wes nodded, barely.

Sam’s fingers touched Wes’s cheek, light as a promise. Sam’s thumb stroked once, gentle.

Wes’s breath shuddered out.

Sam leaned in and kissed him, slow and soft, the kind of kiss that didn’t demand anything except presence.

Wes melted into it, hands fisting in Sam’s sweater like he needed something solid.

Sam pulled back just enough to murmur, “Still okay.”

Wes’s voice came out wrecked. “Yes.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “Good.”

Wes let out a shaky laugh that sounded like surrender. “I hate that word now.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “I’ll try a different one.”

Wes blinked. “Like what.”

Sam’s voice went quiet and steady. “Safe.”

Wes froze.

His eyes stung harder.

Sam’s thumb stroked once. “You feel safe.”

Wes swallowed, chest tight. “Yeah,” Wes whispered. “I do.”

Sam held his gaze, steady and calm.

Then Sam stepped back, just a fraction, giving Wes space like he always did.

“Minute’s almost up,” Sam said softly.

Wes stared at him, heart pounding.

He should’ve let Sam go. He should’ve ended it clean, while it was still simple.

Instead, Wes did something reckless.

He reached out and caught Sam’s sleeve.

Sam stilled, gaze dropping to Wes’s hand, then lifting to Wes’s eyes.

Wes swallowed. “Stay. A little longer.”

Sam’s expression softened in a way that made Wes’s chest ache.

Sam’s voice was quiet. “Okay.”

Wes’s shoulders dropped like his body had been waiting for that permission all his life.

Outside, snow fell against the window in soft quiet taps.

Inside, Wes Candle let someone stay.

**[STOP WRITING]**



Sam didn’t move right away.

He just stood there with Wes’s hand on his sleeve, like he was letting the moment settle into place instead of rushing past it.

Wes’s fingers tightened, not hard, just enough to make the ask real.

Sam’s gaze held Wes’s for a long beat. Then Sam said again, quiet and steady, “Okay.”

Wes’s shoulders dropped like someone had finally loosened a strap he’d been carrying too tight for too long.

Wes let out a shaky breath and immediately tried to ruin the tenderness with sarcasm. “This is not a sleepover.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Then what is it.”

Wes blinked, caught.

Wes looked away, jaw tight. “It’s… a minute,” he muttered. “A longer minute.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Okay.”

Wes huffed a laugh, half embarrassed. “Stop saying okay like it fixes everything.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “It doesn’t fix everything.”

Wes glanced back, surprised by the honesty.

Sam continued, low and steady. “It just tells you I’m here.”

Wes’s throat tightened again. He nodded once, because nodding was still safer than saying what he actually felt.

Wes dropped his hand from Sam’s sleeve and stepped back, gesturing vaguely at the apartment like it was an apology for existing. “You can… sit,” Wes said. “If you want.”

Sam’s gaze flicked to the couch, then to Wes. “Do you want me to sit.”

Wes’s cheeks warmed. “Yes.”

Sam nodded once and moved to the couch, sitting in a way that didn’t sprawl, didn’t dominate the space, didn’t act like he owned anything. Just calm, grounded presence.

Wes hovered by the kitchen counter, hands shoved into his hoodie pocket, looking like a man waiting to be graded.

Sam watched him for a beat. “Come here.”

Wes froze. “Excuse me.”

Sam’s voice stayed gentle. “Come sit. You don’t have to stand like you’re about to bolt.”

Wes opened his mouth to argue, then realized Sam was right, and hatefully, Wes’s body wanted to obey.

Wes muttered, “I can sit.”

Sam lifted a brow.

Wes sighed, defeated by his own patterns, and crossed the room to sit on the far end of the couch like the cushion might accuse him of emotional vulnerability.

Sam didn’t comment on the distance. He didn’t tease. He just sat there, steady and quiet.

For a minute, neither of them spoke.

The apartment hummed with warmth. The heater clicked on and off. Snow tapped softly against the window.

Wes stared at his own hands, then blurted, “So what happens now.”

Sam’s gaze stayed on him. “Now we sit.”

Wes blinked. “That’s… not what I meant.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “What did you mean.”

Wes swallowed hard. “Like. Us.” He made a vague gesture between them like he could physically shape the answer into something manageable. “This.”

Sam didn’t flinch. “We keep it slow.”

Wes exhaled. “Okay.”

Sam’s eyes warmed at the word. “Okay.”

Wes rolled his eyes weakly. “Don’t.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “You started it.”

Wes huffed a laugh and leaned back, staring at the ceiling like it might offer guidance.

Then Wes said, quieter, “I don’t do this.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “What.”

Wes swallowed. “Letting people in.”

Sam nodded once like he’d already known that. “I noticed.”

Wes’s cheeks heated. “Don’t say it like it’s obvious.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “It is obvious. But not in a bad way. In a… learned way.”

Wes stared at him, something in his chest aching at the gentleness.

Wes tried to redirect. “You’re very good at talking like you’ve been to therapy.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “I have.”

Wes blinked. “You have?”

Sam nodded. “Yeah.”

Wes stared, caught off guard in the most inconvenient way. “You… seem like you don’t need it.”

Sam’s expression didn’t change much, but something in his eyes went quieter. “Everyone needs it sometimes.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “Why did you go.”

Sam didn’t answer immediately. He glanced toward the window, then back to Wes.

“Job,” Sam said finally. “Things you see. Things you carry. You either talk about it or it eats you.”

Wes’s chest tightened. He didn’t know what to do with the softness that rose in him.

So he did the only thing he knew: he tried to be funny.

“Well,” Wes muttered, “I cope by baking and pretending I’m a self-sufficient pillar of strength.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “How’s that going.”

Wes snorted. “Badly.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “I noticed.”

Wes groaned and rubbed his face. “You’re unbearable.”

Sam’s voice stayed low. “You asked me to stay.”

Wes peeked at him through his fingers. “That was a moment of weakness.”

Sam’s eyes softened. “Or strength.”

Wes dropped his hands, staring at Sam like Sam had just spoken a foreign language.

Wes swallowed. “Don’t make it poetic.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Okay.”

Wes sighed. “That one was sarcastic.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “A little.”

Wes leaned back again, trying to breathe normally. His heartbeat was still too loud.

After a beat, Wes said, carefully, “Are you… going to leave after this.”

Sam’s gaze stayed on him. “Do you want me to.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “No.”

Sam nodded once. “Then no.”

Wes blinked. “That’s… not how people work.”

Sam’s voice stayed steady. “It’s how I work.”

Wes stared at him, and something in his chest cracked open just a fraction.

Wes whispered, almost angry at himself, “Why are you like this.”

Sam’s expression softened. “Because I want to be.”

Wes’s eyes stung.

Wes turned his head away fast. “I hate feelings.”

Sam didn’t laugh. He just said, quiet and kind, “Yeah. I know.”

The silence that followed wasn’t empty. It was full of everything Wes wasn’t saying.

Wes cleared his throat and stood abruptly, pacing two steps before he caught himself and froze, embarrassed.

Sam didn’t comment. He just watched, calm.

Wes gestured toward the kitchen like it was a distraction. “Do you want tea.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Do you want tea.”

Wes blinked, irritated. “Stop answering questions with questions.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “I’m checking.”

Wes exhaled. “Yes. I want tea.”

Sam nodded. “Then yes.”

Wes’s mouth twitched despite himself. He turned to the kitchen and started filling the kettle, hands moving on autopilot.

Behind him, Sam’s voice was low. “You don’t have to perform.”

Wes’s shoulders tensed. “I’m not performing.”

Sam’s tone stayed even. “You are.”

Wes gripped the kettle handle too tight. “It’s how I keep things… from getting messy.”

Sam didn’t push. He just said, quiet, “Messy is allowed.”

Wes swallowed hard and flicked the kettle on.

The click sounded too loud.

Wes turned back, leaning against the counter with folded arms, trying to find a version of himself that didn’t feel like exposed wiring.

Sam was still on the couch, posture relaxed, gaze steady.

Wes stared at him for a beat too long.

Sam’s eyes softened. “Come here.”

Wes froze again. “You already said that.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “And you’re still hovering.”

Wes’s cheeks warmed. He walked back to the couch and sat again, closer this time, like the movement had happened before he could overthink it.

Sam didn’t make a big deal of the closeness. He didn’t reach for Wes. He just sat there.

Wes stared at the space between their knees.

The kettle began to hiss softly in the kitchen.

Wes’s voice came out low. “That kiss today… outside.”

Sam’s gaze stayed on him. “Yeah.”

Wes swallowed. “I didn’t hate it.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “I noticed.”

Wes glared weakly. “Stop noticing.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes’s throat tightened and he looked away again like he could outmaneuver his own want.

Sam shifted slightly. Not away. Just… closer enough that Wes could feel the warmth of him.

Sam’s voice was quiet. “Do you want another.”

Wes’s breath caught.

Wes should’ve said no. He should’ve deflected. He should’ve done anything except be honest.

He whispered, “Yes.”

Sam didn’t move immediately. He waited, like he always did, giving Wes time to change his mind.

Wes didn’t.

Sam leaned in and kissed him, slow and soft, like a question Wes had already answered.

Wes’s hands rose on their own, fingers curling into Sam’s sweater at the shoulders. He pulled Sam closer with a quiet desperation he would absolutely deny later.

Sam’s hand settled at Wes’s waist, warm and steady, holding without gripping.

Wes let out a breath against Sam’s mouth that sounded suspiciously like relief.

Sam pulled back just enough to murmur, “Still okay.”

Wes’s voice was rough. “Yes.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “Good.”

Wes made a small, helpless sound and hid his face against Sam’s shoulder for half a second like he needed a break from being seen.

Sam’s arm came around him gently, not trapping, not insisting. Just there.

Wes stayed still, breathing.

The kettle clicked off in the kitchen.

Wes didn’t move.

Sam didn’t move.

The world didn’t end.

After a beat, Sam said softly, “Tea.”

Wes exhaled, voice muffled against Sam’s shoulder. “Tea.”

Wes reluctantly pulled back and stood, moving to the kitchen like it was a rescue mission.

He poured water over the tea bag in Hugh’s tin, because of course Hugh had planned for this like a tiny glittery prophet.

Wes brought two mugs back to the couch and handed one to Sam, fingers brushing again.

Sam took it with a small nod. “Thanks.”

Wes sat back down and stared into the mug like it might tell him what to do with his life.

The tea smelled like cinnamon and chamomile and something soothing.

Wes took a sip.

It tasted like permission to stop.

Wes swallowed and said, quietly, “You’re going to wreck me.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “No.”

Wes blinked. “No?”

Sam’s voice was calm. “I’m going to be here. That’s not wrecking you.”

Wes stared at him, chest tight. “It feels like it.”

Sam’s expression softened. “Because you’re not used to it.”

Wes swallowed hard. “Yeah.”

Sam shifted slightly, shoulder brushing Wes’s again, deliberate and gentle. “We can keep it small.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “Okay.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Okay.”

Wes huffed a laugh, shaky but real. “This is ridiculous.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Yeah.”

Wes glanced at him, then looked away fast, voice quieter. “I don’t want the whole town to know.”

Sam nodded once. “They don’t have to.”

Wes swallowed. “But Hugh will.”

Sam’s eyes warmed with amusement. “Hugh will try.”

Wes sighed. “I will kill him.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “No murders.”

Wes glared. “I’ll do a metaphorical murder.”

Sam nodded. “Acceptable.”

Wes leaned back, tea warm in his hands, and for the first time all day, his body stopped bracing.

Sam watched him for a long beat, then said, quietly, “You can sleep.”

Wes blinked. “I’m not sleeping. You’re here.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “That’s why you can.”

Wes swallowed, throat tight again. “You’re not staying the night.”

Sam’s expression didn’t change much. “Do you want me to.”

Wes’s breath caught.

Wes stared at his tea.

Wes whispered, “Yes.”

Sam didn’t rush. He didn’t pounce on it like a victory. He just nodded once, slow.

“Okay,” Sam said softly. “But we keep it simple.”

Wes’s chest ached. “Simple like… what.”

Sam’s voice stayed steady. “I stay. We sleep. No pressure. No pretending. You wake up and I’m still here.”

Wes’s eyes stung so hard he almost laughed to cover it. “That’s not simple.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “It can be.”

Wes swallowed. “Okay.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Okay.”

Wes took another sip of tea and let his shoulders drop. He didn’t say thank you, because the word felt too small.

Instead, he leaned slightly toward Sam and let his head tip, just barely, toward Sam’s shoulder again.

Sam’s arm went around him, steady and warm.

Wes’s eyelids started to feel heavy, like his body had been waiting for safety to get permission to shut down.

Sam’s voice was low. “You did good today.”

Wes’s mouth twitched. “Stop.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “No.”

Wes exhaled a sleepy, annoyed sound that wasn’t really annoyed.

Outside, snow kept falling.

Inside, Wes Candle finally let himself rest.

**[STOP WRITING]**





Wes didn’t fall asleep all at once.

He drifted.

His body did that cautious thing it always did when it finally stopped moving: hovering at the edge of rest like it didn’t trust it. Like if he let go, something would break. Something would be missed.

But Sam’s arm was around him, steady and warm, and the apartment was quiet in a way that felt protected instead of empty.

Wes’s breath slowed.

Sam didn’t move much. Just enough to adjust the blanket when Wes’s shoulders twitched. Just enough to keep Wes from sliding into an awkward angle that would make his neck hate him tomorrow.

Wes muttered something incoherent into Sam’s shoulder.

Sam’s voice came low, calm. “Yeah. I’m here.”

Wes exhaled like his whole body had been holding that question for years.

The last thing Wes registered was Sam’s hand, gentle at the back of his neck, thumb stroking once like a grounding point.

Then Wes was gone.

---

Sam stayed still for a while after Wes fell asleep.

He watched Wes’s face soften, watched the tension drain out of his brow like it had finally been given permission. Watched the way Wes’s hands unclenched, fingers loosening around the mug like he no longer needed to be ready for impact.

Sam had seen a lot of tired.

But this was different. This was *earned* tired. The kind that came from carrying everyone else’s needs like they were a duty instead of a choice.

Sam’s jaw tightened slightly.

He adjusted the blanket again, then carefully eased Wes upright just enough to slide the mug from his hands without waking him. Wes made a small sound of protest, then settled again, head tipping back toward Sam’s shoulder like it belonged there.

Sam exhaled.

“Okay,” Sam murmured, more to himself than anything. “Bed.”

Getting Wes to bed without waking him fully was… a strategy exercise.

Sam moved slow, careful. One hand supporting Wes’s back, one under his arm, lifting like Wes was precious instead of stubborn.

Wes blinked once, groggy. “Don’t,” he mumbled.

Sam paused. “Don’t what.”

Wes’s eyes didn’t open properly. “Don’t leave.”

Sam’s chest went tight.

“I’m not leaving,” Sam said quietly.

Wes’s mouth twitched, like the answer landed somewhere deep. Then Wes slumped forward again, trusting Sam with the weight of him like it was nothing.

Sam guided him into the bedroom, sat him carefully on the edge of the bed, and peeled the hoodie off him with the slow patience of a man defusing a bomb.

Wes, half-asleep, muttered, “This is humiliating.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “You’re fine.”

Wes’s head lolled. “Liar.”

Sam eased Wes back onto the mattress, pulled the blankets up, and paused when Wes’s hand shot out and caught his wrist.

Wes’s eyes cracked open, unfocused but intense. “Stay.”

Sam swallowed. “I’m staying.”

Wes blinked, then whispered, “Promise.”

Sam leaned in, voice low and steady. “I promise.”

Wes’s grip loosened.

Sam stayed there another beat, just watching Wes breathe, watching the lines of strain fade out of his face like someone had finally turned the volume down on his life.

Then Sam stood, turned off the bedside lamp, and moved back out to the living room.

He didn’t crawl into Wes’s bed. Not because he didn’t want to.

Because he’d said simple, and he meant it.

Sam stretched out on the couch, coat folded on the chair, boots lined neatly by the door like he belonged in a life that was calmer than his own.

The apartment creaked softly as it settled.

Outside, snow kept falling.

Inside, Sam Rook stayed.

---

Morning arrived quietly.

Grey light seeped through the curtains. The bakery below began its slow waking hum, pipes ticking, building shifting, the world remembering it had a schedule.

Wes woke once, disoriented, then remembered the weight of last night.

He lay still for a beat, staring at the ceiling, heart thudding.

He listened.

No sound from the bedroom except his own breathing.

He swallowed, chest tightening.

Then, from the living room, a soft noise: the rustle of fabric. The click of the kettle switch. A quiet footstep.

Wes’s breath let out like he’d been holding it in his sleep.

He rolled out of bed, hair a disaster, and padded toward the doorway.

Sam stood in the kitchen area in a plain black t-shirt, sleeves pushed up, quietly making tea like it was the most natural thing in the world.

Wes froze.

This was… domestic.

This was… dangerous.

Sam glanced up, immediately reading Wes’s face. “Morning.”

Wes stared. “You’re here.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “I said I would be.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “Yeah.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “You sleep okay.”

Wes’s mouth opened, and something honest almost came out.

Instead, Wes defaulted to sarcasm like it was a flotation device. “I drooled. If you tell anyone, I’ll burn the town down.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Noted.”

Wes drifted closer without meaning to, stopping near the counter like he belonged there. Like Sam belonged there.

Wes eyed the kettle. “You’re making tea.”

Sam nodded. “Hugh’s tin.”

Wes groaned. “Of course.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “It works.”

Wes muttered, “Hugh will be insufferable when he finds out his tea supported a romantic milestone.”

Sam’s gaze flicked to him, amused. “A milestone.”

Wes’s face warmed. “Don’t.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “You asked me to stay. I stayed.”

Wes stared at the counter, then at Sam’s hands, then at the mug Sam set down for him like Wes was expected.

Wes took it, fingers brushing Sam’s.

His pulse jumped, as if his body hadn’t gotten the memo that they weren’t in crisis anymore.

Wes cleared his throat. “So. Rules still apply.”

Sam nodded once. “Yes.”

Wes took a sip, then realized he was hungry in a quiet, normal way, not in a panic way. It was… new.

Wes blinked at the sensation like it was suspicious. “I’m hungry.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Good.”

Wes glared. “Stop.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Okay.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “That sounded fake again.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “It was.”

Wes huffed a laugh despite himself, then leaned back against the counter, tea warm in his hands, feeling his body slowly remember what it was like to exist without bracing.

Sam watched him for a beat. “You have to be at the bakery soon.”

Wes blinked. “Yeah.”

Sam’s tone stayed even. “Eat first.”

Wes rolled his eyes. “You’re all in a conspiracy.”

Sam’s brows lifted. “Who’s all.”

Wes gestured vaguely. “You. Noel. Eben. Hugh. Probably Harold Gibbons.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Harold definitely.”

Wes groaned and took another sip.

Then, because he apparently couldn’t help himself, Wes asked quietly, “Did I… make you uncomfortable. Sleeping here.”

Sam didn’t hesitate. “No.”

Wes swallowed. “You could’ve left.”

Sam’s gaze held his, steady as ever. “I didn’t want to.”

Wes’s chest tightened so sharply he almost flinched. He looked away fast, because he was still Wes, and receiving care was still a weird skill he hadn’t leveled up in.

The phone on the counter buzzed.

Wes glanced at it and groaned. “No.”

Sam looked at the screen. “Noel.”

Wes squinted. “How does he know you’re here.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “He doesn’t.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “He senses romance. Like a shark.”

Sam handed Wes the phone.

Wes answered with dread. “Noel.”

Noel’s voice burst through, bright and too awake. “WES. GOOD MORNING. ARE YOU ALIVE.”

Wes stared at the ceiling. “Barely.”

Noel didn’t pause. “Fantastic. Emergency but fun. Day One setpiece is moving up. We need Candle & Crumb’s cinnamon knots for the ‘First Light’ welcome table, and also, someone has put googly eyes on the inflatable snowman and Eben is threatening to commit a lawful execution.”

Wes pinched the bridge of his nose. “It’s 7:12 a.m.”

Noel’s voice was delighted. “Correct! The town is thriving!”

Wes sighed. “I’ll bring knots.”

Noel lowered his voice dramatically. “Also. Hugh is acting like he knows something. Do you know something.”

Wes’s face went hot.

Wes glanced at Sam, who watched him with quiet amusement and a patience that felt unfair.

Wes snapped, “Noel.”

Noel went instantly serious in that Noel way that meant he cared. “Wes.”

Wes swallowed. “I’m fine.”

Noel’s voice softened. “Okay.”

Wes blinked.

Noel added, gentler, “But you don’t have to be fine alone.”

Wes’s throat tightened. He glanced at Sam again.

Wes exhaled. “I’ll see you soon.”

Noel brightened back up like he’d been waiting for permission. “Great! Bring knots! Save Christmas! Love you bye!”

The call ended.

Wes stared at the phone in his hand like it had personally attacked him.

Sam’s voice was low. “Cinnamon knots.”

Wes muttered, “I’m going to drown in dough.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “I’ll walk you down.”

Wes blinked. “You don’t have to.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “I want to.”

Wes’s chest tightened again, annoying and warm.

Wes took one more sip of tea, then set the mug down carefully like he was handling something fragile.

He looked at Sam, then away, then back again.

Wes’s voice came out quiet. “Thank you. For staying.”

Sam’s expression softened, real and steady. “You’re welcome.”

Wes swallowed hard, then grabbed his apron like it was armor. “Fine,” Wes muttered. “Let’s go feed the town and prevent Eben from executing a snowman.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Sounds like a plan.”

Wes headed for the stairs, and Sam followed.

Not behind him like a guard.

Beside him like he belonged.

**[STOP WRITING]**



Wes discovered, immediately, that walking down the stairs beside Sam felt… illegal.

Not because it was scandalous. Because it was *simple.* Like it belonged in a life Wes hadn’t let himself imagine.

He tried to ruin it with attitude.

“You don’t have to escort me,” Wes muttered as they descended. “I’m not a fragile antique.”

Sam’s gaze flicked to him, warm. “You’re fragile in different ways.”

Wes’s foot missed a step by half an inch.

He caught himself, glare sharpening. “That was rude.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “True.”

Wes huffed, then pushed open the bakery door.

Candle & Crumb greeted him the way it always did: warmth, the faint hum of old ovens, the smell of yeast like a promise that something good was going to rise if Wes didn’t sabotage it.

Wes stepped in, shoulders squaring automatically, competence clicking into place like a switch.

Then he realized Sam was still there.

Standing in the doorway, watching the bakery like it was a sacred space and a potential crime scene at the same time.

Wes’s throat tightened, stupidly. “You can… come in.”

Sam did, calm and quiet.

Wes went straight to the prep counter, pulling out flour and sugar and butter with the speed of a man trying to outrun his own feelings. “Cinnamon knots,” he muttered. “Fine. Great. Lovely. I’ll just casually produce joy for an entire town before breakfast.”

Sam leaned lightly against the counter edge, hands relaxed, eyes tracking Wes’s movements. “You’re good at it.”

Wes snapped, “Stop.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes’s shoulders twitched with a laugh he tried to swallow. “You’re going to get punched with dough.”

Sam lifted his brows. “Threats.”

Wes glared. “Promises.”

Sam’s mouth tipped, and the tiny smile did something dangerous to Wes’s heart.

Wes turned away fast and started mixing, hands moving like they knew what to do even if Wes’s brain didn’t.

The dough came together under his palms. Smooth. Elastic. Alive.

Wes exhaled, grounding himself in the work. “Okay,” he muttered. “We do knots. We bake them. We save Christmas. We prevent Eben from executing an inflatable.”

Sam’s voice was low. “He would.”

Wes snorted. “He *wants* to.”

Sam watched Wes knead for a minute, then said quietly, “Do you want help.”

Wes blinked. “You can’t help. You’re a cop.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “I have hands.”

Wes’s face warmed. “Everyone has hands.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Let me use mine.”

Wes stared, caught on the phrasing, then hissed, “Not like that.”

Sam’s eyes warmed with amusement. “Like what.”

Wes pointed a floury hand at him. “Stop being calm. It’s suspicious.”

Sam nodded once. “Okay.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “Fake.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Yes.”

Wes exhaled and shoved a clean apron at Sam like it was a dare.

Sam took it without hesitation and tied it on with practiced ease that felt wildly unfair.

Wes stared. “Why do you know how to tie an apron.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “I can cook.”

Wes’s eyes widened. “You’ve been hiding information.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “I’m full of surprises.”

Wes muttered, “I hate that that’s attractive.”

Sam’s eyes flicked to him. “What.”

Wes snapped, “Nothing. Start rolling dough.”

Sam moved in, calm and competent, hands sure as he took a portion and began shaping it. Not perfect like a baker. But good. Careful. Like he respected the work.

Wes watched for half a second too long, then forcibly returned to his own dough.

They worked in a quiet rhythm, flour dust drifting in the warm air like soft snow.

Wes didn’t realize how tense he’d been until his shoulders started to loosen without him having to fight for it.

Sam glanced at him. “Better.”

Wes’s mouth tightened. “Don’t narrate me.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Okay.”

Wes’s lips twitched. “That one sounded real.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “It was.”

Wes’s chest did that tight, warm thing again.

He focused hard on braiding.

---

By the time the first tray went into the oven, the bakery smelled like cinnamon and butter and the beginning of a good day.

Wes packed a box with finished knots, still warm, and slammed the lid shut like he was closing up his dignity too.

“Alright,” Wes said. “We go to Merryweather Hall. We deliver. We flee before Hugh weaponizes crafts.”

Sam picked up the box like it weighed nothing. “I’ll carry it.”

Wes blinked. “You don’t have to.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “I want to.”

Wes’s throat tightened again, because apparently that was his personality now.

They locked up and headed back into the snow.

Walking through town with Sam carrying a box of cinnamon knots felt… dangerously like a couple thing.

Wes tried to scowl at the world to compensate.

It didn’t help.

---

Merryweather Hall was already buzzing when they arrived.

Noel met them at the door like he’d been waiting with a stopwatch and a prayer. “YES,” Noel breathed, eyes on the box. “The knots.”

Wes held up a finger. “No touching until you promise not to cry on them.”

Noel placed a hand on his chest. “I will cry internally.”

Eben appeared behind Noel, gaze landing on the box, then on Sam in the apron.

Eben blinked once.

Slowly.

Like he was processing a system update.

Wes braced for the comment.

Eben’s voice was flat. “Rook.”

Sam nodded. “Frost.”

Eben’s gaze dropped to the apron again. “Apron.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Apron.”

Wes covered his mouth with his fist to hide the laugh.

Noel’s eyes darted between them, delighted. “Okay! Great! Cinnamon knots are secured. Everyone lives. Now. Emergency Two.”

Wes sighed. “The snowman.”

Noel pointed toward the stage with the tragic intensity of a man cursed to care. “Someone put googly eyes on it. Huge ones. It looks… intimate.”

Wes blinked. “Intimate.”

Noel nodded gravely. “It’s looking at people like it knows their secrets.”

Eben’s jaw tightened. “Remove them.”

Noel whispered, “Hugh.”

Wes groaned. “Of course it’s Hugh.”

As if summoned by accusation, Hugh bounded in from the side hallway with a roll of tape and glitter-free cardstock in his arms.

“Good morning!” Hugh announced, too loudly. “I sensed cultural stagnation and intervened.”

Eben’s gaze turned cold. “Everhart.”

Hugh paused mid-bounce, beaming. “Yes, Spreadsheet King.”

Eben’s eyes narrowed. “You altered municipal decor.”

Hugh lifted his chin. “I improved it.”

Eben’s voice stayed calm, terrifyingly. “Remove the eyes.”

Hugh clutched the tape roll. “But it makes him expressive.”

Wes cut in, voice sharp. “Hugh. Those eyes are the size of saucers.”

Hugh nodded. “Correct. It’s called *impact.*”

Wes pointed toward the stage. “Take them off. Now. Before Eben arrests the snowman.”

Noel whispered to Wes, “He would.”

Wes whispered back, “I know.”

Sam, quietly, stepped in beside Wes. His presence shifted the energy like a hand on the volume knob.

Sam’s voice was calm. “Hugh.”

Hugh looked at Sam like he’d just spotted a celebrity. “Officer Rook. My hero.”

Sam didn’t smile, but his eyes warmed. “Remove the eyes.”

Hugh pouted. “Everyone hates art.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “Remove. Them.”

Hugh stared for a beat, then sighed dramatically. “Fine. I’ll downgrade him to… tasteful.”

Eben nodded once, satisfied, as Hugh stomped off toward the stage muttering about oppression and vision.

Noel exhaled. “Thank you.”

Wes glanced at Sam, low. “You’re terrifying.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Only when necessary.”

Wes’s cheeks warmed, because apparently the universe wanted him embarrassed at all times. “Right.”

Noel clapped his hands again, trying to herd momentum. “Okay! First Light welcome table needs knots, cocoa needs refills, and the parade staging is in ten. Wes, you’re on table display. Sam, can you help with crowd flow. Eben, please don’t kill anyone.”

Eben’s voice was flat. “No promises.”

Noel pointed. “Eben!”

Eben blinked slowly. “I will attempt restraint.”

Noel looked relieved like that was the best he’d ever get. “Perfect.”

Wes grabbed the box and started arranging knots at the welcome table with frantic precision. He made them look beautiful, because of course he did. Because if he couldn’t control his feelings, he could at least control pastry aesthetics.

Sam moved through the hall with quiet authority, guiding volunteers, repositioning stanchions, redirecting traffic like a human tide control system.

Wes kept catching glimpses of him. The way he spoke. The way people listened. The way he checked corners without looking like he was checking corners.

Wes’s chest felt too full.

Hugh returned from the stage, triumphant. “Tasteful eyes applied,” he announced.

Noel squinted. “Those are still big.”

Hugh sniffed. “They’re *moderately* big.”

Eben’s gaze narrowed. “Acceptable.”

Noel whispered to Wes, “He said acceptable. He’s basically euphoric.”

Wes snorted.

Then the front doors opened, and the first wave of visitors swept into Merryweather Hall, rosy-cheeked and excited, kids dragging parents toward the cocoa station, couples huddling in scarves, townsfolk greeting each other like the festival was a reunion.

Wes straightened, smile going on like armor.

“Welcome,” Wes said, bright and practiced. “Cinnamon knots are here. Your joy is mandatory.”

A woman laughed. “Wes Candle, you’re a treasure.”

Wes forced a grin. “I’m a carbohydrate dealer.”

Sam’s gaze flicked over, warm, like he liked watching Wes in his element.

Wes immediately looked away and busied himself refilling a tray.

Because being seen like that felt like standing in sunlight with no hat.

---

The morning blurred into motion.

Knot trays emptied. Cocoa refilled. Noel darted like a pinned Christmas ornament. Eben stalked the perimeter like a winter storm disguised as a man.

At one point, a toddler tried to climb the stage stairs, and Sam calmly scooped him up and handed him back to a grateful parent without missing a beat.

Wes watched it happen and felt his heart do something ridiculous.

He wasn’t even sure what it was.

He just knew it was building.

When the crowd finally eased, Wes leaned back against the table and exhaled.

Sam appeared beside him like he’d been waiting for the exact moment Wes’s knees might wobble.

Sam’s voice was low. “You okay.”

Wes stared at him. “Stop asking that in public.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Answer anyway.”

Wes swallowed. The truth rose up like it had been waiting.

“I’m tired,” Wes admitted quietly. “But… not the bad kind.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “Good.”

Wes glared automatically. “Stop.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes’s mouth twitched. “You’re going to get me smiling in front of civilians.”

Sam’s voice went lower, close enough to make Wes’s skin prickle. “Later,” Sam murmured, like he was stacking promises again.

Wes’s pulse jumped. “You love that word.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “I love what it means.”

Wes’s breath caught.

He looked away fast, because he was still learning how to exist when someone spoke to him like he mattered.

A scream erupted from the stage area.

Wes flinched. “What now.”

Noel’s voice rang out, horrified: “THE SNOWMAN HAS LOST AN EYE.”

Eben’s voice followed, flat and lethal: “Everhart.”

Hugh’s voice yelled back, offended: “IT FELL OFF ON ITS OWN. THAT’S CALLED REALISM.”

Wes closed his eyes. “I’m going to bed.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “After breakfast.”

Wes opened one eye, deadpan. “It’s noon.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “Still.”

Wes exhaled a laugh he couldn’t stop, and Sam watched him like the laugh was the point.

Wes’s voice came out quiet, surprised at himself. “I’m glad you’re here.”

Sam didn’t gloat. Didn’t tease. Just nodded, steady.

“I’m glad too,” Sam said softly.

And for one dangerous second, in the middle of Merryweather Hall’s chaos, Wes believed him.

**[STOP WRITING]**



Noel’s scream had the raw, operatic force of a man witnessing the collapse of civilization.

Wes opened both eyes and pushed off the table with a sigh. “I swear to God, if I have to do emergency triage on an inflatable snowman, I’m moving to the woods.”

Sam’s gaze flicked over him, amused. “You’d still end up on a committee.”

Wes muttered, “I hate that you’re right.”

They cut through the hall toward the stage area, weaving around stragglers and half-collapsed garland piles. The snowman loomed near the stage steps, cheerful and massive and now lopsided in a way that made him look… emotionally compromised.

One googly eye remained. The other had vanished.

Hugh Everhart stood beneath it, hands on hips, jaw set like he was a misunderstood artist in a courtroom.

Eben Frost stood opposite him with the expression of a man considering whether inflatable decor could be tried for war crimes.

Noel hovered between them, visibly vibrating. “It was PERFECT,” Noel hissed. “And now it’s… it’s giving *cursed.*”

“It fell off!” Hugh protested. “On its own! That’s physics!”

Eben’s voice was quiet and lethal. “You attached it with tape.”

Hugh lifted a finger. “Yes. And tape is a temporary adhesive. Which means this is not my fault. This is the tape’s fault.”

Eben didn’t blink. “The tape did not choose this.”

Wes arrived beside Noel and looked up at the snowman. “Okay,” Wes said, voice flat. “Where’s the eye.”

Hugh pointed at the floor dramatically. “It bounced. Rolled. Disappeared.”

Noel whispered, “It’s gone to haunt the children.”

Wes crouched and scanned the stage edge, then looked at the crowd nearby.

A little kid stood holding the missing googly eye like it was treasure.

The kid’s mother looked mortified. “I’m so sorry,” she said. “He found it and said it was ‘the snowman’s soul.’”

Noel made a strangled sound. “It *is.*”

Wes stood and smiled at the kid. “Hey, buddy,” Wes said gently. “Can you give the snowman his soul back.”

The kid considered Wes like Wes had asked him to surrender a crown. Then he nodded solemnly and held it out.

Wes accepted the eye with both hands like it was sacred. “Thank you. You saved Christmas.”

The kid beamed.

Noel exhaled like he’d survived a near-death experience. “Okay. Okay. Crisis resolved.”

Eben extended a hand. “Tape.”

Hugh recoiled. “No. Not you. You’ll tape it on with spite.”

Eben’s gaze narrowed. “Correct.”

Sam stepped in quietly, palm open. “Give me the tape.”

Hugh blinked, offended. “Why you.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “Because you’ll accept it, and Eben won’t explode.”

Hugh hesitated, then handed Sam the roll like he was conceding to diplomacy.

Sam crouched, retaped the eye with clinical efficiency, pressed it in place, and gave the snowman a small, firm pat like he was closing a case.

“Done,” Sam said.

Noel clasped his hands under his chin. “He’s whole again.”

Eben nodded once, satisfied, and walked away like he’d been deprived of a righteous execution.

Hugh watched him go and muttered, “That man is a frostbite in pants.”

Wes muttered back, “That is literally his name.”

Hugh gasped. “I’m a genius.”

Noel turned on Wes with sudden intensity. “Okay. Next thing. Day One is stabilized. Day Two is tomorrow. And Day Two is…”

Wes’s eyes narrowed. “Noel.”

Noel’s grin turned feral. “The Mistletoe Mixer.”

Wes closed his eyes. “No.”

Noel grabbed his clipboard like he was about to commit a crime. “Yes.”

Sam’s gaze slid to Wes, amused.

Wes stared at Noel. “That is a recipe for public humiliation.”

Noel’s eyes sparkled. “That is the point.”

Wes whispered, “I hate you.”

Noel whispered back, “You love me.”

Eben’s voice drifted from across the room, flat: “He does.”

Wes snapped, “TRAITORS.”

Noel patted Wes’s shoulder. “Relax. It’s just a mixer. People mingle. They’re tasteful. They kiss their spouses on the cheek. They pretend they’re not dying inside. It’s fine.”

Wes stared. “You are describing my personal hell.”

Noel leaned closer, eyes bright with mischief and something gentler underneath. “Also, it’s a fundraiser. And it’s cute. And the town expects you to make festive pastries shaped like tiny bows.”

Wes’s resolve cracked at the word *pastries.*

Wes grimaced. “Fine. I’ll make bows.”

Noel smiled triumphantly. “Amazing. Now I’m going to say something and you can’t stop me.”

Wes tensed. “Noel.”

Noel’s gaze flicked to Sam, then back to Wes, and his voice went a little softer. “If you don’t want attention, we’ll keep it low-key.”

Wes blinked.

Noel shrugged like it was nothing. “I’m chaos, not cruel.”

Wes’s throat tightened. He nodded once, the only kind of gratitude he could tolerate.

Noel brightened again immediately. “Great! Now I need you to take the welcome table for another hour while I go stop Hugh from crafting propaganda.”

Hugh called from behind them, offended: “I HEARD THAT.”

Noel yelled back, “GOOD.”

Noel disappeared into the crowd like a festive hurricane.

Wes stood there for a beat, watching him go, then let out a slow breath.

Sam stayed beside him, quiet.

Wes glanced at Sam. “You’re smiling.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Noel is… intense.”

Wes muttered, “That’s one word.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “I like him.”

Wes stared. “Why.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “He cares.”

Wes’s chest tightened again, because apparently everything was going to make him emotional today.

Wes looked away. “Yeah.”

Sam’s gaze held him for a beat. “You care too.”

Wes scoffed weakly. “Don’t start.”

Sam didn’t push. He just said softly, “Okay.”

Wes rolled his eyes, but it didn’t have bite.

They drifted back toward the welcome table. The crowd had thinned again, the morning rush settling into a steady festive hum.

Wes rearranged the cinnamon knots like he was building order out of sugar.

Sam hovered nearby, scanning the room, occasionally intercepting small kid-chaos with gentle efficiency.

A woman approached the table and smiled warmly at Wes. “These are beautiful,” she said, taking a knot. “You’re always such a steady presence at this festival.”

Wes forced his practiced smile. “Someone has to stop Noel from ascending into pure holiday energy.”

She laughed, then glanced at Sam. “And you,” she said to Sam, playful, “I didn’t know you did… baking.”

Sam’s gaze flicked to Wes, checking.

Wes’s throat tightened.

The woman continued, teasing but kind: “You two make quite the team.”

Wes’s face warmed. He opened his mouth to deflect.

Sam beat him to it, voice calm. “He’s doing the work,” Sam said, nodding toward Wes. “I’m just helping.”

Wes blinked.

It was perfect. True. Modest. And it didn’t invite more questions.

The woman smiled, satisfied, and wandered off with her knot.

Wes exhaled, relieved.

Sam leaned in slightly, low enough for only Wes. “You okay.”

Wes shot him a look. “Stop asking me that in public.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Answer anyway.”

Wes swallowed, then said quietly, “I’m okay.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “Good.”

Wes muttered, “Stop.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “No.”

Wes’s mouth twitched, half a smile.

After a beat, Wes said, almost against his will, “Thank you.”

Sam’s eyes stayed steady. “For what.”

Wes glanced at the crowd, then back. “For… not making it a thing.”

Sam’s voice was calm. “You asked for private.”

Wes’s chest tightened. “Yeah.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “I heard you.”

Wes swallowed hard and looked away, because he didn’t know what to do with the feeling of being listened to.

---

By mid-afternoon, the hall started to empty. Families drifted home. Volunteers slumped into chairs. Noel finally sat down and stared into the void for ten full seconds.

Eben appeared at Noel’s shoulder and quietly set a bottle of water in front of him like a offering.

Noel blinked at it. “Are you… caring.”

Eben’s voice was flat. “Hydrate.”

Noel stared for a beat, then whispered, delighted, “He loves me.”

Eben didn’t react. But his ear went faintly pink.

Wes caught it, and for once, the sight of someone else being quietly, awkwardly cared for didn’t make him want to run.

It made him feel… less alone.

Sam found Wes near the coat rack, folding tablecloths with grim determination.

Sam’s voice was low. “You’re done.”

Wes didn’t look up. “I’m never done.”

Sam stepped closer. “Wes.”

Wes stopped moving.

Sam’s tone stayed calm. “We’re leaving.”

Wes exhaled. “Bossy.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Yes.”

Wes finally looked up, and his defenses tried to rally. Then he saw Sam’s eyes, steady and warm, and the defenses just… sighed.

Wes nodded once. “Okay.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “Okay.”

Wes grabbed his coat, then hesitated. “Do we… walk.”

Sam nodded. “Yeah.”

Wes swallowed. “Quietly.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Quietly.”

They slipped out into the snow, the town glowing soft behind them.

Wes walked beside Sam, hands in his pockets, shoulders hunched against the cold.

After two blocks, Wes said quietly, “Tomorrow is the Mistletoe Mixer.”

Sam’s gaze flicked to him. “I heard.”

Wes grimaced. “Noel is going to set traps.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Probably.”

Wes muttered, “Hugh will craft propaganda.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Definitely.”

Wes exhaled, then said the thing he was trying not to say. “If there’s… mistletoe.”

Sam didn’t tease. He just waited.

Wes swallowed. “Don’t make me perform.”

Sam’s voice was calm. “I won’t.”

Wes’s chest tightened. “Promise.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “I promise.”

Wes nodded once, and the tension in his shoulders loosened.

They walked in silence for a while, snow crunching under their boots, streetlights turning the world into a quiet glittering tunnel.

Then Sam’s voice came low. “Later.”

Wes’s pulse jumped. “Stop.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes huffed a laugh despite himself, because apparently his body had decided this was safe enough to be happy in.

They reached Candle & Crumb.

Wes unlocked the door and paused.

This time, he didn’t call it a minute.

He just stepped aside.

Sam followed him in.

And the bakery door clicked shut behind them like a promise kept.

**[STOP WRITING]**



The bakery after hours always felt like a different world.

No crowd. No music. No Noel shouting “joy is mandatory” like he could legally enforce happiness. Just the soft hum of refrigeration, the settling creak of old wood, and the lingering smell of cinnamon that clung to everything like a gentle curse.

Wes locked the front door, slid the bolt into place, and leaned his forehead against the glass for one long second.

Sam stood a few feet behind him, quiet, patient, letting Wes decompress without narrating it.

Wes exhaled. “Okay.”

Sam’s voice was low. “Okay.”

Wes didn’t even glare this time. He just turned, shrugged out of his coat, and hung it on the hook with the ritual care of a man who needed small control to keep his brain from sprinting.

Sam followed suit, folding his coat neatly, like he was allergic to mess.

Wes watched him do it and muttered, “You’re unsettling.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Because I fold things.”

“Yes,” Wes said firmly. “It’s suspiciously functional.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “You’re surrounded by sugar chaos all day. Let me have one thing.”

Wes’s mouth twitched. “Fine.”

They stood in the quiet for a beat, then Wes moved behind the counter and started wiping down surfaces that didn’t need wiping, because apparently his hands only knew how to exist when they were doing.

Sam stepped closer, calm. “Wes.”

Wes didn’t look up. “If you tell me to eat, I’m filing a complaint.”

Sam’s voice stayed even. “Sit.”

Wes froze.

He looked up slowly, like Sam had just spoken profanity in a holy place. “Excuse me.”

Sam nodded toward the stool behind the counter. “Sit. Five minutes.”

Wes blinked. “Why.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “Because you’ve been moving since yesterday.”

Wes opened his mouth to argue.

Then he realized he didn’t want to argue. Not really. Not with that look.

Wes muttered, “Bossy.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Yes.”

Wes sighed, defeated, and sat on the stool like it was a personal compromise with the universe.

Sam stepped closer, leaning against the counter across from him. Not crowding. Just there.

For a minute, they listened to the bakery breathe.

Wes’s shoulders dropped a fraction.

Sam’s voice came low. “You did good.”

Wes rolled his eyes automatically, but the reaction had less bite. “Stop.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “No.”

Wes huffed a laugh, then stared at the floor, quieter. “It didn’t feel like good.”

Sam’s expression softened. “It doesn’t have to feel good to be good.”

Wes’s throat tightened. He swallowed. “That’s… annoying.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “True.”

Wes stared at him, and the quiet steadiness of Sam’s presence pressed against all the places Wes usually kept locked.

Wes looked away fast. “Tomorrow is the Mistletoe Mixer.”

Sam’s gaze didn’t waver. “Yeah.”

Wes’s jaw tightened. “It’s going to be… a thing.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “It doesn’t have to be.”

Wes exhaled through his nose. “Noel will make it one.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “We plan around Noel.”

Wes blinked. “We.”

Sam didn’t flinch. “We.”

Wes’s chest went tight and warm, an infuriating combo.

Wes tried to regain control with sarcasm. “Okay, Officer. What’s the plan.”

Sam’s gaze softened, serious. “You tell me what you want. I follow it.”

Wes stared.

That shouldn’t have hit as hard as it did.

Wes swallowed. “I want… slow.”

Sam nodded once. “Okay.”

Wes’s voice got quieter. “And private.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “Okay.”

Wes hesitated, then forced the hardest piece out. “And if the town starts sniffing around, I want you to… not joke about it.”

Sam’s expression softened. “I won’t.”

Wes blinked. “Promise.”

Sam’s voice was calm and certain. “I promise.”

Wes exhaled, and it came out shaky despite his best efforts.

Sam watched him for a beat, then said quietly, “You can also say you don’t want to go.”

Wes’s eyes widened. “I have to go.”

Sam’s brow lifted. “Do you.”

Wes’s mouth opened, then closed.

Because no, he didn’t *have* to. He just… always did. He always filled the gaps. Always kept the machine running.

Wes’s voice came out rough. “If I don’t show up, Noel will try to do everything himself.”

Sam nodded once, like he understood the math of it. “Then we go. We keep it simple. You do what you need to do. I’m there. No games.”

Wes stared at him. “You’re going to be there.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “If you want me there.”

Wes swallowed. “I… yeah. I do.”

Sam didn’t smile like he’d won. He just nodded, steady, like Wes had given him a job he intended to keep.

“Okay,” Sam said softly.

Wes’s mouth twitched despite himself. “Stop.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes’s laugh came out quiet and helpless.

Then the silence returned, softer now.

Wes looked at Sam, then down, then back again, like he was still getting used to the idea that asking didn’t automatically mean losing.

Wes cleared his throat. “You want… tea.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Do you want tea.”

Wes sighed. “Yes. I want tea.”

Sam nodded. “Then yes.”

Wes shook his head like he couldn’t believe this was his life, and hopped off the stool.

He moved to the little back prep area and put the kettle on, the ritual familiar enough to keep him grounded. He pulled out Hugh’s tin again, because apparently Hugh had become an invasive species in Wes’s home ecosystem.

Wes muttered, “I can’t believe I’m drinking Hugh’s feelings tea.”

Sam’s voice drifted from behind him, amused. “It works.”

Wes poured two mugs and carried them back to the counter, setting one down for Sam.

Their fingers brushed.

Wes’s breath caught, stupidly.

Sam didn’t move away. He just looked at Wes, steady.

Wes’s voice came out quiet. “Do you ever… get tired of being the calm one.”

Sam took the mug, gaze still on Wes. “Sometimes.”

Wes blinked. “What do you do.”

Sam’s expression softened. “I find a place that feels safe.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “And that’s… here.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “Yeah.”

Wes stared at him for a long beat, then looked away fast because his eyes were doing that irritating stingy thing again.

Wes lifted his mug and took a sip so he’d have an excuse not to talk.

The tea tasted like cinnamon and permission.

Wes swallowed, then said, very quietly, “I’m not good at this.”

Sam’s voice was gentle. “You don’t have to be good. You just have to be honest.”

Wes’s chest tightened. “I am being honest.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “I know.”

Wes set the mug down carefully, like his hands needed a job. Then he leaned forward on the counter, gaze on the wood grain, and whispered, “I keep waiting for the part where you change your mind.”

Sam didn’t answer with a joke. He stepped closer instead, slow and careful, until Wes could feel his warmth across the counter.

Sam’s voice was low. “Look at me.”

Wes hesitated, then lifted his eyes.

Sam’s gaze was steady, open. “I won’t change my mind without telling you,” Sam said. “And I’m not looking for an exit.”

Wes’s throat tightened painfully. “Why.”

Sam’s expression softened. “Because I like you,” he said simply. “And because you matter.”

Wes’s breath hitched.

He hated how much that landed. Hated how his whole body responded like it had been starving for those words.

Wes’s voice came out rough. “If you say stuff like that, I’m going to do something stupid.”

Sam’s mouth tipped, barely. “Like what.”

Wes swallowed hard. “Like trust you.”

Sam’s gaze warmed, serious. “Okay.”

Wes blinked. “Stop saying okay like it’s a spell.”

Sam’s voice stayed soft. “It’s not a spell. It’s consent.”

Wes’s cheeks warmed.

Then, because Wes was apparently determined to test every boundary he had, he stepped out from behind the counter and into Sam’s space.

He didn’t announce it. Didn’t perform it. He just moved closer until their bodies were almost touching.

Wes’s voice went quiet. “Do you want to stay upstairs again.”

Sam’s eyes held his. “Do you want me to.”

Wes swallowed. “Yes.”

Sam nodded once, slow. “Okay.”

Wes exhaled, and it came out like relief.

Sam lifted a hand, hovering, waiting.

Wes nodded, giving permission he was still learning to give.

Sam’s fingers brushed Wes’s cheek, gentle. Warm.

Wes leaned into it before he could stop himself.

Sam kissed him, slow and soft, not hungry, not demanding. Just steady.

Wes’s hands fisted lightly in Sam’s shirt like he needed something solid to hold onto while his brain tried to sprint away.

Sam pulled back just enough to murmur, “Still okay.”

Wes’s voice was wrecked. “Yes.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “Good.”

Wes closed his eyes for a beat, forehead tipping forward until it rested against Sam’s.

He whispered, barely audible, “Stay.”

Sam’s voice was quiet and absolute. “I’m staying.”

They turned off the bakery lights together.

Then Wes led Sam upstairs, one step at a time, slow like he’d asked for, heart thudding like it finally believed the promise.

And this time, when they reached Wes’s door, Wes didn’t call it a minute.

He just opened it and let Sam in.

**[STOP WRITING]**


Wes’s apartment felt different when Sam walked in like he belonged.

Not like he owned it. Not like he was claiming it. Just… like he didn’t need to be convinced the space could hold him.

Wes shut the door behind them and stood there for a second, hand still on the knob, like he was waiting for the part where he panicked and ruined it.

Sam didn’t rush him. Sam just took off his coat, folded it neatly (because of course he did), and set it on the chair like it was normal to be here.

Wes stared at the coat. “You fold things like you’re auditioning to be a husband.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “I fold things like I don’t want wrinkles.”

Wes scoffed. “Suspicious.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “You’re stalling.”

Wes blinked. “I am not.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “You are.”

Wes exhaled through his nose, caught. “Fine.”

He walked into the living room, paused, then sat on the couch like it was safer than standing. Like if he sat, he couldn’t accidentally sprint away from his own feelings.

Sam followed and sat beside him, leaving space. Not too much. Not too little.
