Wes stared at his hands. “I don’t know what to do with this,” he admitted, voice low.

Sam’s voice was calm. “We don’t have to do anything.”

Wes blinked. “That sounds fake.”

Sam shook his head slightly. “It’s not.”

Wes swallowed. “Then… what are we doing.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “Being here.”

Wes’s chest tightened, because of course it did.

He tried to make it a joke. “So you’re breaking and entering into my emotional ecosystem.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “You invited me.”

Wes muttered, “That was a lapse in judgment.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “Or a choice.”

Wes stared at him, then looked away fast, cheeks hot.

The silence held for a minute, but it wasn’t awkward. It was… careful. Like both of them were handling something fragile without pretending it wasn’t fragile.

Wes cleared his throat. “Tomorrow,” he said, because planning was safer than feelings. “Mistletoe Mixer.”

Sam nodded once. “Yeah.”

Wes grimaced. “Noel is going to try to orchestrate romance like it’s a parade float.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Probably.”

Wes’s mouth tightened. “And if Hugh puts mistletoe over the cocoa station, I’m committing crimes.”

Sam’s tone stayed even. “I’ll stop you.”

Wes glanced at him. “You’re supposed to be on my side.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “I am. I’m also on the side of ‘no crimes.’”

Wes huffed, then rubbed his face. “Okay. Tomorrow. We keep it simple.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “Tell me what simple looks like.”

Wes took a breath. “Simple looks like… we do our jobs. We don’t get cornered. We don’t get forced into a performance.”

Sam nodded. “Okay.”

Wes added, quieter, “And if we do get cornered…”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “Then we leave.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “Yeah.”

Sam’s voice went softer. “And if you want, we can set a signal.”

Wes blinked. “A signal.”

Sam nodded. “Something you say when you’re overwhelmed. I get you out.”

Wes’s chest went tight in that warm, aching way that made him want to throw something and also cry.

Wes swallowed. “That’s… a lot.”

Sam didn’t flinch. “It’s simple.”

Wes stared at him. “You’re going to ruin me with competence.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Okay.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “Don’t.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Sorry.”

Wes blinked. “Was that real.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “Yes.”

Wes exhaled a laugh he didn’t mean to have.

He leaned back against the couch, shoulders dropping. His body was still learning what it felt like to be tired without being afraid.

Wes stared at the ceiling. “So… you’re staying.”

Sam’s voice was calm. “If you want me to.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “I do.”

Sam nodded once. “Okay.”

Wes’s mouth twitched. “Stop saying okay.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes turned his head to look at him, and the way Sam looked back made Wes’s stomach flip.

Not hungry. Not frantic. Just… steady want.

Wes swallowed hard. “Can I…,” he started, then stopped, because asking was still new and terrifying.

Sam’s voice stayed gentle. “Can you what.”

Wes’s cheeks warmed. “Can I have another kiss.”

Sam didn’t tease him. Didn’t pounce. Just nodded slowly, like Wes had asked for a glass of water.

“Yeah,” Sam said softly. “You can.”

Wes leaned in first this time, because if he didn’t, he’d overthink it into dust.

The kiss was slow. Warm. Unhurried. The kind of kiss that didn’t escalate unless both people wanted it to.

Wes’s hand slid to Sam’s shoulder, fingers curling there. Sam’s hand settled at Wes’s waist, grounding.

Wes exhaled against Sam’s mouth like his body had been holding its breath for years.

Sam pulled back just enough to murmur, “Still okay.”

Wes’s voice came out rough. “Yes.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “Good.”

Wes made a small, helpless sound and rested his forehead against Sam’s for a beat.

Then Wes leaned back, blinking like he’d just survived something.

Wes muttered, “This is… dangerous.”

Sam’s voice stayed steady. “We keep it slow.”

Wes nodded. “Slow.”

Sam’s eyes softened. “Do you want me to sleep on the couch again.”

Wes’s chest tightened. He hesitated. Then forced the truth out. “I want you close,” Wes admitted quietly. “But I don’t want… pressure.”

Sam nodded immediately. “No pressure.”

Wes swallowed. “So… bed. But simple.”

Sam’s gaze held his, calm. “Okay. Bed. Simple.”

Wes’s throat tightened again. “Promise.”

Sam’s voice was quiet and absolute. “I promise.”

Wes stood, offering Sam a hand like it was a treaty.

Sam took it, warm fingers closing around Wes’s.

Wes’s pulse jumped, because apparently holding hands was now something that could destroy him.

They moved to the bedroom together, slow, no rushing, no performance. Wes changed into sweatpants and an old t-shirt without looking at Sam too much, because being watched still made him feel exposed.

Sam changed too, calm and unbothered, like bodies were just bodies and this didn’t have to be a test.

Wes slid under the covers and stared at the ceiling like it was going to grade him.

Sam climbed in beside him, leaving space again.

Wes turned his head slightly. “Closer,” he said, voice barely above a whisper.

Sam shifted closer, careful, and Wes felt the warmth of him at his side.

Wes’s breath shuddered out.

Sam’s voice was low. “This okay.”

Wes nodded. “Yeah.”

Sam’s arm came around Wes gently, not pinning, not claiming. Just holding.

Wes’s body softened like it had been waiting for that exact shape.

Wes whispered into the dark, “If you leave tomorrow without telling me, I’ll pretend I’m fine and then I’ll implode.”

Sam’s voice didn’t wobble. “I won’t.”

Wes swallowed. “Okay.”

Sam’s eyes warmed in the dark, like he could hear the relief in the word. “Okay.”

Wes huffed a small laugh, then yawned, exhaustion finally catching him now that he’d stopped fighting it.

Sam’s voice went softer. “Sleep.”

Wes muttered, “You’re bossy.”

Sam’s mouth tipped against Wes’s hair. “Yes.”

Wes’s eyes closed.

And because the world is occasionally merciful, it stayed quiet.

---

Wes woke up to light filtering in through the curtains and the steady warmth of Sam’s arm still around him.

No empty apartment. No panic. No sudden drop.

Wes lay still for a beat, then whispered, barely, “You’re still here.”

Sam’s voice was rough with sleep. “Yeah.”

Wes’s chest tightened. “Good.”

Sam’s arm tightened gently for half a second, like an answer.

Wes blinked, then remembered the calendar like it was a threat.

“Today is Day Two,” Wes muttered.

Sam’s eyes opened slowly. “Mistletoe.”

Wes grimaced. “Mistletoe.”

Sam’s gaze slid to Wes. “We do the signal.”

Wes exhaled. “Right. Signal.”

Sam waited.

Wes thought for a second, then muttered, “If I say ‘I need more cinnamon,’ you get me out.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Understood.”

Wes’s cheeks warmed. “And if you say it.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “Then you get me out.”

Wes blinked. “You’d need out.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “I’m not immune to chaos.”

Wes huffed a laugh. “Fair.”

Sam shifted slightly, eyes on Wes. “Before we go… do you want a kiss.”

Wes’s stomach flipped. He tried to look unimpressed and failed. “Yes.”

Sam kissed him, soft and brief, like a promise with good manners.

Sam pulled back. “Still okay.”

Wes breathed, “Yes.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Good.”

Wes groaned quietly. “I’m never going to survive you.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “We’ll see.”

Wes lay there for a beat longer, warm and held, and thought, with a kind of startled clarity:

This might be what safe feels like.

And he might be ready to want it.

**[STOP WRITING]**


Wes made it exactly four minutes after getting out of bed before his brain tried to sabotage him.

He stood at the sink, splashing water on his face, staring at his reflection like it was an enemy combatant.

“You are fine,” Wes told himself.

His reflection looked unconvinced. His hair was doing something criminal. His eyes were softer than they had any right to be. There was a faint crease at his mouth like he’d forgotten how to *not* smile.

Behind him, Sam moved through the tiny apartment with quiet efficiency, pulling on a sweater, checking his phone, folding a blanket on the couch like it had personally offended him overnight.

Wes watched him in the mirror and felt his chest tighten again.

Stupid.

Wes cleared his throat. “We’re going to get caught.”

Sam’s voice was calm. “Maybe.”

Wes turned, annoyed. “That’s not reassuring.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “You asked for honest.”

Wes glared. “I asked for slow.”

Sam nodded. “And private. We can do both.”

Wes exhaled. “How.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “We don’t linger in doorways. We don’t stand under mistletoe. If Hugh approaches, we pivot.”

Wes blinked. “Pivot.”

Sam nodded like this was normal. “Pivot.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “You have a plan for Hugh.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Always have a plan for Hugh.”

Wes rubbed his face. “Okay. Fine. We go. We do Day Two. We survive Noel. We evade mistletoe traps.”

Sam’s voice was low. “And if you need out.”

Wes swallowed. “Cinnamon.”

Sam nodded once. “More cinnamon.”

Wes’s cheeks warmed. “Don’t say it like that.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Like what.”

Wes pointed at him. “Like it’s a code word that turns you on.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “It’s a code word that gets you safe.”

Wes’s throat tightened. He looked away fast. “Right.”

Sam watched him for a beat, then said softly, “You ready.”

Wes grabbed his coat like it was armor. “No.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “Same.”

Wes stared. “You just said you’re calm.”

Sam shrugged. “I can be calm and still not enjoy mixers.”

Wes huffed a laugh, relieved. “Okay. Good.”

Sam’s eyes softened. “Yeah.”

They headed downstairs and out into the cold.

---

Merryweather Hall looked… suspicious.

It was too decorated.

Too curated.

Too aggressively romantic for a town that usually reserved this level of intention for bake sales and passive-aggressive charity auctions.

Wes stopped at the front steps and stared.

There were ribbons everywhere. Candle clusters. A sign that read:

**MISTLETOE MIXER: FIND YOUR SPARK**

Wes’s soul tried to leave his body.

Noel swept out of the doors like a festive mastermind, eyes bright, cheeks flushed, scarf immaculate.

“WELCOME,” Noel announced. “TO LOVE.”

Wes hissed, “Noel.”

Noel beamed harder. “Wes!”

Wes pointed at the sign. “What is this.”

Noel clasped his hands. “This is community.”

Wes stared. “This is entrapment.”

Noel leaned in, delighted whisper. “This is fundraising.”

Wes’s resolve weakened immediately. “How much.”

Noel held up two fingers. “We raised two thousand already and the doors aren’t even open.”

Wes’s mouth tightened, begrudging. “Fine. But I’m not participating.”

Noel’s eyes widened with theatrical innocence. “Participating in what.”

Wes pointed upward.

Noel followed his finger, then gasped like he’d discovered oxygen. Hanging above the entryway, right in the center, was a lush mistletoe bundle the size of a small animal.

Noel smiled sweetly. “Isn’t it cute.”

Wes’s voice went flat. “No.”

Noel’s grin turned feral. “It’s *adorable.*”

Sam stepped forward slightly, calm presence cutting through Wes’s rising panic like a steady hand on the volume knob.

Sam’s voice was even. “We’ll enter through the side.”

Noel blinked. “There’s a side entrance?”

Eben’s voice drifted from inside, flat as law: “There is always a side entrance.”

Eben appeared in the doorway like a winter omen, clipboard in hand, gaze sweeping the steps with practiced displeasure.

Noel turned, offended. “Eben, don’t undermine my romance architecture.”

Eben didn’t blink. “It is a fire hazard.”

Noel clutched his chest. “It is a *love* hazard.”

Eben’s gaze slid to Wes and Sam, then away. “Side entrance. Now. Before you’re noticed.”

Wes stared at Eben. “Are you… helping.”

Eben’s voice was flat. “Crowd control.”

Noel whispered, starry-eyed, “He loves me.”

Eben didn’t react. “Move.”

Wes exhaled a laugh, half disbelief, half relief.

Sam guided Wes down the steps and around the building, away from the main entrance and the giant mistletoe ambush.

Wes muttered, “I can’t believe Eben is saving me from a shrub.”

Sam’s voice was low. “He’s saving the event from an incident.”

Wes glanced at him. “You mean me punching Noel.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Yes.”

Wes huffed. “Fair.”

They slipped in through the side door.

Inside, Merryweather Hall was transformed: soft lights, garlands draped like intentional art, tables set with little cards that said things like **“Share a favorite holiday memory”** and **“What’s your idea of a perfect winter night?”**

Wes stared at the cards like they were weapons.

“This is a psychological attack,” he whispered.

Sam’s eyes warmed. “You’ll survive.”

Wes muttered, “I hate this.”

Sam’s voice stayed steady. “I know.”

That “I know” landed like care, not dismissal.

Wes swallowed and nodded once.

---

The doors opened.

The town poured in.

Not a riot, thankfully. More like a swarm of cheerful predators.

Couples arrived holding hands. Singles arrived with determination. Everyone arrived with the energy of people who had decided this was their chance to be festive and maybe get kissed.

Wes immediately got put to work at the pastry table, because Noel had correctly assumed that giving Wes a task was the only way to stop him from evaporating.

Wes arranged cookies shaped like bows and stars, trays of cinnamon knots, small pastries dusted with sugar like snow.

A woman leaned in, smiling. “Wes Candle, you’re spoiling us.”

Wes forced his professional grin. “It’s a sickness.”

Sam moved through the crowd near the edges, quiet and steady, keeping pathways clear, redirecting enthusiastic drifters, gently stopping a man from placing a chair directly in front of an emergency exit.

Wes kept catching glimpses of him.

Sam’s calm felt like a rope tied around Wes’s waist.

Not to restrain him. To keep him from drifting out to sea.

Noel drifted by, radiant, wearing a blazer that made him look like a Christmas-themed prince. He gave Wes a thumbs up and whispered, “You’re doing great.”

Wes hissed, “No mistletoe near me.”

Noel whispered back, delighted, “No promises.”

Wes glared.

Noel laughed and vanished into the crowd.

---

An hour in, the mixer was doing what mixers did.

People laughed too loudly. Sipped too much. Played the icebreaker games like their dignity was optional.

Wes was surviving, mostly by staying behind the pastry table like it was a fortress.

Then Hugh Everhart appeared.

Hugh wore a sweater that had a reindeer on it with sequins. The reindeer looked like it was judging everyone.

Hugh clasped his hands. “Wes.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “Hugh.”

Hugh leaned in, stage-whispering. “How is your romantic concealment going.”

Wes’s jaw tightened. “I will end you.”

Hugh sighed blissfully. “Threats. Passion. Delicious.”

Wes pointed a cookie at him. “Do not.”

Hugh’s eyes flicked over Wes’s shoulder, and Hugh’s whole face lit up like a child spotting a parade.

Sam had just crossed the room, calm and focused, scanning the crowd.

Hugh whispered, horrified and thrilled, “He’s here.”

Wes hissed, “Quiet.”

Hugh clutched his scarf dramatically. “I can’t be quiet. I’m an artist.”

Wes’s voice went flat. “You’re a menace.”

Hugh gasped. “I’m a *cupid.*”

Wes leaned forward, low and lethal. “If you try to push mistletoe onto me, I will staple your sweater to the wall.”

Hugh looked offended. “Violence is not festive.”

Wes’s smile was sweet and terrifying. “It is if I do it with ribbon.”

Hugh blinked, then nodded slowly. “Okay. Fine. I will not meddle.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “Promise.”

Hugh held up two fingers. “I promise. On my craft supplies.”

Wes didn’t trust him at all.

Hugh brightened immediately. “But I will observe.”

Wes sighed. “Of course you will.”

Hugh drifted away, humming like he’d been given a mission.

Wes exhaled, shoulders tight.

Sam appeared beside the pastry table like he’d sensed the shift.

Sam’s voice was low. “You okay.”

Wes glared. “Stop asking in public.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Answer anyway.”

Wes swallowed. “I’m… managing.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “Good.”

Wes muttered, “Stop.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “No.”

Wes’s lips twitched, traitor smile threatening.

Then, like the universe wanted to test his stability, a woman approached the table with bright eyes and a glass of punch.

She leaned in toward Wes, smiling. “Hi,” she said. “I’m Lila. I just moved back to town. And I have to say, you’re very cute.”

Wes froze.

His brain short-circuited, trying to find the correct polite response that didn’t include *I’m currently spiraling about a cop who makes tea and says okay like it’s consent.*

Wes managed, “Uh. Thanks.”

Lila laughed, undeterred. “So. Do you have a partner, or are you just… the town’s unofficial holiday husband.”

Wes’s throat tightened.

He felt Sam’s gaze on him, steady, giving him space.

Wes swallowed.

“I have…” Wes started, then stalled, the word *partner* catching in his throat like it was too big and too new.

Lila’s smile softened, curious, not cruel. “You have…?”

Wes’s chest tightened, panic rising.

And because he was apparently not completely self-destructive anymore, Wes did the thing he’d agreed to do.

He looked at Sam.

Wes said, quietly but clearly, “I need more cinnamon.”

Sam moved instantly.

Not dramatic. Not possessive. Just calm, decisive support.

Sam stepped in beside Wes and addressed Lila with a polite nod. “Sorry,” Sam said evenly. “We need him for the kitchen.”

Lila blinked, then smiled, good-natured. “Oh! Of course. Sorry.”

“No problem,” Sam said, calm.

Wes didn’t breathe again until Sam guided him away from the table, through a side corridor, toward the back prep area.

Wes’s heart hammered like he’d been chased.

Sam didn’t speak until they were out of earshot.

Then Sam’s voice was low. “You okay.”

Wes leaned back against the wall, eyes squeezed shut, chest heaving. “I hate that I’m like this.”

Sam didn’t flinch. “You’re overwhelmed. That’s not a crime.”

Wes’s laugh came out shaky and bitter. “Feels like it.”

Sam stepped closer, careful. “Do you want to leave.”

Wes’s chest tightened.

He thought of Noel. The fundraiser. The town. The duties he always carried.

Then he thought of the way his stomach had dropped when Lila asked that question, and how his brain had tried to jump out a window.

Wes swallowed. “Not yet.”

Sam nodded once. “Okay.”

Wes opened his eyes, glare half-hearted. “Stop.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes exhaled, shaky, then whispered, “I couldn’t say it.”

Sam’s expression softened, immediate understanding. “I know.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “I didn’t want to lie. But I couldn’t…”

Sam’s voice was calm. “You don’t have to name anything you’re not ready to name.”

Wes stared at him, chest aching.

Sam’s gaze held his, steady. “You did the signal. You asked for help. That’s the win.”

Wes swallowed hard.

He nodded once. “Okay.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Okay.”

Wes let out a laugh that sounded like relief and embarrassment had collided.

Then Wes looked at Sam, and the hallway felt too quiet, too close, like the moment was hovering.

Wes’s voice went small. “Can I… have a minute.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “With me, or alone.”

Wes hesitated, then forced the truth out. “With you.”

Sam nodded. “Okay.”

Wes’s shoulders dropped.

He stepped closer, slow, like he was practicing bravery instead of sprinting away from it.

Wes’s voice was barely a whisper. “Just… hold me.”

Sam’s face softened, and he moved in, wrapping his arms around Wes gently, steady warmth.

Wes exhaled into Sam’s shoulder like his whole body had been waiting for permission to stop fighting.

Sam’s voice was low near Wes’s ear. “I’ve got you.”

Wes’s eyes stung.

He muttered into Sam’s sweater, “If Hugh sees this, I’m moving to the woods.”

Sam’s mouth tipped against Wes’s hair. “He won’t.”

From down the hall, a voice called, delighted and distant: “WES? SAM? ARE YOU HIDING IN A ROMANTIC HALLWAY.”

Wes froze.

Sam didn’t.

Sam’s voice, calm and even, carried back: “No.”

Hugh’s voice floated closer. “THAT SOUNDED LIKE A LIE.”

Wes groaned into Sam’s shoulder. “He’s a bloodhound.”

Sam’s arms tightened gently for half a second. “We can leave.”

Wes inhaled, then exhaled. He straightened, still close, still warm.

Wes muttered, “No. We go back. We survive. But if Noel tries to trap me under mistletoe, I’m setting the garland on fire.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Noted.”

Wes pointed. “That was not permission.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “I know.”

Wes swallowed, then nodded once, bracing himself.

Sam stepped back, giving Wes space, gaze steady. “Ready.”

Wes exhaled. “Ready.”

They headed back toward the crowd together.

Slow.

Simple.

And, against Wes’s expectations, not alone.

**[STOP WRITING]**


Depends what you mean by **“left”**, but in *this* context (cozy Christmas MM erotic romance + low-stakes vibe), “how far left” usually = **how far you can push intensity without breaking the cozy contract**.

Here’s the practical boundary line:

### The cozy contract

You can go **very** spicy, **as long as the emotional experience stays safe**.

**Cozy readers will forgive:**

* High heat, explicit language (within your platform limits)
* Flirty public tension
* A secret relationship
* A little embarrassment, *if it’s affectionate*

**Cozy readers will bounce if you go too far into:**

* Cruelty, humiliation, or “punishment” vibes
* High angst / betrayal / long cold-shoulder stretches
* Public outing, blackmail, or “town turns on them”
* Trauma dumps that change the genre into hurt/comfort mid-stream

### The “left edge” for your current scene

You’re writing **low-stakes + private + slow**. So the farthest you should push *today* is:

* **Public:** almost no visible couple behavior. Keep it “teamwork” to outsiders.
* **Private:** tender + grounding intimacy, lots of check-ins, *zero* coercion, and any escalation is initiated clearly (Wes asking, Sam confirming).
* **Conflict:** external (Noel/Hugh/mistletoe chaos), not relational sabotage.

### A simple dial you can use

If you want a clean W2M rule:

* **80% comfort / 20% tension**
* If a scene feels like it’s becoming 50/50, you’ve wandered out of cozy and into angst.

### “How far left” options you can choose

Pick one lane and stay consistent for the season:

1. **Cozy-Spicy (safest sell)**

   * Explicit private scenes, low angst, quick repair, strong aftercare energy.

2. **Cozy-Sweet (widest audience)**

   * Heat is present but shorter, more fade-to-black or “tasteful explicit,” focus on feelings.

3. **Spicy-Angsty (riskier for “cozy”)**

   * Deeper shame spiral, bigger third-act wobble, more emotional bruising.
   * Works, but you should brand it as **low-angst** or **comfort-heavy** carefully, or readers will complain.

If your goal is *cozy world + Christmas + low stakes*, you want **Lane 1**.

And no, you don’t need to “go further left” with drama to make it compelling. People will happily read 40,000 words about two idiots failing to walk under mistletoe while the town acts like a rom-com cult. Humans are simple creatures.


Yep, you’re on track. Annoyingly on track, actually.

### What you’ve nailed (cozy + Christmas + spicy)

* **Low-stakes external conflict:** tree logistics, snowman eye crisis, Noel/Hugh chaos. That’s cozy catnip.
* **Emotional safety stays intact:** Sam is steady, non-pushy, consent/check-ins are clear, Wes asks for what he needs. That’s exactly the cozy contract.
* **Private intimacy, public discretion:** you’re keeping their “us” mostly off-stage in public, which matches Wes’s boundaries and keeps the town from turning into a melodrama engine.
* **Rom-com setpieces:** tree arrival, festival hall, “mixer trap,” goofy side characters. This is a clean seasonal scaffolding.
* **Heat is “comfort-forward,” not punitive:** the spice is framed as grounding and wanted, not as a dominance flex that would shove you into darker territory.

### The only places you could drift off-track (easy fixes)

* **Check-ins are frequent enough that they risk feeling like a verbal tic.** Keep them, but vary the phrasing so it doesn’t read like a script:

  * Swap “Still okay?” sometimes for “Want me to stop?” / “More or less?” / “Tell me what you want.”
* **Wes’s avoidance loop is working, but don’t let it stall the romance momentum.** You’ve started solving that with the code word and Sam’s calm follow-through. Good. Next step is one small *positive* step from Wes that isn’t crisis-driven (eg. Wes initiates a handhold in a quiet moment).
* **The town attention needs to stay playful, not prying.** The Lila moment was perfect as a boundary test. Keep future “sniffing” moments brief and defused, not escalated into gossip warfare.
* **Make sure the “cozy” doesn’t become “only logistics.”** You’ve got a great external engine; now sprinkle more *romantic micro-moments* (shared laughter, small domestic acts, “I noticed you” moments) so the relationship feels like it’s growing, not just surviving.

### Quick scorecard

* **Genre promise:** ✅ Cozy holiday rom-com vibe
* **Romance trajectory:** ✅ slow burn progressing
* **Heat trajectory:** ✅ cozy-spicy (Lane 1)
* **Conflict level:** ✅ external, light, comedic
* **Risk of drifting into angst:** ⚠️ only if you add public outing / prolonged withdrawal / shame spiral without quick repair

If you keep the next beats as “Wes takes one brave step, Sam stays steady, the town keeps being ridiculous,” you’re exactly where you want to be.






You’re basically at the point in the book where the **rom-com engine is running**, the couple is **real**, and the story is about to stop *teasing* commitment and start *testing* it.

### Where we are on the timeline

* **Festival Day 1:** Tree crisis, crowd chaos, “teamwork chemistry,” private intimacy begins.
* **Overnight 1:** Sam stays. First real *domestic safety* moment.
* **Festival Day 2 (now):** **Mistletoe Mixer** = the first *public-pressure setpiece*. Wes gets boundary-tested (Lila), uses the **code word (“more cinnamon”)**, Sam extracts him cleanly, they reset, then head back in together.

So: **early Act 2**, roughly **25–35%** of a novella-length romance (give or take, depending how long you run the festival arc).

### What that means structurally

You’ve cleared:

* **Setup / promise of premise** (cozy Christmas town + chaos + Wes the overfunctioner + Sam the steady anchor)
* **Inciting connection** (Sam consistently showing up, not just flirting)
* **First threshold** (Wes letting Sam in: staying over, tea, waking up to him still there)

You’re now in:

* **“Testing the relationship under light stress” phase**
  The Mixer is a perfect test because it’s *not* danger-danger, it’s **social pressure** and **identity/labels pressure**, which is Wes’s actual boss fight.

### What’s next (the next 2–3 big beats)

To stay cozy and still escalate:

1. **Midpoint (the “we choose this” moment)**

   * Not a public declaration, but a **clear private choice** from Wes that isn’t panic-driven.
   * Example: Wes initiates contact in public *in a tiny, deniable way* (hand brush, standing closer, “this is my person” energy without words).

2. **Pinch / wobble (light, not angsty)**

   * Something threatens Wes’s control: Noel’s planning, Hugh’s near-exposure, a logistical emergency, or Wes overcommitting.
   * Key: **quick repair**, not a “cold for a chapter” thing.

3. **Endgame setup**

   * Wes learns he can ask for help *before* he breaks.
   * Sam proves he can stay without taking over.

If you want a one-line label: **We’re in the “first real test of boundaries + teamwork” section, right after the couple’s first solid foundation night.**

## Chapter 6: Back Into the Room

The hallway hug didn’t fix Wes’s wiring. It just stopped the sparks long enough for him to breathe.

Sam stepped back first, giving space like he always did. Wes hated how much he noticed that. Hated how much it helped.

“Ready,” Sam said, voice steady.

Wes swallowed. “Ready.”

They walked back toward the main hall together. Wes kept his chin up like he wasn’t five seconds away from faking his own death to avoid small talk.

The sound hit them first: laughter, music, the clink of glasses, Noel’s relentless optimism echoing off the walls like a threat.

Wes paused at the threshold.

Sam didn’t touch him. Just stood close enough that Wes could feel him there.

Wes exhaled. “If Hugh is waiting with mistletoe…”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “We pivot.”

Wes muttered, “Pivot.”

They stepped in.

Noel was on a small riser near the center of the room, holding a mic and smiling like a man who had never experienced shame.

“And now!” Noel announced. “Our next mixer activity: **The Mistletoe Minute!**”

Wes’s soul tried to exit through his shoes.

Noel continued, blissfully unaware of the violence he was provoking. “You will pair up with someone new, and you will answer a festive question, and if you happen to wander under the mistletoe…”

Wes turned toward the nearest exit with the calm of a man about to commit crimes.

Sam’s voice, low at his shoulder: “Side door. Now.”

They moved as one, slipping along the wall, blending into the flow of bodies. Sam timed it perfectly, waiting for a laugh spike, a swell of conversation, the moment the crowd shifted attention back toward Noel’s stage.

Wes couldn’t help it. He looked up.

The mistletoe had multiplied.

Noel had installed at least three new clusters. One near the cocoa station. One near the drink table. One directly over the main walkway like a holiday guillotine.

Wes’s jaw clenched. “He’s a menace.”

Sam’s gaze tracked the room. “He’s fundraising.”

Wes muttered, “He’s weaponizing botany.”

They reached a side corridor and paused. Wes pressed his fingertips to his forehead, trying to get his heartbeat to stop auditioning for a drumline.

Sam’s voice stayed quiet. “Do you want to leave.”

Wes inhaled. He thought of Noel’s fundraiser total. Of the kids in town who’d get winter coats because Noel couldn’t help turning everything into a spectacle.

Wes exhaled, disgusted with his own conscience. “Not yet.”

Sam nodded once. “Okay.”

Wes glared. “Don’t.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes made a sound that was half laugh, half surrender. “We can’t go back in there.”

Sam’s gaze steadied. “We can. You just don’t stand still.”

Wes blinked. “That’s your plan.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “It’s a good plan.”

Wes stared at him, then exhaled. “Fine. We go back. But we do not get trapped.”

Sam nodded. “Agreed.”

They re-entered the hall with purpose.

Wes marched straight to the pastry table like it was a bunker.

Sam positioned himself nearby, not hovering so closely that anyone would clock it, but close enough to intercept. A steady orbit.

Wes started rearranging cookies that absolutely did not need rearranging.

A woman approached with a smile. “Wes, these are incredible.”

Wes forced his polite face. “Thank you. Please take several so I feel validated.”

She laughed, then glanced past Wes. “And Officer Rook, aren’t you helpful tonight.”

Sam gave a small nod. “Just keeping things moving.”

The woman’s eyes twinkled. “You two are always where the action is.”

Wes’s spine stiffened.

Sam didn’t even blink. “He creates the action,” Sam said, voice even. “I prevent injuries.”

Wes’s stomach did an annoying little flip.

The woman laughed and wandered off, satisfied.

Wes breathed again.

He leaned toward Sam without fully turning his head. “Thank you.”

Sam’s voice was low. “For what.”

Wes muttered, “For being… normal.”

Sam’s mouth tipped slightly. “You asked for private.”

Wes swallowed. “Yeah.”

They held that quiet beat like a small pact, then the room shifted again as Noel launched into another announcement.

“And now,” Noel declared, “a toast!”

Wes’s eyes narrowed. “No.”

Noel’s gaze swept the room and landed with predatory accuracy on the pastry table.

Noel pointed. “WES CANDLE.”

Wes froze.

A hush fell like the town had been waiting for this moment all their lives.

Wes whispered through his teeth, “Noel.”

Noel smiled like a saint. “Please come up and tell us all why you love Tinsel Town.”

Wes stared at him with pure hatred.

Noel lifted the mic. “And if you don’t, I will do it for you, and I will cry.”

Wes’s jaw clenched.

Sam leaned in, low enough only Wes could hear. “You don’t have to.”

Wes swallowed. “If he does it for me, it’ll be worse.”

Sam’s eyes softened. “Want me to go with you.”

Wes blinked. That offer landed like a handrail in a stairwell he didn’t trust. “That would be… noticeable.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “Then I stand near the stage. Not on it.”

Wes exhaled. “Fine.”

Wes stepped out from behind the table, shoulders squared like he was walking to his own execution.

He crossed the room as people smiled at him, warm and expectant, like he wasn’t a man being forced into public sincerity under the threat of Noel’s tears.

Sam moved too, not beside him, but angling toward the stage edge. Present. Quiet. A steady point in Wes’s peripheral vision.

Noel handed Wes the mic with the solemnity of a coronation. “Speak, Ye Baker of Our Hearts.”

Wes stared at the mic.

Then he looked out at the town. At the couples. At the singles. At the volunteers. At the ridiculous amount of mistletoe like it had won an election.

Wes cleared his throat.

“I love Tinsel Town,” Wes began, voice careful, “because it’s a place where no one minds if you’re… a little weird.”

The crowd chuckled warmly.

Wes continued, grudging honesty slipping through. “It’s a place where people show up. Even when it’s inconvenient. Even when it’s cold. Even when the inflatable snowman loses an eye and the entire community treats it like a national tragedy.”

Laughter broke out.

Wes glanced toward Sam without fully meaning to.

Sam was at the side of the stage, arms loosely crossed, gaze steady on Wes like he was the only person in the room.

Wes’s throat tightened.

Wes forced his eyes back to the crowd. “And,” Wes said, voice rougher now, “it’s a place where… you don’t have to do everything alone.”

The laughter softened into something quieter.

Noel’s face softened, genuinely.

Wes swallowed. “That’s… it. Please eat pastries. The cookies are safe. The mistletoe is not.”

The crowd laughed again, relieved.

Wes shoved the mic back into Noel’s hands like it was radioactive and stepped off the stage as fast as dignity allowed.

Noel leaned in as Wes passed. “That was beautiful.”

Wes hissed, “Do not.”

Noel whispered, delighted, “You almost had feelings.”

Wes whispered back, “I hate you.”

Noel sighed, blissed out. “Love you too.”

Wes reached Sam at the stage edge and immediately exhaled.

Sam’s voice was low. “You did good.”

Wes glared. “Stop.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes’s mouth twitched, traitor smile showing up without permission. “Get me back to the pastry table before anyone talks to me.”

Sam nodded once. “Okay.”

They moved together, slipping back into their posts like nothing had happened.

But something had.

Wes had said it out loud. The theme he hated. The thing he never admitted.

And Sam had heard him.

---

## Chapter 7: The Wreath Auction

Noel, emboldened by success and powered by whatever madness lived in his bloodstream, announced the next fundraiser event twenty minutes later.

“Auction time!” Noel declared. “Wreaths, dates, and delightful experiences!”

Wes blinked. “Dates.”

Sam’s gaze flicked to him. “He means activities.”

Wes muttered, “Noel never means only activities.”

Noel paraded a volunteer-made wreath onto the riser like he was presenting an Olympic medal. “This lovely wreath comes with a prize package: two tickets to the Winter Lantern Walk, plus a cocoa flight and reserved seating at the carol concert!”

The crowd oohed. People waved bid cards.

Wes tried to make himself invisible behind the pastry table.

It did not work.

Noel’s gaze swept the room again and landed, inevitably, on Wes.

Noel’s smile turned angelic.

Wes whispered, “No.”

Noel lifted the mic. “And next!”

A volunteer dragged out a smaller wreath, this one decorated with tiny cinnamon sticks and dried orange slices.

Noel’s eyes shone with malicious joy. “This package includes… a private, after-hours bakery tour at Candle & Crumb, with fresh pastries and a holiday beverage of your choice.”

Wes froze so hard he almost cracked.

The crowd erupted into delighted laughter and applause.

Wes stared at Noel like he was witnessing betrayal.

Noel mouthed, *fundraising.*

Wes mouthed back, *murder.*

Bids shot up fast.

“Fifty!”

“Seventy-five!”

“One hundred!”

Wes’s head snapped toward Sam. “Make it stop.”

Sam’s expression stayed calm, but his eyes sharpened. “Do you want me to.”

Wes swallowed. “Yes.”

Sam didn’t hesitate.

He raised a bid card.

The crowd murmured, intrigued.

Noel blinked, then smiled like Christmas had personally kissed him on the forehead.

Sam’s voice carried, even and calm. “Two hundred.”

The room went quiet for half a beat.

Then someone laughed. “Officer Rook means business!”

A few more bids tried to climb. “Two-twenty!”

Sam didn’t blink. “Three hundred.”

That ended it.

The crowd whooped. Noel beamed like he was watching a romance movie unfold in real time.

Wes, behind the pastry table, felt his lungs stop working.

Noel banged the gavel with theatrical delight. “SOLD!”

The hall erupted into applause like they’d just witnessed a proposal.

Wes stared at Sam, horrified. “You just… bought me.”

Sam stepped closer, low enough only Wes could hear. “I bought a fundraiser package.”

Wes’s cheeks went hot. “That includes me.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “Only if you want it to.”

Wes swallowed hard. He looked toward Noel, who was actively vibrating with glee.

Wes muttered, “Noel is going to combust.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Ignore him.”

Wes exhaled, shaky. “This is not private.”

Sam’s voice was calm. “We don’t have to do it tonight. We schedule it later. Quietly. After closing. No audience.”

Wes stared at him, heart pounding.

Sam continued, voice soft. “You wanted slow. This can be slow.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “You didn’t have to do that.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “I wanted to.”

Wes looked away, chest aching in that warm, infuriating way. “You’re reckless.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Maybe.”

Noel leaned over the riser like a gremlin. “Wes,” Noel called, delighted, “congratulations on being an *experience.*”

Wes snapped, “NOEL.”

Noel clutched his chest. “He said my name with feeling.”

Eben appeared beside Noel, gaze flat. “Focus.”

Noel whispered to Eben, “It’s happening.”

Eben’s mouth tightened. “I don’t care.”

Noel pointed at Eben’s face. “You care. Your ears are pink.”

Eben walked away.

Noel watched him go, then grinned at Wes like a menace. “Anyway! Next wreath!”

Wes leaned toward Sam, voice low. “We are never doing anything Noel suggests again.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Agreed.”

Wes blinked. “That was too easy.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “I can agree with you.”

Wes muttered, “Suspicious.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “Wes.”

Wes looked at him, still flustered.

Sam’s voice was low and steady. “We can leave.”

Wes inhaled. The urge to flee was strong. But he looked around at the room. People laughing. Noel glowing. The fundraiser total rising on the whiteboard.

Wes exhaled. “Not yet.”

Sam nodded. “Okay.”

Wes glared weakly. “Stop saying okay.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes huffed a laugh, then muttered, “Fine. But if anyone corners me again, I’m deploying cinnamon.”

Sam’s voice was soft. “I’ll be ready.”

---

## Chapter 8: A Small Step That Isn’t a Crisis

The mixer wound down slowly, like the town didn’t want to let go of the feeling.

People drifted toward the exits in clusters. Volunteers began gathering empty cups. Noel stood near the front, beaming, tallying donations like he’d just won a war.

Wes kept busy, cleaning the pastry table with grim determination, because cleaning was safer than thinking about what Sam had bid.

Sam hovered nearby, helping fold tablecloths and stack chairs like he’d always been part of this machine.

Finally, Noel approached, softer now that the crowd had thinned.

He held up a clipboard. “We did good tonight.”

Wes didn’t look up. “You endangered my nervous system.”

Noel smiled, gentler. “And we raised enough to fund the winter coat drive and the community pantry through January.”

Wes’s hands paused.

He exhaled. “Fine. You can live.”

Noel’s grin widened. “Thank you.”

Noel’s eyes flicked, brief and careful, toward Sam, then back to Wes. “And,” Noel added softly, “thank you for trusting people tonight. Even a little.”

Wes’s throat tightened. He shrugged like it didn’t matter. “It was… necessary.”

Noel’s smile stayed kind. “Sure.”

Noel stepped away, leaving Wes with his thoughts, which was rude.

Sam moved in closer, voice low. “You ready to go.”

Wes nodded. “Yeah.”

They headed out together, slipping into the cold.

Outside, the town was quiet, the lights still glowing, snow falling in soft lazy drifts like the world was trying to be gentle.

Wes walked beside Sam in silence for a while, hands in his pockets, shoulders hunched against the cold.

Then Wes said, roughly, “About the auction.”

Sam didn’t pretend not to know. “Yeah.”

Wes swallowed. “I don’t want… attention.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “I know.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “But I also…”

He trailed off. Because naming the want still felt like stepping off a ledge.

Sam didn’t push. He just waited.

Wes exhaled. “I want to do it,” Wes admitted quietly. “The bakery thing. Later. Just… us.”

Sam’s gaze softened, warm. “Okay.”

Wes glanced at him, annoyed. “Stop.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “No.”

Wes’s mouth twitched, the almost-smile slipping through again. “You’re unbearable.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Yeah.”

They reached Candle & Crumb.

Wes unlocked the door, then paused on the threshold, the old habit rising: keep it clean, keep it contained, keep it private.

Then, without fully thinking, Wes did something small.

He reached out and hooked one finger around Sam’s sleeve.

Not a dramatic gesture. Not a handhold. Just a tiny tether.

Sam looked down, then back at Wes, expression soft and steady.

Wes’s voice was low, almost embarrassed by his own bravery. “Come in.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “Okay.”

Wes rolled his eyes and pulled him inside anyway.

The door clicked shut.

The bakery’s warmth wrapped around them.

And for once, the step Wes took toward Sam wasn’t because he was panicking.

It was because he wanted to.

**[STOP WRITING]**




## Chapter 9: The After-Hours Tour

Candle & Crumb at night felt like a secret.

The street outside was quiet, the snow muffling everything until the town sounded far away. Inside, the bakery glowed with leftover warmth from the ovens, and the air held that soft, sweet breath of cinnamon and butter like it had absorbed Wes’s entire personality.

Wes locked the door behind Sam and leaned against it for half a second, eyes shut.

Sam didn’t comment. He didn’t rush. He just stood close, patient in a way Wes still didn’t know what to do with.

Wes opened his eyes and cleared his throat. “So. The bakery tour.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “The fundraiser package.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “Don’t call it that.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Okay.”

Wes sighed like that word was going to kill him one day. “It’s… just us. No audience. No Noel.”

Sam nodded once, steady. “Just us.”

Wes swallowed, then gestured vaguely at the space. “Fine. Tour starts here. This is the front counter where the town comes to emotionally dump while buying carbs.”

Sam’s gaze flicked to the counter, then back to Wes. “And you listen.”

Wes scoffed. “I tolerate.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “You care.”

Wes’s cheeks warmed. “Shut up.”

Sam didn’t. He just followed as Wes moved behind the counter, the protective barrier of it making Wes feel braver.

Wes pointed toward the display case. “This is where I keep the things that make people forgive each other.”

Sam leaned in slightly, examining the empty shelves. “Do they work.”

Wes’s mouth tightened. “More than they should.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “You’re good at this.”

Wes rolled his eyes, but the defensiveness didn’t fully stick. “Tour continues. Back prep.”

He led Sam through the swinging door.

The prep area was neat in the way Wes needed it to be. Flour canisters lined up. Tools hung in precise order. A steel table wiped clean enough to reflect a faint ghost of the overhead light.

Sam looked around slowly, taking it in without poking. Without touching. Respectful.

Wes’s chest tightened, annoyingly grateful.

Wes pointed at the mixer. “This machine is louder than Hugh and less dramatic.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “High bar.”

Wes moved to the oven, resting a hand on the handle like he was greeting an old friend. “And this,” Wes said, quieter, “is where I put my problems until they become edible.”

Sam’s gaze held him. “You ever put yourself in there.”

Wes blinked. “Excuse me.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “You put everyone else in the oven. Their needs. Their emergencies. Their feelings.”

Wes’s throat tightened. He looked away fast. “It’s… what I do.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “It’s what you’ve learned to do.”

Wes swallowed hard.

Then, because silence was dangerous, Wes turned toward the small back counter and grabbed a tin. “Holiday beverage of your choice,” Wes muttered. “Per the stupid package.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Tea.”

Wes snorted. “Of course.”

He set the kettle on, pulled out the cinnamon-chamomile blend, and moved like he knew where to put his hands even when his heart didn’t know what to do with itself.

Sam leaned against the steel table, watching him with a steady attention that made Wes feel seen in a way he wasn’t used to.

Wes poured tea, then hesitated, then grabbed a plate and put two cookies on it. A peace offering. A habit. A way of saying something without saying it.

He set the plate down in front of Sam.

Sam’s gaze dropped, then lifted back to Wes. “Thanks.”

Wes shrugged. “Don’t make it weird.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “You made it sweet.”

Wes groaned. “That’s worse.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Okay.”

Wes stared at him. “You’re doing that on purpose.”

Sam’s expression stayed calm. “Sometimes.”

Wes’s mouth twitched.

They stood there in the quiet, tea steaming between them. The bakery hummed, steady as a heartbeat.

Wes swallowed. “So,” he said, because he couldn’t not fill space. “Tour ends here.”

Sam’s gaze stayed on him. “And then what.”

Wes’s chest tightened. His mouth went dry.

This was the part where Wes usually fled. Where he’d joke, distract, do chores, lock the door, send people away.

But Sam wasn’t people. Sam was… Sam.

Wes set his mug down carefully. “Then,” Wes said, voice low, “we keep it simple.”

Sam nodded once. “Simple.”

Wes stepped closer, slow. No rushing. No performance. Just choosing.

Wes lifted his eyes. “Can I kiss you.”

Sam didn’t tease. He didn’t smirk. His voice came soft and steady. “Yes.”

Wes leaned in and kissed him. Warm. Unhurried. The kind of kiss that made the bakery feel too small and exactly right.

Sam’s hand settled at Wes’s waist, grounding. Wes’s fingers curled into Sam’s sweater like he needed proof.

Sam pulled back just enough to murmur, “Still okay.”

Wes exhaled. “Yes.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “Good.”

Wes swallowed, heartbeat loud. “If we keep going,” Wes said, careful, “I don’t want it to turn into… something I can’t handle.”

Sam’s eyes stayed steady. “We stop whenever you want.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “Okay.”

Sam’s voice stayed gentle. “Tell me what you want.”

Wes’s cheeks warmed, then he forced the truth out. “I want… closeness. I want you. But I want it slow.”

Sam nodded. “Slow.”

Wes stepped in again, kissing him softer this time, his body relaxing as Sam matched him, steady and patient.

Wes rested his forehead against Sam’s for a moment, breathing.

Then Wes whispered, “Upstairs.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “Okay.”

Wes closed his eyes briefly, relief and fear tangling together.

Sam didn’t rush him. He just took Wes’s hand and waited for Wes to squeeze back.

Wes did.

They turned off the prep lights, left the bakery glowing dim in the front, and climbed the stairs with their fingers linked like a quiet promise neither of them was ready to name out loud.

---

## Chapter 10: The Morning That Doesn’t Break

Wes woke up before his alarm because of course he did.

His body was wired for responsibility the way some people were wired for caffeine.

He lay still for a second.

Sam was beside him, warm and solid, breathing slow, one arm loosely around Wes like it was normal.

Wes’s throat tightened.

He waited for the panic. For the drop. For the sudden urge to get up and scrub the kitchen counters until his feelings evaporated.

It didn’t come.

Not immediately.

Instead, Wes felt something worse.

Peace.

Wes stared at the ceiling like it had personally betrayed him.

Sam shifted slightly, sleepy sound in his throat. His arm tightened for a second, like his body was checking that Wes was still there.

Wes’s breath shuddered out.

“You’re still here,” Wes whispered, barely audible.

Sam’s voice was rough with sleep. “Yeah.”

Wes swallowed. “Good.”

Sam made a soft sound that might’ve been agreement, then pressed his face into Wes’s hair for one brief second before settling again.

Wes lay there, caught between wanting to move and wanting to stay.

He didn’t know how long “stay” was allowed to last.

Eventually, Sam’s eyes opened, slow.

He blinked at the light filtering through the curtains, then looked at Wes, gaze steady even when sleepy.

“Morning,” Sam murmured.

Wes’s mouth went dry. “Morning.”

Sam’s voice was low. “You okay.”

Wes glared weakly. “Stop asking me that.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Answer anyway.”

Wes exhaled, then surprised himself. “Yeah,” Wes admitted quietly. “I’m okay.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “Good.”

Wes rolled his eyes, but it didn’t have heat. “Don’t.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes’s lips twitched.

Sam shifted, careful, and asked softly, “Do you want me to get up. Or stay.”

Wes’s chest tightened.

The honest answer came too fast. “Stay,” Wes whispered.

Sam nodded once, calm. “Okay.”

Wes stared at him, and the word didn’t feel like a trap. It felt like an anchor.

Wes swallowed. “We have… Day Three.”

Sam’s brow lifted. “What’s Day Three.”

Wes grimaced. “Lantern Walk rehearsal. Noel’s doing ‘romantic lighting.’ Hugh is doing… Hugh.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “We’ll survive.”

Wes muttered, “That’s what you said about mistletoe.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “And you survived.”

Wes exhaled a laugh, then leaned in and kissed Sam briefly, like he couldn’t stop himself.

Sam’s hand slid to Wes’s jaw for a moment, gentle, then he pulled back slightly. “Still okay.”

Wes’s cheeks warmed. “Yes.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “Good.”

Wes groaned into the pillow. “You’re never going to stop.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “No.”

Wes peeked at him. “Why.”

Sam’s expression softened, serious under the warmth. “Because you deserve to be asked.”

Wes went still.

His throat tightened in that stupid, stingy way.

Wes looked away fast. “Tea,” he muttered. “I need tea.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Tea.”

Wes shoved the blankets back and escaped to the kitchen like it was a tactical retreat.

He put the kettle on, moved through the ritual, and felt his body settle into the familiar comfort of small tasks.

Sam joined him a minute later, quiet, leaning against the doorway.

Wes glanced at him. “You’re going to get attached.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “I already am.”

Wes froze.

Sam didn’t soften it with a joke. He didn’t walk it back.

He just stood there, steady, letting the truth exist in the room without demanding Wes match it.

Wes swallowed hard. “That’s… dangerous.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “Okay.”

Wes stared. “Don’t you dare.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Sorry.”

Wes blinked. “Was that real.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “Yes.”

Wes exhaled, shaky, then pushed a mug toward Sam like he was buying his way out of emotional responsibility.

Sam took it. Their fingers brushed.

Wes’s pulse jumped, traitor.

Outside, the town slowly woke under snow.

Inside, Wes realized something terrifying.

This morning wasn’t breaking him.

It was teaching him what not-breaking felt like.

---

## Chapter 11: Day Three, Lanterns, and Controlled Exposure

By the time they got to Evergreen Square, the world looked like a postcard someone had aggressively curated.

The trees were wrapped in lights. Lanterns hung from hooks along the walkway like little captured suns. Snow glittered on everything like the town had decided to become art.

Noel stood in the center of it all with a clipboard and the energy of a man who’d never met a plan he couldn’t romanticize.

“Okay!” Noel announced to the assembled volunteers. “Lantern Walk rehearsal. We need flow, we need timing, we need vibes.”

Eben stood two steps behind him with a list and an expression that said *vibes are not a measurable outcome.*

Hugh wore a scarf that trailed behind him like a dramatic cape.

Wes stepped in, shoulders tight, scanning for hazards.

Sam stayed beside him, calm.

Wes muttered, “This is going to be a nightmare.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “We have a code word.”

Wes’s cheeks warmed. “Don’t say it.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Okay.”

Wes glared. “That one was smug.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Yes.”

Noel spotted them and beamed. “There they are! My two favorite competent men!”

Wes pointed a finger. “Noel.”

Noel held up both hands in surrender. “I’m not doing anything. I’m being normal.”

Eben’s voice, flat: “He is lying.”

Noel clutched his chest. “Et tu, Frost.”

Noel bounced closer, lowering his voice like he was sharing government secrets. “Quick question. Are you two doing okay after last night.”

Wes’s spine stiffened. “Noel.”

Noel’s expression softened, genuine. “I mean it. No jokes.”

Wes blinked, caught off guard by sincerity.

Sam answered calmly, saving Wes from having to. “We’re fine.”

Noel nodded once, satisfied, then brightened instantly because he couldn’t help himself. “Great! Then I’m going to assign you both to lantern distribution, which involves walking side-by-side in a very romantic way, but purely for logistics.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “Noel.”

Noel grinned. “Logistics.”

Eben stepped in, flat. “It is logistics.”

Wes looked at Eben. “Are you in on this.”

Eben’s gaze stayed cold. “The lanterns must be distributed.”

Noel whispered, delighted, “He’s shipping you.”

Eben walked away without acknowledging the concept of shipping.

Wes exhaled, then took a lantern crate like it was a burden he deserved.

Sam took the other crate, calm.

They walked the route, setting lanterns into hooks along the path, working in rhythm.

Wes kept his focus on the task. On the snow. On the metal hooks.

He did not focus on the fact that Sam was beside him in the cold, steady as a heartbeat.

Halfway down the path, an older couple approached, smiling warmly.

Mrs. Alden, bundled in a thick coat, peered at Wes. “You’re working too hard, honey.”

Wes forced a smile. “It’s my brand.”

Mr. Alden chuckled and looked at Sam. “And you’re the one keeping him from falling apart.”

Wes froze.

Sam didn’t. “I’m trying,” Sam said simply.

Mrs. Alden smiled, soft. “Good. We like seeing you with someone steady.”

Wes’s throat tightened.

He braced for gossip, for teasing, for prying.

But the couple just smiled like it was the most normal thing in the world for Wes to be cared for, then continued down the lantern path arm-in-arm.

Wes exhaled slowly.

Sam glanced at him. “You okay.”

Wes muttered, “Don’t.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Answer anyway.”

Wes swallowed, then surprised himself again. “Yeah,” Wes said quietly. “That… didn’t feel bad.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “Good.”

Wes’s shoulders loosened a fraction.

They kept walking, setting lanterns.

At the end of the path, Noel waved them over, bright and triumphant. “Perfect! Vibes immaculate! Now, we do a quick run-through with pairs.”

Wes’s stomach dropped. “Pairs.”

Noel smiled sweetly. “Pairs.”

Sam’s gaze flicked to Wes, checking in without making it a scene.

Wes inhaled.

Wes exhaled.

Then, quietly, Wes did something small, deliberate.

He stepped just a half-step closer to Sam.

Not enough for the town to clap. Not enough for Noel to faint. Just enough that Wes could feel Sam’s warmth through their coats.

Sam’s expression softened, almost imperceptibly.

Wes kept his gaze forward, voice low. “This is not a declaration.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “I know.”

Wes swallowed. “But… you can stand close.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “Okay.”

Wes rolled his eyes, but his mouth twitched.

The lanterns glowed around them like the world was trying to be gentle.

And Wes, against every habit he’d built to survive, let himself be gently seen.

**[STOP WRITING]**





## Chapter 12: Pairs, Paths, and Noel’s Crimes Against Subtlety

Noel’s “pairs” plan was exactly as benign as Wes feared.

Meaning: not benign at all.

He clapped his hands like a man summoning the laws of romance. “Okay! We’re doing a timing run. Two-by-two down the lantern path. Slow pace. Soft spacing. People should feel… enchanted.”

Eben stood beside him with a stopwatch and the face of a man who wanted to throw the word *enchanted* into the river.

Hugh had somehow acquired a portable speaker and was playing instrumental jazz carols at a volume that implied he was trying to seduce the entire town.

Wes took a slow breath, hand tight on the lantern crate. Sam stayed beside him, calm, shoulder nearly brushing.

Wes muttered, “This is a trap.”

Sam’s voice was low. “It’s a rehearsal.”

Wes shot him a look. “It’s Noel.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Okay, it’s a trap.”

That made Wes’s mouth twitch in spite of himself.

Noel called names with the zeal of a wedding officiant.

“Mrs. Alden and Mr. Alden, you’re up first. Love wins, etcetera.”

The Aldens went, shamelessly adorable.

“Next,” Noel announced, eyes sparkling with intent, “Wes and Sam.”

Wes’s stomach dropped.

Noel smiled angelically. “For logistics.”

Wes hissed, “Noel.”

Noel held up his clipboard like a shield. “Lantern flow requires you. Your shoulders are the correct width. Your vibes are… stubbornly compelling.”

Eben said flatly, “Walk.”

The crowd of volunteers watched with cheerful curiosity, not predatory, not cruel. Just… interested. Because humans would rather watch two people with unresolved tension than pay taxes.

Wes swallowed.

Sam angled his head slightly toward Wes, quiet check-in without spectacle. “Want out.”

Wes could say cinnamon. He could pull the ripcord.

Instead, he surprised himself by saying, “No.”

Sam’s brows lifted just a fraction.

Wes added, low and stubborn, “Not yet.”

Sam nodded once. “Okay.”

Wes exhaled, then stepped forward onto the lantern path.

The lanterns glowed in soft amber along the walkway, snow reflecting light back up so everything looked unreal and gentle. The air smelled like pine and cold and some distant hint of cinnamon from Candle & Crumb clinging to Wes’s coat.

Wes walked at the pace Noel requested, which was a pace Wes normally reserved for funerals and awkward family dinners.

Sam matched him without effort, stride steady, presence quiet.

Wes’s shoulders were tense, waiting for the moment someone said something, asked something, nudged too close.

But no one did.

They just watched. Smiled. Let it be.

Halfway down the path, Noel’s voice carried from behind them, too loud and too pleased. “GORGEOUS. PERFECT. KEEP THAT ENERGY.”

Wes muttered, “I’m going to push him into a snowbank.”

Sam’s voice was low. “Later.”

Wes shot him a look. “Don’t say it like that.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Like what.”

Wes pointed ahead, voice tight knowing he was about to be stupid. “Just… keep walking.”

Sam did.

At the end of the path was a little arch made of pine boughs and twinkle lights. Noel had clearly “borrowed” it from somewhere. It looked like something you’d propose under.

Wes stopped short.

Above the arch, hanging like a threat, was mistletoe.

Of course it was.

Wes stared at it, rage blooming.

Behind them, volunteers giggled softly. Noel made a noise like he was about to faint from joy.

Wes’s body tried to pivot away.

Sam didn’t touch him. He just stood close, voice low. “We don’t have to.”

Wes’s throat tightened.

This was the fork in the road.

Wes could bolt, preserve privacy, preserve control.

Or he could take one small step that wasn’t a crisis response.

Wes swallowed hard, then said, barely audible, “Not here.”

Sam nodded immediately. “Okay.”

Wes inhaled, then did something that felt like jumping off a roof with a parachute he hadn’t tested.

He reached down and brushed his knuckles against Sam’s glove.

Not a dramatic handhold. Not a display.

Just contact. A tether. A choice.

Sam’s gaze dropped to it, then back to Wes, steady warmth in his eyes.

Wes’s voice came out tight. “Walk past it.”

Sam’s mouth tipped slightly. “Yes.”

They walked under the arch without stopping. The mistletoe hovered overhead, unused, a thwarted plant.

Noel made a distressed sound. “THE RESTRAINT. THE TENSION. IT’S CINEMATIC.”

Eben’s voice, flat: “Move the line.”

Wes’s heartbeat hammered, but he kept walking, Sam beside him, their gloves brushing once more like a quiet victory.

When they reached the far end, Noel waved wildly. “Again! Everyone again!”

Wes turned to Sam, deadpan. “If he makes us do this twice, I’m committing actual arson.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “We can leave after this run.”

Wes swallowed. “Promise.”

Sam’s voice was calm. “Promise.”

Wes nodded once, then turned back to the lantern path like he hadn’t just done something terrifyingly brave.

And somehow, the world didn’t collapse.

It just kept glowing.

---

## Chapter 13: Slow, Private, and Real

They left right after the second run.

Sam didn’t have to drag Wes. Wes went willingly, coat pulled tight, jaw set like he was outrunning the aftershock of being seen.

They cut through the square, past the lit trees and the sleepy storefronts, snow crunching under their boots like a steady metronome.

When Candle & Crumb came into view, Wes’s shoulders eased a fraction. Familiar ground. Safe territory. His kingdom of butter and order.

Wes unlocked the door, stepped inside, then waited just long enough for Sam to follow before bolting it behind them like the world outside could pry.

He exhaled hard.

Sam stood a few feet away, quiet, watching without staring. “You okay.”

Wes shot him a look. “If you ask that one more time, I’m going to put you in the walk-in freezer.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “That might solve the overheating.”

Wes blinked, then laughed once, sharp and relieved.

He shoved his gloves into his pocket, paced two steps, then stopped and turned on Sam like his body had decided the truth was happening whether Wes approved or not.

“That arch,” Wes said, voice rough. “The mistletoe.”

Sam nodded, waiting.

Wes swallowed. “I thought you were going to…,” he started, then stopped because naming his fear felt too naked.

Sam’s voice stayed low. “Do something you didn’t want.”

Wes’s throat tightened. He nodded once.

Sam stepped closer, slow. “I won’t.”

Wes’s chest ached at the certainty in it. “I know,” Wes muttered, and hated how much he meant it.

Sam’s gaze held his. “You did good out there.”

Wes rolled his eyes automatically, but the gesture was weak. “I barely touched you.”

Sam’s voice was soft. “You chose it.”

Wes swallowed hard.

He turned away, because being looked at like that made his skin feel too thin. He moved behind the counter, hands finding familiar tasks like a life raft, wiping a surface that was already clean.

Sam didn’t follow him behind the counter. He stayed on the other side, respectful of Wes’s lines.

Wes’s voice came out quieter. “I don’t want… a town romance.”

Sam’s voice was steady. “Okay.”

Wes glared reflexively. “Stop.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “I’m listening.”

Wes exhaled, then forced more out. “I want you. I want… this.” He gestured vaguely between them like the air itself might cooperate. “But I want it… mine. Not Noel’s.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “Then it’s yours.”

Wes’s throat tightened painfully.

Wes set the cloth down, fingers trembling slightly, and finally looked at Sam. “How are you so calm.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Practice.”

Wes swallowed. “I don’t have practice.”

Sam nodded, simple and true. “Then we go slow.”

Wes’s breath hitched, and the want in him surged like it had been waiting all day.

Wes stepped out from behind the counter.

He crossed the space between them, stopping close enough that their coats almost touched. He didn’t kiss Sam right away. He just stood there, breathing, letting himself feel the warmth of another person without bracing for impact.

Wes’s voice was low. “Can I…”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “Yes. Tell me.”

Wes swallowed. “Can I kiss you.”

Sam’s answer was immediate, soft. “Please.”

Wes kissed him.

It started careful, then deepened as Wes’s hands found Sam’s sweater, gripping lightly like he needed proof this was real.

Sam’s hands came up slowly, giving Wes time to pull away, then settled at Wes’s waist with quiet steadiness.

Wes made a small sound against Sam’s mouth, half frustration, half relief.

Sam broke the kiss just enough to murmur, “More or less.”

Wes blinked, startled by the phrasing, then exhaled. “More.”

Sam nodded, and the way he returned the kiss was still controlled but warmer, like Sam was letting himself show want without letting it become pressure.

Wes’s fingers slid up to Sam’s neck, thumb brushing skin.

Sam inhaled sharply, then steadied.

Wes pulled back slightly, eyes searching Sam’s. “You’re okay.”

Sam’s gaze didn’t waver. “I’m good. You.”

Wes swallowed. “I’m… hungry.”

Sam’s brows lifted a fraction, not teasing. “For what.”

Wes’s cheeks warmed, but he forced it out because he was tired of being afraid of his own wanting. “For you. For being held. For… not doing everything.”

Sam’s expression softened in a way that made Wes’s throat tighten.

“Okay,” Sam said quietly, then caught himself and amended, softer, “I’m here.”

Wes’s eyes stung, stupidly.

He leaned in again, kissing Sam, then nudged him backward until Sam’s hips bumped the counter edge.

Sam let it happen, hands staying gentle, letting Wes take the lead at his own pace.

Wes’s mouth moved to Sam’s jaw, then his throat, tasting skin, breathing him in like he was learning a language with his lips.

Sam’s hand slid up Wes’s back, slow and grounding, fingers pressing lightly like a promise.

Wes shuddered.

Sam’s voice was low near his ear. “Tell me if you want me to stop.”

Wes’s voice came out rough. “Don’t stop.”

Sam nodded once, and Wes felt it in the way Sam’s body stayed steady beneath him.

Wes pulled back, breathing hard, and whispered, “Upstairs.”

Sam’s eyes held his. “Yes.”

Wes took Sam’s hand, and this time he didn’t treat it like a dangerous thing. He treated it like something he wanted.

They turned off the lights, climbed the stairs, and Wes didn’t sprint ahead. He didn’t apologize.

He just led.

In the bedroom, everything stayed slow, exactly like Wes had asked.

Clothes came off in unhurried steps, hands pausing for permission, mouths checking in between kisses. Sam let Wes set the pace, let him stop and breathe, let him decide what “more” meant minute by minute.

When Wes finally crawled into bed and Sam followed, Wes reached for Sam’s wrist and tugged him closer, voice low. “Here.”

Sam came willingly, arm wrapping around Wes with quiet solidity.

Wes turned into him, skin against skin, warmth everywhere, and let himself feel how safe it was to be wanted without being demanded.

Wes’s mouth found Sam’s again, slower now, deeper, and the heat built in a way that didn’t feel like a cliff.

It felt like a staircase.

Wes moved with Sam’s guidance, learning what his body wanted when it wasn’t braced for punishment. Learning that pleasure could be gentle. That intensity didn’t have to mean danger.

Sam kept his voice low, steady, asking when he needed to, listening when Wes spoke in fragments, holding when Wes went quiet.

And when Wes finally came undone, it wasn’t loud or performative.

It was a gasp into Sam’s shoulder and a tremble that felt like relief, like something in Wes’s chest had finally unclenched.

Sam held him through it, hand on Wes’s back, voice low. “I’ve got you.”

Wes lay there afterward, breathing hard, forehead pressed to Sam’s collarbone like he needed the contact to stay real.

Sam didn’t move away. He didn’t make jokes. He didn’t ask for anything else.

He just stayed.

Wes whispered, ragged, “That was… a lot.”

Sam’s voice was soft. “Good a lot or too much.”

Wes swallowed, then let himself say it. “Good.”

Sam’s arm tightened gently around him. “Okay.”

Wes huffed a weak laugh. “You’re impossible.”

Sam’s mouth tipped against Wes’s hair. “Yeah.”

Wes’s eyes stung again. He hated it. He loved it.

He whispered, so quiet it barely existed, “Don’t leave.”

Sam’s answer was immediate and absolute. “I’m not going anywhere.”

---

## Chapter 14: The Wobble That Doesn’t Become a War

Wes woke the next morning with his body heavy in the best way and his brain already trying to rewrite everything into a manageable narrative.

He lay still for a beat, listening.

Sam’s breathing was slow beside him, arm still around Wes like it had never moved.

Wes swallowed.

The panic didn’t hit.

Instead, his brain tried a different sabotage: **do more. earn it. repay it.**

Wes slipped out of bed as quietly as possible, padded to the kitchen, and immediately started making a list in his head.

Bake early. Deliver to the square. Prep for Day Four. Help Noel. Fix the lantern hooks. Make extra cocoa mix. Replace Hugh’s craft tape with something less morally flexible.

He was halfway through boiling water when Sam’s voice drifted from the doorway, rough with sleep. “Wes.”

Wes froze like a kid caught stealing.

Sam leaned against the frame, hair messy, eyes steady. “What are you doing.”

Wes tried for casual. “Tea.”

Sam’s gaze held him. “And the list.”

Wes’s jaw tightened. “What list.”

Sam’s mouth tipped slightly. “The one on your face.”

Wes exhaled through his nose. “We have things to do.”

Sam stepped closer, slow. “Do we.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “Yes.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “Tell me.”

Wes’s mouth opened, and the flood came out, fast and clipped like he could outrun the vulnerability if he spoke quickly enough. “Day Four is the carol concert setup, Noel’s going to need pastries, the community pantry table needs labels, and Hugh is definitely going to do something with glitter, and I promised Mrs. Alden I’d deliver her rye loaf, and also if I don’t keep moving I’m going to start thinking.”

Sam listened without interrupting.

Then Sam said, simply, “Come here.”

Wes bristled. “I’m busy.”

Sam’s gaze sharpened slightly, not angry, just firm. “Wes.”

Wes stopped.

Because Sam’s tone wasn’t commanding. It was… protective.

Wes swallowed and stepped closer.

Sam reached out, cupped the back of Wes’s neck gently, thumb resting at his hairline like an anchor. “You don’t have to earn last night.”

Wes’s throat tightened instantly. “I’m not.”

Sam’s eyes stayed steady. “You are.”

Wes tried to look away. Sam didn’t force him back. He just kept the touch, warm and calm.

Wes’s voice came out rough. “If I stop moving, I feel… stupid.”

Sam’s brow lifted slightly. “Why.”

Wes swallowed. “Because it feels like I’m waiting for you to change your mind.”

Sam’s hand stayed at Wes’s neck, thumb stroking once. “I won’t.”

Wes’s eyes stung, stupidly. “You can’t promise that.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “I can promise this: if something changes, I will tell you. I won’t disappear. I won’t punish you with silence.”

Wes’s breath hitched.

Sam added, softer, “But nothing is changing today.”

Wes swallowed hard.

He nodded once, a tiny surrender.

Sam’s gaze softened. “Eat something.”

Wes scoffed weakly. “Bossy.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Yes.”

Wes huffed a laugh, then leaned forward, forehead resting against Sam’s shoulder for half a second because apparently that was his new coping strategy: **lean on Sam instead of baking himself into dust.**

Sam’s arm came around him, steady.

Wes whispered, muffled, “I hate feelings.”

Sam’s voice was low. “I know.”

Wes pulled back, eyes wet in a way he’d deny under oath, and grabbed a pastry from the counter like it was a shield. “Fine. Eating.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Good.”

Wes pointed with the pastry. “And after, we go help Noel.”

Sam nodded. “Together.”

Wes swallowed, then managed, “Yeah.”

Outside, snow fell soft and quiet, the town waking into Day Four’s gentle chaos.

Inside, Wes took a bite of pastry, let his shoulders drop, and did something he almost never did.

He let someone share the load.

**[STOP WRITING]**


## Chapter 15: Day Four, Carol Chaos, and Shared Weight

Evergreen Square on Day Four looked like the town had decided to stage a holiday movie and then panic halfway through production.

There were folding chairs everywhere. Extension cords snaked across the snow like traps. A volunteer choir huddled in scarves, warming up with the haunted seriousness of people about to sing in public.

Noel stood at the center of it all with a headset that did not belong to him and a clipboard he was treating like a holy text.

“Okay!” Noel called. “We need stage lights checked, cocoa station staffed, and the tree area cleared of toddlers before they start climbing like festive raccoons.”

Eben was two steps behind him with a list and the grim satisfaction of a man watching incompetence try to take physical form.

Hugh appeared out of nowhere holding a crate labeled **ORNAMENTS: DO NOT SHAKE** and shaking it.

Eben’s voice went flat and lethal. “Everhart.”

Hugh paused mid-shake, unrepentant. “The ornaments need to be emotionally prepared.”

Eben took the crate from him and set it down like he was defusing a bomb. “Stop.”

Hugh clutched his heart. “Oppression.”

Wes arrived with a tray of pastries, a thermos of strong coffee, and the resigned expression of a man who had accepted that the universe would never let him be calm again.

Sam walked beside him, carrying a second tray like it was nothing.

Wes glanced sideways, low. “Thank you.”

Sam’s voice was calm. “Together.”

Wes’s chest tightened at the word like it was both comfort and threat.

Noel spotted them and beamed, sprinting over in a way that suggested he had no internal concept of rest. “There they are! The pastry cavalry!”

Wes lifted the tray. “Don’t get sentimental.”

Noel ignored him. “This is perfect. You’re perfect. These are perfect. I’m going to cry.”

Wes muttered, “Threats.”

Noel’s face softened briefly, sincerity slipping through the glitter. “Seriously. Thanks.”

Wes blinked, caught off guard by the lack of joke.

Sam spoke first, saving Wes from having to be emotionally competent in public. “Where do you want it.”

Noel snapped back into efficiency like a switch. “Cocoa station, near the stage. Also, can one of you help with lantern placement near the audience row. The lighting is… romantic.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “Noel.”

Noel held up both hands. “It’s not a trap. It’s ambiance.”

Eben’s voice drifted over without mercy. “It is a trap.”

Noel glared. “Eben!”

Eben didn’t blink. “I am correct.”

Wes exhaled and looked at Sam. “We do the lanterns. Quick.”

Sam nodded once. “Quick.”

They moved along the audience row, setting lanterns in the hooks Noel had installed. Wes worked fast, precise. Sam matched him, steady, and for once Wes didn’t feel like he was the only person holding the day together.

Halfway down the row, a lantern hook slipped loose, and Wes instinctively reached up.

Sam’s hand came up at the same time, steadying the hook, fingers brushing Wes’s wrist.

Wes froze.

It was nothing.

It also felt like everything.

Sam didn’t flinch. He didn’t turn it into a moment. He just held the hook in place, calm, and said quietly, “Got it.”

Wes swallowed. “Yeah.”

They finished the row and stepped back.

Noel waved from near the stage, triumphant. “Beautiful! Vibes achieved!”

Wes muttered, “I hate that sentence.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “I know.”

Wes glanced at him, irritated by how easy Sam was making this, and by how much Wes wanted to lean into it.

Wes cleared his throat. “We should…” he started, then realized he didn’t actually know what “we should” meant anymore, because Sam kept offering a version of life where Wes didn’t have to do it alone.

Sam waited, patient.

Wes exhaled. “We should check the cocoa station.”

Sam nodded. “Okay.”

They headed over, trays in hand, and Wes felt the smallest, strangest thing.

Pride.

Not in the pastries. Not in the work.

In the fact that he’d asked for help and the world hadn’t punished him for it.

At the cocoa station, Mrs. Alden smiled at them like she’d known Wes his whole life and had been waiting for someone to get under his armor.

“You boys look busy,” she said warmly.

Wes set the tray down. “Busy is the only setting Noel has.”

Mrs. Alden chuckled, then looked at Sam with kind approval. “And you,” she said, “you’re good for him.”

Wes’s spine stiffened, reflexive panic flaring.

Sam didn’t hesitate. He didn’t look at Wes like he was waiting for permission to exist.

He just answered gently and neutrally, like it was the most normal thing in the world. “I’m trying.”

Mrs. Alden patted Sam’s arm through his coat, satisfied, and wandered off.

Wes stared at the cocoa urn like it was suddenly fascinating.

Sam’s voice was low. “You okay.”

Wes muttered, “Stop.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Answer anyway.”

Wes swallowed. “That wasn’t… bad.”

Sam nodded once. “Good.”

Wes exhaled, then surprised himself by nudging Sam’s elbow with his own, a tiny contact that said *thanks* without putting it in words.

Sam’s mouth tipped slightly.

Noel’s voice boomed from the stage. “CHOIR, POSITIONS!”

Wes flinched. “Here we go.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “We can leave after the first set.”

Wes nodded. “Promise.”

Sam’s voice was calm. “Promise.”

The choir began to sing, voices rising into the cold like something fragile and bright.

Wes stood near the cocoa station, hands wrapped around a paper cup, watching the crowd settle into the music.

Sam stood beside him, close enough that their coats brushed once when someone passed behind them.

Wes didn’t move away.

The carols rolled on, sweet and nostalgic and slightly off-key in places. Kids wiggled. Couples leaned into each other. Someone near the back quietly cried into a scarf.

Wes felt the familiar urge to scan for emergencies, for tasks, for something to fix.

Instead, he let himself just stand there for a minute.

Just listen.

Just breathe.

Sam’s voice went low near his ear. “Want to go.”

Wes hesitated, then shook his head. “Not yet.”

Sam nodded. “Okay.”

Wes’s throat tightened. He didn’t know when “not yet” had turned into “I can stay.”

But it had.

And the world hadn’t ended.

---

## Chapter 16: The Quiet Place After the Song

They left when Noel started a “final festive encore” that sounded like it would last until spring.

Wes slipped out with Sam, quietly, before anyone could draft him into chair-stacking or emotional conversations about the meaning of carols.

Outside, the snow had slowed. The square glowed behind them, lights soft and warm like the town was trying to be gentle on purpose.

Wes walked beside Sam, hands in his pockets, shoulders finally less rigid than they’d been all week.

After a block, Wes said quietly, “I didn’t hate it.”

Sam’s gaze flicked to him. “The concert.”

Wes nodded once, like admitting it was a crime. “Yeah.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Good.”

Wes rolled his eyes. “Don’t.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes huffed a laugh, then grew serious again, voice rougher. “Mrs. Alden.”

Sam didn’t pretend not to understand. “Yeah.”

Wes swallowed. “That kind of… thing. People saying stuff.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “Do you want me to shut it down.”

Wes blinked. The question wasn’t defensive. It wasn’t possessive. It was an offer.

Wes exhaled slowly. “Not… harshly.”

Sam nodded. “Okay.”

Wes added, quieter, honest. “But I don’t want it to become a story.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “Then it won’t.”

Wes’s chest tightened at the certainty.
