He looked away fast, because his emotions were getting entitled again.

They reached Candle & Crumb.

Wes unlocked the door, stepped inside, and immediately exhaled like his body recognized safety before his brain did.

Sam followed.

Wes locked up, then leaned back against the door and stared at the ceiling.

“Okay,” Wes said, voice tired. “We survived Day Four.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “We did.”

Wes eyed him. “Don’t ‘we’ me.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “You started it.”

Wes blinked, then realized Sam was right, which was rude.

Wes muttered, “Fine.”

He took off his coat, hung it, then hesitated like he didn’t know what to do with the rest of himself.

Sam, quiet, stepped closer. “Want tea.”

Wes stared. “Do you always fix people with tea.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Sometimes.”

Wes sighed. “Yes. Tea.”

Sam put the kettle on like it was a ritual now, like this was something they did after chaos: return to warmth, to simple things, to the small proof that life could be steady.

Wes watched him for a beat, then said, very quietly, “I didn’t run today.”

Sam’s gaze lifted, soft. “No.”

Wes swallowed. “I wanted to.”

Sam nodded once. “I know.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “But I didn’t.”

Sam’s voice went gentler. “That matters.”

Wes exhaled shakily, annoyed by the sting in his eyes. “Stop being right.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes’s mouth twitched, then he crossed the space between them and leaned his forehead against Sam’s shoulder for a moment, just to feel the solid reality of him.

Sam’s hand came up, resting at the back of Wes’s neck, gentle.

Wes whispered, muffled, “Tomorrow is Day Five.”

Sam’s voice was low near his ear. “What’s Day Five.”

Wes groaned. “Cookie decorating for kids.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “That sounds safe.”

Wes muttered, “It sounds like frosting warfare.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “We’ll survive.”

Wes lifted his head, looked at Sam, and the warmth in his chest felt less like fear and more like something he could keep.

Wes swallowed. “Stay.”

Sam didn’t hesitate. “Okay.”

Wes rolled his eyes weakly. “Stop.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “No.”

The kettle clicked.

Sam poured two mugs. Wes took his without speaking, fingers brushing Sam’s again.

Wes didn’t flinch.

He didn’t pull away.

He just let it happen.

And for a man who’d spent years bracing for loss, that was its own kind of bravery.

**[STOP WRITING]**



## Chapter 17: Day Five, Frosting Warfare, and Children as Tiny Gods

Wes learned two things within the first three minutes of the kid’s cookie-decorating event.

One: children have no sense of restraint.

Two: Noel had absolutely planned this as a social experiment.

The community center smelled like sugar, cocoa, and impending disaster. Folding tables were covered with trays of plain cookies, bowls of frosting in every holiday color, and enough sprinkles to qualify as a municipal budget item.

Noel stood at the front wearing an apron that said **SANTA’S FAVORITE MENACE**.

Wes stared. “That’s not funny.”

Noel beamed. “It’s accurate.”

Eben stood near the sign-in sheet with his clipboard, looking like a man watching chaos gather mass.

Hugh wore a wreath on his head like a crown and was already teaching a child how to make an “edible glitter halo.”

Wes inhaled, steadying. “This is going to be… loud.”

Sam stood close beside him, calm in a plain sweater, hands in his pockets like he was ready to intercept tiny disasters.

Sam’s voice was low. “We can leave early.”

Wes muttered, “No. I’m not letting Noel win.”

Sam’s mouth tipped slightly. “Okay.”

Wes shot him a look. “Don’t.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “You walked under mistletoe yesterday without stopping. I’m not worried about Noel.”

Wes hissed, “That was not the point.”

Noel clapped his hands and called out, “Welcome, tiny artists! Welcome, sugar warriors! Please remember: frosting is for cookies, not hair.”

A child immediately put frosting in his hair.

Noel’s smile didn’t even wobble. “Great. Love the commitment.”

Wes moved to the cookie table like it was his battlefield. He started laying out extra napkins, spacing bowls, setting “safe zones” like he could impose order by force of will.

Sam hovered nearby, quietly taking on the tasks Wes didn’t even realize he was assigning.

A little girl with pigtails stared up at Sam with grave intensity. “Are you a real cop.”

Sam blinked once. “Yes.”

The girl nodded solemnly. “My dad says cops are boring.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Sometimes.”

The girl narrowed her eyes. “Can you arrest my brother.”

Her brother, across the table, was eating sprinkles straight from a bowl like a raccoon.

Sam’s gaze flicked over, calm. “Only if he confesses.”

The boy froze mid-sprinkle, eyes wide. “I confess nothing.”

Wes snorted before he could stop himself.

The girl looked pleased. “You’re funny.”

Sam’s gaze slid to Wes briefly, warm, like he liked the sound of Wes laughing.

Wes immediately pretended he hadn’t.

---

Ten minutes in, frosting became an actual weapon.

A kid fired a stream of green frosting across the table. Another kid retaliated with red sprinkles like shrapnel. A third kid made a cookie that was just frosting with a cookie underneath as structural support.

Noel floated through the carnage like a cheerful disaster consultant. “Yes! Creativity! Expression!”

Eben’s voice was flat. “This is a biohazard.”

Hugh, reverent, whispered, “It’s modern art.”

Wes wiped frosting off his sleeve and muttered, “I hate everyone.”

Sam leaned in, low. “You love them.”

Wes glared. “Don’t psychoanalyze me.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Noted.”

Wes squinted. “That was sarcasm.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Also noted.”

Wes’s mouth twitched, traitor.

A mom approached the table, smiling politely in the way people do when they’re about to ask Wes for something. “Wes, can I borrow you for a second.”

Wes’s spine tightened automatically. “Depends.”

She laughed. “My son has a nut allergy, and I just want to make sure…”

Wes softened immediately, focus snapping into place. “Of course,” he said, already moving. “We’ll keep him to the left side. No cross-contact.”

Sam stayed close without hovering, helping direct kids like a gentle traffic controller.

The mom glanced at Sam, then back to Wes, smiling. “It’s nice you’ve got help.”

Wes felt the familiar panic twitch.

Sam answered before Wes had to, calm and neutral. “We’re all here to keep the kids from eating glitter.”

The mom laughed and nodded. “Bless you.”

She walked away.

Wes exhaled like he’d been holding his breath.

He glanced at Sam, low. “Thanks.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “Anytime.”

That *anytime* hit Wes harder than it should have.

Because Wes was starting to believe it.

---

Half an hour later, Noel announced, “Cookie showcase! Bring your masterpieces!”

Kids surged toward the front with plates held high like offerings.

One cookie had a face. Another had what looked like a tiny frosting pentagram.

Wes stared at the pentagram cookie. “That’s not… festive.”

The kid holding it shrugged. “It’s a snowflake.”

Hugh leaned in, delighted. “Symbolic.”

Eben’s voice was flat. “No.”

Noel held up a cookie covered in sprinkles. “This one is called ‘Santa Explodes!’ Incredible!”

Wes muttered, “Noel is going to get us all cursed.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Probably.”

Wes glanced at Sam’s hands. “You haven’t made a cookie.”

Sam blinked. “I wasn’t assigned.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “That’s not how this works. You’re here. You have to participate.”

Sam’s mouth tipped slightly. “Do I.”

Wes gestured toward the frosting. “Make one. A normal one. For the town’s morale.”

Sam looked at the frosting like it was evidence. “Okay.”

Wes watched, baffled, as Sam calmly assembled a cookie with a neat white frosting base, a simple green tree, and a tiny red dot.

Wes blinked. “That’s… competent.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “It’s a tree.”

Wes stared at it, then muttered, “Fine. Give it here.”

Wes grabbed another cookie and, without fully thinking, drew a messy little spiral of frosting on it. Then he added a tiny red dot next to Sam’s dot, like an ornament pairing.

He slid it closer to Sam without making eye contact.

Sam looked down at it.

Then Sam looked up at Wes, warmth in his eyes that made Wes’s throat tighten.

Sam’s voice was low. “Is that… mine.”

Wes muttered, “It’s a cookie.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Okay.”

Wes hissed, “Stop saying okay like you’re holding something precious.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “You are.”

Wes’s stomach flipped. He looked away fast, cheeks hot.

From across the room, Noel’s voice rose in delighted accusation: “DID YOU TWO JUST MAKE MATCHING COOKIES.”

Wes snapped, “NO.”

Noel clutched his chest. “I’m going to die.”

Eben’s voice was flat. “Don’t.”

Hugh gasped. “This is art.”

Wes pointed at Sam, low. “We’re leaving.”

Sam nodded calmly. “Okay.”

Wes glared. “Stop.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes grabbed his coat like it was a tactical retreat and marched them out before Noel could produce mistletoe and a photographer.

---

## Chapter 18: The Walk Home and the Thing Wes Says First

The snow had stopped.

The world looked clean and quiet, as if Tinsel Town had decided to apologize for the children.

Wes walked fast at first, hands jammed in his pockets, shoulders tight.

Sam matched his pace without crowding.

After a block, Wes slowed, breath steaming in front of him.

Wes muttered, “I hate Noel.”

Sam’s voice was calm. “You like him.”

Wes glared. “Don’t.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Okay.”

Wes exhaled a laugh, then rubbed his face like he could wipe away the lingering embarrassment.

“That cookie thing,” Wes said, voice rough.

Sam waited, steady.

Wes swallowed. “I didn’t mean to… do that.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “You didn’t have to mean to. You did it.”

Wes’s chest tightened. “That’s worse.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Why.”

Wes stopped walking.

Sam stopped too, turning slightly, giving Wes space while still staying close enough to feel like an option.

Wes stared at the snow at his feet. “Because it was… easy,” Wes admitted quietly. “And I don’t trust easy.”

Sam didn’t argue. He just nodded once, like he understood that sentence from the inside.

Sam’s voice was low. “Easy doesn’t mean unsafe.”

Wes swallowed hard. “Sometimes it does.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “Then we make it safe.”

Wes’s throat tightened, and he hated that he wanted to believe him.

Wes exhaled slowly, then said the thing his body had been circling all day.

“I liked it,” Wes admitted. “The matching cookies. I liked… being on a team with you.”

Sam’s expression softened in a way that made Wes’s chest ache.

Sam’s voice was quiet. “Me too.”

Wes nodded once, like that was all he could handle.

Then, because he was apparently allergic to vulnerability unless it came with forward motion, Wes started walking again.

Sam fell in beside him.

Wes’s voice came out low, almost reluctant. “When people say stuff… like Mrs. Alden… I don’t want to freak out anymore.”

Sam’s gaze slid to him, warm. “Okay.”

Wes rolled his eyes. “Stop.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Tell me what you want instead.”

Wes blinked, caught.

He thought for a second, then said, carefully, “I want… to be able to say *something* that’s true. Without making it a headline.”

Sam nodded once. “Then we pick something true.”

Wes swallowed. “Like what.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “Like ‘He’s with me.’ Or ‘We’re together.’ Or ‘He’s my…’”

Sam stopped himself, watching Wes’s face. “Or we keep it simpler.”

Wes’s breath hitched. He stared ahead, heart thudding.

Then Wes said, quietly, “I can do ‘I’m seeing him.’”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “That’s true.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “And it’s not… a label that feels like a trap.”

Sam nodded. “Okay.”

Wes huffed a laugh. “Stop.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

They reached Candle & Crumb.

Wes unlocked the door, then paused on the threshold like he always did.

This time, he didn’t bolt it immediately.

He stood there, looking at the bakery, then at Sam.

Wes’s voice was low. “I’m seeing you.”

Sam didn’t smile like he’d won. He didn’t tease. His eyes just softened.

Sam’s voice came quiet and steady. “I’m seeing you too.”

Wes swallowed hard, then stepped aside, letting Sam in.

The door clicked shut behind them.

And for the first time, the words didn’t feel like a threat.

They felt like a small, real thing Wes could hold.

**[STOP WRITING]**



## Chapter 19: The Bakery, the Words, and the Part Where Wes Doesn’t Backpedal

Candle & Crumb felt warmer after the words.

Not because the thermostat changed. Because Wes did.

He’d said it. Out loud. In the doorway. Like a man who wasn’t terrified of his own truth.

Wes stood there for a beat, hand still on the door, heart thudding like it wanted to argue.

Sam didn’t rush him. Sam didn’t fill the silence with jokes or reassurance.

Sam just stood inside the bakery, calm, eyes soft.

Wes swallowed. “So that’s… a thing I said.”

Sam’s mouth tipped slightly. “Yeah.”

Wes narrowed his eyes, defensive reflex twitching. “Don’t act like you’re pleased.”

Sam’s gaze held his, steady. “I am pleased.”

Wes froze.

It wasn’t smug. It wasn’t teasing. It was simple. Honest.

Wes’s chest tightened in that infuriating, warm way that made him want to throw a chair and also curl up and sleep for a year.

Wes muttered, “That’s… dangerous.”

Sam’s voice was calm. “Okay.”

Wes shot him a look. “Stop.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes exhaled a laugh, sharp and embarrassed, then shrugged off his coat and hung it up like he needed his hands to remember how to exist.

Sam followed suit, folding his coat neatly because of course he did.

Wes pointed at him. “You’re still doing that. The folding.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “It calms me.”

Wes blinked. “You have calming behaviors.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “Everyone does.”

Wes stared at him like that was new information. “Mine is baking until my feelings evaporate.”

Sam nodded once. “I’ve noticed.”

Wes grumbled, “Don’t.”

Sam’s eyes stayed steady. “I’m not judging.”

Wes’s throat tightened.

He walked behind the counter because he needed the barrier, then immediately started wiping down a surface that was already clean.

Sam stayed on the customer side, respectful of Wes’s lines, leaning lightly against the counter edge.

Wes worked in silence for a minute, letting the routine settle him.

Then Wes said, very quietly, “When you looked at me after I said it… I thought you might make it bigger.”

Sam’s voice was low. “Bigger how.”

Wes swallowed. “Like… a moment.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “It was a moment.”

Wes flinched.

Sam continued, gentle. “But I don’t have to make it a show.”

Wes’s shoulders loosened a fraction. “Good.”

Sam nodded once. “You get to choose the speed.”

Wes exhaled. “Slow.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Slow.”

Wes’s hands paused. He looked up, meeting Sam’s eyes.

Wes’s voice came rough. “I don’t want to hide like I’m ashamed.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “Then don’t.”

Wes swallowed. “But I also don’t want the town… in it.”

Sam nodded. “Then the town doesn’t get in.”

Wes’s throat tightened again, because Sam kept saying things like they were obvious and possible.

Wes set the cloth down and stepped out from behind the counter.

He crossed the space between them slowly, stopping close enough to feel Sam’s warmth.

Wes didn’t kiss him right away. He just stood there, breathing, letting his body adjust to closeness that wasn’t a trap.

Wes’s voice was low. “Can you… stay tonight.”

Sam’s answer was immediate and calm. “Yes.”

Wes blinked, caught off guard by how easy it was.

Wes muttered, “Stop being reliable.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “No.”

Wes’s lips twitched, then he leaned in and kissed Sam, soft and brief, like punctuation more than escalation.

Sam returned it gently, then pulled back just enough to murmur, “Still okay.”

Wes’s cheeks warmed. “Yes.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Good.”

Wes groaned quietly. “You’re never going to stop.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “No.”

Wes pressed his forehead to Sam’s for a beat, breathing, then whispered, “Tomorrow is Day Six.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “What’s Day Six.”

Wes sighed like the universe was personally mocking him. “Gingerbread House Contest.”

Sam’s brows lifted. “That sounds… messy.”

Wes deadpanned. “It’s structural engineering with sugar and grief.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “We’ll survive.”

Wes muttered, “If Hugh doesn’t weaponize icing.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Probably.”

Wes pulled back, gaze softening despite himself. “If it gets weird tomorrow…”

Sam’s voice was calm. “We leave.”

Wes swallowed. “And if I can’t… say things.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “Then you don’t have to. I’ll follow your lead.”

Wes’s chest tightened.

He nodded once. “Okay.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Okay.”

Wes huffed a laugh, then turned away to hide how relieved he was.

Humans were ridiculous.

Wes was worse.

---

## Chapter 20: Day Six, Gingerbread Politics, and the First Time Wes Isn’t the Only One Watching

The community center on Day Six looked like a sugar bomb went off.

Long tables were covered in gingerbread panels, icing bags, bowls of candy, tiny bottles of edible glue, and instruction sheets that no one was going to read.

A banner hung over the front that said:

**GINGERBREAD HOUSE CONTEST: BUILD YOUR DREAMS (AND YOUR ROOF)**

Wes stared at the banner. “That’s ominous.”

Noel, already wearing another themed apron (this one read **I’M ONLY HERE FOR THE DRAMA**), waved cheerfully. “Isn’t it inspiring.”

Eben stood beside the sign-in table with a clipboard and the grim expression of a man who understood that dreams were flammable.

Hugh had brought a rolling cart of decorations and was humming like he’d entered a sacred temple.

Wes arrived with Sam, shoulders tense but not locked.

Sam stayed close, steady as always, scanning the room like it was both a crowd and a responsibility.

Noel spotted them and beamed. “There they are! Our brave volunteers!”

Wes pointed at Noel. “If you say anything about gingerbread being romantic, I’m leaving.”

Noel clasped his hands. “I was going to say it’s architectural.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “Noel.”

Noel grinned. “Fine. I’ll say nothing.”

Eben’s voice, flat: “He is lying.”

Noel clutched his chest. “Eben!”

Eben didn’t blink. “Focus.”

Wes and Sam got assigned to “roof support and icing triage,” which was a polite way of saying: stop children from building structural death traps.

At the first table, a kid was trying to attach a roof at a ninety-degree angle.

Wes leaned in. “Buddy. Gravity exists.”

The kid frowned. “Not in my house.”

Sam crouched slightly beside Wes, calm. “Your house is ambitious.”

The kid brightened. “Thanks.”

Wes blinked at Sam, then muttered, “You’re good with kids.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “They’re honest. It’s easier.”

Wes’s chest tightened. “Yeah.”

They moved down the tables, fixing slumping walls, reattaching chimneys, saving candy windows from collapse.

Wes stayed focused. Tasked. Contained.

Then, about twenty minutes in, the room shifted.

Not dramatically. Just… subtly.

Wes noticed people looking.

Not in a cruel way. Not in a prying way.

In the way a small town looks at a pattern forming.

Wes’s shoulders tightened.

A woman near the coffee table smiled at Wes and said, casually, “Officer Rook’s been around the festival a lot this year.”

Wes’s pulse jumped.

Before he could scramble for a reply, Sam answered calmly from beside him, voice neutral. “I’m on community detail.”

The woman nodded, satisfied. “Good. It’s nice having you.”

And then she moved on.

No interrogation. No wink-wink. No headline.

Wes exhaled slowly.

He glanced at Sam, low. “Thank you.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “For what.”

Wes muttered, “For… not making me do a speech.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “No speeches.”

Wes’s shoulders loosened a fraction.

They continued the route.

A few tables later, Hugh glided over like a festive shark.

Hugh’s scarf today was red and dramatic. His cart glittered with candy.

“I have a proposal,” Hugh announced, eyes shining.

Wes immediately tensed. “No.”

Hugh ignored him. “A judging twist. A surprise category.”

Eben’s voice drifted over from the sign-in desk, flat and threatening. “No twists.”

Hugh clasped his heart. “Eben, you hate joy.”

Eben didn’t look up. “Correct.”

Hugh turned to Wes and Sam, conspiratorial. “A category for… Most Romantic Gingerbread.”

Wes stared at him. “Hugh.”

Hugh’s eyes sparkled. “Picture it: two gingerbread men. One roof. A shared chimney. Love. Frosting.”

Wes hissed, “Absolutely not.”

Hugh looked wounded. “Why does everyone hate art.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “Because you weaponize it.”

Hugh gasped. “Even you, Officer.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “Especially me.”

Hugh blinked, then nodded slowly, like he’d been honored. “Fine. No romance category.”

Hugh leaned in toward Wes, whispering like he couldn’t help himself. “But I do love watching you two orbit.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “Go away.”

Hugh drifted off, humming.

Wes stared after him, mortified.

Sam’s voice went low. “Want to leave.”

Wes inhaled.

He thought about bolting. About retreating into the bakery where no one could look at him.

Then he thought about yesterday. About the lantern path. About the way it hadn’t killed him to be seen, just a little.

Wes exhaled. “Not yet.”

Sam nodded. “Okay.”

Wes muttered, “Stop.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes’s mouth twitched, then he turned back to the table in front of him.

A little boy was struggling to attach a roof panel.

Wes crouched, focused. “Okay. More icing here. Hold for ten seconds. Don’t panic.”

The boy frowned. “I always panic.”

Wes deadpanned. “Same.”

Sam crouched too, steadying the wall with one hand. “Ten seconds,” Sam said calmly. “You can do ten.”

The boy held, counting under his breath.

The roof stuck.

The boy’s eyes widened. “I did it.”

Wes felt something soften in his chest. “You did.”

The boy beamed, then looked between Wes and Sam and said, matter-of-fact, “You guys are a good team.”

Wes froze.

The boy didn’t smirk. Didn’t tease. Didn’t even realize he’d said something that hit Wes like a bell.

He just went back to slapping candy onto his house with reckless delight.

Wes swallowed hard, staring at the roof like it had suddenly become very interesting.

Sam’s voice was low. “He’s right.”

Wes muttered, “Don’t.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “Okay.”

Wes shot him a look. “Stop.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “No.”

Wes exhaled a shaky laugh and, without fully thinking, bumped his shoulder lightly against Sam’s.

A tiny contact. A tiny yes.

Sam didn’t make a big deal of it.

He just stayed.

And for the first time, Wes realized something new.

Sam wasn’t only watching Wes.

Wes was starting to watch Sam too.

Not for danger.

For care.

For what Sam needed.

For how this could be mutual.

Wes swallowed, then said quietly, “After this… you get tea. And you get to be off duty.”

Sam’s brows lifted. “I get tea.”

Wes glared, cheeks warm. “Don’t be smug.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “I’m not smug.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “You’re smug.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Okay.”

Wes groaned, then turned back to the icing station and kept working.

But the warmth in his chest didn’t feel like fear anymore.

It felt like something he could carry.

**[STOP WRITING]**





## Chapter 21: Day Seven, Snowman Triage, and Wes Learning to Give Back

By Day Seven, Wes had accepted that Tinsel Town was not a town.

It was an organism.

And during the holidays, it developed opinions, cravings, and emergencies the way a human body developed fevers.

Today’s emergency arrived at 8:12 a.m. in the form of Noel bursting into Candle & Crumb like a festive hurricane.

Wes didn’t look up from his dough. “If someone’s on fire, put them out. If the snowman lost another eye, I don’t care.”

Noel slapped both hands on the counter, breathless. “The snowman is missing his *nose*.”

Wes stared at him. “It’s a snowman.”

Noel’s face was solemn. “It’s the town snowman.”

Wes exhaled through his nose. “Still a snowman.”

Sam, seated at the small table with a mug of tea like he actually believed mornings could be peaceful, lifted his gaze. “What happened to the nose.”

Noel looked personally wounded by the universe. “A raccoon.”

Wes paused, hands in flour. “A raccoon stole a carrot.”

Noel nodded, eyes shining with outrage. “A bold criminal.”

Sam’s mouth tipped slightly. “That tracks.”

Wes glared at them both. “We are not doing snowman surgery.”

Noel leaned forward, pleading. “Wes. The snowman is the symbol of togetherness.”

Wes deadpanned. “The snowman is a pile of snow.”

Noel clasped his hands like he was praying. “Please.”

Wes turned toward Sam, low. “Tell him no.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Do you want me to tell him no.”

Wes blinked, annoyed by the question being reasonable. “Yes.”

Sam looked at Noel. Calm. “No.”

Noel held the gaze for three seconds, then pivoted smoothly. “Okay, but will you come help me anyway.”

Wes’s soul left his body.

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Maybe.”

Wes snapped, “SAM.”

Sam’s gaze slid to Wes, steady. “We can make it quick.”

Wes stared at him. “Why do you want to help with a snowman.”

Sam’s expression stayed calm, but there was a softness under it. “Because it matters to people.”

Wes’s throat tightened, because of course that was the answer.

Wes muttered, “Fine. But we’re doing it efficiently. Like a medical procedure.”

Noel beamed. “Bless you.”

Wes pointed a floury finger. “No. Don’t.”

Noel ignored him and practically floated out the door. “Meet at the square! Bring… carrot vibes!”

Wes watched Noel leave, then turned to Sam. “If I die in a snowman-related incident, you have to burn this town down.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Noted.”

Wes squinted. “That’s not a promise.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Okay. I promise not to burn the town down.”

Wes sighed. “Unhelpful.”

Sam stood, calm, and reached for his coat. “You still want me off duty today.”

Wes paused.

He’d said that yesterday without thinking. A tiny offer. A tiny care.

Wes swallowed. “After the snowman. Yes.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “Okay.”

Wes rolled his eyes, but his mouth twitched.

They left together.

---

Evergreen Square was bright with fresh snow and the kind of cold that made your nose sting the second you inhaled. The big town snowman stood near the tree, majestic and ridiculous, its face now disturbingly incomplete.

Noel stood beside it with Eben and Hugh like a crisis committee.

Eben held a replacement carrot in his gloved hand like evidence.

Hugh looked mournful. “He looks… unfinished.”

Wes approached, taking in the scene like a man evaluating a battlefield. “Okay. We attach the carrot. We leave.”

Noel nodded vigorously. “Yes. Simple.”

Hugh gasped. “Simple? This is art restoration.”

Wes ignored him and stepped up to the snowman.

The nose-hole was… worse than Wes expected. It wasn’t a clean missing-carrot situation. It looked like something had gnawed at the face.

Wes grimaced. “That raccoon was committed.”

Sam stepped closer, assessing. “We need to pack snow.”

Wes blinked. “Why are you good at this.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Snow is like… structural filler.”

Wes stared, then muttered, “Of course you’d know.”

Sam didn’t tease. He just crouched and started packing snow into the gap with slow, competent hands.

Wes found himself mirroring the motion, packing snow, smoothing it, shaping a proper base like he was icing a cake.

Noel watched, hands clasped, reverent. “Look at you two. Working in harmony.”

Wes snapped, “Noel.”

Noel made a zip-it gesture but couldn’t stop smiling.

Eben handed Sam the carrot like he was handing over an official document.

Sam took it, steady, then paused and looked at Wes. “You want to do it.”

Wes blinked. “Me.”

Sam nodded. “It’s your snowman.”

Wes’s chest tightened at the phrasing like it meant something more than it did.

Wes huffed. “Fine.”

He took the carrot and pressed it into place.

It stuck.

The snowman looked whole again. Silly and complete under the tree lights.

Noel exhaled dramatically like he’d been holding his breath for hours. “He lives.”

Hugh whispered, “A miracle.”

Eben said flatly, “It is a carrot.”

Noel turned, delighted. “Eben, you’re allergic to wonder.”

Eben walked away.

Noel looked back at Wes and Sam, eyes soft for once. “Thank you,” Noel said quietly. “I know this is ridiculous.”

Wes shrugged, trying not to feel anything. “It’s fine.”

Noel smiled. “It’s not fine. It’s kind.”

Wes’s throat tightened. He looked away fast.

Sam spoke, calm and neutral, saving Wes again. “We should go.”

Noel nodded. “Yes. Go. Be… normal people.”

Wes muttered, “We’re literally repairing snowmen.”

Noel beamed. “Normal!”

Wes and Sam turned away, walking back through the square.

After a few steps, Wes said quietly, “You did that thing again.”

Sam glanced at him. “What thing.”

Wes swallowed. “You gave me… the last step. The carrot.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “You like finishing things.”

Wes’s chest tightened. “That’s not…”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “It is.”

Wes exhaled through his nose, annoyed that Sam kept being correct.

They reached the bakery.

Inside, warmth wrapped around them. Safety.

Wes locked the door, then turned to Sam, hesitating like he didn’t know how to offer care without making it into a transaction.

Wes cleared his throat. “Okay. Off duty.”

Sam’s brows lifted. “Off duty.”

Wes nodded toward the little table. “Sit. I’ll make something. Food. Tea. Whatever you want.”

Sam watched him for a beat, expression soft. “You don’t have to.”

Wes glared, weak. “I want to.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “Okay.”

Wes hissed, “Stop.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “No.”

Wes turned away to hide how the warmth hit him, then started moving through the bakery like the act of feeding someone was the only love language he trusted.

He made grilled cheese on the small back griddle. Cut apples. Put out crackers. Poured tea. Added honey without asking, because he’d noticed Sam took it that way.

Sam sat at the table quietly, watching, not stopping him, not making it weird.

When Wes set the plate down, Sam’s voice was low. “You noticed.”

Wes froze. “What.”

Sam nodded toward the honey. “That.”

Wes’s cheeks warmed. “It’s… obvious.”

Sam’s eyes softened. “No. It’s care.”

Wes swallowed hard, then shoved his own plate into place and sat like he was annoyed at the chair.

They ate in quiet.

After a few bites, Sam said softly, “This is nice.”

Wes muttered, “It’s a grilled cheese.”

Sam’s gaze held him. “It’s you taking care of me.”

Wes’s throat tightened. He looked down at his food like it had suddenly become fascinating.

Wes forced out, quiet and rough, “I’m not good at that.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “You are.”

Wes exhaled, shaky. “Stop being… kind about it.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes huffed a laugh, then finally looked up at Sam.

Wes’s voice came low. “Tomorrow is Day Eight.”

Sam’s mouth tipped slightly. “What’s Day Eight.”

Wes grimaced. “Cookie exchange.”

Sam blinked. “We already did cookies.”

Wes deadpanned. “Noel thinks the town hasn’t reached peak cookie yet.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Of course.”

Wes leaned back in his chair, exhaustion settling in, but it wasn’t the sharp, lonely kind.

It was shared.

Wes watched Sam sip tea, calm and present, and realized something that made his chest ache in a softer way.

Wes wasn’t only being rescued anymore.

Wes was starting to learn how to stay.

**[STOP WRITING]**

---

## Chapter 22: Day Eight, Cookie Exchange, and the Line Wes Finally Uses

Day Eight happened with less fanfare and more flour.

The cookie exchange filled the community center with trays and tin boxes and the smell of sugar so thick it practically had its own weather system.

Noel stood at the front with a microphone. Again. Because Noel believed silence was a character flaw.

“Welcome to the Great Cookie Swap!” Noel announced. “Please remember: you are here to share, to trade, and to refrain from fistfights over shortbread.”

Wes muttered, “That’s aimed at Hugh.”

Hugh, already clutching a tin like it was a sacred relic, whispered, “These are my children.”

Eben stood near the doorway doing crowd control like the town’s last line of defense.

Wes arrived carrying three tins: peppermint pinwheels, ginger snaps, and the emergency batch of “please don’t cry” sugar cookies.

Sam arrived beside him with exactly one tin.

Wes eyed it. “You made cookies.”

Sam nodded once. “I had help.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “Who helped you.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Mrs. Alden.”

Wes froze. “She… helped you.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “She likes projects.”

Wes’s cheeks warmed. “This town is… meddling.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Yes.”

They found a spot on the table and set their tins down. People swarmed around, trading, laughing, complimenting.

Wes kept his hands busy, organizing his tins like it was a form of emotional regulation.

Then Lila appeared.

Same bright eyes. Same friendly energy. Same ability to turn Wes’s bloodstream into panic.

She smiled. “Hi again.”

Wes’s spine tightened.

Sam was beside him, calm, not staring, not claiming. Just present.

Lila glanced between them, still kind, still not predatory. “I didn’t mean to put you on the spot the other night,” she said softly. “I’m new again. I’m… awkward.”

Wes blinked, caught by the sincerity.

Wes swallowed. “It’s fine.”

Lila’s smile warmed. “So. Are you… seeing someone.”

Wes’s heartbeat kicked.

His old instincts screamed: deflect, joke, flee, cinnamon.

But Wes felt Sam beside him. Quiet. Steady. Not demanding.

Wes inhaled.

Wes exhaled.

Then Wes said, clearly and calmly, “Yeah. I’m seeing someone.”

Lila’s smile didn’t falter. “Oh,” she said gently. “Okay. Good.”

Wes’s chest loosened a fraction, shocked.

Lila nodded once, sincere. “He’s lucky.”

Wes’s throat tightened, and he didn’t know what to do with that, so he did the only thing he could.

He glanced at Sam.

Sam met his eyes, warmth there, no pressure.

Lila followed the glance, then smiled like she’d just been given a simple answer to a simple question. “Cool,” she said. “Then I’ll trade you for those peppermint ones and leave you alone.”

Wes blinked. “You want peppermint.”

Lila grinned. “I have flaws.”

Wes huffed a laugh, startled.

They traded cookies. Lila winked once, friendly, and moved on without turning it into gossip.

Wes stood there for a moment, stunned by how… normal it had been.

Sam’s voice was low. “You okay.”

Wes rolled his eyes automatically, but he didn’t feel like he was bleeding inside. “Yes.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Good.”

Wes muttered, “Stop.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes’s mouth twitched, and then, because his body was apparently determined to keep learning, Wes did something tiny.

He let his hand brush Sam’s for half a second behind the cookie tins.

A private, small contact.

Sam’s fingers curled lightly, a quiet answer.

Wes exhaled, and for once, it didn’t feel like he was bracing for impact.

It felt like he was choosing something.

**[STOP WRITING]**



## Chapter 21: Day Seven, Snowman Triage, and Wes Learning to Give Back

It was an organism.

Noel slapped both hands on the counter, breathless. “The snowman is missing his *nose*.”

Wes stared at him. “It’s a snowman.”

Noel’s face was solemn. “It’s the town snowman.”

Wes exhaled through his nose. “Still a snowman.”

Sam, seated at the small table with a mug of tea like he actually believed mornings could be peaceful, lifted his gaze. “What happened to the nose.”

Noel looked personally wounded by the universe. “A raccoon.”

Wes paused, hands in flour. “A raccoon stole a carrot.”

Noel nodded, eyes shining with outrage. “A bold criminal.”

Sam’s mouth tipped slightly. “That tracks.”

Wes glared at them both. “We are not doing snowman surgery.”

Noel leaned forward, pleading. “Wes. The snowman is the symbol of togetherness.”

Wes deadpanned. “The snowman is a pile of snow.”

Noel clasped his hands like he was praying. “Please.”

Wes turned toward Sam, low. “Tell him no.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Do you want me to tell him no.”

Wes blinked, annoyed by the question being reasonable. “Yes.”

Sam looked at Noel. Calm. “No.”

Noel held the gaze for three seconds, then pivoted smoothly. “Okay, but will you come help me anyway.”

Wes’s soul left his body.

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Maybe.”

Wes snapped, “SAM.”

Noel beamed. “Bless you.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Noted.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Okay. I promise not to burn the town down.”

Wes sighed. “Unhelpful.”

Wes paused.

Sam’s gaze softened. “Okay.”

They left together.

---

Wes cleared his throat. “Okay. Off duty.”

Wes glared, weak. “I want to.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “Okay.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “No.”
