Wes froze. “What.”

Sam nodded toward the honey. “That.”

Wes’s cheeks warmed. “It’s… obvious.”

Sam’s eyes softened. “No. It’s care.”

Wes swallowed hard, then shoved his own plate into place and sat like he was annoyed at the chair.

They ate in quiet.

After a few bites, Sam said softly, “This is nice.”

Wes muttered, “It’s a grilled cheese.”

Sam’s gaze held him. “It’s you taking care of me.”

Wes’s throat tightened. He looked down at his food like it had suddenly become fascinating.

Wes forced out, quiet and rough, “I’m not good at that.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “You are.”

Wes exhaled, shaky. “Stop being… kind about it.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes huffed a laugh, then finally looked up at Sam.

Wes’s voice came low. “Tomorrow is Day Eight.”

Sam’s mouth tipped slightly. “What’s Day Eight.”

Wes grimaced. “Cookie exchange.”

Sam blinked. “We already did cookies.”

Wes deadpanned. “Noel thinks the town hasn’t reached peak cookie yet.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Of course.”

Wes leaned back in his chair, exhaustion settling in, but it wasn’t the sharp, lonely kind.

It was shared.

Wes watched Sam sip tea, calm and present, and realized something that made his chest ache in a softer way.

Wes wasn’t only being rescued anymore.

Wes was starting to learn how to stay.

**[STOP WRITING]**

---

## Chapter 22: Day Eight, Cookie Exchange, and the Line Wes Finally Uses

Day Eight happened with less fanfare and more flour.

The cookie exchange filled the community center with trays and tin boxes and the smell of sugar so thick it practically had its own weather system.

Noel stood at the front with a microphone. Again. Because Noel believed silence was a character flaw.

“Welcome to the Great Cookie Swap!” Noel announced. “Please remember: you are here to share, to trade, and to refrain from fistfights over shortbread.”

Wes muttered, “That’s aimed at Hugh.”

Hugh, already clutching a tin like it was a sacred relic, whispered, “These are my children.”

Eben stood near the doorway doing crowd control like the town’s last line of defense.

Wes arrived carrying three tins: peppermint pinwheels, ginger snaps, and the emergency batch of “please don’t cry” sugar cookies.

Sam arrived beside him with exactly one tin.

Wes eyed it. “You made cookies.”

Sam nodded once. “I had help.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “Who helped you.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Mrs. Alden.”

Wes froze. “She… helped you.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “She likes projects.”

Wes’s cheeks warmed. “This town is… meddling.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Yes.”

They found a spot on the table and set their tins down. People swarmed around, trading, laughing, complimenting.

Wes kept his hands busy, organizing his tins like it was a form of emotional regulation.

Then Lila appeared.

Same bright eyes. Same friendly energy. Same ability to turn Wes’s bloodstream into panic.

She smiled. “Hi again.”

Wes’s spine tightened.

Sam was beside him, calm, not staring, not claiming. Just present.

Lila glanced between them, still kind, still not predatory. “I didn’t mean to put you on the spot the other night,” she said softly. “I’m new again. I’m… awkward.”

Wes blinked, caught by the sincerity.

Wes swallowed. “It’s fine.”

Lila’s smile warmed. “So. Are you… seeing someone.”

Wes’s heartbeat kicked.

His old instincts screamed: deflect, joke, flee, cinnamon.

But Wes felt Sam beside him. Quiet. Steady. Not demanding.

Wes inhaled.

Wes exhaled.

Then Wes said, clearly and calmly, “Yeah. I’m seeing someone.”

Lila’s smile didn’t falter. “Oh,” she said gently. “Okay. Good.”

Wes’s chest loosened a fraction, shocked.

Lila nodded once, sincere. “He’s lucky.”

Wes’s throat tightened, and he didn’t know what to do with that, so he did the only thing he could.

He glanced at Sam.

Sam met his eyes, warmth there, no pressure.

Lila followed the glance, then smiled like she’d just been given a simple answer to a simple question. “Cool,” she said. “Then I’ll trade you for those peppermint ones and leave you alone.”

Wes blinked. “You want peppermint.”

Lila grinned. “I have flaws.”

Wes huffed a laugh, startled.

They traded cookies. Lila winked once, friendly, and moved on without turning it into gossip.

Wes stood there for a moment, stunned by how… normal it had been.

Sam’s voice was low. “You okay.”

Wes rolled his eyes automatically, but he didn’t feel like he was bleeding inside. “Yes.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Good.”

Wes muttered, “Stop.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes’s mouth twitched, and then, because his body was apparently determined to keep learning, Wes did something tiny.

He let his hand brush Sam’s for half a second behind the cookie tins.

A private, small contact.

Sam’s fingers curled lightly, a quiet answer.

Wes exhaled, and for once, it didn’t feel like he was bracing for impact.

It felt like he was choosing something.

**[STOP WRITING]**







## Chapter 23: Day Nine, Cocoa Crawl, and the First Hint of an End Date

Day Nine’s event sounded harmless on the flyer.

**COCOA CRAWL: VISIT LOCAL STOPS, COLLECT STAMPS, WIN PRIZES.**

In reality, it was an organized migration of sugar-hyped citizens moving through town like a cheerful herd.

Wes stood behind the portable Cocoa Crawl table outside Candle & Crumb, stamping little cards with a grim focus that suggested he was auditing taxes.

Sam stood beside him, steady, gloved hands resting on the table edge, scanning the crowd with the calm of a man who’d accepted that small towns were a contact sport.

Noel had stationed himself three feet away wearing a scarf the size of a blanket, “helping” by flirting with everyone.

Eben was across the street directing traffic like the holiday season was a threat he’d personally vowed to contain.

Hugh had created a “cocoa playlist” and was playing it from a speaker that was definitely not approved for outdoor use.

Wes stamped another card and muttered, “This is a cult.”

Sam’s mouth tipped slightly. “It’s a game.”

Wes glanced at him. “It’s a cult with marshmallows.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Okay.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “Stop.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “No.”

A couple approached, laughing, and held out their stamp cards.

“Two peppermint mochas,” the woman said, delighted. “And two stamps, please.”

Wes stamped with mechanical precision. “Congratulations. You’re closer to winning a basket of artisanal jams and emotional closure.”

The man laughed. “Wes Candle never misses.”

Sam’s gaze flicked to Wes, warm, like he enjoyed the bluntness.

The couple wandered off, sipping cocoa and leaning into each other.

Wes watched them for half a second too long.

Sam’s voice went low, gentle. “You okay.”

Wes’s shoulders tightened. “Stop asking.”

Sam’s eyes stayed steady. “Answer anyway.”

Wes exhaled, annoyed at himself. “I’m fine.”

Sam didn’t challenge it. He just nodded once and stayed.

That was his whole thing: **stay**.

An hour in, the crowd shifted and a new wave rolled up the street, laughing, stamping, comparing cocoa flavors like it was a competitive sport.

Then Wes felt it.

A change in the air.

Sam’s posture tightened slightly, subtle enough most people wouldn’t notice.

Wes noticed because Wes noticed everything.

A man in a dark uniform jacket approached, taller than Sam, familiar in the way of someone who belonged to Sam’s world.

He stopped near the table and smiled like he’d already decided he was going to be annoying.

“Rook,” the man said.

Sam’s voice stayed even. “Mason.”

Wes’s brain immediately filed it: name, tone, history.

Mason’s gaze slid to Wes. “And you are.”

Wes kept his stamping hand steady. “Wes. Baker. Local menace.”

Mason laughed. “Aren’t you charming.”

Sam’s eyes narrowed a fraction. “What are you doing here.”

Mason lifted a cocoa stamp card like it was evidence. “Cocoa crawl. I’m on a quick check-in. Chief wanted eyes on the crowd. Holiday events attract… idiots.”

Wes muttered, “True.”

Mason’s eyes flicked back to Wes, amused. “You always this blunt.”

Wes stamped another card. “Only when I’m breathing.”

Mason chuckled, then turned back to Sam. “Anyway. Your detail ends soon.”

Wes’s hand paused mid-stamp.

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “I know.”

Mason’s grin sharpened like he’d enjoyed watching the effect. “Couple more days. Then you’re back to regular rotation. Maybe even out of town for a bit. Depends.”

Wes’s chest tightened hard enough it almost hurt.

Sam’s gaze flicked to Wes for half a second, then back to Mason. “Not decided yet.”

Mason nodded like he didn’t care either way. “Just giving you the heads up.”

Mason’s eyes slid to Wes again, lingering. “Good luck with the crawl.”

Wes gave him a polite smile that contained exactly zero warmth. “Thanks.”

Mason wandered off into the crowd, already forgotten by everyone else.

Wes couldn’t forget.

Sam stayed still beside him, steadying his voice before he said anything.

Sam’s tone was low, only for Wes. “Hey.”

Wes kept stamping like his life depended on it. “Don’t.”

Sam didn’t push. “Okay.”

Wes hated that word and also needed it.

They worked in silence for a few minutes, the crowd flowing, the stamps thudding onto cards like a heartbeat.

Finally, when the next lull hit, Wes’s voice came out tight. “Your detail ends soon.”

Sam nodded once. “Yeah.”

Wes swallowed. “And then you might… leave.”

Sam’s gaze held his, steady. “I might get reassigned for a week or two. Or I might not.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “You didn’t tell me.”

Sam’s voice stayed gentle. “You didn’t ask.”

Wes flinched, because that was fair, and he hated fairness.

Sam added softly, “I wasn’t hiding it. I just didn’t want to drop it on you in the middle of… everything.”

Wes’s jaw clenched. “It still feels like a drop.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Okay.”

Wes’s shoulders rose with a sharp inhale. “Stop.”

Sam’s voice went quieter. “Wes. Look at me.”

Wes didn’t want to.

Wes did anyway.

Sam’s gaze was steady, no pity, no pressure. “If I leave town for work, it doesn’t mean I disappear. It doesn’t mean I’m done.”

Wes swallowed hard. “You can’t promise that.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “I can promise I’ll tell you what’s happening. No vanishing. No silence.”

Wes’s chest hurt. “Why do you keep saying the exact things I don’t know how to ask for.”

Sam’s mouth tipped, soft. “Practice.”

Wes huffed a shaky laugh that sounded like relief trying to pretend it wasn’t.

A new group approached with stamp cards, saving Wes from further emotional competence.

Wes stamped their cards, gave them cocoa, smiled like a professional.

But inside, a quiet clock had started ticking.

---

## Chapter 24: Day Ten, the Small Ask, and Wes Choosing the Hard Kind of Brave

Day Ten’s event was a “Holiday Market Setup,” which meant Noel had convinced half the town to haul folding tables through snow while pretending it was fun.

Wes arrived early with a thermos of coffee and the expression of a man reporting for duty.

Sam arrived beside him, steady, carrying an extra box of extension cords because he’d apparently decided Wes wasn’t allowed to be the only adult.

Noel was already there, directing volunteers like a conductor.

Eben was there too, somehow already tired.

Hugh had brought banners.

Wes eyed the banners. “Why do they sparkle.”

Hugh clutched his heart. “Because hope sparkles.”

Eben’s voice was flat. “Because Hugh is a hazard.”

Noel spotted Wes and Sam and immediately tried to assign them to the most “romantic” tasks available.

“Great!” Noel announced. “You two can string lights on the stall frames. Very… efficient.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “Noel.”

Noel smiled sweetly. “Logistics.”

Eben said without looking up, “It is, unfortunately, logistics.”

Wes muttered, “I hate all of you.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Okay.”

Wes glared. “Stop.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “No.”

They worked.

Wes held the ladder. Sam climbed and looped lights along the frame with calm precision, fingers fast even in gloves.

Every so often, Sam glanced down and Wes’s stomach did the annoying flip again.

Not panic. Not fear.

Just… **want**.

When they finished the last frame, Sam climbed down and stepped close, still in work-mode.

Wes’s hand came up automatically, steadying Sam’s elbow as he landed.

Their gloves brushed.

Wes didn’t yank his hand away.

Sam’s gaze flicked to the contact, then to Wes’s face, softening.

Wes’s throat tightened.

The clock in his chest ticked.

Wes cleared his throat. “After this… can we talk.”

Sam’s gaze steadied. “Yeah. Where.”

Wes hesitated, then said the thing that was actually hard. “My place.”

Sam nodded once. “Okay.”

Wes’s mouth twitched. “Don’t.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “I’m not teasing.”

Wes swallowed. “Good.”

They finished the setup, helped Noel wrangle a pile of tablecloths, saved Hugh from attempting to tape a banner directly to a historic lamppost, and finally escaped.

Wes walked fast back to the bakery apartment, heart pounding with the stupidest fear: **if I ask for too much, it breaks.**

Inside, warmth wrapped around them.

Wes locked the door, then stood there for a beat like he’d forgotten what people did next.

Sam waited, calm, giving space.

Wes exhaled. “Okay. So.”

Sam’s voice was low. “So.”

Wes paced two steps, then stopped, then turned. “That guy. Mason.”

Sam nodded. “Yeah.”

Wes swallowed hard. “You might leave town.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “For work, maybe. Temporarily.”

Wes’s jaw clenched. “Temporarily still counts.”

Sam didn’t argue. “It does.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “I don’t do… waiting.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “Tell me what you need.”

Wes blinked, caught. “I don’t know.”

Sam nodded once, like that was allowed. “Okay. Then we figure it out.”

Wes’s chest ached. He forced the truth out anyway, rough and unpolished. “I need to know you’re not going to just… stop showing up.”

Sam’s gaze didn’t waver. “I won’t.”

Wes’s breath hitched. “You can’t promise the schedule.”

Sam’s voice stayed steady. “I can promise communication. I can promise I won’t punish you with silence. And I can promise… if I leave town, I’m coming back.”

Wes swallowed hard.

Sam added, softer, “And if you want, you can come with me sometimes. When it makes sense. When you want.”

Wes froze.

That offer landed like a door cracking open into a life Wes hadn’t let himself imagine.

Wes stared. “I can’t just… leave the bakery.”

Sam’s expression stayed gentle. “Not forever. Not for a dramatic rom-com montage. But for a weekend. For a day. For a drive.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “I don’t know how to do that.”

Sam’s voice was low. “Then I’ll teach you.”

Wes’s eyes stung, annoying and immediate.

Wes looked down, jaw tight. “I hate that you make this sound possible.”

Sam’s mouth tipped, soft. “Okay.”

Wes glared, half-hearted. “Stop.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes exhaled a laugh that sounded like surrender.

Then Wes did the hard thing.

Wes said, quietly, “If you do get reassigned… I want a plan.”

Sam nodded once. “Okay. A plan.”

Wes swallowed. “Calls. Texts. Something predictable.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “Done.”

Wes’s breath hitched. “And… I want to see you before you go. If you go.”

Sam’s voice was soft. “Yes.”

Wes’s throat tightened again. “And when you come back… I don’t want to start over.”

Sam’s eyes softened. “We won’t.”

Wes blinked hard, like he could hold back the stupid sting.

Wes whispered, almost embarrassed by his own want, “Stay tonight.”

Sam’s answer was immediate. “Yes.”

Wes nodded once, like that was all he could handle.

Then he stepped in and kissed Sam, slow and grounding, as if his body needed proof more than his mind did.

Sam returned it gently, one hand at Wes’s waist, steady.

Sam broke the kiss just enough to murmur, “Still okay.”

Wes exhaled. “Yes.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “Good.”

Wes pressed his forehead to Sam’s for a beat and whispered, “I’m seeing you.”

Sam’s voice was quiet and sure. “I’m seeing you too.”

And the words didn’t feel like a trap.

They felt like a plan.

**[STOP WRITING]**



## Chapter 25: Day Eleven, Market Day, and the Plan That Holds

Day Eleven arrived with the holiday market in full swing and the town acting like commerce was a spiritual practice.

Evergreen Square was lined with stalls: handmade ornaments, knitted scarves, jars of jam, carved wooden toys, and one suspicious booth Hugh claimed was “avant-garde wreath theory.”

Wes ran the Candle & Crumb stall with the grim competence of a man protecting the realm. Cookies stacked. Labels straight. Cash box guarded like it contained state secrets.

Sam stood nearby, not looming, just… present. Helping when asked. Carrying boxes. Being the kind of steady that made Wes’s nervous system unclench without permission.

Noel floated through the market like a benevolent menace, collecting donations and compliments.

Eben directed foot traffic with the intensity of airport security.

Hugh was, unfortunately, thriving.

Wes handed a bag of ginger snaps to a customer, then glanced sideways at Sam. “You’re doing that thing again.”

Sam’s brows lifted. “What thing.”

Wes nodded toward the crowd. “Standing close enough to help. Far enough to not make it… a scene.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “You asked for slow.”

Wes’s cheeks warmed. “Don’t say it like I’m reasonable.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “You are.”

Wes snorted and turned back to the register, because admitting he liked that would be emotionally irresponsible.

A lull hit around mid-afternoon.

Noel was busy charming a donor. Eben was confiscating Hugh’s glitter tape. The crowd thinned to a manageable hum.

Wes breathed out, and for the first time all week, he let himself *pause*.

Sam leaned in slightly, voice low. “You want to take five.”

Wes blinked. “I can’t just… take five.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “You can. I’ll run the stall.”

Wes stared like Sam had offered to lift a car. “You don’t know the prices.”

Sam nodded once. “Tell me two prices. I’ll improvise the rest.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “This is how you get audited.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Okay.”

Wes groaned. “Stop saying okay like you’re a monk.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Take five.”

Wes hesitated. Then he did the wildest thing.

He stepped back from the stall.

Not far. Just to the side, behind a little display of pine boughs and lanterns where he could breathe without eyes on him.

Sam slid into place behind the table like he belonged there.

A customer approached. Sam gave a calm smile and somehow managed to be polite without being syrupy, which was frankly unfair.

Wes watched for half a minute, chest tight, waiting for his body to freak out.

It didn’t.

It settled.

When Wes stepped back to the stall, Sam’s gaze flicked to him. “Better.”

Wes muttered, “Slightly.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Good.”

Wes glared. “Don’t.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes exhaled, then said quietly, “We should talk about the plan again.”

Sam’s expression softened. “Okay. Tonight.”

Wes swallowed. “Before the Lantern Walk.”

Sam nodded. “Before.”

Wes’s throat tightened. A plan. A timeline. Predictable contact.

Not romance-movie grand.

Just… stable.

The market continued, and the town kept doing its ridiculous holiday thing.

Wes kept selling cookies.

Sam kept staying.

And for once, the stability didn’t feel like it was about to vanish the second Wes looked at it.

---

## Chapter 26: Day Twelve, The Lantern Walk, and the Choice That’s Visible but Not Loud

Night fell early, as it always did in December, and Tinsel Town turned on its lights like a decision.

The lantern path glowed. The tree shimmered. The square filled with bundled-up townspeople holding paper cups of cocoa like it was sacred.

Wes stood at the edge of the crowd, hands in his coat pockets, scanning for hazards out of habit.

Sam stood beside him, shoulder close enough to brush, not quite touching.

Noel bounced nearby in a scarf that could qualify as a duvet, microphone in hand because Noel believed the human voice was meant to fill all silence.

“Welcome,” Noel announced, softer tonight, “to the Lantern Walk. Take someone you like. Take someone you love. Take someone you just met. Walk together. Breathe together. Try not to slip on the icy patch by the third lantern, because Hugh refuses to salt ‘for aesthetic reasons.’”

Hugh gasped. “It ruins the mood.”

Eben muttered, “Salt saves lives.”

Wes snorted under his breath.

Sam’s gaze flicked to him, warm.

Noel’s eyes scanned the crowd and landed, predictably, on Wes and Sam.

Noel’s smile softened, almost gentle.

Wes braced for sabotage.

Noel just nodded once, like a silent *good*, then turned back to the mic. “Okay. Pairs whenever you’re ready.”

The crowd began to move.

Couples, friends, families. A slow, glowing river down the path.

Wes didn’t move immediately.

Sam stayed still too, waiting.

Wes’s throat tightened. The old fear tried to flare: *If I step forward, everyone will see. If everyone sees, it becomes a story. If it becomes a story, it can get taken.*

Wes inhaled.

He exhaled.

Then he looked at Sam.

“Hand,” Wes said, barely audible, like he was ordering a pastry.

Sam didn’t smile. Didn’t tease. Just lifted his gloved hand a little, offering.

Wes took it.

The contact was warm even through gloves. A steady tether.

Wes’s chest hurt in the way a muscle hurt when it finally got used.

They stepped forward together and joined the slow river of people.

Noel didn’t clap.

Hugh didn’t scream.

Eben didn’t die of emotion.

The town just… let it be.

The lanterns glowed around them like the world had decided to be kind.

Halfway down the path, they reached the arch again. The one with pine boughs and lights.

The mistletoe still hung there, unrepentant.

Wes felt his body tense, instinct wanting to pivot.

Sam’s voice went low, only for him. “We can walk past.”

Wes swallowed.

He thought of the plan they’d made. Calls. Texts. No disappearing. Coming back.

He thought of Sam standing behind his stall while Wes took five minutes to breathe.

He thought of how “seeing him” hadn’t exploded the town into gossip, it had just… existed.

Wes tightened his grip on Sam’s hand and said, quiet and rough, “Not for them.”

Sam’s gaze held his, steady. “For you.”

Wes’s heart hammered.

Wes leaned in and kissed Sam once under the arch.

Quick. Private in the way a small thing could be private even in public.

A kiss that said: *I’m choosing this.* Not as a performance. Not as a headline. As a fact.

When Wes pulled back, Sam’s eyes were warm, almost stunned.

Sam’s voice was low. “Still okay.”

Wes’s cheeks warmed. “Yes.”

Sam’s mouth tipped, soft. “Good.”

They kept walking.

And the town kept walking.

And the world didn’t end.

It just glowed.

---

## Chapter 27: The Night After, and the Happily-For-Now That Doesn’t Feel Like a Lie

Back at Candle & Crumb, Wes locked the door and leaned his forehead against the wood for a second, breathing like he’d run a marathon.

Sam stood a few feet away, quiet, letting Wes come down on his own terms.

Wes turned, eyes bright with something he still didn’t know how to hold.

“That was…” Wes started.

Sam waited, steady.

Wes swallowed. “A lot.”

Sam’s voice was gentle. “Good a lot or too much.”

Wes exhaled, shaky. “Good.”

Sam’s shoulders loosened slightly, like he’d been holding his breath too.

Wes paced two steps, then stopped like his body couldn’t decide whether to flee or stay.

Wes looked at Sam. “If you get reassigned…”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “I’ll tell you. We’ll use the plan.”

Wes nodded once. “And if I freak out…”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “You will.”

Wes glared. “Rude.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Accurate.”

Wes huffed a laugh, then stepped closer, slow. “And you’ll still… come back.”

Sam’s voice was calm and sure. “Yes.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “Okay.”

Sam’s eyes softened. “Okay.”

Wes stared for a beat, then leaned in and kissed Sam again. Longer this time. Not rushed. Not desperate.

When Sam’s hands came up, they were gentle, asking without words.

Wes answered by staying.

They moved upstairs with the quiet inevitability of two people who’d finally stopped pretending this was temporary.

In the bedroom, they took their time. Kissing. Holding. Warmth and closeness that didn’t need narration to prove anything.

When it became more than kissing, it stayed private, slow, and chosen, the way Wes needed.

Afterward, Wes lay curled against Sam, breathing steady, fingers lightly gripping Sam’s shirt like he was anchoring himself to something real.

Sam’s arm held him, quiet and solid.

Wes whispered, half embarrassed, “You’re still here.”

Sam’s voice was low. “Yeah.”

Wes swallowed. “Good.”

Sam’s mouth brushed Wes’s hair, almost a kiss. “Good.”

Wes closed his eyes and let the warmth settle in his chest like it belonged there.

Not forever. Not with guarantees carved into stone.

But for now. For this winter. For this strange little town with its lanterns and its meddling and its ridiculous snowman emergencies.

For a plan.

For a man who stayed.

Wes exhaled, finally letting his body rest.

And for the first time in a long time, “happily for now” didn’t feel like a lie.

**[STOP WRITING]**



## Chapter 28: The Morning After the Lantern Walk

Wes woke up to quiet.

Not the brittle, lonely quiet he used to live in. Not the “no one is here and you should be productive about it” quiet.

This was… soft.

Snow-light filtered through the curtains. The bakery below was still asleep. The town outside was probably still hungover on cocoa and carols.

Sam was beside him, warm and solid, breathing slow.

Wes lay still for a minute, waiting for his brain to do its usual thing. The sabotage. The list. The compulsion to sprint downstairs and start proofing dough just to prove he was functional.

The list tried.

But it didn’t win.

Wes turned his head slightly and watched Sam sleep, the calm of it making something in Wes’s chest loosen in a way that felt unfamiliar.

Sam’s eyes opened after a minute, slow.

He blinked once, then looked at Wes like it was normal to wake up and find someone you wanted still there.

“Morning,” Sam murmured.

Wes swallowed. “Morning.”

Sam’s mouth tipped, sleepy. “Still okay.”

Wes groaned into the pillow. “You’re obsessed.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Answer anyway.”

Wes exhaled. “Yeah. I’m okay.”

Sam’s shoulders loosened, like he’d been waiting for the confirmation even though he already knew. “Good.”

Wes stared at him, caught off guard by how much the word *good* landed.

Wes muttered, “Don’t be pleased.”

Sam’s eyes softened. “I am pleased.”

Wes’s throat tightened. Again.

He rolled onto his side, closer, and pressed his forehead against Sam’s for a beat because apparently that was a thing he did now instead of running.

Wes whispered, rough, “We kissed under the arch.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady, warm. “Yeah.”

Wes swallowed. “That was… public.”

Sam didn’t tease. “It was small.”

Wes’s breath shuddered. “It felt big.”

Sam’s voice was quiet. “To you.”

Wes nodded once, not trusting his mouth.

Sam’s hand slid up to the back of Wes’s neck, thumb resting there like an anchor. “Do you regret it.”

Wes blinked hard. “No.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “Good.”

Wes huffed. “Stop.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “No.”

Wes stared at him for a second, then admitted the second truth, quieter. “I’m scared it means you’ll leave and it’ll hurt more.”

Sam didn’t flinch.

Sam didn’t give a big speech.

He just said, steady and plain, “If it hurts more, it’s because it mattered.”

Wes’s eyes stung. He hated it. He loved it.

Wes swallowed and shoved his face into Sam’s shoulder like that solved anything.

Sam’s arm tightened around him, gentle.

After a beat, Sam murmured, “We can stay in bed a little.”

Wes hesitated, then let himself say it. “Yeah.”

Sam’s breath warmed Wes’s hair. “Okay.”

Wes muttered, muffled, “Stop saying okay.”

Sam’s mouth tipped against his hair. “No.”

And Wes stayed.

---

## Chapter 29: The Reassignment Call

The call came at noon, because of course it did.

Wes was downstairs prepping a new batch of dough, pretending he wasn’t still warm from the morning. Sam was at the table with coffee and a folded paper map of town that he absolutely did not need, because Sam was the kind of person who could make “prepared” look like a personality trait.

Sam’s phone buzzed.

Wes felt it in his body before Sam even moved.

Sam glanced at the screen, expression tightening just a fraction. “It’s the station.”

Wes’s hands stilled.

His brain lit up like a warning panel. *Here it is. Here it is. Here it is.*

Sam stood, calm, and walked a few steps away to take the call, voice low.

Wes kept his eyes on the dough like it had suddenly become a life-or-death situation.

He heard fragments.

“…yeah.”

“…understood.”

“…two weeks.”

Wes’s stomach dropped hard enough he almost felt sick.

Sam ended the call and came back slowly, not rushing, not bracing for impact like Wes was a bomb.

Wes forced his hands to keep moving, kneading like he could knead the fear out of his bloodstream.

Sam stopped at the edge of the prep counter. “Hey.”

Wes didn’t look up. “Don’t.”

Sam’s voice stayed gentle. “Okay. But I’m telling you.”

Wes swallowed hard. “Two weeks.”

Sam nodded. “Two weeks. Starting after tomorrow.”

Wes’s chest tightened. “Out of town.”

Sam nodded again. “Out of town.”

Wes’s mouth went dry. “You’re leaving.”

Sam’s gaze held him. “For work. And I’m coming back.”

Wes’s breath hitched. The old instincts screamed at him to get angry, to get cold, to get ahead of the hurt by pretending he didn’t care.

Instead, Wes heard himself say, rough, “Plan.”

Sam’s shoulders eased a fraction, like the word was a bridge. “Plan.”

Wes finally looked up, eyes bright in a way he hated. “You call. You text. Predictable.”

Sam nodded. “Yes.”

Wes’s voice went sharper, because fear always tried to disguise itself as control. “If something changes, you tell me. Immediately.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “Yes.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “No disappearing.”

Sam’s voice was calm and sure. “No disappearing.”

Wes swallowed hard, then forced the last part out, quiet and raw. “And I want to see you before you go.”

Sam’s expression softened. “You will.”

Wes stared at him for a beat, chest heaving like he’d run a mile.

Sam stepped closer, slow. “Do you want me to stay tonight.”

Wes’s laugh came out sharp and bitter, because his brain was trying to be cruel for protection. “Obviously.”

Sam didn’t flinch at the tone. “Okay.”

Wes squeezed his eyes shut for a second, then opened them again, jaw tight. “I’m not mad at you.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “I know.”

Wes swallowed. “I’m mad at… timing.”

Sam nodded. “That’s fair.”

Wes’s hands were shaking. He set the dough down and scrubbed his palms on his apron like that could clean off the feeling.

Sam watched him for a beat, then said softly, “Come here.”

Wes’s reflex was to say no.

Wes didn’t.

He walked around the counter and stopped in front of Sam like he was stepping into weather he wasn’t sure he could handle.

Sam lifted a hand, not touching yet. “Okay if I hold you.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “Yes.”

Sam pulled him in, steady and warm, arms wrapping around Wes like it was the most normal thing in the world to hold someone who was trying not to fall apart.

Wes pressed his forehead to Sam’s shoulder and breathed.

Sam’s voice was low near his ear. “We’re not starting over.”

Wes’s breath hitched. “Promise.”

Sam’s answer was immediate. “Promise.”

Wes’s body sagged a fraction, like it had been waiting for that.

He whispered, muffled, “I hate that this hurts.”

Sam’s hand rested at the back of his neck, gentle. “It hurts because you’re doing the brave thing. You’re staying in it.”

Wes exhaled shakily. “Don’t make it sound noble.”

Sam’s mouth tipped, quiet warmth. “Okay. Then it’s just real.”

Wes nodded once into Sam’s shoulder.

Real.

Fine.

Manageable.

They stood there for a moment, the bakery warm around them, the town outside continuing its ridiculous winter life.

And Wes didn’t bolt.

---

## Chapter 30: The Last Festival Night

The final night of the festival wasn’t a big setpiece.

It was smaller. Softer. Like Tinsel Town had spent its fireworks and decided to end with lanterns instead.

Noel held a short “thank you” speech in the square, shockingly contained. Eben looked like he’d personally supervised every word.

Hugh wore a velvet coat and tried to make “one last magical flourish” happen with confetti. Eben confiscated the confetti.

The town laughed. The tree glowed. People hugged. People promised to see each other soon, even though they lived ten minutes apart.

Wes stood near the edge of it all with Sam, hands in his pockets, shoulders not as tight as they used to be.

Noel wandered over, cheeks pink from cold and effort.

He looked at Wes and Sam for a beat, expression softer than usual. “You did good,” Noel said quietly.

Wes squinted. “Don’t start.”

Noel lifted both hands. “Not starting. Just… saying.”

Wes’s throat tightened, and he hated Noel for being sincere.

Noel’s gaze flicked to Sam. “Take care of him.”

Sam’s voice was calm. “I will.”

Wes bristled automatically. “I’m right here.”

Noel smiled. “I know.”

Eben appeared beside Noel like a shadow with opinions. “Stop hovering.”

Noel gasped. “I’m blessing them.”

Eben’s gaze slid to Wes, then Sam, then away. “Don’t make it weird.”

Wes muttered, “Thank you.”

Eben blinked like he wasn’t used to gratitude, then nodded once and walked off.

Noel watched Eben go, eyes softening, then looked back at Wes. “Text me,” Noel said suddenly.

Wes frowned. “Why.”

Noel’s mouth tipped. “Because you’re going to spiral when he leaves and I’m going to bully you into eating.”

Wes stared. “You can’t just say that.”

Noel grinned. “I can and I did.”

Wes exhaled, half annoyed, half… grateful, which was disgusting. “Fine.”

Noel beamed like he’d won something and wandered off to harass another volunteer into accepting a hug.

Wes turned slightly toward Sam, voice low. “I’m not going to be normal about this.”

Sam’s mouth tipped, warm. “I know.”

Wes swallowed. “But I’m not going to be terrible either.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “That’s huge.”

Wes rolled his eyes. “Don’t.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes stood there for a beat, watching the town glow, feeling the clock tick in his chest.

Then Wes did it.

Wes reached out and took Sam’s hand. No hiding behind a table. No pretending it was accidental.

Just… choice.

Sam’s fingers curled around his, steady and warm even through gloves.

Wes didn’t look around to see who noticed.

He didn’t need to.

He leaned in slightly, voice rough and private. “When you’re gone… call me every night.”

Sam’s eyes softened. “Okay.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “Real okay.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Real okay.”

Wes swallowed, then added the second ask, quieter. “And when you come back… come straight here.”

Sam’s gaze held him like a promise. “I will.”

Wes nodded once, breathing hard like he’d run again.

Sam lifted Wes’s gloved hand and pressed a brief kiss to his knuckles. Small. Quick. For Wes. Not for the town.

Wes’s chest ached, warm and sharp.

He whispered, “Still okay.”

Sam’s eyes warmed, pleased at the theft of his line. “Yes.”

Wes’s mouth twitched. “Good.”

They stayed until the lights dimmed and the crowd thinned.

Then they walked home together through snow and lantern glow, hand in hand like it was allowed.

Like it could be real even when it wasn’t forever.

And Wes, for once, let “real” be enough.

**[STOP WRITING]**


## Chapter 31: The Goodbye That Isn’t a Breakup

The morning Sam left, the bakery woke up like it was trying to pretend this was normal.

The ovens hummed. The kettles clicked. The snow outside held the street in soft silence.

Wes moved through the prep space with sharp, efficient hands. Coffee. Toast. A small paper bag of pastries he absolutely did not need to send, because Sam could eat literally anywhere, but Wes needed **something** to do with his hands.

Sam stood by the small table, coat on, duffel at his feet, watching Wes without hovering.

Wes kept his back turned for a full minute longer than necessary.

Finally, Sam said quietly, “Wes.”

Wes froze, then exhaled. “Don’t.”

Sam’s voice stayed steady. “I’m leaving in ten.”

Wes nodded once like he’d been told the weather.

He turned, shoved the pastry bag toward Sam. “Food. You’ll forget.”

Sam took it gently. “Thanks.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “Don’t make it a thing.”

Sam’s eyes softened. “You made it a thing.”

Wes glared, but it had no bite. “Shut up.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Okay.”

Wes rolled his eyes hard enough to qualify as cardio, then stepped closer anyway.

They stood in the middle of the bakery, warm air and sugar and wood around them like a familiar blanket.

Wes swallowed. “The plan.”

Sam nodded. “The plan.”

Wes said it like a checklist because that was the only way his brain could hold it. “Text when you arrive. Call every night. If anything changes, you tell me immediately.”

Sam’s gaze didn’t waver. “Yes.”

Wes’s voice went tighter. “And you come back.”

Sam’s answer was immediate. “I come back.”

Wes’s breath hitched, and he hated himself for it.

Sam waited a beat, then asked quietly, “Do you want a goodbye kiss.”

Wes’s cheeks warmed. He tried for sarcasm and failed. “Obviously.”

Sam stepped in slowly, giving Wes time to bolt.

Wes didn’t bolt.

Wes kissed him. Not rushed. Not desperate. A steady, chosen kiss that made Wes’s chest ache with warmth and fear tangled together.

When Sam pulled back, his eyes stayed on Wes’s like he was anchoring the moment in memory.

“Still okay,” Sam murmured.

Wes swallowed. “No.”

Sam’s expression softened, calm. “Okay. What do you need.”

Wes stared at him, throat tight, then forced it out, low and raw. “Just… don’t make me guess.”

Sam nodded. “You won’t have to.”

Wes exhaled, shaky. “Good.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Good.”

Sam reached up, thumb brushing Wes’s cheek once, quick and gentle.

Wes flinched like it meant too much.

It did.

Sam’s voice was low. “I’ll call tonight.”

Wes nodded once, jaw tight. “Tonight.”

Sam picked up his duffel.

Wes followed him to the front door like he couldn’t not.

Sam paused on the threshold. Looked back. “You’ll eat.”

Wes snapped, “Yes.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Lie.”

Wes glared. “Noel will bully me.”

Sam nodded, satisfied. “Good.”

Wes swallowed hard. “Go.”

Sam’s gaze held him one last second, soft and steady. “I’m coming back.”

Wes couldn’t trust his voice, so he just nodded.

Sam stepped out into the cold.

Wes watched him go until the street swallowed him.

Then Wes locked the door and pressed his forehead against the wood like it was the only thing keeping him upright.

---

## Chapter 32: The First Night

Wes made it through the day by pure spite.

He baked. He cleaned. He organized a shelf that didn’t need organizing. He rearranged cookie tins by flavor, by size, by “how much emotional collapse they could prevent.”

Noel stopped by at exactly 6:04 p.m. with a casserole like a threat.

Wes stared at it. “What is that.”

Noel smiled sweetly. “Food. You’re going to eat it.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “I have food.”

Noel walked past him into the bakery like he owned it. “You have *ingredients*. Not the same thing.”

Eben followed, silent as a judgment.

Wes’s mouth tightened. “Traitor.”

Noel set the casserole on the counter. “Listen,” Noel said, softer now, “you did good. He left. You didn’t implode. That’s growth.”

Wes muttered, “He left today.”

Noel nodded. “Yes. And you are upright.”

Wes glared. “Barely.”

Noel pointed at him. “Still counts.”

Eben, from the doorway, said flatly, “Eat.”

Wes blinked at Eben. “You’re also here.”

Eben’s gaze was impassive. “Noel is dramatic. Someone must supervise.”

Noel gasped. “I’m supportive.”

Eben stared at him until Noel stopped talking.

Wes exhaled a laugh he didn’t fully mean, then slumped onto a chair like he’d run a marathon.

Noel served him a portion like a nurse. Wes ate because he was too tired to fight.

Eben stayed for exactly twelve minutes, then stood. “He will call.”

Wes’s chest tightened. “I know.”

Eben nodded once, like that was settled, and left without another word.

Noel lingered, eyes softer than usual. “Text me if you start spiraling,” Noel said, quiet.

Wes rolled his eyes. “Get out.”

Noel smiled. “Love you too,” and left.

The bakery went quiet again.

At 9:00 p.m. on the dot, Wes’s phone rang.

Wes stared at it like it was a live wire.

He answered on the second ring because he was not going to be pathetic about this, absolutely not.

Sam’s voice came through, low and steady. “Hey.”

Wes’s throat tightened instantly. “Hey.”

Sam paused like he was listening for damage. “You okay.”

Wes exhaled. “No.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “Okay. Tell me.”

Wes swallowed hard. “It’s… quiet.”

Sam’s tone softened. “Yeah.”

Wes stared at the darkened prep space, lights low, ovens off. “It’s stupid.”

Sam’s voice was gentle. “It’s not stupid.”

Wes’s mouth tightened. “Did you arrive.”

Sam answered steadily, like a checklist because he knew Wes liked those. “Arrived. Checked in. Hotel’s fine. I ate.”

Wes blinked. “You ate.”

Sam’s mouth tipped, audible in his voice. “I ate the pastries.”

Wes’s chest ached, warm. “Good.”

Sam’s voice went lower. “I miss you.”

Wes froze.

He hated how fast his eyes stung.

Wes forced it out anyway, rough and honest. “I miss you too.”

Sam didn’t make a big deal of it. He just let it sit there like a truth that didn’t need defending.

They talked for twenty minutes about nothing: the drive, the snow, the shift schedule, a terrible diner Sam had passed, the raccoon Noel was now apparently “tracking.”

And when Wes’s voice finally started to steady, Sam said quietly, “Same time tomorrow.”

Wes swallowed. “Same time.”

Sam’s tone warmed. “Still okay.”

Wes sighed into the phone, exhausted. “I’m… better.”

Sam’s voice was soft. “Good.”

Wes muttered, “Stop.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “No.”

Wes closed his eyes and let himself smile, small and tired.

“Goodnight,” Wes said.

“Goodnight,” Sam answered. “I’m coming back.”

Wes whispered, barely, “I know.”

The call ended.

Wes sat in the quiet for another minute.

Then he stood up, turned off the remaining lights, and went upstairs.

Not because he was fine.

Because he was still here.

---

## Chapter 33: Two Weeks Later

The day Sam came back, Tinsel Town was thawing.

Not fully. Winter still had its claws in the sidewalks. But the sun was brighter, the snow softer, the air less sharp.

Wes was downstairs pretending to be normal, which meant he’d baked twice as much as necessary and cleaned every surface like it had personally offended him.

Noel had texted him that morning: **HE’S COMING. DO NOT PANIC-BAKE.**

Wes had replied: **I’M NOT.**

Noel had replied: **LIE.**

At 4:17 p.m., the bell over the bakery door chimed.

Wes’s whole body went still.

He didn’t move for one full second, like his brain needed to confirm reality.

Then he turned.

Sam stood in the doorway, coat dusted with cold, duffel in hand, eyes steady and soft like he’d been walking toward this moment the whole drive.

Wes’s throat tightened so hard it almost hurt.

Sam’s voice was quiet. “Hey.”

Wes’s voice came out rough. “Hey.”

Sam stepped inside and set his duffel down. He didn’t rush forward. He didn’t demand anything.

He just looked at Wes like Wes was real.

Wes crossed the space between them in three strides and grabbed Sam’s coat with both hands like he needed to anchor himself to something solid.

Wes kissed him before he could talk himself out of it.

Sam kissed back immediately, warm and steady, hands coming up to hold Wes like he’d been doing it in his head for two weeks.

When Wes pulled back, he was breathing hard like he’d run again.

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Still okay.”

Wes huffed a shaky laugh. “Yes.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Good.”

Wes swallowed, gaze sharp because he needed one last confirmation. “You came straight here.”

Sam’s voice was calm. “I said I would.”

Wes’s chest ached, warm and relieved and furious at how much he’d needed that.

Wes muttered, “I hate that I need you.”

Sam’s expression softened. “You don’t need me.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “Don’t you dare.”

Sam’s mouth tipped, gentle. “Okay. You want me. And I want you.”

Wes stared at him, throat tight, then finally exhaled like his body remembered how to breathe.

Wes nodded toward the back, voice rough. “Upstairs.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Okay.”

Wes rolled his eyes through the sting. “Stop.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “No.”

Wes grabbed Sam’s hand and pulled him toward the stairs like he wasn’t letting the world take him away again.

And this time, when the door closed behind them, it didn’t feel like hiding.

It felt like coming home.

**[STOP WRITING]**



You’re near the **end**. Specifically:

* We’ve covered **Days 3–12** of the festival arc, including the public “I’m seeing him” moment, the **Lantern Walk kiss**, the **reassignment reveal**, the **departure**, the **first-night call**, the **two-week gap**, and the **return + reunion kiss**.
* That puts us at the book’s **final act / resolution stretch**. The external plot (festival) is basically wrapped, and the internal plot (Wes learning to stay, not bolt) has hit its payoff.

**Current location:** **After the climax** (Lantern Walk + commitment) and **past the separation beat** (temporary reassignment). We’re now in the **“HEA/HFN landing zone”**: reunion, solidifying the plan, and closing with one last cozy setpiece + emotional button.

**What’s left to write (roughly 2–4 chapters, depending on how long you like endings):**

1. **Aftercare + recalibration scene** upstairs (reunion becomes “we’re okay, for real”).
2. **Town epilogue beat**: Noel/Eben/Hugh cameo that confirms normalcy, maybe a last joke about the arch/mistletoe.
3. **Future-proofing**: explicit agreement on what “seeing” means now (labels optional), how they’ll handle future reassignments, maybe a weekend away as a symbol of growth for Wes.
4. **Final image**: Candle & Crumb at night, snow outside, Sam in the doorway or behind the counter, Wes choosing closeness without flinching.

So yeah: you’re basically on the last page, emotionally. Humans love wrapping bows on trauma, and this book is currently tying that bow.






## Chapter 34: Upstairs, After the Return

Upstairs felt different when Sam came back.

Not because the room changed. Same worn quilt. Same soft lamp. Same faint scent of flour that lived in Wes’s life like a second skin.

But the air had shifted, like Wes’s body finally believed what his mouth had been practicing for two weeks: **he comes back**.

Wes tugged Sam into the bedroom and stopped only when Sam’s duffel hit the floor with a soft thud.

Sam’s hands settled at Wes’s waist, steady. “Hey.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “Don’t ‘hey’ me like you weren’t gone for fourteen years.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “It was fourteen days.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “Feels illegal.”

Sam’s gaze softened, warm and serious under the humor. “I’m here.”

Wes swallowed hard.

His chest still held the two-week ache like bruising. He hated that tenderness in himself. He hated that it meant he cared. He hated that he was relieved enough to feel faint.

Wes pressed his forehead to Sam’s shoulder and breathed.

Sam’s arms came around him, calm and grounding.

Wes whispered, muffled, “I didn’t like it.”

Sam’s voice was low. “I know.”

Wes pulled back just enough to look at him. “I did the plan. I answered your calls. I ate Noel’s casserole.”

Sam’s brows lifted. “You ate.”

Wes glared. “Don’t.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “That’s huge.”

Wes exhaled sharply. “Stop praising me like I’m a rescue dog.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Okay.”

Wes rolled his eyes, then said the part that actually mattered, voice rough. “I didn’t shut down.”

Sam’s expression softened. “I know.”

Wes swallowed. “And you didn’t disappear.”

Sam’s gaze held him, steady as a vow. “I won’t.”

Wes’s breath hitched.

Then Wes did what he should’ve done two weeks ago, if he’d been capable of asking without choking on it.

Wes said quietly, “Tell me what you want from this.”

Sam didn’t flinch. He didn’t treat it like a trap. He just answered plainly.

“I want you,” Sam said. “I want the slow version. The real version. I want to keep showing up. I want to be yours in a way that doesn’t scare you into running.”

Wes’s eyes stung, immediate and stupid.

Wes forced the words out like he was prying them from his own ribs. “And if you get reassigned again.”

Sam nodded once. “Then we do the plan again. And we adjust if you need it. But I don’t go silent. I don’t punish you. I come back.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “And if I… spiral.”

Sam’s mouth tipped slightly. “You will.”

Wes glared. “Rude.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Accurate. And I’ll still be here.”

Wes stared at him, chest aching in a way that felt like healing.

Wes whispered, almost embarrassed, “I want to try… a label.”

Sam’s brows lifted. Gentle. “Okay. What label.”

Wes swallowed. “Not in public. Not for the town. Just… us.”

Sam nodded, steady. “Okay.”

Wes’s voice came low. “Boyfriend.”

Sam went still for half a second.

Then Sam’s gaze softened like someone had finally placed something precious in his hands.

“Yeah,” Sam said quietly. “Boyfriend.”

Wes’s breath shuddered out. “Still okay.”

Sam’s eyes warmed, pleased at the theft. “Yes.”

Wes exhaled, shaky and relieved, and leaned in to kiss him again.

It started soft, then deepened into something warm and familiar and hungry in a way that made Wes’s hands tighten at Sam’s sweater like he needed proof.

Sam kissed him back with steady patience, letting Wes set the pace, letting it be sweet instead of urgent.

When things started to tip toward more, Sam paused just enough to check in, voice low against Wes’s mouth. “Still okay.”

Wes swallowed, breath coming faster. “Yes. And… slow.”

Sam nodded. “Slow.”

They moved to the bed without rushing.

The rest was warmth, hands, quiet laughter when Wes’s sock caught on the quilt, and the kind of closeness that didn’t need to be explained to count.

After, Wes lay on Sam’s chest, listening to his heartbeat like it was a metronome re-teaching Wes how to exist without bracing.

Sam’s arm stayed around him, steady.

Wes whispered, half asleep already, “You’re really here.”

Sam’s voice was low. “Yeah.”

Wes’s chest eased, finally. “Good.”

Sam’s mouth brushed his hair. “Good.”

---

## Chapter 35: Downstairs, The World Notices Like It’s Normal

The next morning, Wes woke up and didn’t immediately sprint into productivity as penance.

Progress. Horrifying.

Sam was still in bed, eyes half-open, watching Wes like he’d been waiting to see what version he got.

Wes muttered, “Don’t look at me like that.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Like what.”

Wes grimaced. “Like you’re… happy.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “I am happy.”

Wes exhaled, grumpy because happiness still felt suspicious. “Fine.”

They went downstairs together.

Wes turned on the lights, checked the ovens, started coffee. Sam moved quietly in the background, washing a mug, setting out plates like he’d been here for months instead of one night back.

The bell over the door chimed.

Wes looked up automatically, expecting a customer.

It was Noel.

Of course it was Noel.

Noel swept inside in a dramatic coat and immediately froze, eyes narrowing like a predator sensing weakness.

He stared at Sam. He stared at Wes. He stared at the way Sam reached for a mug like he belonged.

Noel’s face split into a grin. “He’s back.”

Wes deadpanned, “Noel.”

Noel ignored him and marched right up to Sam, poked his shoulder like Sam was a lamp he was testing. “You. You came back.”

Sam blinked. “Yes.”

Noel nodded, satisfied. “Good. Because Wes was one bad day away from making his feelings into laminated spreadsheets.”

Wes snapped, “I did not.”

Noel smiled sweetly. “Lie.”

Sam’s mouth tipped slightly.

Wes glared at Sam. “Don’t you start.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Okay.”

Wes rolled his eyes, then pointed at Noel. “Why are you here at eight in the morning.”

Noel produced a paper bag like a magician. “Breakfast pastries, which is hilarious because you are a bakery, but I’m making a point. Also, I have information.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “What information.”

Noel’s grin sharpened. “Mrs. Alden says the cocoa crawl is officially over and the town will now return to regular levels of meddling.”

Wes squinted. “That’s not information.”

Noel leaned in and lowered his voice like he was sharing state secrets. “She also says… you looked happy at the Lantern Walk.”

Wes’s cheeks warmed. “Mrs. Alden needs a hobby.”

Noel’s eyes twinkled. “She has one. It’s you.”

Wes groaned.

The bell chimed again.

This time it was Eben, stepping in like he’d been summoned by Noel’s volume.

Eben’s gaze flicked to Sam. A beat.

Then Eben nodded once. “You’re back.”

Sam nodded. “I’m back.”

Eben’s gaze shifted to Wes like he was assessing structural integrity. “You functioning.”

Wes deadpanned, “Define functioning.”

Eben’s mouth did something that might’ve been the beginning of a smile if Eben had any interest in joy. “Eat.”

Wes pointed. “Everyone stop telling me to eat.”

Noel said brightly, “We won’t.”

Eben said flatly, “We will.”

Hugh breezed in right on cue like the universe was a comedian with terrible timing.

He took one look at Sam, gasped theatrically, and clutched his chest. “The prodigal returns!”

Wes groaned into both hands. “God help me.”

Hugh floated closer, eyes shining, and said to Wes in a loud whisper, “Did you cry.”

Wes hissed, “No.”

Noel whispered back just as loud, “He cried.”

Wes snapped, “NOEL.”

Sam’s shoulders shook once, silent laughter.

Wes looked at Sam, betrayed. “Don’t.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “I’m not teasing. I’m… enjoying.”

Wes muttered, “Disgusting.”

Eben, mercifully, cut through it. “Good. He’s back. You’re fine. Stop performing.”

Hugh looked offended. “But I am an artist.”

Eben stared him into silence.

Noel took his bag, kissed Wes’s cheek without permission, and vanished out the door like a festive ghost.

Hugh followed, muttering about “unappreciated romance arcs.”

Eben paused at the threshold, glanced between Wes and Sam, and said flatly, “Don’t screw it up.”

Wes blinked. “Eben.”

Eben left.

Wes stared at the closed door, then turned to Sam. “Did that just happen.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Yes.”

Wes exhaled, incredulous. “They’re… fine.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “It’s a small town. They wanted you happy.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “That’s gross.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “It is.”

Wes shook his head like he could shake off the tenderness, then pointed at the coffee. “Sit. Drink. You’re off duty. I’m… practicing.”

Sam sat without argument, eyes soft.

Wes poured two cups and slid one over.

Sam took it. “Thank you.”

Wes muttered, “Don’t make it weird.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Okay.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “Stop.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes huffed a laugh and sat across from him.

The bakery hummed.

The town moved on.

And Wes, impossibly, felt like he could too.

---

## Chapter 36: The Closing Image

That night, Candle & Crumb glowed the way it always did after hours.

Soft light. Warm wood. The air carrying traces of sugar and winter and something steadier underneath.

Wes locked the door, flipped the sign to CLOSED, and turned.

Sam was behind the counter, sleeves rolled up, washing dishes like he was just… part of the place.

Wes stared, chest tight.

Sam glanced up. “What.”

Wes swallowed. “You’re doing dishes.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Yes.”

Wes blinked. “You don’t have to do dishes.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “I want to.”

Wes’s throat tightened, and he hated that he still didn’t know how to take kindness without trying to pay for it.

Wes stepped closer, slow, and leaned his hip against the counter. “We should plan a weekend.”

Sam paused, surprised. “A weekend.”

Wes nodded once, jaw tight like he was making a confession. “If you get reassigned again, I want… proof I can leave. Even just for two days.”

Sam’s gaze softened like he’d just been handed something fragile and brave. “Okay. Where.”

Wes hesitated, then said, quiet and real, “Somewhere with snow. Somewhere anonymous. Somewhere no one knows I’m the baker who holds the town together.”

Sam nodded. “Okay.”

Wes exhaled. “Stop saying okay like it fixes me.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “It doesn’t fix you. It just… stays with you.”

Wes’s chest ached, warm.

Wes whispered, “Boyfriend.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Yeah.”

Wes swallowed. “Still okay.”

Sam’s gaze warmed, steady. “Yes.”

Wes reached across the counter and tugged Sam closer by the sleeve, kissing him once, soft and sure.

When Wes pulled back, he rested his forehead against Sam’s and breathed.

Outside, snow drifted quietly down the street.

Inside, the bakery held them like a small safe world.

And Wes, finally, stopped waiting for the other shoe to drop.

He just let himself be here.

With Sam.

With the warmth.

With the plan.

With the kind of ending that didn’t scream forever, but still meant something solid and true:

**We’re here. We’re trying. We’re staying.**

**[STOP WRITING]**




## Chapter 37: The Weekend Plan Becomes a Real Calendar Event

Wes regretted saying “weekend” the second the word left his mouth.

Because now it existed.

It could be scheduled. It could be planned. It could become a thing with expectations, which Wes hated almost as much as he hated feelings.

Sam dried his hands and leaned against the counter, watching Wes with that steady patience that made Wes want to both kiss him and throw flour at him.

“A weekend,” Sam repeated, calm.

Wes bristled on instinct. “Don’t say it like that.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Like what.”

Wes gestured vaguely, because language was a trap. “Like it’s… simple.”

Sam’s gaze stayed warm. “It can be simple.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “Nothing is simple.”

Sam nodded once. “Okay. Then we make it small.”

Wes swallowed. “How small.”

Sam held up two fingers. “Two nights. One drive. No itinerary. No charming locals trying to adopt you.”

Wes scoffed. “That’s not how towns work.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Not a town. A cabin. Outside a town.”

Wes blinked. A cabin. Quiet. Anonymous. Controlled.

His chest loosened a fraction.

Wes muttered, suspicious, “Do you already have a cabin.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “My aunt does. It’s empty in January.”

Wes’s throat tightened at the casualness of it. Like Sam already had a place in his life where Wes could fit, if Wes let him.

Wes forced his voice to stay flat. “January is not Christmas.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “No. It’s calmer.”

Wes swallowed. “Okay.”

Sam’s brows lifted slightly.

Wes immediately scowled. “Don’t look smug.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “I’m not smug.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “You’re smug.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Okay.”

Wes groaned and scrubbed a hand over his face, then reached for the little calendar by the register like he was about to perform surgery.

“Pick a weekend,” Wes said, all business.

Sam stepped closer, shoulder nearly brushing Wes’s. “Second weekend.”

Wes wrote it down with aggressive pen pressure. “Two nights.”

Sam nodded. “Two nights.”

Wes pointed the pen at him like a weapon. “If you get reassigned again, you tell me before I find out from some guy named Mason with a smug face.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “I will.”

Wes swallowed. “And if I panic.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “You will.”

Wes glared. “Rude.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Accurate.”

Wes exhaled through his nose, then circled the date anyway.

A real circle. On paper. In ink.

The universe did not collapse.

Wes stared at it, unsettled by how much relief he felt.

Sam’s voice went low. “Still okay.”

Wes sighed. “Yes.”

Sam’s eyes softened. “Good.”

Wes muttered, “Stop.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “No.”

Wes looked up and caught Sam watching him like Wes had done something heroic instead of writing on a calendar.

Wes’s throat tightened. “It’s just a weekend.”

Sam’s voice was quiet. “It’s you choosing to leave your world for a minute.”

Wes swallowed hard, then shrugged like he didn’t care. “Don’t make it poetic.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Okay.”

Wes pointed. “Stop that.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes leaned forward and kissed him once, quick and annoyed, like punctuation.

Sam’s hands came up lightly at Wes’s waist, grounding.

Wes pulled back, cheeks warm. “Fine. Weekend.”

Sam’s voice was soft. “Weekend.”

---

## Chapter 38: The Key That Isn’t a Trap

Three days later, Sam showed up after his shift with snow on his shoulders and a small paper bag in his hand.

Wes was behind the counter, half-covered in flour, brow furrowed at a tray like the cookies had personally insulted him.

Sam set the bag down gently. “Brought dinner.”

Wes didn’t look up. “Noel already fed me yesterday. The town is in a conspiracy.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Yes.”

Wes glanced up. “You’re agreeing too easily.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “I’m learning.”

Wes huffed a laugh, then took the bag and peeked inside. Soup. Bread. A slice of pie.

Wes’s chest tightened. “You didn’t have to.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “I wanted to.”

Wes hated that sentence because it made him feel cared for without letting him argue.

He carried the bag toward the little table, then paused when he noticed Sam wasn’t moving.

Sam’s hand was in his coat pocket.

Wes’s nerves jumped. “What.”

Sam blinked. “What.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “You’re doing the thing where you stand there like you’re about to say something reasonable that will ruin my ability to function.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Okay.”

Wes pointed. “Stop.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Then Sam pulled his hand out of his pocket and held something small in his palm.

A key.

Not a dramatic key ring. Not a shiny new symbol. Just a plain, used key on a small tag.

Wes went still.

Sam’s voice was low and steady, like he was disarming a bomb. “It’s for the bakery.”

Wes stared. “I already have keys.”

Sam nodded. “I know.”

Wes’s throat went tight. “Why.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “Because you shouldn’t have to be the only one who can open your door.”

Wes’s chest hurt.

The old fear rose up fast: *If I give this, I lose control. If I lose control, I lose safety.*

Wes’s fingers flexed at his side like he was fighting the urge to back away.

Sam didn’t push the key closer. He didn’t step forward.

He just held it there, an option, not a demand.

Sam’s voice stayed gentle. “You don’t have to take it today. Or ever.”

Wes swallowed hard.

His brain screamed cinnamon.

His body did something else.

Wes stepped forward and took the key.

It was cold in his palm.

Heavy in a way that wasn’t about metal.

Sam exhaled softly, relief controlled but real.

Wes stared at the key like it might bite him. “This is… a lot.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “Okay. We can call it… practical.”

Wes blinked. “That’s a lie.”

Sam’s mouth tipped slightly. “Yes.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “I’m scared.”

Sam nodded once, no judgment. “I know.”

Wes swallowed. “But I want it.”

Sam’s eyes softened. “Okay.”

Wes rolled his eyes through the sting. “Stop.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “No.”

Wes set the key on the table very carefully, like placing it down too hard would make it real.

Then he sat, rubbed his face, and said the only honest thing he had. “I don’t know how to do this without feeling like I’m about to drop it.”

Sam sat across from him, close but not crowding. “Then we hold it together.”

Wes looked up, eyes bright. “You can’t fix me.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “I’m not trying to fix you. I’m trying to be with you.”

Wes’s breath hitched, and he hated how much that helped.

Wes nodded once, then shoved the soup toward himself like it was a shield. “Fine. Dinner.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Dinner.”

Wes muttered, “Boyfriend.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Yeah.”

Wes swallowed. “Still okay.”

Sam’s voice was quiet and sure. “Yes.”

They ate, the bakery warm around them, the key sitting between them like a small promise that didn’t need to shout.

---

## Chapter 39: Epilogue, Quiet Snow, and the New Normal

A week later, it snowed again.

Not festival snow. Not postcard snow.

Just normal snow that fell because winter did what winter did, without anyone throwing glitter at it.

Wes stood at the front window of Candle & Crumb after closing, watching flakes drift down under the streetlamp.

Sam was behind him, moving through the bakery with calm familiarity, wiping counters, stacking plates, existing in Wes’s space like he’d always belonged there.

Wes still felt the occasional twitch of fear. The old reflex to brace for loss.

But now, the fear didn’t get to drive.

Wes turned away from the window and looked at Sam.

Sam glanced up. “What.”

Wes swallowed, then said quietly, “I’m still not good at this.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Okay.”

Wes glared automatically. “Stop.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes exhaled a laugh, then stepped closer, slow. “But I’m… doing it.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “You are.”

Wes nodded once, then reached out and touched Sam’s sleeve, grounding himself in something real. “Weekend’s in three weeks.”

Sam nodded. “Yeah.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “Don’t let me cancel.”

Sam’s mouth tipped slightly. “I won’t.”

Wes huffed. “Bossy.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Yes.”

Wes’s mouth twitched, then he leaned in and kissed Sam, soft and sure.

When he pulled back, he rested his forehead against Sam’s for a beat.

Wes whispered, almost embarrassed by the simplicity of it, “Stay.”

Sam’s voice was quiet and steady. “I’m staying.”

Outside, snow fell.

Inside, the bakery glowed.

Wes didn’t feel fixed. He didn’t feel cured.

He felt something better.

He felt chosen.

And, finally, like he could choose back without flinching.

**[STOP WRITING]**



## Bonus Epilogue 1: The Cabin Weekend, a Bag of Flour, and Wes Learning to Leave

The second Friday of January arrived like a test.

Wes had been weird all week. Not “snippy” weird. Not “silent punishment” weird.

More like… *hovering over his own life*, as if he could prevent anxiety by pacing around it.

Sam showed up after his shift, duffel in hand, and found Wes behind the counter with three bags of supplies laid out like he was prepping for a long-haul expedition.

Sam stared. “What’s that.”

Wes didn’t look up. “Food.”

Sam’s brows lifted. “We’re going for two nights.”

Wes nodded stiffly. “Yes.”

Sam’s gaze flicked over the items. “Why is there flour.”

Wes’s jaw tightened. “In case.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “In case the cabin has an emergency croissant situation.”

Wes glared. “Don’t.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Okay. Why flour.”

Wes exhaled, annoyed at himself. “Because if I can bake, I can… regulate.”

Sam’s expression softened, no teasing now. “Okay.”

Wes narrowed his eyes automatically. “Stop saying okay like you’re holding a baby bird.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “I am holding a baby bird.”

Wes blinked. “Excuse me.”

Sam stepped closer, voice low and steady. “You’re scared. That’s fine. Bring flour. Bring a whole bakery if you want. But you’re still leaving.”

Wes swallowed, throat tight. “I hate that you can do that.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Do what.”

Wes gestured vaguely. “Say things that make me… move.”

Sam nodded once. “I’m not dragging you. I’m just… here.”

Wes stared at him for a beat, then shoved the flour into a tote bag with unnecessary force. “Fine. Flour comes.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Flour comes.”

Wes pointed at him. “Stop.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

They locked up the bakery together.

Wes did his usual ritual: lights, ovens, locks, the extra lock, the “did I lock it” second check.

Sam waited without rushing him.

When Wes finally turned, jaw set, Sam held out his hand.

Wes hesitated.

Then he took it.

They walked out into the cold.

And Wes did not turn back a third time.

Progress. Disgusting.

---

The cabin was exactly what Sam promised.

Small. Quiet. Anonymous.

No twinkle lights. No Noel. No Hugh’s glitter. No community events involving children as tiny gods.

Just snow, pine trees, and the kind of silence that felt like a soft blanket instead of a threat.

Wes stepped inside, took one look at the simple kitchen, and immediately relaxed by two degrees.

Sam watched him, warm. “Okay.”

Wes glared. “Stop.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “No.”

Wes dropped the tote bag on the table and immediately started unloading. “I’m making soup.”

Sam blinked. “We just arrived.”

Wes nodded. “Soup doesn’t care about your timeline.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Okay.”

Wes snapped, “STOP.”

Sam laughed quietly, then rolled up his sleeves and moved to help without being asked.

Wes noticed. Of course he noticed.

Wes muttered, trying to sound annoyed. “You don’t have to do that.”

Sam’s voice was calm. “I want to.”

Wes’s chest tightened the way it always did when he couldn’t argue his way out of being cared for.

He turned back to the cutting board. “Fine. Chop onions.”

Sam nodded once. “Yes, chef.”

Wes shot him a look. “Don’t call me chef.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Okay, baker.”

Wes huffed a laugh and focused on the soup, because soup was manageable and feelings were not.

---

That night, after dinner, they sat on the couch with a blanket and a mug of tea each.

Outside, snow fell steadily, the world muffled and calm.

Wes stared into his mug like it held instructions for how to be normal.

Sam’s voice was low. “You did it.”

Wes frowned. “Did what.”

Sam nodded toward the window. “Left.”

Wes swallowed. “It’s just a cabin.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “It’s you trusting that the bakery will survive without you for forty-eight hours.”

Wes muttered, “The bakery is ancient. It would outlive me.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “True.”

Wes’s throat tightened anyway. “I almost didn’t come.”

Sam didn’t flinch. “I know.”

Wes swallowed. “I almost made up a reason. A fake oven issue. A sudden need to reorganize the sprinkles.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “I know.”

Wes’s jaw clenched. “And you would’ve let me.”

Sam nodded. “Yes.”

Wes blinked, caught. “Why.”

Sam’s gaze stayed steady. “Because it has to be your choice. I can hold the door open. I can’t carry you through it.”

Wes’s chest ached in a way that felt like relief and fear tangled together.

Wes whispered, rough, “I hate that you’re good at this.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Okay.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “Stop.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes exhaled a laugh, then shifted closer, shoulder brushing Sam’s. “Boyfriend.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “Yeah.”

Wes swallowed. “Still okay.”

Sam’s voice was quiet and sure. “Yes.”

Wes leaned in and kissed him, slow and grounding.

No audience. No arch. No town.

Just warmth and quiet and the sound of snow outside.

After, Wes rested his forehead against Sam’s and breathed.

Wes whispered, small and honest, “This is… nice.”

Sam’s mouth tipped gently. “Yeah.”

Wes swallowed. “I could get used to this.”

Sam’s eyes warmed, soft but careful. “You don’t have to rush.”

Wes huffed. “I’m not rushing. I’m just… admitting.”

Sam nodded. “Okay.”

Wes groaned. “You’re going to say okay until I die.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Yes.”

Wes laughed into his shoulder and let himself be held without bracing.

---

## Bonus Epilogue 2: Back Home, and the Key Gets Used

When they returned to Tinsel Town two days later, the bakery was fine.

The ovens still hummed. The wood still smelled warm. The flour was still exactly where Wes had left it.

The world hadn’t punished him for leaving.

Wes stood in the doorway for a beat, staring like he was waiting for a trapdoor to open.

Sam, behind him, said quietly, “See.”

Wes muttered, “Don’t.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Okay.”

Wes rolled his eyes, then stepped inside and locked up.

An hour later, Wes was in the back kneading dough when the bell chimed at the front door.

Wes’s body tensed on reflex.

Then he heard Sam’s voice, calm: “Hey. We’re closed.”

A voice answered, cheerful. “I know! I’m not here to buy anything. I’m here to deliver gossip!”

Noel.

Wes closed his eyes.

Sam’s voice stayed even. “No.”

Noel gasped like he’d been stabbed. “Officer! Betrayal!”

Wes marched out front, wiping his hands on his apron. “Noel, why are you here.”

Noel beamed. “To confirm you didn’t die in the wilderness.”

Wes deadpanned. “It was a cabin.”

Noel’s eyes sparkled. “A romantic cabin.”

Wes hissed, “Noel.”

Noel ignored him and leaned toward Sam. “Did he bring flour.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Yes.”

Wes snapped, “SAM.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “You did bring flour.”

Wes glared, cheeks hot. “Traitors. Both of you.”

Noel clasped his hands, delighted. “I knew it. The man is healing.”

Wes pointed at Noel. “Get out.”

Noel held up both hands. “I will! I will! One more thing.”

Wes’s patience thinned. “What.”

Noel lowered his voice theatrically. “Mrs. Alden says you looked happy when you came back.”

Wes groaned. “Mrs. Alden is a menace.”

Noel nodded solemnly. “She’s an icon.”

Sam, calm, stepped closer to Wes, shoulder brushing. “You were happy.”

Wes muttered, “Don’t psychoanalyze me.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Not psychoanalyzing. Observing.”

Wes exhaled, annoyed at himself for not denying it harder.

Noel beamed like he’d just won something and finally left, humming.

Wes watched the door close, then turned to Sam. “This town is unbearable.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Yes.”

Wes swallowed. “And… I’m still okay.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “Good.”

Wes muttered, “Stop.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes shook his head and walked back toward the prep counter.

Halfway there, the bell chimed again.

Wes froze.

Sam didn’t.

Sam glanced at Wes, then calmly took the key off the hook by the counter, moved to the front, and locked the door properly.

A small, ordinary action.

The key being used like it belonged.

Wes’s throat tightened hard enough it almost hurt.

Sam came back, steady. “There.”

Wes stared at him for a beat, then forced out, rough and real, “That did something to my brain.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “Yeah.”

Wes swallowed. “Still okay.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Yes.”

Wes stepped forward and kissed him once, quick and sure.

Then he went back to the dough, shoulders lighter than they had any right to be.

Because this wasn’t just a holiday fever dream anymore.

It was… a new normal.

And Wes, somehow, was letting it happen.

**[STOP WRITING]**

## Bonus Epilogue 2: Back Home, and the Key Gets Used
