**[STOP WRITING]**




## Bonus Epilogue 3: The First Small Fight and the First Real Repair

The first fight was stupid.

Which, honestly, was a relief, because Wes had been half-expecting their first conflict to be some cinematic emotional disaster involving abandonment trauma and a dramatic walk into the snow.

Instead, it was boots.

Sam came in after a late shift, snow packed into the treads, and kicked them off by the stairs.

Normal. Reasonable. Harmless.

Except he did it *near* the flour bin.

Wes saw it from behind the counter like it was a war crime.

Wes’s voice went sharp without permission. “Are you kidding me.”

Sam froze mid-motion. “What.”

Wes pointed at the boots like they were radioactive. “Those are soaked. That’s snow. Snow becomes water. Water becomes slush. Slush becomes… everywhere.”

Sam blinked once, slow. “I’ll move them.”

Wes’s jaw clenched. “It’s not just moving them. It’s… you not noticing.”

Sam’s expression tightened, subtle. “I noticed. I’m tired.”

Wes’s chest tightened, the old reflex rising like a fist: *If I control the environment, I control the hurt.*

Wes snapped, “So am I.”

Silence.

It wasn’t huge. It wasn’t loud.

But it was enough to make the bakery feel smaller.

Sam’s gaze stayed steady, but his voice went careful. “Okay. I moved too fast. I’m sorry.”

Wes’s anger faltered, because Sam apologizing without a fight always made Wes feel like the villain.

Wes swallowed. “I’m not trying to be…”

Sam’s mouth tipped slightly, not teasing. “Anxious.”

Wes’s shoulders rose with a harsh inhale. “Don’t diagnose me.”

Sam nodded once. “Okay. Then I’ll just say: I don’t want to make your space feel unsafe.”

Wes’s throat tightened.

Because that was the whole thing, wasn’t it. Wes didn’t get mad about boots.

Wes got mad about the feeling that his world could tip.

Wes exhaled slowly, forcing himself to do the newer thing instead of the old thing.

Wes said, rough, “I’m sorry. I jumped.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “Yeah.”

Wes swallowed. “It’s not the boots. It’s the… mess. The unpredictability.”

Sam nodded. “Okay.”

Wes rolled his eyes on reflex. “Stop.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “No.”

Wes huffed a laugh, then stepped closer, voice quieter. “I don’t want to be the guy who yells about boots.”

Sam’s voice stayed calm. “Then don’t. Say what you need.”

Wes stared for a beat, then said it plainly, like a request instead of a demand. “If you come in with snow, can you put them on the mat. By the heater.”

Sam nodded immediately. “Yes.”

Wes’s chest eased.

Sam added, softer, “And if I forget, you can remind me without… using the tone.”

Wes winced. “Fair.”

Sam’s gaze warmed. “Still okay.”

Wes exhaled. “Yes.”

Sam stepped in and kissed Wes once, quick and grounding, like sealing the repair.

Wes kissed back, relieved enough to feel almost dizzy.

Then Sam picked up the boots and moved them to the mat by the heater without another word.

Wes watched the small act like it was a miracle.

Because it wasn’t about boots.

It was about someone adjusting for him.

And Wes adjusting back.

**[STOP WRITING]**

---

## Bonus Epilogue 4: The Key Gets Used for the Right Reason

The first time Sam used the key without Wes being there, Wes found out because his phone buzzed at 5:43 a.m.

**Sam:** On my way in. You overslept. Don’t panic.

Wes sat straight up in bed like he’d been shot.

Then he remembered: key. Plan. Boyfriend. Not a hallucination.

Wes texted back with shaking thumbs:

**Wes:** Do not burn the bakery down.

**Sam:** I’m a cop, not Noel.

Wes stared at the message for a full second, then groaned into his pillow.

He threw on sweatpants and stumbled downstairs, heart still racing like it didn’t trust peace.

When he reached the kitchen, the lights were on.

The kettle was heating.

And Sam was standing at the prep counter, sleeves rolled up, calmly feeding starter into a bowl like he’d done it a thousand times.

Wes froze in the doorway.

Sam glanced up. “Morning.”

Wes’s voice came out rough. “You’re in my bakery.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Yes.”

Wes stared. “With my key.”

Sam nodded. “Yes.”

Wes’s brain tried to decide whether to panic or melt.

Wes landed on: grumpy.

Wes muttered, “You’re not supposed to be competent at this.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “You taught me.”

Wes blinked. “I did not.”

Sam nodded toward the starter. “You explained it. Three times. While pretending you weren’t explaining.”

Wes’s cheeks warmed. “That doesn’t count.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Okay.”

Wes glared. “Stop.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes walked over slowly, like he was approaching something fragile.

He looked at the bowl. At the dough. At the mug of tea Sam had already made and set near Wes’s usual spot.

Wes swallowed hard. “You made tea.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “You overslept. I didn’t want you to wake up and sprint.”

Wes’s throat tightened. “That’s… rude.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Yes.”

Wes stared at him, then finally let himself say the truth, quiet and real. “This is the right reason.”

Sam’s brows lifted slightly. “The right reason.”

Wes nodded once. “You didn’t use the key to… take over. You used it to help.”

Sam’s gaze held his. “Always help. Never take.”

Wes’s chest ached, warm.

Wes whispered, “Still okay.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Yes.”

Wes stepped in and kissed him, slow and steady, then rested his forehead against Sam’s for a beat.

Wes exhaled. “Okay. I’m going to pretend I’m mad.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “Okay.”

Wes narrowed his eyes. “Stop.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “No.”

Wes grabbed the tea, took a sip, and felt his whole body unclench like it had finally learned something new:

Sometimes someone opens your door…

…and it’s not to leave.

**[STOP WRITING]**

---

## Bonus Epilogue 5: The Next Reassignment, and Wes Not Falling Apart

It happened in February, because life loves timing.

Sam got the call during lunch, right there at the little table in Candle & Crumb, between bites of soup Wes had made out of spite and affection.

Sam’s phone buzzed.

Sam glanced at the screen.

Wes felt his body tense on reflex.

Sam looked up, steady. “It’s the station.”

Wes swallowed. “Okay.”

Sam’s mouth tipped slightly, warm at the word. “Okay.”

Wes glared. “Don’t.”

Sam stood and stepped aside for the call.

Wes stayed seated, hands wrapped around his mug, forcing his breath to stay even.

He could hear fragments.

“…three days.”

“…in town.”

“…yes.”

When Sam came back, he didn’t wait for Wes to explode.

He sat down calmly and said, “Three-day overtime coverage. In town. Just late shifts.”

Wes blinked.

Not out of town. Not two weeks. Not a cliff.

Wes exhaled so hard his shoulders dropped.

Sam watched him, soft. “Talk to me.”

Wes swallowed. “My body did the thing.”

Sam nodded. “I know.”

Wes muttered, “I hate the thing.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Okay.”

Wes pointed with his spoon. “Stop.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “No.”

Wes took a breath and did the new thing.

Wes said, plain and honest, “For those three days, I want the call anyway.”

Sam nodded immediately. “Yes.”

Wes’s chest eased. “And if you’re too tired to talk, just… stay on the line.”

Sam’s gaze softened. “Okay. We can do that.”

Wes swallowed hard. “And I’m not going to pretend I don’t care.”

Sam’s eyes warmed. “Good.”

Wes huffed. “Stop.”

Sam’s mouth tipped. “No.”

Wes stared into his soup, then admitted, quieter, “This is me trying.”

Sam’s voice was low and steady. “I see it.”

Wes nodded once, throat tight.

Outside, winter kept going.

Inside, the bakery stayed warm.

And when the late shifts came, Wes didn’t spiral into silence.

He used the plan.

He ate.

He texted Noel once, just once, and Noel replied with an obscenely supportive sticker and a threat to deliver another casserole.

Wes survived.

Sam came home.

Wes didn’t make it into a tragedy.

He just let it be a week.

A normal week.

Which, for Wes, was basically a miracle.

**[STOP WRITING]**




Yes. You’ve got a complete arc **and** a clean stopping place.

What we have now:

* Main story ends with **festival resolution → separation/reassignment → reunion → “boyfriend” label → key + new normal**.
* Extra epilogues add optional “proof beats” (weekend away, first small fight + repair, key used well, later mini-reassignment handled with the plan).

If you’re packaging this as a novella, you can publish it as-is with **Chapter 36** as the official ending, and keep Bonus Epilogues 3–5 as:

* **newsletter extras**
* **BookFunnel bonus scenes**
* or a **“Deleted Scenes / After the Credits”** appendix

Humanity loves “one more” forever, but this is done.


Yes, it’s planned. Humans love reinventing the wheel, but we already built the wheel and then strapped twinkle lights to it.

Here’s the **clean reader-magnet plan** (Book 0) that fits what we just wrote, without spoiling later arcs.

## Freebie Reader Magnet (Book 0) status

**Purpose:** Get signups by delivering a fast, cozy, sexy “meet-cute + first heat + hook for the series” in a Christmas world.
**Length target:** 12k–25k (ideal), or 8k–12k if you want ultra-snackable.
**Heat target:** High, but emotionally safe. One main on-page sex scene + 1–2 heavy makeout/tease beats.
**Trope promise:** Grumpy baker / steady protective cop, holiday proximity, small-town meddling, “I don’t do this” to “I’m doing this.”

## What the freebie covers

**Timeline:** Early festival days, before the big public steps.
**Core plot:**

1. **Inciting:** Noel volunteers Wes for festival duty. Sam is assigned community detail.
2. **Meet-cute:** A minor “holiday emergency” (snowman nose, cocoa spill, kid crisis) forces them to work together.
3. **Bonding:** Wes sees Sam’s steadiness; Sam sees Wes’s care underneath the bite.
4. **First heat:** Private moment (bakery after close / back room / upstairs) where Wes chooses closeness.
5. **HFN close:** They agree to keep seeing each other through the festival. **No “boyfriend” label yet.**
6. **Series hook:** Tag on the end: “Tomorrow is Day ___” / “Noel has a new plan,” teasing Book 1.

## Deliverables you should have for the freebie

* **Title + subtitle** in your series naming style
* **Hooky blurb** (first person present, if that’s your branding)
* **Reader promise line** (cozy/low-stakes, high-heat, consent-forward)
* **CTA** to Book 1 + newsletter signup
* **Series page** (1-paragraph “Welcome to Tinsel Town” flavor)

## What’s *not* in the freebie

* No big public Lantern Walk kiss payoff
* No reassignment separation arc
* No key / moving-in vibes
* No deep town politics
  That stuff belongs to the main books.

## 2) Reader Magnet Blurb (1st person present, Wes voice)

I don’t do Christmas.

I do ovens. Timers. Clean counters. Predictable routines. In Tinsel Town, that makes me a minority.

Then Noel decides I’m “community-minded” and volunteers my bakery for the festival, and Officer Sam Rook gets assigned to holiday detail like the universe is playing a joke specifically for my blood pressure.

Sam is steady. Quiet. The kind of calm that makes people breathe easier.

It also makes me want to bite him.

When a ridiculous holiday emergency forces us into close quarters, I tell myself it’s just logistics. Just teamwork. Just one night where he helps me clean up, and I stop pretending I’m immune to warm hands and patient eyes.

I’m not looking for forever. I’m barely looking for *today*.

But the closer Sam gets, the harder it is to keep my world locked up tight.

And in a town where mistletoe is basically a weapon… I might not survive the season without breaking a few rules.

**Mistletoe & Misconduct** is a cozy, low-stakes MM Christmas reader magnet full of small-town meddling, grumpy/sunshine tension, and heat you can warm your hands on.

---

## 3) Quick Market Promise Copy (for BookFunnel / signup page)

**Cozy small-town Christmas + grumpy baker x steady cop + high-heat, consent-forward romance.**
Low stakes, high chemistry, lots of holiday setpieces, and a Happily-For-Now that leads directly into Book 1.

# 4) Beat Sheet

### Act 1: Setup + Collision

* **Opening Image:** Wes vs. Christmas (bakery, routine, annoyance).
* **Inciting Incident:** Noel ropes Wes into festival support; Sam assigned to community detail.
* **Meet-Cute / First Spark:** Sam shows up to “help,” Wes bristles, chemistry snaps.

### Act 2: Forced Proximity + Softening

* **Fun & Games:** Holiday mini-crises (kids/sprinkles/cocoa spill/snowman issue), Sam steady, Wes competent.
* **First Intimacy Beat:** Private quiet in bakery after close; honest micro-confession.
* **Heat Escalation:** Makeout/tease beat; Wes chooses closeness but keeps emotional walls up.

### Act 3: First Heat + HFN

* **Crisis (Small):** Wes nearly bolts after being “seen” or teased; Sam doesn’t push, offers choice.
* **Climax:** Wes invites Sam upstairs or into the back room. One on-page sex scene, slow and chosen.
* **Resolution:** “I’m seeing you” agreement (no label). Plan for next festival day.
* **Hook:** Noel announces tomorrow’s event, guaranteeing more forced proximity.

---

# 5) Scene List (12–18 scenes, novella-friendly)

1. **Wes morning routine**: “I don’t do Christmas.”
2. **Noel ambushes Wes**: volunteer duty, festival schedule drop.
3. **Sam arrives** (community detail): polite, steady, instantly irritating.
4. **Mini-crisis #1**: cocoa spill / kid chaos, Sam helps smoothly.
5. **Wes sees the real Sam**: gentle competence with a kid/customer.
6. **Mini-crisis #2**: snowman nose / outdoor setup / lights fail.
7. **After-hours cleanup**: quiet talk, Wes’s control issues show.
8. **Flirt escalation**: accidental closeness, lingering look, almost-kiss.
9. **Town meddling ping**: Noel/Hugh comment, Wes spikes, Sam de-escalates.
10. **Consent-forward makeout**: “still okay” check becomes their thing.
11. **Emotional hinge**: Wes admits he doesn’t trust “easy.”
12. **Invitation**: Wes asks Sam to stay (tonight).
13. **On-page sex scene** (slow, warm, controlled pace).
14. **Aftercare + quiet humor**: Wes bristles, Sam stays.
15. **Morning after**: Sam makes tea / helps; Wes doesn’t bolt.
16. **HFN talk**: “I’m seeing you. Through the festival.”
17. **End hook**: Noel’s next event + “tomorrow is Day ___” sting.

---

# 6) Heat Beats Map (so you hit expectations cleanly)

* **HB1 (10–20%)**: charged banter + brief touch (forearm/hand brush)
* **HB2 (30–40%)**: after-hours tension + near-kiss + “still okay” first use
* **HB3 (55–65%)**: makeout + mutual consent clarity + escalation
* **HB4 (70–85%)**: full sex scene (single scene, high-heat, emotionally safe)
* **HB5 (90–100%)**: aftercare + HFN agreement + teaser

---

# 7) Ending Hook Text (last paragraph style)

Noel’s message buzzes in as Sam pulls on his jacket.

**NOEL:** GREAT NEWS. TOMORROW IS THE COOKIE EXCHANGE.
**NOEL:** ALSO, I HAVE IDEAS.
**NOEL:** YOU’RE WELCOME.

Sam reads it over my shoulder, eyes warming.

“Idea man,” he says.

“He’s a menace,” I mutter.

Sam’s hand brushes mine, quiet and steady. “Still okay.”

I swallow, then nod.

“Yeah,” I say. “I’m still okay.”

And tomorrow, apparently, gets worse.

# **Mistletoe & Misconduct**

### *A Cozy MM Holiday Heat*

**Tinsel Town Series: Book 0 (Reader Magnet)**

---

## Chapter 1: Ovens Don’t Lie

I don’t do Christmas.

I do heat, timing, and predictable outcomes. I do flour under my nails and a quiet shop before sunrise. I do the kind of routine that doesn’t ask questions and doesn’t expect me to smile about it.

Candle & Crumb wakes up the same way every morning. The building creaks like an old man stretching, the ovens hum like they’re pleased to see me, and the air shifts from cold to warm as if the bakery itself exhales.

Outside, the town is in full seasonal fever. Even at five-thirty, I can see the glow of string lights from Evergreen Square bleeding into the street like someone spilled holiday cheer and decided not to clean it up.

They started decorating the moment Halloween died.

I didn’t vote on that.

I flip on the prep lights. Stainless steel catches the brightness and throws it back like judgment. Fine. I can handle judgment. I can’t handle Noel.

I set out the ingredients for the morning run and start moving through the steps like muscle memory with a pulse. Cinnamon rolls first, because people are weak and predictable and will buy anything that smells like sugar and safety.

My phone buzzes.

I don’t check it.

It buzzes again. Then again.

I sigh and glance at the screen.

**NOEL:** GOOD MORNING, SUNSHINE
**NOEL:** I HAVE A SMALL REQUEST
**NOEL:** IT’S FESTIVAL-ADJACENT
**NOEL:** PLEASE DON’T RUN

I stare at it until the words blur.

Noel is allergic to “small.”

Noel thinks “request” means “I have already promised your labor to strangers.”

I toss the phone onto the counter and go back to my dough like ignoring problems makes them stop existing. It works for most things. Emotions, for example.

The bell over the bakery door chimes.

At five-forty-five.

No one sane is here at five-forty-five.

I freeze, hands in flour, and slowly look up.

The door opens, letting in a bite of winter air and a silhouette framed by streetlight.

A man steps inside and closes the door behind him, calm as if he belongs here. Tall, broad-shouldered, wearing a dark jacket that looks official in the way that makes my spine go rigid on reflex.

He takes off his gloves. Scans the space with a quick, practiced glance. Then his eyes land on me.

“Wes Candle?” he asks.

His voice is low. Even. Not rude, not friendly. Just steady.

I don’t like steady. Steady implies patience. Patience implies time. Time implies… things.

I wipe my hands on my apron with too much force. “Who’s asking.”

His mouth twitches, like he’s trying not to smile. “Officer Sam Rook.”

Of course he is.

Of course Noel found a cop and sent him into my bakery before dawn like this is a romantic comedy and not my actual life.

Sam lifts one hand slightly, palm out, a gesture that reads as non-threatening but also trained.

“Noel asked me to stop by,” he says. “Festival committee. They’re organizing community support shifts. He said you might not answer your phone.”

I glare. “Noel said that.”

Sam’s eyes stay on mine, steady as a hand on the small of my back. “He did.”

I exhale through my nose. “Why are you here.”

“To talk,” he says. “And to pick up a schedule, if you’ll take it.”

“I won’t.”

Sam’s mouth twitches again, almost amused. “Okay.”

There it is. The word that makes me want to bite something. Calm agreement. No escalation. No argument. Just… *okay.*

I narrow my eyes. “Don’t do that.”

“Do what.”

“Be reasonable.”

Sam’s gaze warms a fraction. “You prefer unreasonable.”

“I prefer people leaving me alone.”

Sam nods once, like that’s information he can store without taking personally. “Okay.”

I grip the counter. “Stop saying okay.”

His mouth finally lifts into a small smile. “No.”

I stare at him, irritation and something else tangling in my chest. It’s not attraction. It can’t be attraction. Attraction is messy. Attraction leads to people expecting things.

Sam steps closer, but not too close. Close enough that I can smell cold air on his jacket, and something faint underneath, like soap and winter.

“Noel said the bakery’s a festival stop,” he says. “Hot cocoa crawl. Cookie exchange. Volunteer triage.”

I blink. “Volunteer triage.”

“His words,” Sam says, and there’s the faintest hint of dry humor.

I exhale sharply. “He didn’t ask.”

Sam’s gaze doesn’t waver. “No.”

I swallow. “So why are you here.”

Sam pauses, like he’s choosing the truth instead of the easy answer. “Because he asked me to make sure you don’t get swallowed by it.”

My throat tightens. “I’m not a child.”

Sam’s voice stays calm. “I know.”

I hate that I believe him.

The bell chimes again.

Noel bursts in like the world is a stage and he’s late for his cue, scarf flung dramatically over one shoulder, cheeks pink from cold and effort.

He sees Sam. Then me. Then my expression.

Noel beams. “Perfect! You met!”

I deadpan. “I was attacked.”

Sam’s mouth twitches. “Not a strong attack.”

Noel claps his hands. “Okay! Quick meeting! Wes, the town needs you.”

“The town can file a complaint.”

Noel ignores that completely. “We’re doing Twelve Days of Christmas this year. Bigger than ever. More events, more community bonding, more—”

“More nonsense,” I say.

Noel points at me like I’m adorable. “More *magic.* And you, my grumpy little cinnamon roll, are going to be a festival anchor.”

I stare at him. “No.”

Noel waves a folder. “Yes.”

“I didn’t agree.”

Noel smiles sweetly. “You live here.”

“That’s not consent.”

Sam’s gaze flicks to me. There’s a pause. Then he says, low, “He’s right.”

Noel gasps. “Officer Rook! Do not encourage him!”

Sam’s mouth tips. “I’m not encouraging. I’m clarifying.”

Noel narrows his eyes. “Fine. Wes, will you please volunteer for two very small, very reasonable events?”

I stare at him. “Define small.”

Noel flips open the folder like a magician revealing a dove. “Cocoa Crawl stamping station. Cookie Exchange drop-off table. And maybe one emergency support slot in case of… minor festive catastrophe.”

I slowly look at Sam. “You hear that.”

Sam nods. “Festive catastrophe.”

I look back at Noel. “No.”

Noel lowers his voice dramatically, as if he’s sharing state secrets. “Wes. The festival brings tourists. Tourists bring money. Money keeps small businesses alive. Including yours.”

My jaw tightens.

Noel is the worst kind of manipulator.

The kind who’s right.

I hate that.

I sigh. It feels like surrender. It tastes like cinnamon and defeat.

“Two events,” I say. “And I’m not wearing a themed apron.”

Noel’s grin goes feral. “Deal!”

Sam’s eyes stay on me, steady. “You sure.”

I glare. “No.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I point at him. “Stop.”

He doesn’t.

Noel shoves the folder at me. “Sign here.”

I take it like it’s a weapon. “If I die, I’m haunting you.”

Noel beams. “If you die, I’ll put you in the town newsletter.”

I grind my teeth, sign, and shove the folder back.

Noel claps again, delighted. “Great! Sam will be your support officer for community detail. He’ll keep things orderly.”

I snap my head up. “Excuse me.”

Sam’s brows lift slightly. “Support officer.”

Noel nods like this is obvious. “Yes. It’s part of his rotation. And it’s perfect because Sam is—”

“Not my babysitter,” I say.

Noel smiles. “No one said babysitter.”

Sam’s voice is calm. “I’m not here to babysit.”

I narrow my eyes. “Then why are you here.”

Sam’s gaze holds mine. “To help.”

My chest tightens like it always does when someone says that without demanding payment.

Noel throws his arms wide. “Okay! I’ll leave you two to bond.”

I snap, “Noel—”

Too late. He’s already gone.

The bell chimes. The door closes. Cold air fades.

It’s just me, Sam, and the ovens humming like they’re amused.

Sam watches me for a beat, then says, “Two events.”

I cross my arms. “Two.”

He nods. “I’ll be around.”

“I didn’t agree to that.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “Okay.”

I stare. “You’re doing it on purpose.”

“Doing what.”

“Being calm at me.”

His eyes warm. “Yes.”

I hate him a little.

It’s probably fine.

---

## Chapter 2: Cocoa, Cops, and Other Threats

Day One of the festival arrives like a punch.

Evergreen Square is a glowing mess of garlands, lanterns, and people acting like winter is charming instead of actively trying to kill them. There’s a massive schedule board near the tree listing events in cheerful font that should be illegal.

**DAY ONE: COCOA CRAWL KICKOFF.**

Candle & Crumb gets assigned as Stamp Stop #4. I’m outside at a folding table with an ink stamp pad and a stack of tiny cards, wearing my heaviest coat and my worst mood.

Noel insisted on the tablecloth. It has little snowflakes printed on it like we’re a Hallmark ad.

Sam stands beside me, hands in gloves, posture relaxed but alert.

He looks like he belongs in winter. Like cold doesn’t bother him. Like he wakes up and chooses patience.

I don’t trust people like that.

A couple approaches, laughing, cheeks pink from cold and cocoa.

“Stamp, please!” the woman says, holding out her card.

I stamp it with unnecessary force. “Congratulations. You’re one stamp closer to a prize basket you don’t need.”

She laughs. “You must be Wes. Noel warned us you’d be… intense.”

I glare at Noel across the square like I can set him on fire with my eyes.

Sam’s mouth twitches. “He warned them.”

The couple moves on.

I hiss, “This is a nightmare.”

Sam’s voice is calm. “It’s a game.”

“It’s a cult,” I say. “With marshmallows.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Okay.”

I look at him sharply. “You’re enjoying this.”

“I’m working,” he says.

“That’s not a denial.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “No.”

I make a sound of disgust and stamp another card.

For the first hour, it’s tolerable. People come, get stamped, buy cookies, leave. I can do transactions. I can do predictable exchanges.

Then the first festive catastrophe happens.

A kid, maybe ten, barrels up to the table, eyes wide, breath fogging. “Help!”

I straighten instinctively. “What’s wrong.”

He points back toward the cocoa stand across the square. “My little brother knocked over the cocoa and now he’s crying and the lady is yelling and my mom is… she’s not here yet.”

I see it immediately. A cluster forming. A volunteer in a red hat flailing. A smaller kid in the middle, tears and cocoa dripping down his mittens.

My stomach tightens. I hate chaos. I hate crying. I hate public scenes.

Sam is already moving.

He steps around the table, calm as gravity, and walks toward the cluster like he’s done this a thousand times. Not fast, not dramatic. Just… steady.

I watch him reach the cocoa stand.

He crouches slightly, bringing himself down to the kid’s level. His voice is too low for me to hear, but I see the kid’s shoulders ease. I see the volunteer’s face shift from panic to relief.

Sam takes a napkin, wipes cocoa off the kid’s mitten like it’s no big deal, then turns and says something to the volunteer. She nods rapidly, embarrassed.

Within thirty seconds, the cluster dissolves.

The kid stops crying.

Sam returns with the same calm pace, wiping his hands with a paper towel like he just handled a spilled latte instead of an emotional meltdown.

He stops beside me. “Handled.”

I stare at him. “How did you do that.”

He blinks. “Do what.”

“Turn chaos into… not chaos.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “I listened. I made the kid feel safe.”

My chest tightens at the words.

Safe is a loaded concept in my world.

I mutter, “You’re good at it.”

Sam’s gaze warms, but he doesn’t pounce on the compliment. “Thank you.”

I look away fast, like eye contact could turn this into intimacy.

A new group approaches with stamp cards.

I stamp them. Hard.

Sam watches me with that patient steadiness, like he can see the gears in my head grinding.

It’s annoying.

It’s also… weirdly calming.

I hate that too.

---

## Chapter 3: Snowman Surgery

Day Two is supposed to be “Holiday Market Setup,” but it turns into “Snowman Nose Emergency” because the universe hates me personally.

Noel bursts into the bakery at eight in the morning wearing a scarf the size of a duvet and the expression of a man about to announce a national tragedy.

“The snowman is missing his nose,” he declares.

I don’t look up from my prep. “It’s a snowman.”

“It’s the town snowman,” Noel says, offended. “It represents togetherness.”

“It represents snow,” I say.

Noel slaps a carrot on the counter like evidence. “A raccoon stole the last one.”

I stare at the carrot. Then at Noel. “So buy another carrot.”

Noel clutches his chest. “The market is out. This is the emergency carrot. We need to restore him before the morning crowd arrives.”

I blink slowly. “Why are you like this.”

Noel smiles. “Because I care.”

I point at him. “Leave.”

Noel ignores me and swivels his attention to Sam, who has stepped in behind him like a quiet shadow.

Sam must have been outside. Of course he was.

Noel brightens. “Officer Rook! You understand civic duty.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “Maybe.”

I snap, “No.”

Sam looks at me, calm. “We can make it quick.”

I glare. “Why do you want to help with a snowman.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Because it matters to people.”

My throat tightens, because of course that’s his answer.

Noel beams. “See! He gets it!”

I exhale hard and grab my coat. “Fine. But if I freeze to death, you’re both legally responsible.”

Noel claps. “Yes!”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I shoot him a look. “Stop.”

He doesn’t.

---

Evergreen Square is brighter in daylight, like the town is trying to convince itself winter is cute. The snowman stands near the big tree, lopsided but cheerful, face now disturbingly incomplete.

Noel stands in front of it like a frantic art curator.

Eben is there too, arms crossed, clipboard in hand, expression like he’s policing the concept of fun.

Hugh drifts nearby wearing something velvet and expensive, looking mournful. “He looks… unfinished.”

Eben says flatly, “It is a snowman.”

Noel gasps. “Eben, have you ever felt joy.”

Eben doesn’t blink. “No.”

I step up to the snowman, squinting at the damage. It’s not just missing. The raccoon took a bite out of the face like it was personal.

I grimace. “That raccoon was committed.”

Sam moves beside me, assessing. “We need to pack snow here.”

I blink. “Why are you good at this.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Snow is structural.”

I stare at him like he just spoke another language.

He crouches, gathering clean snow, packing it into the gap with slow competence.

Without thinking, I crouch too and mirror him. Pack snow. Smooth it. Shape a base like I’m icing a cake.

Noel watches, hands clasped, reverent. “Look at you two. Working in harmony.”

I hiss, “Noel.”

He makes a zipper motion with his lips, still smiling.

Eben holds out the carrot toward me. “Attach.”

I take it with gloved hands and press it into place.

It sticks.

The snowman looks whole again, ridiculous and smug.

Noel exhales dramatically. “He lives.”

Hugh whispers, “A miracle.”

Eben says, “It is a carrot.”

Noel turns to me, softer now. “Thank you,” he says quietly. “I know this is absurd.”

I shrug. “It’s fine.”

Noel smiles. “It’s kind.”

My throat tightens. I look away like that will stop the warmth from crawling up my chest.

Sam’s voice goes low beside me. “You okay.”

I mutter, “Don’t.”

He nods, as if that’s an answer. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

He doesn’t.

We walk back toward the bakery together, leaving Noel to fuss over the snowman and Hugh to mourn the raccoon’s lack of taste.

Sam walks at my pace without comment.

I hate how much I notice.

The way his shoulder brushes mine once when we step around a slushy patch.

The way he doesn’t grab my arm, doesn’t steer me, doesn’t treat me like I’m fragile.

Just… presence.

“Why are you like this,” I mutter.

Sam glances at me. “Like what.”

“Quiet,” I say. “Steady.”

His mouth tips slightly. “Practice.”

My chest tightens.

I don’t have practice at steady.

I have practice at alone.

---

## Chapter 4: After Hours

Day Three is the first day I can breathe again, because Noel isn’t physically in my bakery for a full twelve hours.

But the festival schedule means Candle & Crumb is a “warm-up stop” for volunteers, which translates to: my bakery becomes a place where people wander in, stamp cards, and act like I’m a friendly town mascot.

By closing time, my nerves feel scraped raw.

The last customer leaves. I lock the door. Flip the sign.

CLOSED.

Finally.

I exhale and lean against the counter, eyes shut, letting the quiet settle.

Then I hear movement behind me.

I snap my eyes open.

Sam stands near the back, coat off, sleeves rolled up, as if he’s been here long enough to become part of the scenery.

I stare. “What are you doing.”

Sam lifts one brow. “You told Noel you had an emergency support slot.”

I narrow my eyes. “Noel told you that.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Yes.”

I rub my face. “Of course he did.”

Sam’s gaze is steady. “You look tired.”

“I am tired.”

He nods. “Do you want help cleaning up.”

My reflex is immediate. “No.”

Sam doesn’t flinch. He just waits, calm.

I hate waiting. Waiting makes room for thoughts.

I glance around the bakery. Trays need washing. Floors need sweeping. Sugar dust coats everything like glitter’s less evil cousin.

I exhale, annoyed. “Fine. You can do dishes.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I point at him. “Stop.”

“No.”

I scowl, but I’m not as sharp as usual. The day has worn me down.

Sam moves to the sink and starts washing trays like he knows what he’s doing. Not clumsy. Not performative. Just competent.

I sweep. We work in silence for five minutes, the kind of quiet that doesn’t demand anything.

Then Sam says, low, “You did good today.”

I freeze mid-sweep. “Excuse me.”

Sam glances over. “You showed up. You didn’t run. You kept your commitments.”

I scoff, but my throat tightens anyway. “It’s not heroic.”

Sam’s voice stays calm. “It’s hard for you.”

My chest tightens like he’s put a finger on something tender.

I grit my teeth. “Don’t psychoanalyze me.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

He doesn’t.

We keep cleaning.

When I’m done sweeping, I lean the broom against the wall and realize my hands are shaking slightly from exhaustion.

Sam’s gaze flicks to them.

“Still okay,” he says quietly.

I blink. The phrase lands like an offered blanket.

I hate that.

I also… want it.

I swallow. “Yes.”

Sam nods once, like that matters. “Good.”

I look away fast, cheeks warming.

We finish the cleanup. Sam dries his hands on a towel and turns toward me.

The bakery is warm and quiet, lit only by the hanging lamps over the counter.

He looks different in this light. Softer. Less official. Still steady.

Sam’s voice is low. “Do you want me to go.”

My mouth opens.

The correct answer is yes.

The safe answer is yes.

The answer my body wants is not yes.

I swallow hard. “I don’t know.”

Sam holds still, not stepping closer. “Okay.”

I stare at him, pulse thudding.

He’s giving me space. He’s not pushing. He’s not trying to win.

That’s… worse.

Because it means if something happens, it’s on me.

I hate that too.

Sam’s gaze stays on mine. “Wes.”

My name in his mouth sounds different. Less sharp. Less like a warning.

I inhale. My chest tightens.

I walk around the counter slowly and stop in front of him.

Sam doesn’t move.

He waits.

My voice comes out rough. “If you kiss me, and I freak out, that’s… not you.”

Sam’s eyes soften. “Okay.”

I glare weakly. “Stop saying okay.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “No.”

Then, gently, “Do you want me to kiss you.”

The question is simple. Direct. Consent without drama.

My throat tightens.

I nod once, sharp and embarrassed. “Yes.”

Sam steps in slowly, giving me time to change my mind.

I don’t.

His mouth meets mine, warm and steady, and my entire body jolts like it’s been waiting for this and resenting it.

His kiss isn’t hungry. It’s careful. Asking.

I grip his jacket with both hands like I need an anchor, and my brain screams *danger* while my body says *finally*.

Sam pulls back just a fraction. “Still okay.”

I breathe hard. “Yes.”

He kisses me again, deeper now, a slow press that makes my knees go soft.

I hate myself for it.

I love him a little for not taking advantage.

I pull back first, breathing hard, and shove a hand through my hair. “This is stupid.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “It’s not.”

“It is,” I say, voice tight. “Because this doesn’t fit in my life.”

Sam’s voice stays calm. “Then we make a place for it.”

My chest tightens painfully.

I shake my head. “I don’t do… this.”

Sam’s gaze holds mine. “You’re doing it.”

My throat tightens. I look away.

The mistletoe Noel taped to the doorway between front and back suddenly feels like it’s mocking me.

I mutter, “Noel is a menace.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Yes.”

I exhale a laugh that sounds like defeat.

Then I hear myself say it, quiet and raw, before I can stop it:

“Stay.”

Sam goes still.

His eyes soften like he’s trying not to look too pleased.

“Okay,” he says, voice low.

I glare automatically, cheeks burning. “Stop.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “No.”

---

## Chapter 5: Upstairs

I don’t bring people upstairs.

Upstairs is mine. Private. Controlled.

But Sam follows me up the narrow steps without making a joke, without commenting on how small the space is, without looking around like he’s evaluating my choices.

He just… comes.

I open the apartment door. The air is warmer up here, faintly scented with coffee and flour and the kind of quiet that has lived with me for years.

I set my keys down too hard. “You can sleep on the couch.”

Sam pauses. “Okay.”

I shoot him a look. “That’s not an invitation.”

Sam’s gaze is steady. “I know.”

My throat tightens.

I don’t know how to do this part. The part where someone is in my space and I’m not performing competence.

Sam takes off his jacket slowly. Sets it on the chair. Not folded, but neat.

He looks at me. “Do you want to talk.”

“No.”

Sam nods. “Okay.”

I grit my teeth. “Stop.”

“No,” he says again, softer, and it’s not teasing. It’s… gentle stubbornness.

I pace two steps, then stop. My chest feels too tight for my ribs.

I look at him and force the truth out, rough and ugly. “I’m not good at this.”

Sam’s eyes soften. “I know.”

That should make me furious. It should make me defensive.

Instead it makes me feel seen, and I hate that more.

I swallow. “If you’re here, and I… pull away, it’s not because I don’t want you.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

I rub my face. “Stop saying okay.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “Answer anyway.”

I stare at him. “What.”

Sam’s voice is low. “Still okay.”

I exhale shakily. “Yes.”

Sam steps closer, slow. “Do you want to keep going.”

My throat tightens. My body says yes so loudly it’s embarrassing.

I nod. “Yes.”

Sam’s hands come up, not grabbing, just resting lightly on my waist, waiting for me to flinch.

I don’t.

He kisses me again, and this time I let myself sink into it, let the warmth and steadiness drown out the part of my brain that insists closeness is dangerous.

His mouth is firm, patient, and when I make a small, helpless sound, his hands tighten just enough to tell me he heard it.

I pull back to breathe, forehead pressed to his. “Slow.”

Sam nods. “Slow.”

We move toward the bed like gravity.

Clothes come off in stages, unhurried. Sam checks in with his eyes as much as his words, and every time he asks, I answer, because something about his steadiness makes honesty feel possible.

When he touches me, it’s careful at first, then warmer, more sure as I relax into it. Hands, mouth, the kind of attention that feels like reverence without being weird about it.

It’s been too long since anyone touched me like this, like I’m not a problem to solve.

I grip his shoulders, nails digging in, and he pauses immediately.

“Still okay,” he breathes.

I swallow, breath shaking. “Yes. Don’t stop.”

His eyes darken a fraction, heat controlled behind patience. “Okay.”

I glare weakly even as my body melts. “Stop.”

He doesn’t.

When we finally come together, it’s slow and grounded and chosen, and the way he holds me makes my chest ache like I’m trying to learn a new language with my skin.

I don’t say sweet things. I don’t know how.

I make sounds instead, and Sam listens like they matter.

After, I’m trembling, half from release and half from the unfamiliar safety of not being alone.

Sam pulls me close, arm around my shoulders, steady warmth at my back.

He doesn’t smother. He doesn’t disappear.

He just stays.

My throat tightens. I whisper, rough, “You’re still here.”

Sam’s voice is low. “Yeah.”

I swallow hard. “Good.”

Sam’s mouth brushes my hair like a quiet kiss. “Good.”

I close my eyes and let myself breathe.

Not fixed.

Not cured.

Just… here.

With him.

---

## Chapter 6: Morning, and the Hook

Morning arrives softly, snow-light filtering through the curtains like the world is trying to be gentle.

I wake up and immediately panic because someone is in my bed.

Then I remember.

Sam is beside me, eyes open, watching me like he’s been awake for a while.

“Morning,” he says.

I groan into the pillow. “Don’t.”

His mouth tips. “Still okay.”

I exhale, annoyed by how much I need the question. “Yes.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Good.”

I push myself upright, hair a mess, chest tight in a way that’s half fear and half… something I don’t have a name for.

Sam sits up too, calm. “What happens now.”

I swallow. “We go downstairs. We act normal.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Define normal.”

“Don’t,” I say, but it comes out weaker than I mean.

Sam watches me for a beat, then says quietly, “I’m not asking for labels. I’m not asking you to declare anything. I just want to know if you want me to keep showing up.”

My throat tightens.

The safe answer is no.

The answer that makes my chest feel less like it’s collapsing is yes.

I nod once. “Through the festival.”

Sam’s eyes soften. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No,” he says, voice warm.

I exhale a shaky laugh, then stand and pull on a shirt like clothing can protect me from feelings.

We go downstairs together.

The bakery is quiet, warm, familiar. I flip on lights. Start the kettle.

Sam moves like he’s careful not to disrupt anything, but also like he’s not afraid to exist in the space.

It should annoy me.

It doesn’t.

My phone buzzes.

I stare at it, dread blooming.

**NOEL:** GREAT NEWS. TOMORROW IS THE COOKIE EXCHANGE.
**NOEL:** ALSO, I HAVE IDEAS.
**NOEL:** YOU’RE WELCOME.

I make a sound somewhere between a laugh and a threat.

Sam leans in slightly to read it over my shoulder, eyes warming.

“Idea man,” he says.

“He’s a menace,” I mutter.

Sam’s hand brushes mine on the counter, small and steady.

“Still okay,” he asks, low.

I swallow, then nod.

“Yeah,” I say. “I’m still okay.”

And tomorrow, apparently, gets worse.

**[END]**


## **Tinsel Town Book 1**

### Chapter 1: The Cookie Exchange Incident

I make it exactly twelve minutes into Day Four before Noel ruins my life again.

Which is honestly impressive restraint for him.

Candle & Crumb is open, the morning rush is tapering off, and my body is trying to pretend last night didn’t happen. If I keep moving, if I keep the trays rotating and the register singing and the espresso machine hissing like a threat, maybe I can treat “Sam slept in my bed” as a temporary weather event.

Then the bell over the door chimes, and in walks the weather event himself.

Sam Rook steps inside with snow on his shoulders and a calm expression that should be illegal this early.

He doesn’t say anything at first. He just looks at me.

That look hits my sternum like a fist.

Not because it’s hungry.

Because it’s steady. Because it says *I’m here* like that’s a normal thing to offer.

I grip the counter. “You’re early.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “It’s my shift.”

“It’s my bakery,” I say, like that’s a boundary.

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

I narrow my eyes. “Don’t.”

He doesn’t stop looking at me.

There are customers in line. I can feel their attention skittering between us like curiosity with legs.

Mrs. Alden is at the display case, pretending she’s deciding between ginger snaps and shortbread. She has been deciding for ten minutes. She is not a subtle woman.

I slide a bag across the counter to a customer and force my voice into normal. “Have a good day.”

The customer grins. “You too, Wes.”

Then they add, bright as a tack, “And you too, Officer Rook!”

I freeze for half a second. Sam gives a polite nod like he’s always belonged here.

I hate him a little for that.

The line dwindles. The room quiets.

Sam steps closer, low voice meant only for me. “Still okay.”

My throat tightens. I hate that my body relaxes at the question.

“Yes,” I say, rough. “Stop asking in front of people.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

I exhale through my nose, furious at my own face for heating up like I’m seventeen.

Before I can recover, the door bursts open.

Noel storms in wearing a Christmas sweater that looks like it lost a fight with a craft store.

He is carrying two giant plastic bins like a man hauling evidence.

He spots Sam, beams, and then spots me and beams harder, which is never a good sign.

“Perfect!” Noel announces. “My favorite emotionally constipated baker and my favorite calm authority figure.”

Sam’s mouth twitches. “Morning, Noel.”

I deadpan, “No.”

Noel ignores me and sets the bins on the nearest table with a thunk. “Day Four: Cookie Exchange!”

I stare at the bins. “Those are… a lot of cookies.”

Noel claps once. “Yes! Everyone brings a dozen! Everyone swaps! Everyone feels community!”

“Everyone gets food poisoning,” I mutter.

Noel gasps. “Wes, don’t be a villain.”

“I’m not a villain,” I say. “I’m a realist.”

Noel flips open a clipboard. “Great. Realists can label allergens. You’ll help.”

I blink. “I will not.”

Noel’s smile sharpens. “You already volunteered.”

I stare at him. “I volunteered for stamping cards and not dying.”

Noel nods briskly. “Yes. This falls under ‘not dying.’ Also, the exchange is happening here.”

My spine goes rigid. “Noel.”

Noel leans in like a conspirator. “The square is too cold. The community center is booked for a wreath seminar. The library is hosting ‘Silent Night: A Quiet Reading Hour’ and apparently you’re not allowed to scream in there.”

I glance at Sam, betrayed. “You’re letting him do this.”

Sam’s gaze stays steady. “It’s… cookies.”

“It’s chaos,” I hiss.

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I narrow my eyes. “Stop.”

“No.”

Noel looks between us, delighted. “So. Adorable. Anyway, Officer Rook, you’re in charge of crowd flow and vibes.”

Sam blinks. “Vibes.”

Noel nods solemnly. “Critical. Wes, you’re in charge of table layout and not biting anyone.”

I glare. “I don’t bite.”

Noel’s eyes sparkle. “Last night says otherwise.”

I go completely still.

The air in the bakery shifts.

Sam’s posture tightens a fraction, subtle but immediate.

I feel my soul leave my body and file a complaint.

“Noel,” I say, voice dangerously calm. “Say one more word.”

Noel lifts both hands. “Kidding! Kidding. Just… joking.”

Mrs. Alden makes a noise at the display case that is definitely a laugh disguised as a cough.

I shoot her a look that should curdle milk.

Sam steps closer, low voice. “Do you want me to handle him.”

I swallow. The idea of Sam “handling” Noel is both comforting and terrifying.

“I want him dead,” I mutter.

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

Noel claps again, too loud. “Alright! Stations! The exchange starts at noon!”

He points at me. “Tables.”

He points at Sam. “Crowd control.”

He points at himself. “Charm.”

He points at Mrs. Alden, who is absolutely not a volunteer. “Judgment.”

Mrs. Alden smiles like she’s proud.

Noel whirls and exits before I can grab him and throw him into the snow.

The bell chimes cheerfully behind him.

I stand there, breathing hard, hands clenched around the edge of the counter like it’s the only thing keeping me from committing a felony.

Sam watches me for a beat, calm.

“You okay,” he says quietly.

I exhale. “No.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

I point at him, trembling. “Stop saying okay.”

His eyes warm, gentle. “Answer anyway.”

I stare at him. My chest hurts.

Then I do the stupidest thing possible.

I tell him the truth.

“I don’t like being… talked about,” I say, voice rough. “Not like that. Not in public. Not when I didn’t agree.”

Sam’s expression softens immediately, like the words matter. “Okay. I won’t let Noel do that again.”

I swallow. “You can’t control Noel.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “I can try.”

I huff a humorless laugh. “Good luck.”

Sam steps closer, careful. “Do you want a plan.”

My throat tightens. “What kind.”

Sam’s voice stays low. “For today. Cookie exchange. How close is okay. What you need when the town starts watching.”

I stare at him.

He’s giving me choice. Again.

Like I’m not a problem.

Like my boundaries are normal.

It makes something in my chest unclench. It also makes me want to run because I don’t know how to accept that without feeling like I owe him.

I swallow hard. “No touching in front of people.”

Sam nods. “Okay.”

I narrow my eyes. “That okay counts.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “That okay counts.”

I exhale. “And if Noel says something like that again…”

Sam’s gaze is steady. “I shut it down.”

My chest tightens. “And if I get overwhelmed…”

Sam’s voice is calm. “You step into the back. I cover.”

I blink. “You’ll cover.”

Sam nods once. “Yes.”

I stare at him, caught off guard by how simple that sounds.

I clear my throat. “Fine. That’s the plan.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Good.”

I hate the warmth spreading in my chest. I hate how much I want it.

“Don’t look at me like that,” I mutter.

Sam blinks. “Like what.”

“Like I’m… worth the effort.”

Sam’s gaze stays on mine, steady as a hand offered. “You are.”

My throat tightens painfully.

I look away fast and grab the stack of spare tablecloths like they’re a weapon.

“Fine,” I snap. “Help me move tables.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I glare over my shoulder. “Stop.”

“No.”

We start dragging tables into position, setting up the cookie exchange like a war room.

At eleven-fifty, the first wave of townspeople arrives carrying tins and trays and Tupperware with holiday patterns.

By noon, Candle & Crumb is full.

It’s not violent chaos, but it’s chaos. People talking. Laughing. Swapping cookies like trading secrets.

Noel stands at the center like a ringmaster, guiding people from station to station.

Eben appears near the door with a clipboard and the expression of a man prepared to enforce order through sheer willpower.

Hugh floats in with a velvet scarf and a tray that might contain cookies or might contain a conceptual art piece.

Sam stands near the entrance, calm, scanning, gently redirecting traffic when people bottleneck. Not barking orders. Just… shifting the flow.

It’s infuriating how effective he is.

I’m behind the counter, plating samples, answering questions, pretending I’m not hyperaware of Sam’s presence like it’s a second heartbeat in the room.

I keep my hands busy to keep my brain from spiraling.

It works until Noel, of course, makes it worse.

Noel clinks a spoon against a mug for attention. “Everyone!”

The room quiets.

I tense.

Sam’s gaze flicks to me. Quick check.

I hate it. I need it.

Noel beams. “Today is about sharing! Community! Connection!”

People murmur agreement like they’ve been hypnotized.

Noel’s eyes sweep the room, then land, inevitably, on me.

“And,” Noel announces brightly, “we have a special thank-you for our local grump, Wes Candle, for hosting!”

People clap.

I flinch internally.

Noel continues, “And for Officer Sam Rook, who has been keeping this whole festival from descending into anarchy!”

More clapping.

Sam nods politely, unfazed.

Then Noel’s smile sharpens.

“And,” Noel adds, voice dripping with mischief, “I would also like to acknowledge…”

My blood goes cold.

Sam’s posture tightens.

Noel pauses dramatically.

“…that some of us are learning to accept help!”

I blink.

That’s… not as bad as it could’ve been.

I hate that my body relaxes by a millimeter.

Noel looks directly at me and winks.

I want to kill him anyway.

The crowd laughs, gentle, not cruel.

Mrs. Alden smiles like she’s witnessed the Second Coming.

Eben looks like he’s considering arson.

Hugh claps too loudly.

Noel wraps it up, sends people back to cookie swapping, and the noise returns.

I exhale, shaky.

Sam appears at my elbow a moment later, voice low. “You okay.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

Sam’s eyes warm slightly. “Good.”

I mutter, “Back room. Two minutes.”

Sam nods once. “Go. I’ll cover.”

I slip through the doorway, the little mistletoe Noel taped there dangling like a threat.

In the back, the noise dims. The lights are softer.

I lean against the counter and breathe, hands shaking.

Two minutes. That’s the plan. That’s manageable.

The door opens quietly.

Sam steps in, then stops, giving me space.

He doesn’t touch. Doesn’t crowd.

Just… stands there like the calm part of the world made human.

I exhale. “They’re watching.”

Sam nods. “Small town.”

I grit my teeth. “I hate it.”

Sam’s voice is low. “You didn’t do anything wrong.”

My throat tightens. “It feels like I did.”

Sam’s gaze holds mine. “That’s not the same thing.”

I swallow hard.

Sam waits a beat, then says gently, “You want me to say it.”

I blink. “Say what.”

Sam’s voice stays calm. “That I’m here. That I’m not going anywhere. That the attention doesn’t change what happened between us.”

My chest hurts.

I look away fast. “Don’t make it dramatic.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I narrow my eyes. “That okay counts too.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “That okay counts.”

I swallow. “Still okay.”

Sam’s brows lift slightly, warmed by the theft. “Yes.”

I exhale, then nod toward the door. “Let’s go back out before Noel announces my social security number.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

We go back out together, not touching, just moving in sync.

The crowd doesn’t part like the Red Sea. No one cheers. No one points.

It’s… normal.

As normal as Tinsel Town ever gets.

And somehow, that’s worse.

Because normal means this can keep happening.

Normal means I might not be able to shove it back into the box and pretend I’m fine.

Normal means Sam might keep showing up.

My chest tightens.

Sam glances at me once, quick and steady.

“Still okay,” he murmurs, low enough only I can hear.

I swallow and nod.

“Yeah,” I say. “I’m still okay.”

And for the first time, I believe it.

---

If you want me to keep rolling, next is **Chapter 2: The Cookie Exchange Aftermath** (Noel escalates, Wes panics about “being seen,” Sam sets a boundary with Noel, and Wes chooses a small public step that’s *not* a big kiss, but still growth).


## Chapter 2: The Cookie Exchange Aftermath

By two o’clock, Candle & Crumb smells like sugar and chaos.

The cookie exchange is still going, which is insane because no one needs this many baked goods. People are swapping tins like they’re exchanging dowries. Someone’s kid is trying to stack macarons into a tower. Hugh is loudly explaining the “mouthfeel” of something that looks like it was made in a lab.

I’m behind the counter, hands moving automatically, keeping myself busy so my brain doesn’t have room to panic.

Sam is still at the door, steady as a lighthouse.

Noel is still Noel, which is the primary problem with the world.

At two-fifteen, I watch Noel angle toward the counter with purpose.

My spine goes rigid.

Sam sees it too. His gaze flicks to Noel, then back to me.

He doesn’t move yet. He waits, like he’s giving Noel one chance to behave.

Noel doesn’t.

Noel approaches with a clipboard and a smile that means trouble.

“Wes,” Noel chirps, “quick question.”

I deadpan. “No.”

Noel continues anyway. “We need a photo for the festival newsletter! Something warm and candid. Like… you hosting.”

My stomach drops. “Absolutely not.”

Noel’s eyes widen dramatically. “Wes, it’s for community engagement.”

“It’s for you,” I snap.

Noel clasps his hands. “And the town!”

I feel my pulse spike. Photos mean permanence. Photos mean people talking when I’m not there to control the narrative. Photos mean someone freezing a moment I didn’t approve and deciding it belongs to them.

I grip the edge of the counter hard enough my fingers ache. “Noel.”

Noel softens his voice, as if that makes him less threatening. “Just you with the cookie table. Maybe with Sam, since he’s on duty and it’s cute.”

I go cold.

Sam’s posture shifts. Subtle. Dangerous.

Noel doesn’t notice because Noel has never noticed danger in his life.

I breathe in through my nose, slow, trying to keep my voice from turning into a weapon. “We are not taking photos.”

Noel’s brows lift. “Why not.”

Because I don’t like being seen.

Because being seen makes me feel like I’m about to lose something.

Because every time I’ve been visible, it’s been used.

But I don’t say any of that because Noel will turn it into an inspirational monologue and I will die.

I say, flat, “Because I said no.”

Noel’s smile falters for half a second. “Wes…”

Sam steps in.

Not fast. Not aggressive.

Just… present.

He moves to Noel’s side, calm but firm.

“No,” Sam says.

Noel turns, startled. “Officer Rook, I’m not asking you. I’m asking Wes.”

Sam’s gaze stays steady. “He said no.”

Noel blinks. “Yes, but he says no to everything. It’s his personality.”

Sam’s voice stays even. “Then today it’s still no.”

There’s a beat of silence where the air shifts.

Noel’s mouth opens, closes.

For the first time since I’ve known him, Noel looks like he’s met a wall that doesn’t move.

Noel recovers with drama, of course. He presses a hand to his chest. “Wow. Okay. Boundaries. Love that for us.”

Sam doesn’t smile. “Good.”

Noel looks between us, eyes narrowing with sudden interest. “Oh.”

I freeze.

Noel leans in slightly, voice dropping. “So we’re doing boundaries together now.”

I hiss, “Noel.”

He lifts both hands. “Not teasing. Not teasing. Just… noticing.”

My face heats with pure rage.

Noel backs up quickly. “Okay! No photo. Fine. I will… find a different angle.”

He turns to leave, then pivots back like he can’t resist. “But, for the record, you’d look very handsome in a candid.”

“I’m going to throw you into the river,” I say.

Noel beams. “See? Holiday spirit.”

He disappears back into the crowd before I can commit murder.

I stand there, breathing hard, hands clenched.

Sam stays beside the counter, not touching me, just close enough to be felt.

“You okay,” he asks quietly.

I swallow. “No.”

Sam nods. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

Sam’s eyes warm a fraction. “Answer anyway.”

I exhale shakily. “I hate photos.”

Sam nods once, immediate understanding. “Okay. No photos.”

My throat tightens. “It’s not just photos. It’s… being… presented.”

Sam’s gaze holds mine, steady. “Like an exhibit.”

I flinch, because yes. Exactly.

I swallow hard. “Yes.”

Sam’s voice is calm. “Then we don’t do that.”

A wave of relief rolls through me so sharp it almost hurts.

It shouldn’t matter this much.

But it does.

I look away, annoyed at myself for needing it. “Thanks.”

Sam’s eyes soften. “You’re welcome.”

I swallow. “Still okay.”

Sam’s brows lift, warmed by the theft. “Yes.”

I exhale slowly, shoulders loosening by a degree.

The exchange continues. People laugh. People swap cookies. The town keeps being the town.

And Sam stays near me like a guardrail I didn’t ask for but desperately needed.

---

At three o’clock, the first real problem hits.

Not a spilled cocoa. Not a crying kid.

A man I don’t recognize steps into the bakery with the posture of someone who thinks he owns rooms.

He’s in his forties, expensive coat, hair too perfectly styled for a town like this. He scans the space with mild disdain, then locks eyes on me.

My stomach drops.

Because I know that look.

It’s the look of someone who wants something from me, not because he cares, but because he thinks he can take it.

He strides toward the counter, ignoring the cookie tables like they’re beneath him.

“Wes Candle,” he says, voice smooth. “Finally.”

My throat tightens. “Can I help you.”

He smiles like we’re old friends. “Name’s Grant. Grant Holloway.”

I blink. The name pings something faint, distant. Like a ghost of a memory I don’t want.

He leans his elbows on my counter like he belongs. “I’m writing a piece about Tinsel Town. The festival. The charming small businesses. The local color.”

Noel’s doing. Of course it is.

My jaw tightens. “We’re busy.”

Grant waves a hand. “It’ll be quick. I just need a quote. Something punchy about community. And perhaps a photo of you working.”

My blood goes cold.

No.

Sam’s presence shifts instantly at my side. He’s not touching me, but he’s closer now, his calm sharpening into attention.

Grant’s gaze flicks to Sam, dismissive. “And you are?”

Sam’s voice is even. “Officer Rook.”

Grant smiles like that’s cute. “Lovely. I’m sure you keep the peace.”

Sam doesn’t respond.

Grant turns back to me, eyes bright with entitlement. “So, Wes. Tell me. What does Christmas mean to you.”

My hands go numb.

The crowd is still talking, laughing, but suddenly it feels like the whole room is too loud and too close.

Grant pulls out his phone, camera already open.

“I’d love a candid,” he says, raising it slightly. “Just you behind the counter, with the holiday bustle…”

My chest tightens violently.

I can’t breathe.

Sam steps between us.

Not aggressive. Not shouting.

Just… a wall.

Grant’s brows lift, annoyed. “Excuse me.”

Sam’s voice is calm and flat. “No photos.”

Grant laughs like Sam is joking. “It’s public.”

Sam doesn’t move. “Private business. No consent. No photos.”

Grant’s smile sharpens. “Are you going to arrest me for taking a picture in a bakery.”

Sam’s gaze is steady. “I’m going to ask you to leave.”

Grant’s eyes narrow, flicking past Sam to me. “Wes, is this really how you want to handle press.”

I feel the old reflex ignite: *Be polite. Be useful. Don’t make a scene.*

But my chest is tight and my hands are shaking and all I can see is that phone.

I can’t do it.

Sam glances back at me, quick check. “Still okay.”

I swallow hard. My voice barely works. “No.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

Then, to Grant, calm as ice: “Leave. Now.”

Grant’s face flushes. “This is ridiculous.”

Sam’s voice doesn’t change. “Now.”

Grant hesitates.

Eben appears out of nowhere like a summoned demon with a clipboard. “Problem.”

Grant turns, startled. “Who are you.”

Eben’s expression is dead. “Someone who dislikes problems.”

Noel pops up too, because Noel can smell tension like blood in water. “Oh! Grant! You’re here!”

Grant seizes on it. “Noel, thank God. Tell your… officer… to move.”

Noel blinks, taking in Sam’s posture, my face, the phone in Grant’s hand.

Noel’s expression shifts. The showmanship drops away.

“No photos,” Noel says, voice suddenly sharp. “Not without consent. Not here.”

Grant looks offended. “It’s for publicity.”

Noel smiles coldly. “We don’t need publicity that violates our people.”

The words hit me hard.

*Our people.*

Eben steps closer, expression blank but radiating threat. “Leave.”

Grant’s jaw clenches. He lowers his phone slowly, like he’s conceding under protest.

“This town is… unbelievable,” he mutters.

Noel’s smile turns sweet again, but it’s all teeth. “We are. Thank you for noticing.”

Grant turns and stalks out.

The door shuts behind him.

The bakery keeps buzzing, most people oblivious, because small towns are experts at pretending not to see things until they’ve decided what story they want.

My knees feel weak.

Sam turns back to me, gaze steady, voice low. “You okay.”

I swallow, throat tight. “No.”

Sam nods. “Okay. Back room. Now.”

I don’t argue.

I slip through the doorway, hands shaking, breath shallow.

Sam follows a second later, closing the door behind him.

The noise becomes muffled.

My chest is still tight, panic clawing.

I lean against the prep counter, head down, trying to breathe.

Sam stands a few feet away, giving space, voice low. “You’re safe.”

I shake my head. “I’m not. He… he had the phone.”

Sam’s tone stays calm, grounding. “He didn’t get a photo.”

I swallow hard. “But he tried.”

Sam nods. “Yes. And you said no.”

My throat tightens. “I didn’t say anything.”

Sam’s gaze holds mine. “You didn’t have to. I heard you.”

Something in my chest cracks open.

I hate that it feels like relief and grief at the same time.

I whisper, rough, “Why does that make me want to cry.”

Sam’s voice is gentle. “Because someone listened.”

My eyes sting.

I scrub a hand over my face hard. “Don’t.”

Sam doesn’t move closer. Doesn’t touch.

He just says, steady, “Still okay.”

I breathe in, shaky.

Then I do the new thing.

I answer honestly.

“No,” I whisper. “But I’m… here.”

Sam nods once, like that’s everything. “Good.”

I swallow. “Stop.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly, warm even in the seriousness. “No.”

I exhale, shaky laugh breaking through the panic.

It doesn’t fix it.

But it cracks the pressure enough that I can breathe.

Sam’s voice stays low. “Do you want to go upstairs. Or do you want to finish the event.”

My chest tightens.

Choice.

I swallow. “Finish.”

Sam nods immediately. “Okay. We’ll do it together.”

I blink. “No touching.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “No touching.”

I exhale, straighten my shoulders, and wipe my face like it didn’t just almost betray me.

Then I nod toward the door.

“Let’s go,” I say.

Sam opens it and steps out first, calm as a shield.

I follow.

The bakery lights are still warm. The cookie tables are still full. Noel is already redirecting the crowd like nothing happened.

Eben returns to his post like a sentry.

Hugh is loudly praising a peppermint bark that tastes like paint.

The world keeps turning.

But something is different.

Because when the world tried to take something from me…

Someone stood in front of it and said no.

And I didn’t collapse.

I just kept going.

---

If you want to keep rolling, **Chapter 3** is the fallout: Noel apologizes for the “joke,” Wes and Sam negotiate “public proximity” (not touching, but maybe standing together at the counter), and we set up the next setpiece: **Day Five: The Wreath-Making Fiasco** or **Day Five: The Caroling Disaster**.



## Chapter 3: Damage Control

The rest of the cookie exchange runs on momentum and spite.

Not *my* spite, though I have plenty. The town’s spite. The kind that shows up when someone from outside tries to take something that isn’t theirs.

Grant Holloway is gone. The air in Candle & Crumb settles back into its usual chaos: laughing voices, clattering tins, powdered sugar dusting everything like a threat.

I keep moving. Plate samples. Refill napkins. Answer questions I don’t care about.

Sam stays in his spot near the entrance, steady as a boundary line. Every so often, his gaze flicks to me. A check. A quiet confirmation.

No touching.

But I feel him anyway.

Noel floats through the crowd like he’s trying to re-inflate the mood. People let him. Hugh waxes poetic about “the emotional resonance of shortbread.” Eben watches the door like the building might try to escape.

By four-thirty, the crowd finally thins.

The last tins are swapped. The last overly enthusiastic “Merry Christmas!” is shouted into my nervous system. The last kid is dragged away from the macaron tower.

When the door closes behind the final customer, Candle & Crumb goes quiet in that soft, exhausted way a place gets after surviving something.

Noel exhales dramatically. “We did it.”

Eben says flatly, “It ended.”

Hugh wipes a tear like he’s witnessed art. “Community.”

I turn the CLOSED sign with more force than necessary.

Then I face Noel.

Noel flinches.

Good.

I walk toward him slowly, like I’m deciding whether to commit a crime.

Noel lifts both hands. “Okay. Before you start, I would like to say: I’m sorry.”

I stop.

Not because I forgive him. Because Noel apologizing without a joke is rare enough to require attention.

Noel’s voice is quieter, less theatrical. “The comment earlier. The… joke.”

My chest tightens.

Sam’s presence shifts behind me. Not intervening. Just there.

Noel continues, eyes on the floor for once. “I didn’t mean to put you on display. I got… excited. I thought it was cute. I forgot it’s not cute for you.”

My throat tightens in a way that makes me want to be mean so I don’t have to be soft.

I choose mean.

“You forgot,” I say flatly, “that I’m a person.”

Noel winces. “Yes. I did.”

He looks up, eyes earnest. “I’m sorry.”

The apology lands uncomfortably. It doesn’t fix what happened. But it does something else. It makes the room feel… less like a trap.

I swallow hard. “Don’t do it again.”

Noel nods fast. “Never again. Scout’s honor.”

“I will bury you,” I say.

Noel’s mouth twitches. “Fair.”

Eben steps closer, clipboard tucked under his arm. “He did wrong.”

Noel looks at Eben like he’s been blessed. “Thank you.”

Eben stares. “Do not interpret this as affection.”

Hugh drifts over, hand to his chest. “What a beautiful moment of accountability.”

Eben says, “Leave.”

Hugh leaves.

Noel shifts his attention to Sam, voice softer. “Thank you,” Noel says. “For… earlier. With Grant.”

Sam’s voice is calm. “He didn’t have consent.”

Noel nods, jaw tight. “We don’t do that here.”

Sam’s gaze flicks briefly to me. Then back. “Good.”

Noel clears his throat and does the merciful thing for once. “Okay. I’m going to… go. And not touch anything else. And not say anything else. I’m going to be so quiet you’ll forget I exist.”

“That would be a Christmas miracle,” I mutter.

Noel smiles weakly. “Love you too.”

He slips out, scarf trailing behind him like he’s fleeing the scene of a crime.

Eben watches him go, then looks at me. “You functioning.”

I exhale. “Define functioning.”

Eben’s expression doesn’t change. “You did not run.”

My chest tightens.

Eben nods once like that’s the only praise he’s physically capable of, then leaves without another word.

Now it’s just me and Sam in the bakery, warm lights and sugar dust and the quiet hum of refrigeration.

I feel suddenly… exposed.

Like the crowd leaving means there’s no distraction left.

Sam takes off his gloves slowly. Sets them on the counter. His voice is low. “You want a minute.”

It’s not a question. It’s an offer.

I swallow. “Back room.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

I push through the doorway first. The mistletoe sways above it, smug.

In the back, the quiet hits like a blanket.

I lean against the prep counter and stare at nothing, trying to tell my body the danger is over.

My hands are still shaking.

Sam steps in and closes the door behind him, then stops a few feet away. No crowding. No touching.

He waits.

My throat tightens. “He was going to take a picture.”

Sam’s voice is steady. “Yes.”

I exhale sharply. “I hate that I reacted like that.”

Sam’s brows lift slightly. “Like what.”

“Like I’m…” I swallow hard. “Like I’m broken.”

Sam’s gaze softens, but he doesn’t jump to comfort like a performance. He keeps it simple.

“You reacted like someone whose boundaries matter,” he says.

My chest aches.

I look away, annoyed at the sting in my eyes. “Don’t make it noble.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I glare reflexively. “Stop.”

“No,” he says, gentle.

I press my palms to the counter, grounding. “You stepped in.”

Sam nods. “Yes.”

I swallow. “Why.”

Sam’s gaze holds mine. “Because I wanted to. Because you didn’t deserve that. Because you looked like you couldn’t breathe.”

My throat tightens.

I hate that being seen makes me feel like crying.

I whisper, rough, “I don’t like being watched.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

His eyes warm. “That okay counts.”

I swallow hard, then force myself to say the thing that’s been lurking under my ribs all day.

“I don’t want the town to think…” My voice breaks. I clear my throat. “I don’t want them to think they get to comment on it.”

Sam’s expression stays steady. “They don’t.”

I blink. “But they will.”

Sam nods, honest. “Yes.”

My chest tightens. “So what do we do.”

Sam pauses, then says calmly, “We decide what’s public. We decide what’s private. And we hold the line.”

The simplicity of it makes my shoulders drop a fraction.

I exhale, shaky. “Okay.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “That okay counts.”

I glare weakly, but there’s no real bite left. “Still okay.”

Sam’s brows lift, warmed by the theft. “Yes.”

I swallow. “Plan.”

Sam’s voice stays low. “Plan.”

I take a breath and say it like a list because that’s how I survive.

“No touching in front of people,” I repeat. “No comments. If Noel slips, you shut it down. If anyone tries photos, you shut it down.”

Sam nods. “Yes.”

“And,” I add, throat tight, “if someone says something… about us.”

Sam’s gaze holds mine. “What do you want me to do.”

I stare at him.

The answer is: fight them. Destroy them. Make the world shut up.

But Sam isn’t asking what my anger wants. He’s asking what I need.

I swallow hard. “Redirect. Neutral. No fuel.”

Sam nods. “Okay.”

I breathe out. “And…”

My voice catches. I hate it.

Sam doesn’t move closer. He just waits.

I force it out, low and honest. “I don’t want you to look like you’re ashamed.”

Sam’s eyes soften immediately. “I’m not ashamed.”

My throat tightens. “Then… don’t.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

I blink. “Okay.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “That okay counts too.”

I groan softly and rub my face. “You’re going to say okay until I die.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Yes.”

I huff a laugh that breaks the tension just enough.

Then I take a breath and do the brave, stupid thing.

I nod toward the doorway. “When we go back out there… stand near the counter.”

Sam blinks. “Near.”

I swallow, heart thudding. “Not touching. Just… near. Like it’s normal.”

Sam’s gaze warms, careful. “That’s a public step.”

I grit my teeth. “Don’t name it.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I glare. “That okay counts.”

“It counts,” he agrees.

I swallow and nod once. “Good. Let’s do it.”

Sam’s voice is low. “Still okay.”

I exhale. “Yes.”

Sam opens the door.

We step into the front of the bakery together.

The room is mostly empty now. A couple lingering volunteers, Hugh trying to “help” by reorganizing something that doesn’t need reorganizing.

Sam walks with me toward the counter and stops at my side.

Near.

Not touching.

Just… present.

Hugh looks up, eyes immediately sparkling with narrative.

I shoot him a look that could freeze water.

Hugh lifts both hands. “I am silent.”

He is not silent, but he does retreat.

I focus on cleaning up the cookie exchange like my life depends on it, and Sam helps without being asked. He stacks tins. Wipes down a table. Throws out trash.

Normal.

It’s terrifying how normal it is.

I keep waiting for the shame to hit. For the panic to spike.

It doesn’t. Not fully.

My chest stays tight, but I can breathe.

Sam’s shoulder brushes mine once by accident when we both reach for the same stack of napkins.

I flinch internally, then realize… nothing happens.

No one screams. No one points. The world doesn’t end.

Sam glances at me. Quiet check.

“Still okay,” he murmurs.

I swallow. “Yes.”

He nods once like that matters.

It does.

---

Later, when the last table is wiped and the last tin is gone, Sam pulls on his jacket.

He pauses near the doorway like he’s waiting for me to say something.

I hate that I want him to stay. I hate that I want him to leave. I hate wanting at all.

I clear my throat. “Tomorrow.”

Sam’s brows lift. “Tomorrow.”

“Day Five,” I mutter. “What fresh hell is it.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Wreath seminar.”

I freeze. “No.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Noel texted the schedule.”

I grab my phone with shaking fingers and check.

**NOEL:** TOMORROW: WREATH-MAKING AT THE COMMUNITY CENTER
**NOEL:** YOU ARE COMING
**NOEL:** DO NOT ARGUE
**NOEL:** ALSO THERE’S HOT GLUE

I stare at the message like it’s a death certificate.

Sam watches me, amused. “Hot glue.”

I whisper, horrified, “I can’t do crafts.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

I exhale, tight. “If I get attacked by pine needles, it’s on you.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “I’ll protect you.”

My chest tightens, warm and annoyed. “Don’t say things like that.”

Sam’s gaze stays steady. “Why.”

“Because,” I mutter, “I might believe you.”

Sam goes quiet for a beat, then says softly, “Still okay.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

Sam nods, then opens the door. “I’ll see you tomorrow.”

I hesitate, then force the words out, rough and real. “Yeah. Tomorrow.”

Sam’s eyes warm like he’s storing the sound of it.

Then he leaves.

The bell chimes.

The door closes.

The bakery quiets.

And for once, the quiet doesn’t feel like abandonment.

It feels like… a pause.

Before the next disaster.

Before pine needles and hot glue and whatever Noel thinks is character development.

Before me doing this again.

**[STOP WRITING]**





## Chapter 4: Pine Needles and Hot Glue

The community center smells like cinnamon, pine sap, and mild despair.

Someone has cranked the thermostat up like they’re trying to bake the building. The windows are fogged from bodies and breath and the kind of holiday enthusiasm that should require licensing.

Every table is covered in wreath supplies: evergreen branches, ribbon, dried oranges, little bells, glitter (why), and hot glue guns that look like weapons.

Noel stands at the front like a motivational speaker who has never met a boundary.

“Welcome, artists!” he announces.

I mutter, “I’m leaving.”

Sam’s voice stays low at my side. “We said we’d try.”

I glare at him. “You said that. I said I’d survive.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I narrow my eyes. “Stop.”

“No.”

I hate him for making that sound like a promise instead of a joke.

Noel claps once. “Pairs!”

My spine goes rigid.

Noel beams. “Because wreath-making is about community and teamwork and learning to accept help.”

I stare at him. “Noel.”

Noel ignores the warning in my voice and points dramatically around the room. “You will work in pairs, and you will bond.”

Hugh gasps. “Bond.”

Eben says flatly, “No.”

Noel smiles brighter. “Yes.”

He scans the room and, as if guided by demonic instinct, locks eyes with me.

“And Wes,” Noel says sweetly, “you will be paired with Officer Rook.”

I go cold.

Sam’s posture tightens a fraction beside me.

The room collectively leans in with their faces. Tinsel Town has never met a situation it didn’t want to turn into gossip.

I feel the urge to run crawl up my throat like bile.

Then I remember the plan.

Neutral. No fuel. Public proximity, minimal.

I exhale slowly through my nose.

Sam glances at me, quick check. *Still okay?* without saying it.

I swallow and nod once.

Noel’s grin turns feral. “Wonderful! Table seven!”

He points to a table near the center of the room, directly in the line of sight of literally everyone.

Of course he does.

Sam leans in slightly, voice for me only. “Back room option if you need it.”

My throat tightens. “There is no back room.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “We can find a closet.”

I shoot him a look. “Do not tempt me with closets.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

We walk to Table Seven like two people heading to their own execution.

The chair legs scrape. People chatter. Someone in a Santa sweater waves at Sam. Sam nods politely. People adore him. It makes me want to commit property damage.

We sit.

Noel drops a wreath base in front of us like a judge sentencing us. “Create beauty,” he says, then floats away.

I stare at the pile of greenery. “I can bake. I cannot craft.”

Sam picks up a sprig of pine and examines it like evidence. “It’s assembly.”

“It’s chaos with glue,” I mutter.

Sam’s eyes warm. “Okay. Then we do it like your baking. Steps.”

My chest tightens, because of course he’d translate it into something I can handle.

I exhale. “Fine. Steps.”

Sam nods. “Step one: decide a shape.”

“It’s a circle,” I snap.

Sam’s mouth tips. “Step two: choose a theme.”

I blink. “Theme.”

Sam gestures at the ribbon pile. “Classic. Minimal. Loud.”

I stare at the glitter. “Not that.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Okay. Not that.”

I point at a simple red ribbon. “That.”

Sam nods. “Red. Classic.”

I feel my shoulders loosen by one degree.

We start building the wreath. Sam holds the base steady. I arrange the greenery the way I arrange pastries: symmetrical enough to be satisfying, imperfect enough to look human.

Sam watches me for a beat, then says quietly, “You’re good at this.”

I scoff. “Don’t lie.”

Sam’s voice stays calm. “Not lying.”

I look away fast, cheeks warming.

Across the room, Hugh says loudly, “Wreaths are like relationships! Fragile and beautiful!”

Eben says, “Stop talking.”

Hugh keeps talking.

I press the greenery down harder than necessary.

Sam’s hand comes near mine to steady a branch. He doesn’t touch. He pauses, waits.

My chest tightens at the restraint.

I clear my throat. “Still okay.”

Sam’s brows lift slightly, warmed. “Yes.”

I adjust the branch. “Okay.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “That okay counts.”

I glare weakly. “You two are ruining a perfectly usable word.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Okay.”

“Stop.”

“No.”
