We keep working.

And, annoyingly, it starts to look… good.

A woman at the next table glances over and smiles. “Oh, that’s lovely.”

I tense.

Then she adds, warmly, “Very elegant. Not too busy.”

I exhale. Compliments about wreaths I can handle. Compliments about my private life, not so much.

Sam nods politely. “Thank you.”

The woman looks at him, then at me, then back at him, eyes bright with curiosity.

Here it comes.

“Are you two…?” she starts.

My stomach drops.

Sam’s posture stays relaxed, but his voice turns gently firm. “We’re making a wreath.”

The woman blinks, then laughs awkwardly. “Right. Of course. Sorry.”

She turns back to her table.

My heart is still pounding.

I stare at the wreath like it betrayed me.

Sam’s voice stays low. “You did good.”

I swallow. “I didn’t say anything.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “You stayed.”

My throat tightens.

I hate how much that matters.

---

Half an hour later, Noel circulates like a shark.

He stops at our table and peers down at the wreath.

His face shifts into something almost genuine. “Oh,” he says. “This is… actually stunning.”

I mutter, “It’s a circle.”

Noel ignores me. “Minimal. Tasteful. Masculine. I love it.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Thanks.”

Noel’s eyes flick between us with too much interest. “You two are good together.”

My blood spikes.

Sam’s voice stays calm. “Noel.”

Noel lifts his hands. “I know, I know. No commentary. Boundaries. I’m learning.”

I narrow my eyes. “Barely.”

Noel smiles sweetly. “Still. I’m proud of you.”

“I will throw hot glue at you,” I say.

Noel gasps. “Violence!”

Eben’s voice drifts from across the room. “Do it.”

Noel flounces away, offended.

Sam’s shoulder shakes once with silent laughter.

I glare at him. “Don’t.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Okay.”

“Stop.”

“No.”

I exhale, then do the thing I’m not supposed to do in public.

I lean in slightly, close enough that only he can hear.

“Thank you,” I whisper.

Sam’s expression softens so fast it almost scares me. “You’re welcome.”

My chest aches.

And then, because the universe can’t let me have a quiet moment, the hot glue gun at our table makes a sad little pop and dies.

I stare at it.

Sam looks at it like it personally insulted him. “Out of sticks.”

I exhale, irritated. “Of course.”

Sam stands. “I’ll get more.”

I blink. “You don’t have to.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “I want to.”

My throat tightens.

He walks toward the supply table, calm as ever.

I watch him go, and the stupid part of my brain that hates joy whispers: *He belongs here.*

The even stupider part whispers: *So do you.*

I hate both.

---

Sam returns with glue sticks… and an extra ribbon spool he didn’t need.

He sets them down, then pauses like he’s about to say something.

I tense on reflex.

Sam’s voice is low. “The town’s watching. You’re doing it anyway.”

I swallow. “Don’t make it a thing.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

I take the ribbon from him and start wrapping it around the wreath.

My hands are steady now.

Not because I’m suddenly fine with being seen.

Because someone is here with me who doesn’t make “seen” feel like “owned.”

When the wreath is finished, Noel insists everyone holds theirs up for a group “showcase.”

I nearly pass out.

Sam’s voice stays low. “Do you want to step out.”

My chest tightens. The safe answer is yes.

But I can feel the last hour in my bones. The tiny win. The boundary held. The question redirected. The fact that I didn’t crumble.

I swallow hard.

“No,” I whisper. “I’ll… hold it up.”

Sam’s eyes warm, careful. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he agrees.

Noel counts down like he’s directing a film.

Everyone lifts wreaths.

I lift mine.

For two seconds, my face is visible in a room full of people.

No one throws stones. No one laughs. No one makes it into a spectacle.

There are a few smiles. A few nods. Normal human reactions to a wreath.

My lungs unlock.

Sam glances at me, quick check. “Still okay.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

And I mean it.

---

When we leave the community center, the cold air hits like a reset.

The street is quiet. Snow crunches under our boots.

We walk side by side, not touching, but close enough that our shoulders almost align.

Sam’s voice is low. “You were brave.”

I scowl. “Don’t call it that.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

I exhale, then mutter, “It was… not as awful as I expected.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Good.”

I swallow. “Tomorrow.”

Sam nods. “Tomorrow.”

I pull out my phone and check the schedule like I’m checking my own sentence.

**DAY FIVE: CAROLING DISASTER PREP. VOLUNTEERS NEEDED.**

I stare.

Sam’s mouth tips. “Caroling.”

I whisper, horrified, “I don’t sing.”

Sam’s gaze warms. “Okay.”

I point at him without looking. “Stop.”

“No.”

We keep walking through the snow, and for once, the town lights don’t feel like they’re mocking me.

They feel like… a path.

Which is a disgusting thought, and I will not be examining it further.

**[STOP WRITING]**

---

## Chapter 5: The Caroling Disaster

Day Five starts with Noel texting at 7:02 a.m.

**NOEL:** TODAY WE BRING JOY TO THE STREETS
**NOEL:** DO NOT HIDE
**NOEL:** I HAVE SHEET MUSIC
**NOEL:** AND A THERMOS

I stare at the message like it’s a threat.

I text back:

**WES:** I WILL KILL YOU.

Noel replies instantly:

**NOEL:** FESTIVE!

By noon, the carolers have assembled in Evergreen Square like a choir that lost a bet.

There are about twenty people, half of them wearing Santa hats, most of them holding lyric sheets like they’re about to take an exam they didn’t study for.

Hugh is in a velvet cape. Of course he is.

Eben is there too, which makes me deeply suspicious.

Eben doesn’t carol. Eben enforces.

Sam stands at the edge of the group, on duty, calm and watchful.

I am here against my will, holding a paper cup of coffee like it’s a life raft.

Noel bounces in front of the group. “Okay! We’re doing three stops. The retirement home, the library steps, and the town tree!”

I whisper to Sam as he passes near me, “I’m going to fake my death.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

Noel claps. “Warm-up!”

The group starts singing.

It is… not good.

It’s loud and enthusiastic and wildly off-key in a way that makes my teeth hurt.

A woman beside me sings with the confidence of someone who has never heard herself.

Hugh sings like he’s trying to seduce the concept of Christmas.

Eben stares into the middle distance like he’s imagining escape.

I stay silent, lips pressed together, because I did not sign up for public humiliation.

Noel glides over and shoves a lyric sheet at me. “Wes! Sing!”

I hiss, “No.”

Noel lowers his voice. “Just mouth it.”

“I won’t,” I snap.

Noel’s eyes narrow, then soften, because he’s trying to be better. “Okay. Then stand with us.”

I freeze.

That’s… a compromise.

I swallow hard and step a half pace closer to the group, still not singing.

Sam’s gaze flicks to me. Quick check. Approval without fuss.

I hate it. I need it.

We march toward the first stop, the retirement home, singing something vaguely recognizable as “Deck the Halls.”

The residents come out to watch, bundled in scarves and blankets, smiling like this is the best entertainment they’ve had all week.

A woman in a red cardigan sees me and points, delighted. “That’s the bakery boy!”

I go rigid.

Noel beams at her. “Yes! Wes Candle! Our local genius!”

I hiss, “Noel.”

Sam’s posture shifts, subtle.

The woman laughs warmly. “His cinnamon rolls saved my winter!”

My chest tightens. That’s… not hostile. That’s… kind.

I swallow hard and force a nod.

The woman smiles like I’ve given her a gift.

I hate how much my throat tightens.

Sam leans near me, voice low. “Still okay.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

And it’s true.

Because somehow, in the middle of a terrible carol, surrounded by people who are too loud and too cheerful, I’m not drowning.

I’m… standing.

---

That’s when the real disaster happens.

Noel announces, “Solo time!”

The group cheers.

I go cold.

Noel points at me.

I stare.

Noel’s smile is too bright. “Wes will sing a line!”

I hiss, “Absolutely not.”

Noel whispers, “It’s just one line. You can do one line.”

“I can do zero lines,” I whisper back, shaking.

Sam steps in, calm and firm. “Noel.”

Noel blinks, then sighs dramatically. “Fine. No solo.”

Relief hits me so hard my knees go weak.

Noel pivots smoothly and points at Hugh instead. “Hugh will sing a line!”

Hugh looks thrilled. “My time has come.”

Eben mutters, “God help us.”

Hugh belts out a line so dramatic a passing dog starts barking.

The residents clap anyway.

I exhale, shaky, and glance at Sam.

Sam’s eyes warm like he’s silently telling me I handled it.

I hate that I’m proud of myself.

I hate that I’m grateful.

Humans are so inconvenient.

**[STOP WRITING]**





## Chapter 5: The Caroling Disaster (continued)

The retirement home stop ends with Hugh bowing like he’s just closed Broadway, Noel waving at everyone like he’s running for office, and me standing there holding a lyric sheet I never sang from, sweating in the cold like I just survived a hostage situation.

The residents clap anyway.

They clap for Hugh’s theatrical suffering. They clap for Noel’s enthusiasm. They clap for the idea of Christmas existing near them.

Then the woman in the red cardigan looks right at me again and says, loud enough for half the group to hear, “Bring your cinnamon rolls tomorrow, bakery boy!”

A few people laugh warmly.

My face heats. My instinct is to retreat into sarcasm and hide inside my own skull.

Instead, because my brain is apparently broken now, I nod and say, “Okay.”

The word comes out softer than I like.

The woman beams like I handed her a crown.

Noel’s eyes go wide with pride, like I’ve just learned to walk.

I want to kill him.

Sam’s gaze flicks to me, quick and steady, and he says quietly, “That okay counts.”

I glare, but there’s no real heat behind it. “Stop.”

“No.”

We move to the next stop: the library steps.

This is where caroling becomes a crime.

The library is hosting “Silent Night: A Quiet Reading Hour,” which Noel knew, because Noel knows everything, and he has decided that means we should sing *quietly*.

Caroling quietly is not a thing that exists. It’s like asking a raccoon to be subtle.

We line up on the steps. Noel whispers frantic stage directions. Hugh whispers *nothing* and continues to sing like he’s wooing the moon.

A librarian comes out and does the slow, controlled walk of someone ready to end a bloodline.

She raises one finger.

Noel nods rapidly. “Yes! We are whisper-caroling.”

The librarian’s eyes narrow. “No.”

Noel blinks. “No?”

“No,” she repeats, with the calm of someone who has put down louder, more dangerous things than Noel. “No singing.”

Noel’s face collapses like a deflated balloon. “But… joy.”

The librarian points at the door. “Joy can be silent.”

Eben murmurs, “Finally.”

Noel turns, devastated. “Okay. We will do… a silent carol.”

Hugh looks personally injured.

Noel whispers, “We will *mime*.”

Hugh mouths words at the air like he’s still performing. It’s worse.

Sam’s shoulder shakes once in silent laughter, and I hate how much I like seeing him lose control by even a millimeter.

My phone buzzes.

**NOEL:** WE ARE STILL JOYFUL
**NOEL:** EVEN IF THE LIBRARY IS A TYRANT

I don’t respond.

We march toward the town tree for the final stop, the group half-singing under their breath like a cursed procession.

Snow falls lightly now, catching in Noel’s hair and Hugh’s velvet cape like the universe is trying to make this cinematic.

Evergreen Square is brighter at dusk, the big tree lit up like a beacon. People have gathered around, hot drinks in hand, kids weaving between legs, tourists taking photos of literally anything that looks quaint.

I see Grant Holloway’s face in my mind and my stomach tightens.

No.

Not tonight.

Noel steps forward, claps his hands, and announces, “Final performance!”

The crowd cheers.

My heart spikes.

Sam shifts closer, not touching, just… aligning himself between me and the worst parts of the world.

He leans in slightly. “Still okay.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

Noel launches into “Silent Night,” which is ironic because this is not silent and not night enough to justify it.

We sing.

I do not.

I stand.

I keep my hands in my pockets and my mouth shut and my eyes forward, and somehow that counts as participating in this town’s deranged idea of togetherness.

The crowd smiles anyway.

A kid points at the group and tugs his mom’s sleeve. A couple takes a selfie. Someone laughs when Hugh hits a note that doesn’t exist in nature.

I’m getting through it.

Then someone slips.

It’s not me, which feels unfair.

It’s Noel.

Noel’s boot catches on a patch of ice near the tree base, and he goes down hard, arms flailing like he’s trying to hug the air.

The crowd gasps.

Noel yelps, “I’m fine!”

He is not fine. He is dramatically sprawled in front of the town tree like a fallen angel made of scarves.

People surge forward.

Chaos.

My body tightens, ready to bolt.

Sam moves first, steady and immediate.

He steps in, crouches, holds out a hand to Noel without fanfare. “Easy.”

Noel grabs it with an offended huff. “I’m okay.”

Sam’s voice stays calm. “Sure.”

Noel stands, wincing. The crowd laughs kindly, tension releasing.

Then Noel does what Noel does best.

He turns embarrassment into theater.

He throws his arms wide and announces, “And that, my dear town, is why we have Officer Rook!”

The crowd cheers.

My stomach drops.

Noel, still flustered, adds, “He keeps us safe! And he’s also extremely—”

Sam’s voice cuts in, calm but firm. “Noel.”

Noel freezes, mid-sentence, blinking like he’s been gently slapped with reality.

Sam keeps his tone level. “Not the time.”

Noel swallows, nods quickly. “Right. Yes. Sorry. I’m concussed.”

Sam’s eyes narrow. “Are you.”

Noel brightens. “No!”

I exhale hard through my nose.

Sam glances back at me, a quick check.

He doesn’t have to say it. I feel the question anyway.

I swallow. “Still okay.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Yes.”

I blink. “That’s not how it works.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “It is if I’m checking too.”

My chest tightens in a way I don’t have a defense for.

Noel recovers and announces, “Okay! Everyone, hot chocolate at the square! On me!”

The crowd cheers and begins to drift toward the cocoa stand.

The caroling disaster dissolves into normal festival chaos.

I exhale like I’ve been holding my breath for hours.

Sam steps closer, still not touching. “You did it.”

I scowl. “I stood there.”

Sam’s gaze is steady. “You stayed.”

My throat tightens.

I look away, because if I look at him too long in front of the town tree, I might do something reckless like smile.

Noel limps up to us, putting on a brave face. “We lived.”

I deadpan. “You fell.”

Noel waves. “Details.”

He looks at Sam, then at me, then softens, voice quieter. “Hey. I’m sorry about earlier. The… solo thing. I got carried away.”

I stare at him. Noel apologizing twice in one day is a sign of the apocalypse.

I swallow. “Don’t do it again.”

Noel nods. “Never.”

Then he adds, carefully, “You’re doing really well.”

My chest tightens. My instinct is to snarl.

Instead, I force out, “Thanks.”

Noel’s eyes shine like he’s witnessing God.

He immediately ruins it by whispering, “Proud of you,” and scuttling away before I can bite him.

Sam watches Noel go, then turns back to me. “You want to go home.”

My body sags with exhaustion. “Yes.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

I glare weakly. “Stop.”

“No.”

We walk away from Evergreen Square together, not touching, just close enough that the cold doesn’t feel as sharp.

Halfway down the street, Sam says quietly, “You know you don’t have to earn being treated well.”

I freeze.

My throat tightens like my body wants to reject the sentence.

I keep walking so I don’t have to look at him.

I mutter, “Don’t get philosophical.”

Sam’s voice stays calm. “Okay.”

I point at him without turning. “Stop.”

“No.”

And I hate him for making that sound like devotion.

---

## Chapter 6: Snow, Soup, and the Smallest Public Step

We end up at my place above the bakery, because of course we do.

It’s become the place where my body stops bracing.

Which is a terrifying development I’m choosing not to analyze.

I take off my coat and hang it up like routine can keep me sane. Sam does the same, quiet and careful, like he respects my space without making me feel like a museum exhibit.

I go straight to the kitchen. “Soup.”

Sam blinks. “It’s late.”

“Soup doesn’t care,” I say, already pulling a pot down.

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

He moves to help, not because he’s trying to be useful, but because he’s trying to be present. He chops onions without slicing his finger off, which is honestly a point in his favor.

We cook in quiet for a while, the apartment filling with warmth.

Then Sam says, low, “Noel tried to bait you into singing.”

I snort. “He tried to bait me into dying.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “You handled it.”

I swallow. “You handled it. You stopped him.”

Sam shrugs. “You asked for a plan. I followed it.”

My throat tightens. “It worked.”

Sam nods. “Yeah.”

We eat at the small table, soup steaming, bread between us.

Outside, snow falls.

Inside, the world is calm.

Sam watches me over his mug. “Tomorrow’s Day Six.”

I grimace. “What is it.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Gingerbread House Contest.”

I freeze. “No.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Yes.”

“I can’t,” I say, horrified. “I will commit violence. That’s structural engineering made of sugar.”

Sam nods, dead serious. “It is.”

I stare at him. “Why are you encouraging this.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Because watching you judge other people’s gingerbread architecture might be the funniest thing I’ll see all year.”

I glare. “Rude.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Accurate.”

I huff a laugh, then stop because laughter feels intimate and I’m still not used to that.

Sam’s voice goes softer. “Today… when Noel fell. You checked on him without thinking.”

I frown. “He fell in front of a crowd.”

“And you didn’t disappear,” Sam says. “You stayed near. You kept breathing.”

My throat tightens.

I look down at my soup. “I’m trying.”

Sam nods once. “I see it.”

The words hit too hard. My eyes sting, ridiculous.

I scrub a hand over my face. “Don’t.”

Sam doesn’t move closer. He just says, steady, “Still okay.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Good.”

I exhale. “Stop.”

“No.”

---

The next morning, Day Six, the festival decides to test me again.

We’re downstairs in the bakery early, Sam on shift nearby, me trying to pretend I’m normal.

Noel comes in, as always, like he’s been shot out of a cannon.

“Emergency!” Noel announces.

I don’t look up. “If this is another snowman situation, I’m quitting.”

Noel gasps. “No. Worse.”

Sam’s gaze flicks over, alert. “What.”

Noel holds up a clipboard. “The gingerbread house contest needs a judge.”

I go cold. “No.”

Noel’s eyes widen. “Wes, you are the only person in town with standards.”

“That’s not a compliment,” I say.

“It is,” Noel insists. “Also, the other judge canceled because he ate too much peppermint and claimed he saw the face of God.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “That sounds like Hugh.”

Noel nods solemnly. “It was Hugh.”

I rub my face. “Absolutely not.”

Noel leans in, whispering like this is a hostage negotiation. “Please. The contest starts in one hour. The children are excited. The parents are competitive. The structural integrity is… questionable.”

I stare at him. “I hate you.”

Noel beams. “Festive!”

Sam’s voice cuts in, calm. “Wes can say no.”

Noel looks wounded. “Officer!”

Sam’s gaze stays steady. “He can say no.”

My chest tightens.

Because that’s the whole point. Choice. Consent. Not being volunteered like a decorative object.

Noel looks at me, then softens. “Okay,” Noel says, quieter. “If you don’t want to, I’ll figure it out.”

I stare at Noel, startled by him backing off like a normal person.

That’s suspicious.

I glance at Sam.

Sam’s eyes are steady, asking without pushing.

And the disgusting thing is: I can feel a part of myself wanting to do it. Not for Noel. Not for the town.

For me.

Because running isn’t the only option anymore.

I swallow hard.

“I’ll judge,” I say, voice tight. “But if anyone tries to take my picture, I will burn their gingerbread down.”

Noel’s face lights up like a sun. “Yes!”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

Noel claps his hands and spins toward the door. “I’ll set up! This is going to be so good!”

He pauses, turns back, and adds carefully, “Thank you.”

I nod once, because if I speak, I’ll regret it.

Noel leaves.

The bakery quiets again.

Sam steps closer, still not touching. “You chose it.”

I swallow. “Don’t make it a thing.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he agrees.

We start prepping for the morning rush before the contest, moving around each other in practiced space.

At one point, as I pass him at the sink, Sam’s gloved hand hovers near my back, not touching, like he’s asking a question without words.

I pause.

My heart thuds.

No touching in front of people was the plan.

But right now, the bakery is empty except for us. The morning customers haven’t arrived yet. The world is quiet. Safe.

I turn my head slightly, meet his gaze.

He waits.

I hate waiting. Waiting makes this my decision.

I exhale, then nod once.

Sam’s hand touches my back lightly, just a brief, grounding contact.

Not possessive. Not claiming.

Just… there.

My chest tightens like my body is learning what comfort feels like.

Sam’s voice is low. “Still okay.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

He lets his hand fall away immediately, respecting the line.

And I stand there for a beat, stunned by how much the smallest touch can change the temperature of my whole day.

Outside, the town wakes up in lights and snow and ridiculous holiday plans.

Inside, for the first time in my life, I’m not facing it alone.

And that’s… inconveniently good.



## Chapter 5: The Caroling Disaster (continued)

The retirement home stop ends with Hugh bowing like he’s just closed Broadway, Noel waving at everyone like he’s running for office, and me standing there holding a lyric sheet I never sang from, sweating in the cold like I just survived a hostage situation.

The residents clap anyway.

They clap for Hugh’s theatrical suffering. They clap for Noel’s enthusiasm. They clap for the idea of Christmas existing near them.

Then the woman in the red cardigan looks right at me again and says, loud enough for half the group to hear, “Bring your cinnamon rolls tomorrow, bakery boy!”

A few people laugh warmly.

My face heats. My instinct is to retreat into sarcasm and hide inside my own skull.

Instead, because my brain is apparently broken now, I nod and say, “Okay.”

The word comes out softer than I like.

The woman beams like I handed her a crown.

Noel’s eyes go wide with pride, like I’ve just learned to walk.

I want to kill him.

Sam’s gaze flicks to me, quick and steady, and he says quietly, “That okay counts.”

I glare, but there’s no real heat behind it. “Stop.”

“No.”

We move to the next stop: the library steps.

This is where caroling becomes a crime.

The library is hosting “Silent Night: A Quiet Reading Hour,” which Noel knew, because Noel knows everything, and he has decided that means we should sing *quietly*.

Caroling quietly is not a thing that exists. It’s like asking a raccoon to be subtle.

We line up on the steps. Noel whispers frantic stage directions. Hugh whispers *nothing* and continues to sing like he’s wooing the moon.

A librarian comes out and does the slow, controlled walk of someone ready to end a bloodline.

She raises one finger.

Noel nods rapidly. “Yes! We are whisper-caroling.”

The librarian’s eyes narrow. “No.”

Noel blinks. “No?”

“No,” she repeats, with the calm of someone who has put down louder, more dangerous things than Noel. “No singing.”

Noel’s face collapses like a deflated balloon. “But… joy.”

The librarian points at the door. “Joy can be silent.”

Eben murmurs, “Finally.”

Noel turns, devastated. “Okay. We will do… a silent carol.”

Hugh looks personally injured.

Noel whispers, “We will *mime*.”

Hugh mouths words at the air like he’s still performing. It’s worse.

Sam’s shoulder shakes once in silent laughter, and I hate how much I like seeing him lose control by even a millimeter.

My phone buzzes.

**NOEL:** WE ARE STILL JOYFUL
**NOEL:** EVEN IF THE LIBRARY IS A TYRANT

I don’t respond.

We march toward the town tree for the final stop, the group half-singing under their breath like a cursed procession.

Snow falls lightly now, catching in Noel’s hair and Hugh’s velvet cape like the universe is trying to make this cinematic.

Evergreen Square is brighter at dusk, the big tree lit up like a beacon. People have gathered around, hot drinks in hand, kids weaving between legs, tourists taking photos of literally anything that looks quaint.

I see Grant Holloway’s face in my mind and my stomach tightens.

No.

Not tonight.

Noel steps forward, claps his hands, and announces, “Final performance!”

The crowd cheers.

My heart spikes.

Sam shifts closer, not touching, just… aligning himself between me and the worst parts of the world.

He leans in slightly. “Still okay.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

Noel launches into “Silent Night,” which is ironic because this is not silent and not night enough to justify it.

We sing.

I do not.

I stand.

I keep my hands in my pockets and my mouth shut and my eyes forward, and somehow that counts as participating in this town’s deranged idea of togetherness.

The crowd smiles anyway.

A kid points at the group and tugs his mom’s sleeve. A couple takes a selfie. Someone laughs when Hugh hits a note that doesn’t exist in nature.

I’m getting through it.

Then someone slips.

It’s not me, which feels unfair.

It’s Noel.

Noel’s boot catches on a patch of ice near the tree base, and he goes down hard, arms flailing like he’s trying to hug the air.

The crowd gasps.

Noel yelps, “I’m fine!”

He is not fine. He is dramatically sprawled in front of the town tree like a fallen angel made of scarves.

People surge forward.

Chaos.

My body tightens, ready to bolt.

Sam moves first, steady and immediate.

He steps in, crouches, holds out a hand to Noel without fanfare. “Easy.”

Noel grabs it with an offended huff. “I’m okay.”

Sam’s voice stays calm. “Sure.”

Noel stands, wincing. The crowd laughs kindly, tension releasing.

Then Noel does what Noel does best.

He turns embarrassment into theater.

He throws his arms wide and announces, “And that, my dear town, is why we have Officer Rook!”

The crowd cheers.

My stomach drops.

Noel, still flustered, adds, “He keeps us safe! And he’s also extremely—”

Sam’s voice cuts in, calm but firm. “Noel.”

Noel freezes, mid-sentence, blinking like he’s been gently slapped with reality.

Sam keeps his tone level. “Not the time.”

Noel swallows, nods quickly. “Right. Yes. Sorry. I’m concussed.”

Sam’s eyes narrow. “Are you.”

Noel brightens. “No!”

I exhale hard through my nose.

Sam glances back at me, a quick check.

He doesn’t have to say it. I feel the question anyway.

I swallow. “Still okay.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Yes.”

I blink. “That’s not how it works.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “It is if I’m checking too.”

My chest tightens in a way I don’t have a defense for.

Noel recovers and announces, “Okay! Everyone, hot chocolate at the square! On me!”

The crowd cheers and begins to drift toward the cocoa stand.

The caroling disaster dissolves into normal festival chaos.

I exhale like I’ve been holding my breath for hours.

Sam steps closer, still not touching. “You did it.”

I scowl. “I stood there.”

Sam’s gaze is steady. “You stayed.”

My throat tightens.

I look away, because if I look at him too long in front of the town tree, I might do something reckless like smile.

Noel limps up to us, putting on a brave face. “We lived.”

I deadpan. “You fell.”

Noel waves. “Details.”

He looks at Sam, then at me, then softens, voice quieter. “Hey. I’m sorry about earlier. The… solo thing. I got carried away.”

I stare at him. Noel apologizing twice in one day is a sign of the apocalypse.

I swallow. “Don’t do it again.”

Noel nods. “Never.”

Then he adds, carefully, “You’re doing really well.”

My chest tightens. My instinct is to snarl.

Instead, I force out, “Thanks.”

Noel’s eyes shine like he’s witnessing God.

He immediately ruins it by whispering, “Proud of you,” and scuttling away before I can bite him.

Sam watches Noel go, then turns back to me. “You want to go home.”

My body sags with exhaustion. “Yes.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

I glare weakly. “Stop.”

“No.”

We walk away from Evergreen Square together, not touching, just close enough that the cold doesn’t feel as sharp.

Halfway down the street, Sam says quietly, “You know you don’t have to earn being treated well.”

I freeze.

My throat tightens like my body wants to reject the sentence.

I keep walking so I don’t have to look at him.

I mutter, “Don’t get philosophical.”

Sam’s voice stays calm. “Okay.”

I point at him without turning. “Stop.”

“No.”

And I hate him for making that sound like devotion.

---

## Chapter 6: Snow, Soup, and the Smallest Public Step

We end up at my place above the bakery, because of course we do.

It’s become the place where my body stops bracing.

Which is a terrifying development I’m choosing not to analyze.

I take off my coat and hang it up like routine can keep me sane. Sam does the same, quiet and careful, like he respects my space without making me feel like a museum exhibit.

I go straight to the kitchen. “Soup.”

Sam blinks. “It’s late.”

“Soup doesn’t care,” I say, already pulling a pot down.

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

He moves to help, not because he’s trying to be useful, but because he’s trying to be present. He chops onions without slicing his finger off, which is honestly a point in his favor.

We cook in quiet for a while, the apartment filling with warmth.

Then Sam says, low, “Noel tried to bait you into singing.”

I snort. “He tried to bait me into dying.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “You handled it.”

I swallow. “You handled it. You stopped him.”

Sam shrugs. “You asked for a plan. I followed it.”

My throat tightens. “It worked.”

Sam nods. “Yeah.”

We eat at the small table, soup steaming, bread between us.

Outside, snow falls.

Inside, the world is calm.

Sam watches me over his mug. “Tomorrow’s Day Six.”

I grimace. “What is it.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Gingerbread House Contest.”

I freeze. “No.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Yes.”

“I can’t,” I say, horrified. “I will commit violence. That’s structural engineering made of sugar.”

Sam nods, dead serious. “It is.”

I stare at him. “Why are you encouraging this.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Because watching you judge other people’s gingerbread architecture might be the funniest thing I’ll see all year.”

I glare. “Rude.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Accurate.”

I huff a laugh, then stop because laughter feels intimate and I’m still not used to that.

Sam’s voice goes softer. “Today… when Noel fell. You checked on him without thinking.”

I frown. “He fell in front of a crowd.”

“And you didn’t disappear,” Sam says. “You stayed near. You kept breathing.”

My throat tightens.

I look down at my soup. “I’m trying.”

Sam nods once. “I see it.”

The words hit too hard. My eyes sting, ridiculous.

I scrub a hand over my face. “Don’t.”

Sam doesn’t move closer. He just says, steady, “Still okay.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Good.”

I exhale. “Stop.”

“No.”

---

The next morning, Day Six, the festival decides to test me again.

We’re downstairs in the bakery early, Sam on shift nearby, me trying to pretend I’m normal.

Noel comes in, as always, like he’s been shot out of a cannon.

“Emergency!” Noel announces.

I don’t look up. “If this is another snowman situation, I’m quitting.”

Noel gasps. “No. Worse.”

Sam’s gaze flicks over, alert. “What.”

Noel holds up a clipboard. “The gingerbread house contest needs a judge.”

I go cold. “No.”

Noel’s eyes widen. “Wes, you are the only person in town with standards.”

“That’s not a compliment,” I say.

“It is,” Noel insists. “Also, the other judge canceled because he ate too much peppermint and claimed he saw the face of God.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “That sounds like Hugh.”

Noel nods solemnly. “It was Hugh.”

I rub my face. “Absolutely not.”

Noel leans in, whispering like this is a hostage negotiation. “Please. The contest starts in one hour. The children are excited. The parents are competitive. The structural integrity is… questionable.”

I stare at him. “I hate you.”

Noel beams. “Festive!”

Sam’s voice cuts in, calm. “Wes can say no.”

Noel looks wounded. “Officer!”

Sam’s gaze stays steady. “He can say no.”

My chest tightens.

Because that’s the whole point. Choice. Consent. Not being volunteered like a decorative object.

Noel looks at me, then softens. “Okay,” Noel says, quieter. “If you don’t want to, I’ll figure it out.”

I stare at Noel, startled by him backing off like a normal person.

That’s suspicious.

I glance at Sam.

Sam’s eyes are steady, asking without pushing.

And the disgusting thing is: I can feel a part of myself wanting to do it. Not for Noel. Not for the town.

For me.

Because running isn’t the only option anymore.

I swallow hard.

“I’ll judge,” I say, voice tight. “But if anyone tries to take my picture, I will burn their gingerbread down.”

Noel’s face lights up like a sun. “Yes!”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

Noel claps his hands and spins toward the door. “I’ll set up! This is going to be so good!”

He pauses, turns back, and adds carefully, “Thank you.”

I nod once, because if I speak, I’ll regret it.

Noel leaves.

The bakery quiets again.

Sam steps closer, still not touching. “You chose it.”

I swallow. “Don’t make it a thing.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he agrees.

We start prepping for the morning rush before the contest, moving around each other in practiced space.

At one point, as I pass him at the sink, Sam’s gloved hand hovers near my back, not touching, like he’s asking a question without words.

I pause.

My heart thuds.

No touching in front of people was the plan.

But right now, the bakery is empty except for us. The morning customers haven’t arrived yet. The world is quiet. Safe.

I turn my head slightly, meet his gaze.

He waits.

I hate waiting. Waiting makes this my decision.

I exhale, then nod once.

Sam’s hand touches my back lightly, just a brief, grounding contact.

Not possessive. Not claiming.

Just… there.

My chest tightens like my body is learning what comfort feels like.

Sam’s voice is low. “Still okay.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

He lets his hand fall away immediately, respecting the line.

And I stand there for a beat, stunned by how much the smallest touch can change the temperature of my whole day.

Outside, the town wakes up in lights and snow and ridiculous holiday plans.

Inside, for the first time in my life, I’m not facing it alone.

And that’s… inconveniently good.


## Chapter 7: Gingerbread Judgement

Evergreen Square is a war zone made of frosting.

They set the gingerbread house contest up under a heated tent near the town tree, as if warmth can somehow civilize what’s about to happen. Long tables hold entries like a museum exhibit curated by sugar addicts with unresolved issues.

There are candy canes used as load-bearing beams.

There are gumdrops in patterns that suggest advanced mathematics.

There is, horrifyingly, a gingerbread lighthouse with pretzel rods for support columns, and I can already tell it’s going to collapse the moment someone breathes wrong.

Noel bounces beside me like a proud stage mom. He’s wearing a “JUDGE” sash that he absolutely made himself.

“I cannot believe you said yes,” he whispers, practically vibrating.

“I didn’t say yes,” I mutter. “I said I’d prevent structural collapse.”

Noel gasps. “Same thing.”

Sam stands a few feet away, technically on duty, but close enough that my body doesn’t feel like it’s free-falling. He’s in uniform, shoulders squared, face calm.

I hate how safe it makes me feel.

I also… don’t hate it.

Noel hands me a clipboard. “We have categories.”

I stare. “Categories.”

Noel nods solemnly. “Taste. Design. Festive Spirit. Structural Integrity.”

I exhale. “Finally, a category I can respect.”

Noel beams. “I knew this would activate you.”

“It’s not activation,” I snap. “It’s standards.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly from his post. “Okay.”

I glare at him automatically. “Stop.”

“No,” he says, soft enough only I can hear.

The tent fills quickly. Parents herding kids, tourists with phones, locals who treat this like a sport. Someone hands Noel a microphone like this is an actual event. Of course they do.

Noel taps the mic. “Testing, testing!”

A squeal of feedback hits my teeth.

I flinch, then immediately regret the flinch because a nearby mom notices and smiles like I’m adorable.

I am not adorable.

Noel announces, “Welcome to the Tinsel Town Gingerbread House Contest! Judges are myself…”

He gestures dramatically to himself, because Noel can’t resist.

“…and Wes Candle, our beloved baker and the only man in town who has ever told me the word ‘no’!”

The crowd laughs.

My shoulders go tight.

Sam’s gaze flicks to me, quick check.

I breathe in through my nose and keep my face neutral. No fuel.

Noel continues, “And we have a special safety consultant, Officer Sam Rook!”

The crowd cheers, because of course they do.

Sam nods politely, expression calm, like he isn’t being turned into a mascot.

Noel’s voice drops conspiratorially. “He’s here in case anyone tries to bribe the judges with candy.”

A kid yells, “I WILL!”

Laughter.

I focus on the clipboard like it’s a shield.

Noel leads us down the table.

First entry: a tiny cottage made by a kid and what looks like pure hope. The roof is crooked. The icing is thick. A gummy bear is lying on the porch like a casualty.

The kid looks up at me with huge eyes. “Do you like it?”

My throat tightens in an unexpected place.

I clear my throat and force my face into something not terrifying. “It’s… ambitious.”

The kid grins.

I add, more honestly, “And the support beams are good.”

The kid’s grin gets wider, like I just knighted him.

Noel whispers, delighted, “Wes is praising a child. Mark the calendar.”

“Don’t,” I mutter.

We move to the next one: a gingerbread barn with pretzel fencing and a frosting snowdrift that’s genuinely well-done.

I mark notes.

Taste: decent. Design: solid. Structural: surprisingly excellent.

I’m in it now. I hate it. I love it.

Then we reach the lighthouse.

It’s gorgeous. It’s also a death trap.

The creator is a mom with perfect hair and an expression that says she wins things.

“Handmade,” she says brightly. “No kits. No shortcuts.”

I stare at the pretzel columns. The candy windows. The tower that is absolutely too tall for that base.

I don’t even have to touch it to see the stress points.

“This,” I say slowly, “is not safe.”

Her smile twitches. “It’s gingerbread.”

“It’s architecture,” I correct.

Noel leans in, whispering, “Wes. Please. She’s on the PTA.”

“I don’t care,” I hiss back.

The mom laughs as if I’m flirting. “Oh, he’s serious.”

Sam steps a little closer, not inserting himself, just present.

The mom’s gaze flicks to him, then back to me with renewed interest. “Can I get a photo of you judging? For my socials?”

My stomach drops.

The reflex hits like a punch. Heat in my chest, throat closing, the world going sharp.

Before I can lock up completely, Sam’s voice cuts in, calm but firm. “No photos.”

The mom blinks. “I’m not taking pictures of the kids, just him.”

Sam doesn’t move. “No photos.”

Noel, bless his meddling heart, tries to smooth it over. “We have an official photographer later, darling!”

The mom’s smile thins. “It’s a public event.”

Sam’s tone stays flat. “Private boundary.”

The mom huffs like she’s been denied oxygen, but she lowers her phone.

I swallow hard, still shaky, and force myself to breathe.

Then I look at the lighthouse again because anger is easier than panic.

“This tower is going to collapse,” I say.

The mom scoffs. “No it won’t.”

I point. “That icing seam is under stress. The pretzel columns aren’t aligned. Your base is soft. It’s going to slide.”

Noel whispers, “He’s so hot right now,” like the world’s most inappropriate narrator.

I ignore him.

The mom crosses her arms. “It’s fine.”

I stare at her, deadpan. “Do you want to win or do you want a disaster in front of the town tree.”

Her nostrils flare. “It won’t collapse.”

As if summoned by spite, the tower makes a tiny, ominous crack.

Everyone goes still.

The mom’s eyes widen.

Noel gasps like he’s watching a murder.

I don’t move. “I told you.”

The tower leans.

A kid shrieks.

Sam moves instantly, stepping in with steady hands, catching the whole gingerbread structure before it can smash onto the table and explode into a candy massacre.

He holds it like it’s a live bomb, calm even as frosting smears onto his gloves.

The crowd erupts in laughter and applause.

The mom looks like she wants to disappear into the snow.

Noel grabs the mic and announces, “AND THAT, MY FRIENDS, IS WHY WE HAVE OFFICER ROOK!”

More cheering.

My heart is still pounding, but the panic has shifted into something else.

Gratitude, hot and inconvenient.

Sam sets the lighthouse down carefully, then looks at me.

His eyes are steady.

A quiet question.

*Still okay.*

I swallow and nod once.

“Yeah,” I say, rough.

Sam’s mouth tips, subtle. “Okay.”

I glare, but there’s no bite. “Stop.”

“No.”

---

By the time we finish judging, I’ve written three pages of notes and threatened four people with structural critiques. It’s almost… fun.

Noel reads out winners like it’s the Oscars.

Kids cheer. Parents clap. Hugh cries.

When Noel announces the kid-cottage as “Most Festive Spirit,” the kid looks at me like I’ve personally given him the sun.

I nod once, stiff and uncomfortable.

The kid beams anyway.

Then Noel announces the overall winner: the barn, which genuinely deserves it.

The PTA mom with the lighthouse tries to smile like she’s not fantasizing about arson.

Noel wraps things up, everyone disperses, and the tent empties into the cold.

I exhale like I’ve been holding my breath for three hours.

Sam steps closer, still careful about distance in public. “You were good.”

I scowl. “I was harsh.”

Sam’s gaze warms. “You were honest. And you didn’t run.”

My chest tightens.

I look away, because my face is doing something soft I don’t approve of. “Don’t make it a thing.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he agrees.

Noel pops up between us like a demon. “You were AMAZING.”

I glare. “Noel.”

Noel holds up both hands. “No commentary. No teasing. Boundaries. I’m learning.”

I narrow my eyes. “Barely.”

Noel’s smile turns genuinely soft for half a second. “I’m proud of you.”

I hate that the words hit. I hate that they don’t feel like a trap.

I mutter, “Go away.”

Noel beams. “Festive!” and vanishes into the crowd.

Sam watches him go, then turns back to me, voice low. “Home.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

Sam nods. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

And we walk back to the bakery together, snow crunching under our boots, the town lights flickering like they’re trying to pretend everything is simple.

It isn’t.

But it’s… working.

Somehow.

---

## Chapter 8: The Gingerbread Afterglow

Back inside Candle & Crumb, warmth hits my face like relief.

I lock the door behind us and flip the sign.

CLOSED.

Sam takes off his gloves. Frosting smears on the leather like proof of what happened.

I stare at it, then at him.

“You caught it,” I say, because my brain can’t figure out how to say *thank you* without choking.

Sam’s voice is calm. “It was falling.”

“I mean,” I mutter, “you didn’t hesitate.”

Sam’s gaze holds mine. “Neither did you. You spoke.”

My throat tightens. “I was annoyed.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Still spoke.”

I exhale hard and pace two steps, then stop.

This is the part where my brain tries to turn everything into danger.

Because gratitude is vulnerability and vulnerability is risk.

Sam stays still, giving me space like he always does.

Finally I force it out, rough and real. “Thanks.”

Sam’s eyes soften immediately. “You’re welcome.”

My chest aches.

I look away fast, then mutter, “Also you have frosting on your gloves.”

Sam glances down. “Yeah.”

“It’s… stupidly attractive,” I say before I can stop myself.

Silence.

I go cold.

Sam’s brows lift slightly, surprise and warmth flickering. “Is it.”

I scrub a hand over my face. “Don’t make me regret talking.”

Sam’s voice stays low. “Okay.”

I point at him. “Stop.”

“No.”

He steps closer, slow and careful, giving me time to flinch.

I don’t.

He stops just inside my space, gaze steady. “Still okay.”

My throat tightens. “Yes.”

Sam’s mouth meets mine, warm and unhurried, and the kiss is like a reset. Like the world outside can be loud and invasive, but in here, he’s still asking.

I kiss him back hard enough to make my own knees go weak.

He pulls back a fraction. “Slow.”

I exhale, shaking. “Yes. Slow.”

We move together toward the back room, the bakery dim, the ovens humming like they’re amused at my lack of self-control.

Sam’s hands hover at my waist, waiting.

I nod once.

He touches me, gentle, and my whole body loosens like it’s been waiting all day.

I hate how much I need this.

I love that he gives it without taking more than I offer.

When he checks in, I answer.

When I get tight, he slows.

When I finally break, it’s into warmth instead of panic.

After, we end up sitting on the prep counter, boots dangling, breathing in the quiet.

Sam’s shoulder rests near mine, not pressing, just there.

I stare at the floor and mutter, “This is not sustainable.”

Sam’s brows lift. “What.”

“Feeling okay,” I say, voice rough. “It’s suspicious.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I sigh. “Stop.”

“No.”

I swallow, then admit the truth I don’t want to admit. “I liked today.”

Sam’s gaze warms. “Yeah?”

“I didn’t hate it,” I correct.

Sam’s eyes soften. “That’s a win.”

My throat tightens.

I stare at him, then look away like eye contact might break me.

Sam’s voice stays low. “Tomorrow’s Day Seven.”

I groan. “What is it.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Ice sculpture walk.”

I freeze. “No.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Yes.”

“I will slip and die,” I mutter.

Sam’s voice is calm. “I’ll catch you.”

My chest tightens, warm and annoyed. “Don’t say things like that.”

Sam’s gaze holds mine. “Why.”

“Because,” I mutter, “I might believe you.”

Sam goes quiet for a beat, then asks softly, “Still okay.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

He nods once like it matters.

It does.

Outside, the town keeps glowing.

Inside, for once, I’m not bracing for impact.

I’m… leaning.

And that’s the scariest part of all.

---

## Chapter 9: Ice and Other Bad Ideas

Day Seven arrives with fresh snow and an ice sculpture walk, because Noel has never met a situation he couldn’t make colder and more public.

By the time we hit Evergreen Square, the sculptures are lined up like glittering traps: swans, reindeer, a dragon (of course), a ridiculously detailed “Santa’s sleigh” that looks like it could cut someone.

Tourists drift through taking photos.

I tense.

Sam stays near, not touching, but present.

Noel bounces ahead, shouting fun facts like a tour guide in hell.

“THIS ONE IS A POLAR BEAR,” Noel announces. “SYMBOLIZING RESILIENCE!”

Eben murmurs, “It is ice.”

Hugh whispers, “It’s ephemeral art.”

I’m walking carefully, because the paths are slick and my dignity is fragile.

Sam glances at me. “You want my arm.”

I freeze. “No.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I take one step.

My boot slides.

My stomach drops.

Sam’s hand shoots out and catches my elbow, steady and fast, preventing me from eating snow in front of half the town.

His grip is light. Not possessive. Just… saving me.

My face heats instantly.

A tourist laughs softly, not cruel, just amused by the near-slip.

My chest tightens.

Sam releases me immediately, respect snapping back into place.

He leans in, voice low. “Still okay.”

I swallow hard.

The old Wes would’ve snapped, would’ve yanked away, would’ve turned it into anger to hide the embarrassment.

Today, my chest is tight, but I can breathe.

“Yeah,” I say, rough. “I’m okay.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Okay.”

I glare weakly. “Stop.”

“No.”

And then Noel turns around, sees us, and his eyes light up like he’s about to ruin everything.

So, naturally, I brace for impact.

Because this town might be cozy, but it is relentless.

And I’m still learning how to stand in the light without burning.

(Keep going.)



## Chapter 9: Ice and Other Bad Ideas (continued)

Noel turns around, spots Sam’s hand having just saved my dignity, and his face lights up like he’s about to set my life on fire for sport.

“Oh!” Noel says, too loud. “LOOK AT THAT. SAFETY. TEAMWORK. TRUST.”

I go still.

Sam’s posture doesn’t change, but his voice goes calm and flat in the way that means he’s done playing. “Noel.”

Noel blinks, mid-gush. “Yes?”

Sam holds his gaze. “Don’t.”

Noel’s smile wobbles. The performative sparkle dims a notch. “Right. Boundaries. I’m learning.”

Eben appears like a summoned consequence. “Stop talking.”

Hugh whispers, delighted, “Aromantic tension.”

Eben turns his head slowly. “Leave.”

Hugh leaves, but not far enough.

Noel clears his throat and pivots hard, pointing at the nearest sculpture like it’s a distraction grenade. “THIS ONE IS A… VERY FESTIVE FISH.”

“It’s a swan,” Eben says.

Noel nods emphatically. “A SWAN-FISH.”

I exhale through my nose, trying not to laugh because laughter is dangerous. Laughter turns into softness and softness turns into people thinking they can touch your life.

Sam glances at me, quick and steady.

“Still okay,” he murmurs.

My chest is tight, but it’s not panic-tight. It’s… awareness-tight. The kind that happens when you realize the world could take something and someone might block it for you.

I swallow. “Yeah.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “Okay.”

I glare weakly. “Stop.”

“No.”

We keep walking.

The path is slick. The ice sculptures glitter. Tourists raise phones. My body keeps tensing every time I see a camera angle toward us.

Sam tracks it without making it a spectacle. He subtly shifts where he stands so I’m not the obvious subject. He slows when I slow. He pauses when I pause, like he’s matching my nervous system instead of forcing his own pace.

It’s infuriatingly competent.

Noel keeps chirping facts no one asked for. “THIS REINDEER IS SYMBOLIC OF—”

Eben says, “No.”

Noel keeps going anyway.

Then a tourist, phone held high, steps backward without looking and bumps straight into me.

My boot skids.

The world tilts.

My stomach drops.

Sam’s hand catches my forearm immediately. Firm. Steady. No hesitation.

The tourist turns, startled. “Oh my gosh, sorry!”

Sam releases me the second my balance returns, like his touch is a tool, not a claim.

I stand there, cheeks hot, heart thudding.

The tourist’s eyes flick between us with interest. The kind of interest that turns into stories.

I feel the old reflex spike: *Run. Bite. Burn.*

Sam’s voice is calm and neutral. “No worries. Watch your step.”

The tourist nods rapidly and hurries away.

Noel, of course, sees the entire thing and makes a delighted noise like a gremlin.

I shoot him a look.

Noel clamps his lips shut so hard it looks painful.

Progress.

Sam leans in slightly, voice low. “You want my arm.”

I go rigid. “No.”

Sam’s eyes warm, patient. “Okay.”

I take a careful breath. The path ahead is worse. A sheen of ice. People milling. Cameras everywhere. If I fall, it will be a public spectacle. If I don’t fall, I’ll still spend the next hour bracing.

My chest tightens.

I hate the next part, because it makes this my choice.

I swallow hard and say, very quietly, “Not your arm.”

Sam’s brows lift slightly. “Okay.”

I exhale. “Just… walk closer.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Close.”

I glare at him. “Don’t make it cute.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Okay.”

He steps closer, still not touching, but near enough that my brain stops screaming about open space.

We walk like that for the rest of the sculpture line. Parallel. Controlled. Normal enough that no one points.

The world doesn’t end.

Which is suspicious.

By the time Noel declares the walk “a triumph of civic artistry,” I’m exhausted in that strange way that comes from holding yourself together in public.

Sam looks at me, assessing without judgment. “Home.”

I nod once. “Home.”

Noel claps like he’s just watched me complete an obstacle course. “Wes did it! He didn’t even die!”

“I almost did,” I mutter.

Noel beams. “Almost doesn’t count!”

Sam’s voice is calm. “Noel.”

Noel zips his lips again, then points at the schedule board nearby like he can’t help himself. “Tomorrow is Day Eight!”

I don’t like how my body reacts to “tomorrow” now. Not dread, exactly. More like… anticipation with claws.

I mutter, “What is it.”

Noel’s eyes sparkle. “Ice rink charity night.”

I freeze.

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “Skating.”

I whisper, horrified, “I will die.”

Noel beams. “You will wobble. And it will be adorable.”

Sam’s voice stays calm. “He can say no.”

Noel’s smile softens a notch. “He can.”

I stare at Noel like he’s grown a second head.

Noel shrugs. “I’m learning.”

I hate it. I also… don’t hate it.

I swallow and force the words out. “I’ll go.”

Noel brightens like a thousand suns. “YES!”

Sam’s gaze flicks to me, steady. “You chose it.”

I grit my teeth. “Don’t make it a thing.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he agrees.

And my chest tightens again, warm and inconvenient, because I’m starting to understand something I never wanted to learn:

Choice doesn’t trap you.

Choice sets you free.

Which is a disgusting inspirational thought, and I’m going to pretend I didn’t have it.

---

## Chapter 10: The Rink

The ice rink is set up in Evergreen Square like a dare.

String lights hang overhead. Music plays from speakers that crackle like they’re haunted. A little booth sells cocoa and rental skates. Kids zip around like they were born with blades on their feet.

I stand at the edge, staring at the ice like it personally insulted me.

Noel bounces beside me wearing earmuffs shaped like reindeer. “Isn’t it magical?”

“It’s a trap,” I say.

Sam stands nearby, on duty but not looming, calm as a snowdrift. He watches the crowd. He watches the perimeter. He watches me like he’s tracking weather patterns.

“You don’t have to skate,” he says.

I glare. “Noel will force me.”

Noel gasps. “I would never force you.”

I stare at him.

Noel smiles brightly. “I would only *encourage* you with guilt.”

Sam’s voice is calm. “Noel.”

Noel puts a hand to his chest. “Boundaries. Fine. No guilt. Only… gentle peer pressure.”

Sam’s gaze goes flat. “Noel.”

Noel zips his lips and prances away toward the cocoa booth.

I look at the skates. The blades. The laces. The inevitability of humiliation.

I exhale. “I can’t do this.”

Sam’s eyes are steady. “What part.”

“All of it,” I snap, then regret the snap because Sam doesn’t deserve my teeth.

Sam doesn’t flinch. “Okay. Then we don’t.”

The simplicity of it makes my throat tighten.

I swallow hard. “But I said I’d go.”

Sam nods once. “You went. You’re here. That’s already a public step.”

My chest tightens.

He’s right. And I hate that he’s right, because it means this doesn’t have to be all-or-nothing.

I look at the ice again. Kids laughing. Couples holding hands. People being normal about touch like it’s not a weapon.

I swallow. “I could… try. Ten minutes.”

Sam’s gaze warms. “Okay. Ten.”

I narrow my eyes. “That okay counts.”

“It counts,” he says, mouth tipping slightly.

Noel materializes with two cups of cocoa like a summoned devil. “Courage juice.”

I take it and mutter, “If I fall, I’m suing.”

Noel beams. “Sign the waiver in your heart.”

I glare.

Noel backs away, hands up. “I’m silent.”

He is not silent, but he leaves.

Sam gestures toward the skate booth. “Rental.”

I stare. “You’re doing this.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “You chose ten minutes.”

I grind my teeth and march to the booth like I’m going to war.

The teenager behind the counter looks me up and down. “Size?”

“Hell,” I mutter.

Sam’s mouth tips. “Twelve.”

I shoot him a look.

Sam’s gaze stays steady. “You’re a twelve.”

I hate that he knows that.

I hate more that he’s right.

Ten minutes later, I’m standing on the ice in rental skates that feel like torture devices.

My ankles are screaming. My dignity has filed for divorce.

I clutch the railing with both hands.

Sam steps onto the ice smoothly, balanced and calm, like gravity likes him.

He stops a careful distance away. “You want help.”

“No,” I snap automatically.

Sam nods. “Okay.”

I take one step.

My blade skitters.

I grab the railing harder.

Sam doesn’t laugh. Doesn’t comment. Just waits.

I hate waiting. Waiting makes me admit things.

I swallow hard. “How do you… do it.”

Sam’s voice is calm. “Small steps. Bend your knees. Keep your center low.”

“Low,” I repeat, like I’m reading instructions for disarming a bomb.

Sam’s eyes warm. “Yeah.”

I bend my knees slightly. Take another step. Another.

I move like a newborn deer.

Noel, somewhere nearby, makes an excited squeak.

I glare at him from across the rink.

Noel pretends he’s looking at the tree.

Sam skates backward slowly, facing me, matching my pace. “You’re doing it.”

I scowl. “I’m surviving.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Same thing.”

I take another step.

Then a kid zips past too close, clipping my skate.

My foot shoots out.

My stomach drops.

This is it. Public death.

Sam moves instantly, catching me by the elbow. Firm. Steady. No panic.

I clutch his sleeve on reflex.

He stabilizes me, then pauses.

His grip loosens slightly, waiting.

Asking.

I breathe hard. My heart is pounding. My hand is still on his sleeve.

This is… touching.

In public.

My chest tightens like a wire being pulled.

Sam’s voice is low. “Still okay.”

I swallow.

The old Wes wants to jerk away and make a joke and pretend this never happened.

But I’m upright. I’m safe. And the contact is… grounding, not claiming.

I can feel the warmth of his arm through fabric.

I hate how much my body settles.

I whisper, rough, “Yes.”

Sam’s gaze warms, careful. “Okay.”

I don’t let go immediately.

That’s the smallest rebellion against my own fear I’ve ever committed.

Then I release his sleeve and grip the railing again, like I didn’t just do something huge.

Sam doesn’t make it a thing. He just says, low, “We can do the rest with a handhold if you want. For balance.”

My stomach flips.

Handhold. Public. Visible.

I stare at him. “People will talk.”

Sam nods, honest. “Yes.”

I swallow hard. “I don’t want to be… discussed.”

Sam’s eyes soften. “Then we don’t. You can keep the railing. Or we can leave.”

Choice again.

I hate choice. Choice makes me responsible for my own courage.

I glance around. People laughing. Kids skating. Noel pretending not to watch while watching like it’s his religion. Eben standing off the ice with his arms crossed like he’s prepared to throw hands at the concept of fun.

No one is staring at me. Not really. Not yet.

I exhale slowly.

“Not a handhold,” I whisper.

Sam’s brows lift slightly. “Okay.”

I grit my teeth. “Just… two fingers. Like a… safety tether. Not romantic.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Two fingers.”

I glare. “Stop smiling.”

“I’m not,” he says, absolutely lying.

He glides closer, slow, and holds out his hand palm-up like an offering.

I stare at it like it’s a live wire.

Then I do the bravest, dumbest thing I’ve done all week.

I place two fingers into his palm.

Not my whole hand. Not a grip. Just… contact.

Sam’s fingers curl lightly around mine, careful and controlled, and my chest tightens so hard it almost hurts.

He starts moving, slow, guiding me away from the railing.

I take small steps. He matches me. Two fingers between us like a secret.

My pulse is roaring.

But I’m skating. Barely. Still.

And I’m not falling.

And for the first time, “public” doesn’t feel like a firing squad.

It feels like… air.

Noel lets out a sound like he’s going to explode.

Eben barks, “NOEL.”

Noel clamps his mouth shut and vibrates with restrained joy.

Sam leans in slightly, voice low. “Still okay.”

I breathe hard, eyes stinging for no reason I’m willing to admit.

“Yeah,” I whisper. “I’m okay.”

Sam’s gaze warms. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he agrees.

And we glide, slowly, under the lights, two fingers at a time.

Not a declaration.

Not a label.

Just a choice.

Just me, learning that maybe being seen doesn’t have to mean being taken.

---

## Chapter 11: After

By the time my ten minutes are up, my legs feel like I’ve fought a bear.

I wobble back to the edge, release Sam’s hand, and step off the ice like I’m escaping a battlefield.

Noel practically sprints over. “YOU DID IT.”

I glare. “Volume.”

Noel covers his mouth, eyes shining. “Sorry. Sorry. I’m normal.”

He is not normal.

Sam steps off the ice after me, calm as ever, and my body immediately misses the tiny tether like it’s gotten used to something it shouldn’t.

I hate that.

Sam’s gaze flicks to me. “You did good.”

I scowl. “I did two fingers.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Two fingers is still a choice.”

My chest tightens.

Noel whispers, “Two fingers,” like he’s going to write poetry about it.

Eben appears behind Noel and says, deadpan, “Stop.”

Noel stops.

Sam’s voice goes low, for me only. “You want to go.”

I swallow. The rink is still loud, still bright, still public.

I’m proud in a way that makes me want to throw up.

“Yes,” I say. “Home.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

I glare, softer. “Stop.”

“No,” he says, and it sounds like warmth instead of defiance.

We walk back through the snow toward Candle & Crumb.

And it’s stupid, but my fingers still feel the memory of his.

Like my body is keeping proof.

Like I might actually be changing.

Which is inconvenient.

But… not awful.

Not anymore.



## Chapter 9: Ice and Other Bad Ideas (continued)

Noel turns around, spots Sam’s hand having just saved my dignity, and his face lights up like he’s about to set my life on fire for sport.

“Oh!” Noel says, too loud. “LOOK AT THAT. SAFETY. TEAMWORK. TRUST.”

I go still.

Sam’s posture doesn’t change, but his voice goes calm and flat in the way that means he’s done playing. “Noel.”

Noel blinks, mid-gush. “Yes?”

Sam holds his gaze. “Don’t.”

Noel’s smile wobbles. The performative sparkle dims a notch. “Right. Boundaries. I’m learning.”

Eben appears like a summoned consequence. “Stop talking.”

Hugh whispers, delighted, “Aromantic tension.”

Eben turns his head slowly. “Leave.”

Hugh leaves, but not far enough.

Noel clears his throat and pivots hard, pointing at the nearest sculpture like it’s a distraction grenade. “THIS ONE IS A… VERY FESTIVE FISH.”

“It’s a swan,” Eben says.

Noel nods emphatically. “A SWAN-FISH.”

I exhale through my nose, trying not to laugh because laughter is dangerous. Laughter turns into softness and softness turns into people thinking they can touch your life.

Sam glances at me, quick and steady.

“Still okay,” he murmurs.

My chest is tight, but it’s not panic-tight. It’s… awareness-tight. The kind that happens when you realize the world could take something and someone might block it for you.

I swallow. “Yeah.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “Okay.”

I glare weakly. “Stop.”

“No.”

We keep walking.

The path is slick. The ice sculptures glitter. Tourists raise phones. My body keeps tensing every time I see a camera angle toward us.

Sam tracks it without making it a spectacle. He subtly shifts where he stands so I’m not the obvious subject. He slows when I slow. He pauses when I pause, like he’s matching my nervous system instead of forcing his own pace.

It’s infuriatingly competent.

Noel keeps chirping facts no one asked for. “THIS REINDEER IS SYMBOLIC OF—”

Eben says, “No.”

Noel keeps going anyway.

Then a tourist, phone held high, steps backward without looking and bumps straight into me.

My boot skids.

The world tilts.

My stomach drops.

Sam’s hand catches my forearm immediately. Firm. Steady. No hesitation.

The tourist turns, startled. “Oh my gosh, sorry!”

Sam releases me the second my balance returns, like his touch is a tool, not a claim.

I stand there, cheeks hot, heart thudding.

The tourist’s eyes flick between us with interest. The kind of interest that turns into stories.

I feel the old reflex spike: *Run. Bite. Burn.*

Sam’s voice is calm and neutral. “No worries. Watch your step.”

The tourist nods rapidly and hurries away.

Noel, of course, sees the entire thing and makes a delighted noise like a gremlin.

I shoot him a look.

Noel clamps his lips shut so hard it looks painful.

Progress.

Sam leans in slightly, voice low. “You want my arm.”

I go rigid. “No.”

Sam’s eyes warm, patient. “Okay.”

I take a careful breath. The path ahead is worse. A sheen of ice. People milling. Cameras everywhere. If I fall, it will be a public spectacle. If I don’t fall, I’ll still spend the next hour bracing.

My chest tightens.

I hate the next part, because it makes this my choice.

I swallow hard and say, very quietly, “Not your arm.”

Sam’s brows lift slightly. “Okay.”

I exhale. “Just… walk closer.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Close.”

I glare at him. “Don’t make it cute.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Okay.”

He steps closer, still not touching, but near enough that my brain stops screaming about open space.

We walk like that for the rest of the sculpture line. Parallel. Controlled. Normal enough that no one points.

The world doesn’t end.

Which is suspicious.

By the time Noel declares the walk “a triumph of civic artistry,” I’m exhausted in that strange way that comes from holding yourself together in public.

Sam looks at me, assessing without judgment. “Home.”

I nod once. “Home.”

Noel claps like he’s just watched me complete an obstacle course. “Wes did it! He didn’t even die!”

“I almost did,” I mutter.

Noel beams. “Almost doesn’t count!”

Sam’s voice is calm. “Noel.”

Noel zips his lips again, then points at the schedule board nearby like he can’t help himself. “Tomorrow is Day Eight!”

I don’t like how my body reacts to “tomorrow” now. Not dread, exactly. More like… anticipation with claws.

I mutter, “What is it.”

Noel’s eyes sparkle. “Ice rink charity night.”

I freeze.

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “Skating.”

I whisper, horrified, “I will die.”

Noel beams. “You will wobble. And it will be adorable.”

Sam’s voice stays calm. “He can say no.”

Noel’s smile softens a notch. “He can.”

I stare at Noel like he’s grown a second head.

Noel shrugs. “I’m learning.”

I hate it. I also… don’t hate it.

I swallow and force the words out. “I’ll go.”

Noel brightens like a thousand suns. “YES!”

Sam’s gaze flicks to me, steady. “You chose it.”

I grit my teeth. “Don’t make it a thing.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he agrees.

And my chest tightens again, warm and inconvenient, because I’m starting to understand something I never wanted to learn:

Choice doesn’t trap you.

Choice sets you free.

Which is a disgusting inspirational thought, and I’m going to pretend I didn’t have it.

---

## Chapter 10: The Rink

The ice rink is set up in Evergreen Square like a dare.

String lights hang overhead. Music plays from speakers that crackle like they’re haunted. A little booth sells cocoa and rental skates. Kids zip around like they were born with blades on their feet.

I stand at the edge, staring at the ice like it personally insulted me.

Noel bounces beside me wearing earmuffs shaped like reindeer. “Isn’t it magical?”

“It’s a trap,” I say.

Sam stands nearby, on duty but not looming, calm as a snowdrift. He watches the crowd. He watches the perimeter. He watches me like he’s tracking weather patterns.

“You don’t have to skate,” he says.

I glare. “Noel will force me.”

Noel gasps. “I would never force you.”

I stare at him.

Noel smiles brightly. “I would only *encourage* you with guilt.”

Sam’s voice is calm. “Noel.”

Noel puts a hand to his chest. “Boundaries. Fine. No guilt. Only… gentle peer pressure.”

Sam’s gaze goes flat. “Noel.”

Noel zips his lips and prances away toward the cocoa booth.

I look at the skates. The blades. The laces. The inevitability of humiliation.

I exhale. “I can’t do this.”

Sam’s eyes are steady. “What part.”

“All of it,” I snap, then regret the snap because Sam doesn’t deserve my teeth.

Sam doesn’t flinch. “Okay. Then we don’t.”

The simplicity of it makes my throat tighten.

I swallow hard. “But I said I’d go.”

Sam nods once. “You went. You’re here. That’s already a public step.”

My chest tightens.

He’s right. And I hate that he’s right, because it means this doesn’t have to be all-or-nothing.

I look at the ice again. Kids laughing. Couples holding hands. People being normal about touch like it’s not a weapon.

I swallow. “I could… try. Ten minutes.”

Sam’s gaze warms. “Okay. Ten.”

I narrow my eyes. “That okay counts.”

“It counts,” he says, mouth tipping slightly.

Noel materializes with two cups of cocoa like a summoned devil. “Courage juice.”

I take it and mutter, “If I fall, I’m suing.”

Noel beams. “Sign the waiver in your heart.”

I glare.

Noel backs away, hands up. “I’m silent.”

He is not silent, but he leaves.

Sam gestures toward the skate booth. “Rental.”

I stare. “You’re doing this.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “You chose ten minutes.”

I grind my teeth and march to the booth like I’m going to war.

The teenager behind the counter looks me up and down. “Size?”

“Hell,” I mutter.

Sam’s mouth tips. “Twelve.”

I shoot him a look.

Sam’s gaze stays steady. “You’re a twelve.”

I hate that he knows that.

I hate more that he’s right.

Ten minutes later, I’m standing on the ice in rental skates that feel like torture devices.

My ankles are screaming. My dignity has filed for divorce.

I clutch the railing with both hands.

Sam steps onto the ice smoothly, balanced and calm, like gravity likes him.

He stops a careful distance away. “You want help.”

“No,” I snap automatically.

Sam nods. “Okay.”

I take one step.

My blade skitters.

I grab the railing harder.

Sam doesn’t laugh. Doesn’t comment. Just waits.

I hate waiting. Waiting makes me admit things.

I swallow hard. “How do you… do it.”

Sam’s voice is calm. “Small steps. Bend your knees. Keep your center low.”

“Low,” I repeat, like I’m reading instructions for disarming a bomb.

Sam’s eyes warm. “Yeah.”

I bend my knees slightly. Take another step. Another.

I move like a newborn deer.

Noel, somewhere nearby, makes an excited squeak.

I glare at him from across the rink.

Noel pretends he’s looking at the tree.

Sam skates backward slowly, facing me, matching my pace. “You’re doing it.”

I scowl. “I’m surviving.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Same thing.”

I take another step.

Then a kid zips past too close, clipping my skate.

My foot shoots out.

My stomach drops.

This is it. Public death.

Sam moves instantly, catching me by the elbow. Firm. Steady. No panic.

I clutch his sleeve on reflex.

He stabilizes me, then pauses.

His grip loosens slightly, waiting.

Asking.

I breathe hard. My heart is pounding. My hand is still on his sleeve.

This is… touching.

In public.

My chest tightens like a wire being pulled.

Sam’s voice is low. “Still okay.”

I swallow.

The old Wes wants to jerk away and make a joke and pretend this never happened.

But I’m upright. I’m safe. And the contact is… grounding, not claiming.

I can feel the warmth of his arm through fabric.

I hate how much my body settles.

I whisper, rough, “Yes.”

Sam’s gaze warms, careful. “Okay.”

I don’t let go immediately.

That’s the smallest rebellion against my own fear I’ve ever committed.

Then I release his sleeve and grip the railing again, like I didn’t just do something huge.

Sam doesn’t make it a thing. He just says, low, “We can do the rest with a handhold if you want. For balance.”

My stomach flips.

Handhold. Public. Visible.

I stare at him. “People will talk.”

Sam nods, honest. “Yes.”

I swallow hard. “I don’t want to be… discussed.”

Sam’s eyes soften. “Then we don’t. You can keep the railing. Or we can leave.”

Choice again.

I hate choice. Choice makes me responsible for my own courage.

I glance around. People laughing. Kids skating. Noel pretending not to watch while watching like it’s his religion. Eben standing off the ice with his arms crossed like he’s prepared to throw hands at the concept of fun.

No one is staring at me. Not really. Not yet.

I exhale slowly.

“Not a handhold,” I whisper.

Sam’s brows lift slightly. “Okay.”

I grit my teeth. “Just… two fingers. Like a… safety tether. Not romantic.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Two fingers.”

I glare. “Stop smiling.”

“I’m not,” he says, absolutely lying.

He glides closer, slow, and holds out his hand palm-up like an offering.

I stare at it like it’s a live wire.

Then I do the bravest, dumbest thing I’ve done all week.

I place two fingers into his palm.

Not my whole hand. Not a grip. Just… contact.

Sam’s fingers curl lightly around mine, careful and controlled, and my chest tightens so hard it almost hurts.

He starts moving, slow, guiding me away from the railing.

I take small steps. He matches me. Two fingers between us like a secret.

My pulse is roaring.

But I’m skating. Barely. Still.

And I’m not falling.

And for the first time, “public” doesn’t feel like a firing squad.

It feels like… air.

Noel lets out a sound like he’s going to explode.

Eben barks, “NOEL.”

Noel clamps his mouth shut and vibrates with restrained joy.

Sam leans in slightly, voice low. “Still okay.”

I breathe hard, eyes stinging for no reason I’m willing to admit.

“Yeah,” I whisper. “I’m okay.”

Sam’s gaze warms. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he agrees.

And we glide, slowly, under the lights, two fingers at a time.

Not a declaration.

Not a label.

Just a choice.

Just me, learning that maybe being seen doesn’t have to mean being taken.

---

## Chapter 11: After

By the time my ten minutes are up, my legs feel like I’ve fought a bear.

I wobble back to the edge, release Sam’s hand, and step off the ice like I’m escaping a battlefield.

Noel practically sprints over. “YOU DID IT.”

I glare. “Volume.”

Noel covers his mouth, eyes shining. “Sorry. Sorry. I’m normal.”

He is not normal.

Sam steps off the ice after me, calm as ever, and my body immediately misses the tiny tether like it’s gotten used to something it shouldn’t.

I hate that.

Sam’s gaze flicks to me. “You did good.”

I scowl. “I did two fingers.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Two fingers is still a choice.”

My chest tightens.

Noel whispers, “Two fingers,” like he’s going to write poetry about it.

Eben appears behind Noel and says, deadpan, “Stop.”

Noel stops.

Sam’s voice goes low, for me only. “You want to go.”

I swallow. The rink is still loud, still bright, still public.

I’m proud in a way that makes me want to throw up.

“Yes,” I say. “Home.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

I glare, softer. “Stop.”

“No,” he says, and it sounds like warmth instead of defiance.
