And it’s stupid, but my fingers still feel the memory of his.

Like my body is keeping proof.

Like I might actually be changing.

Which is inconvenient.

But… not awful.

Not anymore.



## Chapter 12: Two Fingers, Two Truths

The walk back to Candle & Crumb is quiet in the way that makes my brain want to start inventing problems.

Snow crunches under my boots. The lights in the square fade behind us. The rink music becomes a muffled thump, like the town’s heartbeat finally leaving my ear.

Sam matches my pace without trying to steer me.

Which is, infuriatingly, perfect.

I shove my hands into my coat pockets. My fingers are still buzzing like they’ve been holding a live wire.

Two fingers.

That’s all it was.

And somehow it feels like I just walked into traffic and survived.

Sam glances over, voice low. “You did more than you planned.”

I scowl. “I did exactly ten minutes.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “And the tether.”

I glare. “Don’t call it that.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Okay.”

“Stop.”

“No.”

We reach the bakery. I unlock the door, flip the sign, and let us into the warm quiet like it’s a sanctuary.

My shoulders drop the second the door shuts.

Sam notices. Of course he does.

He takes off his coat. Sets it neatly on the chair. Not like a guest, exactly. Not like he owns the place either. Just… respectful presence.

I don’t know what to do with that.

I clear my throat and default to what I know. “Tea.”

Sam nods. “Okay.”

I shoot him a look. “You’re doing it again.”

He doesn’t even pretend innocence. “Yes.”

I put the kettle on and fuss with mugs so I don’t have to process how my chest keeps tightening every time he exists near me.

Sam watches me for a beat. “You’re quiet.”

“I’m tired,” I say.

Sam’s gaze stays steady. “And.”

I grip the mug too hard. “And I hate that I… liked it.”

Sam doesn’t move closer. Doesn’t pounce on the vulnerability like a greedy animal. He just nods once like I told him I prefer my coffee black.

“You’re allowed,” he says.

I scoff. “Allowed.”

Sam’s voice stays calm. “To like something. To be seen. To not hate every part of it.”

My throat tightens.

I hate that my eyes sting. It’s humiliating.

I scrub a hand over my face. “Don’t psychoanalyze me.”

Sam nods. “Okay.”

I point at him without looking. “Stop.”

“No.”

I pour hot water into mugs. Steam rises. The smell of chamomile fills the space like an attempt at peace.

We sit at the small table in the back, because the front of the bakery still feels like the public world even when it’s empty.

Sam wraps his hands around the mug. “Two fingers,” he says, voice careful.

I flinch.

Sam immediately softens. “Not teasing.”

I swallow. “Okay.”

His mouth tips. “That okay counts.”

I glare. “You’re both ruining language and my life.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Do you regret it.”

I freeze.

Because the answer should be complicated. Because I’m me. Because I don’t trust good things.

But my body remembers standing on the ice, terrified, and then not falling because someone steady was there.

I exhale slowly. “No.”

Sam nods once, like he’s storing it somewhere safe. “Okay.”

I glare out of habit. “Stop.”

“No,” he says, and it’s softer now, less teasing, more… anchored.

I stare at my mug. “It felt… safe.”

Sam’s expression shifts. Not triumph. Not smugness. Just something quiet and serious. “Good.”

My chest tightens harder. “That’s not normal for me.”

Sam’s voice stays low. “I know.”

I look up sharply. “How.”

Sam meets my gaze. “Because you keep telling me what’s hard. And because I watch you brace every time someone looks at you too long.”

My throat tightens. I hate being read like this.

I hate more that it feels like relief.

I whisper, rough, “I don’t want you to think you have to fix it.”

Sam’s gaze stays steady. “I’m not fixing you.”

I blink.

Sam continues, calm and firm. “I’m just here. You’re the one choosing.”

My chest aches.

Choice again.

Always that.

I swallow. “So what happens now.”

Sam leans back slightly, giving space even while answering. “Tomorrow happens. The festival happens. And we keep doing what works.”

I frown. “What works.”

Sam’s voice is quiet. “Consent. Plans. Space. And… small public steps you choose.”

I stare at him, throat tight.

Then I say the thing I didn’t plan to say. “Do you… want more.”

Sam’s brows lift slightly, careful. “More what.”

I swallow hard. “Not just… the festival.”

Sam goes still, like he doesn’t want to scare the question away.

His voice is low. “Yes.”

My chest tightens like a trap snapping shut, except it doesn’t feel like danger. It feels like standing on ice and realizing you can balance.

I whisper, “That’s inconvenient.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I glare weakly. “Stop.”

“No.”

We sit there with the truth between us, warm and terrifying.

Then my phone buzzes.

Because humans can’t have a single quiet emotional breakthrough without technology trying to ruin it.

I glance at the screen.

**UNKNOWN NUMBER:** Still think you can control the narrative?
**UNKNOWN NUMBER:** Cute.
**UNKNOWN NUMBER:** Article drops tomorrow morning.

My stomach goes cold.

My throat closes.

My hands go numb around the mug.

Sam’s posture shifts instantly. “What.”

I swallow. “Grant.”

Sam’s eyes narrow. “He texted you.”

I nod once, shaky.

Sam holds his hand out, palm-up. Not demanding. Asking.

I hesitate, then hand him the phone.

He reads the messages, jaw tightening.

When he looks back up, his voice is calm, but there’s steel under it. “Okay.”

I flinch. “That okay is not comforting.”

Sam sets the phone down gently. “Then here’s the comforting part: we handle it. Together.”

My throat tightens. “I don’t want the town reading about me.”

Sam nods, honest. “I know.”

I whisper, “What if he makes it… a thing.”

Sam’s gaze holds mine. “Then we don’t feed it.”

My chest aches. “What if he posts a photo.”

Sam’s voice stays steady. “If he took one without consent, we address it.”

I shake my head, panic rising. “I can’t do this.”

Sam leans forward slightly, still not touching. “Still okay.”

I swallow hard. My chest is tight, the panic licking at my ribs.

But I’m here. In my bakery. With him.

I breathe in, shaky.

“No,” I whisper. “But… I’m here.”

Sam nods once. “Good. Then we start there.”

---

## Chapter 13: The Article

Morning comes too fast, as usual.

I open the bakery like I always do, because routine is my religion. The ovens hum. The air warms. The world tries to be normal.

It fails.

At 6:12 a.m., Noel bursts in, face pale, phone in hand.

“Oh no,” Noel whispers.

My stomach drops. “What.”

Noel holds the phone out like he’s presenting a corpse.

On the screen is a headline from some regional lifestyle site that thinks it’s important:

**TINSEL TOWN’S FESTIVAL OF LOVE: SMALL-TOWN MAGIC AND A BAKER’S MELT-DOWN**

My vision goes sharp.

“Festival of love,” I say flatly. “He wrote that.”

Noel nods, stricken. “He wrote that.”

My throat tightens. “Read it.”

Noel scrolls, eyes darting, voice quiet. “He calls Candle & Crumb ‘the beating heart of Tinsel Town.’ He describes the cookie exchange. He mentions your ‘strict standards’ at the gingerbread contest.”

I swallow. “Any photos.”

Noel scrolls again, face tightening. “No photos of you. Just… the bakery storefront. The tent. The tree. But…”

He hesitates.

“But what,” I snap.

Noel swallows. “He describes you as ‘icy,’ ‘guarded,’ and… ‘mysteriously close with the town’s most eligible officer.’”

My chest goes cold.

The room tilts.

I grip the counter. “He did not.”

Noel’s eyes shine with anger now, real anger. “He did.”

My stomach churns. The panic spikes, hot and fast. Not because it’s a secret exactly. Because it’s *mine.* Because someone took something I didn’t offer and tried to sell it.

The door chimes.

Sam steps in, calm but alert, like he sensed the weather shift.

He takes one look at my face and Noel’s and goes still.

“What,” Sam says, voice low.

Noel shoves the phone at him.

Sam reads.

His jaw tightens.

When he looks up, his voice is calm enough to be terrifying. “Okay.”

I shake my head, breathing hard. “I can’t.”

Sam sets the phone down gently, then looks at me, steady. “He didn’t post your photo.”

I swallow. “He still… wrote about me.”

Sam nods. “Yes.”

My throat tightens. “People will talk.”

Sam nods again, honest. “Yes.”

I stare at him, furious at how calm he stays when my insides are on fire. “So what do we do.”

Sam’s gaze holds mine. “We do what you wanted. Neutral. No fuel.”

Noel splutters. “But it’s unfair!”

Sam looks at Noel, calm but firm. “It is. And reacting publicly will feed it.”

Noel’s hands curl into fists. “So we just let him.”

Sam’s voice stays even. “We document. We deal with him through the right channels. And we keep Wes safe.”

The word *safe* hits me like a bruise.

My throat tightens. “I don’t want to be handled.”

Sam’s gaze softens immediately. “Okay. Then: we keep you in control.”

I swallow hard.

Noel exhales, shaky. “I can mobilize the town. We can boycott. We can—”

I snap, “Noel, stop.”

Noel freezes.

I force myself to breathe, slow. “No war. No spectacle.”

Noel’s eyes widen. “But he hurt you.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

Saying it out loud makes my chest ache.

Sam’s voice is low. “You want to keep it small.”

I nod once, shaky. “Small.”

Sam nods. “Okay.”

Noel looks between us, then slowly nods too, swallowing his anger. “Okay. Small.”

I stare at the ovens and try to remember how to be a person.

Sam steps closer, careful. “Back room.”

I whisper, “Yes.”

---

In the back, the quiet hits like a wall.

My hands shake. I press them flat to the counter.

Sam stands a few feet away, giving space, voice low. “Still okay.”

I swallow. “No.”

Sam nods. “Okay.”

I glare at him through the panic. “Stop saying okay like it fixes anything.”

Sam’s eyes warm, serious. “It doesn’t fix it. It anchors it.”

My throat tightens.

I whisper, rough, “I feel like… everyone’s allowed to have an opinion on me.”

Sam’s voice stays calm. “They’re not.”

“But they will,” I say, voice cracking. “They always do.”

Sam nods, honest. “Yes.”

I squeeze my eyes shut. “I hate this.”

Sam’s voice lowers. “I know.”

Silence stretches.

Then Sam says, steady, “Do you want to shut the bakery today.”

My chest tightens.

Closing feels like losing.

But staying open feels like standing in the street.

I swallow hard. “No. I want… normal.”

Sam nods once. “Okay. Then I stay nearby.”

I blink. “You can’t just… linger.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “I can be on patrol.”

I exhale, shaky laugh. “You’re ridiculous.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Okay.”

I huff. “Stop.”

“No.”

I wipe my face hard and force myself to stand upright.

My voice is rough. “If anyone says something, I will bite.”

Sam’s gaze holds mine. “Then I redirect.”

I swallow. “Neutral.”

Sam nods. “Neutral.”

I breathe in, shaky, and nod toward the door. “Let’s go.”

---

The morning rush comes.

People file in.

And the miracle is: most of them act normal.

Mrs. Alden orders her usual and pretends she’s not burning with questions. A couple of tourists buy cinnamon rolls and leave. A kid asks if we have sprinkles.

Normal.

Then, inevitably, someone tests it.

A woman I don’t know, phone in hand, leans toward the counter with a too-bright smile.

“I read the article,” she says. “You’re famous!”

My stomach drops.

I feel my teeth sharpen.

Sam shifts subtly at my side, present but not crowding.

I take a slow breath.

Neutral.

“No,” I say flatly. “I’m a baker.”

The woman blinks. “But it said—”

Sam’s voice cuts in, calm. “He’s working. Please order or step aside.”

The woman flushes, startled, and mutters an order.

I ring it up. Hand over the bag. Transaction. Done.

My hands are still shaking, but I didn’t explode.

Sam glances at me, quick check. “Still okay.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

And the word feels like power instead of denial.

---

## Chapter 14: Candlelight

That night, the festival has a candlelight walk.

Noel calls it “a gentle moment of collective warmth.” Eben calls it “a fire hazard.” I call it “public emotional terrorism.”

But I show up anyway, because apparently I’m making terrible choices now.

Evergreen Square glows with tiny lights. People hold candles in paper cups. The air is cold enough to sting.

I stand near the edge of the crowd, hood up, hands in pockets, trying to be invisible.

Sam is there, on duty, scanning the perimeter.

He spots me and starts to move toward me, then pauses like he’s remembering the rules.

No touching.

No spectacle.

He stops a careful distance away, close enough that my body settles anyway.

Noel appears like an angel with a clipboard. “You came.”

I glare. “Don’t.”

Noel holds up both hands. “No commentary. Boundaries.”

Eben stands nearby, arms crossed, watching the crowd like he’s prepared to punch gossip in the mouth.

The mayor says a few words. People murmur. Candles flicker.

Then the crowd starts walking, slow, a river of light through the square.

I walk too, at the edge.

Halfway through, someone near me whispers to their friend, “That’s him. The baker.”

My stomach drops.

The old panic flares.

Sam’s voice goes low, for me only. “Keep walking.”

I swallow and do.

Another whisper. “And that’s the officer.”

My chest tightens.

Sam shifts slightly closer, not touching, but aligning himself between me and the worst angles of the crowd.

Neutral.

No fuel.

We keep walking.

And then, without warning, a gust of wind blows through the square and snuffs a cluster of candles.

Someone yelps.

The crowd stutters.

In the confusion, a kid stumbles toward me, candle tilting dangerously.

Instinct hits.

I reach out, steady the kid’s candle cup so it doesn’t spill wax. My hand brushes the kid’s glove.

The kid looks up, wide-eyed. “Thanks!”

A couple of adults nearby see it. Smile. Not nosy. Just… human.

My chest tightens in a new way.

Because that’s being seen too.

And it’s not awful.

Sam’s voice is low beside me. “Still okay.”

I swallow hard.

Then I do something reckless.

I answer with truth, not armor.

“Yeah,” I whisper. “I’m okay.”

Sam nods once, and for a second, his hand hovers near mine in the dark, not touching, asking.

A choice.

My heart pounds.

I look around. Candles. Darkness. People focused forward. No one staring.

And I’m so tired of living like every gentle thing is a trap.

I inhale, then slide my gloved hand into his.

Not two fingers.

A full hand.

For one steady, silent beat.

Sam’s hand closes around mine, careful and warm, like he understands the weight of what I just did.

Then, before the moment can become a story for anyone else, I let go.

I keep walking.

My heart is roaring.

Sam doesn’t react. Doesn’t smile. Doesn’t squeeze like a claim.

He just stays near, like he always does.

And my chest aches, not with panic.

With something that feels dangerously like hope.

So, naturally, I hate it.

But I keep walking anyway.




## Chapter 15: Handprint

I let go of Sam’s hand and keep walking like I didn’t just do something that would’ve made Old Me spontaneously combust.

The candlelight river keeps moving. People murmur softly. Someone laughs up ahead. The world pretends it’s gentle and harmless, like it hasn’t spent my whole life proving otherwise.

My palm still remembers his.

Sam stays close, not touching, not pressing, just… there. Like he understands the rule: if you make it a thing, it becomes a thing. And then the town owns it.

We finish the walk. People blow out candles. Noel cries a little because Noel treats shared oxygen like a spiritual experience.

Eben stands in the snow looking like he wants to arrest sentimentality.

The crowd disperses.

And somehow, no one stops me.

No one corners me with a question. No one asks if the baker and the officer are “together” like it’s their business. No one reaches for their phone like I’m content.

My nervous system doesn’t know what to do with that.

I walk back toward the bakery, boots crunching, shoulders tight, heart still hammering.

Sam keeps pace beside me, still not touching.

Halfway there, he says quietly, “You did that.”

I stare straight ahead. “Did what.”

Sam’s voice stays low. “Chose it.”

My throat tightens, hot and stupid. “It was one second.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “One second is still a choice.”

I hate him for saying it like it’s not terrifying.

I mutter, “Don’t make it inspirational.”

Sam’s voice is calm. “Okay.”

I point at him without looking. “Stop.”

“No.”

We reach Candle & Crumb. I unlock the door and let us in, warmth hitting my face like relief.

I lock it behind us.

The quiet of the bakery settles around my shoulders.

Sam takes off his coat. Hangs it neatly. The routine of him here makes my chest ache in a way I don’t want to interrogate.

I clear my throat. “Tea.”

Sam nods. “Okay.”

I glare. “You’re enjoying that word too much.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Yes.”

I make tea because it gives my hands something to do besides shake.

We end up in the back again, mugs steaming, the world outside muffled.

I stare at my cup for a long time, then finally mutter, “I didn’t mean to.”

Sam’s brows lift slightly. “You didn’t mean to hold my hand.”

“I meant to,” I correct immediately, because I’m not a liar. “I didn’t mean to… do it where it mattered.”

Sam’s gaze holds mine. “It mattered because you chose it.”

My throat tightens. I look away, annoyed at the sting behind my eyes.

“I hate that it mattered,” I mutter.

Sam’s voice stays steady. “Why.”

“Because,” I say, rough, “if it matters, then the next part matters. And then the part after that. And suddenly I’m… visible.”

Sam nods once, like he’s heard this a thousand times already from the way my body flinches. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

Silence stretches.

Then Sam says quietly, “Do you want to talk about the article.”

My stomach drops.

I swallow hard. “No.”

Sam nods. “Okay.”

My chest tightens. “But we have to.”

Sam’s gaze is calm. “We do. When you’re ready.”

I hate that he gives me time. Time makes me responsible for my own courage.

I take a slow breath.

“Tomorrow,” I say. “We deal with it tomorrow.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he agrees.

I sip tea and stare at nothing for a minute, then hear myself say, “I didn’t hate tonight.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Good.”

I swallow. “I also didn’t hate… the hand.”

Sam goes still, careful. “Okay.”

I glare reflexively. “Don’t you dare make a face.”

Sam’s mouth tips, faint. “I’m not.”

He is. The liar.

I exhale. “Still okay.”

Sam’s brows lift slightly, warmed. “Yes.”

And in that stupid moment, with tea and warm light and the ovens humming like they’re eavesdropping, I realize something awful:

I’m starting to trust that “yes.”

Which is, frankly, terrible for my brand.

---

## Chapter 16: The Plan

Morning comes with snow and consequences.

Noel arrives at 6:30 a.m. like a man who has not slept and has instead been possessed by civic rage.

He slams his phone on the counter. “We are not letting this stand.”

I don’t look at it. “Noel.”

Noel points at the screen anyway. “He updated it! He added a line about ‘holiday romance blooming under twinkle lights’!”

I go cold.

Sam steps in from the doorway, on duty, calm but sharp. “What’s the plan.”

Noel blinks at him like he’s never seen an adult in the room before. “Plan?”

Sam’s gaze stays steady. “We said neutral. No fuel. But neutral doesn’t mean passive.”

Noel inhales. “Yes. Correct. I am being neutral.”

Noel is not being neutral. Noel is vibrating.

Sam turns to me. “You want control.”

I swallow hard. “Yes.”

Sam nods. “Okay. Then you decide what we do today.”

My chest tightens. The urge to run is immediate. Because deciding means committing. Committing means being seen.

But I also feel last night in my palm, like proof I can survive a choice.

I breathe in, slow.

“We don’t talk about it,” I say. “Not with customers. Not with the town. We keep the bakery normal.”

Noel looks personally offended. “But—”

Sam’s voice cuts in, calm. “Okay. That’s step one.”

I continue, because if I stop, I’ll fall apart. “Step two: we document everything. Screenshots. Dates. Whatever.”

Sam nods. “Already started.”

Noel’s eyes widen. “Oh.”

I swallow. “Step three…”

My throat tightens, because step three is the part that feels like swallowing glass.

“If Grant shows up,” I say, voice rough, “I want him out. Quietly.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

Noel opens his mouth, probably to offer violence.

I look at him. “Noel.”

Noel clamps his lips shut.

Progress. Barely.

Sam’s gaze stays on mine. “Anything else.”

I hesitate.

Because there’s a fourth step that scares me more than Grant.

The town.

Their curiosity. Their whispers. Their “just asking.”

The way attention can turn into ownership if you let it.

I exhale slowly. “If someone brings it up… we redirect. We keep it boring.”

Sam nods. “Neutral.”

Noel makes a strangled noise. “But we could also bury it with a better story.”

I stiffen. “Noel.”

Noel raises both hands. “Not about you. Not about Sam. About the festival. About the charities. About the community. I can flood the internet with cinnamon roll content.”

I stare at him.

Noel’s eyes are wide and earnest. “Let him write his weird little article. We drown it in wholesome.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “That’s… actually neutral.”

Noel beams, vindicated. “THANK YOU.”

I stare at Sam. “Is it neutral.”

Sam’s gaze stays steady. “It’s not a reaction. It’s a replacement.”

My chest tightens.

I hate that it sounds smart.

I exhale. “Fine. Flood the internet with cinnamon roll content. But if you mention me, I will end you.”

Noel nods fast. “No names. No hints. Just buns.”

I pinch the bridge of my nose. “That sentence shouldn’t exist.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

---

The bakery opens.

Customers come.

And the miracle continues: most people act normal.

A few linger too long. A few eyes flick to Sam, then to me, then away. But no one says anything outright.

Until, of course, someone does.

A middle-aged guy in a puffy coat steps up to the counter, smirking. “So, Wes. The article, huh.”

My stomach drops.

Old Wes rises like a demon.

But Sam’s presence is at my side, steady and quiet. A reminder: neutral doesn’t mean helpless.

I take a slow breath.

“I don’t discuss my private life at work,” I say flatly.

The guy blinks, surprised by the firmness.

Then Sam adds, calm and polite, “What can we get you.”

The guy flushes, mutters, “Uh. Two cinnamon rolls.”

I ring him up. Hand him the bag. End of story.

My hands shake under the counter, but my face stays still.

The guy leaves.

No spectacle.

No spiral.

Sam glances at me, quick check. “Still okay.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

And it’s true.

Because I’m holding the line.

---

## Chapter 17: Grant Holloway Gets Bored

At 11:40 a.m., the bell chimes.

Grant Holloway strolls in like he owns the air.

He’s wearing that expensive coat again, hair perfect, smile sharp.

My stomach drops so hard I feel it in my knees.

Noel is in the corner frosting something aggressively, jaw set.

Sam is by the door immediately, posture calm but unmistakably blocking.

Grant’s gaze flicks to Sam, then to me, then back, like he’s assessing where the leverage is.

“Officer,” Grant says smoothly. “Didn’t expect you here.”

Sam’s voice is flat. “Leave.”

Grant laughs softly. “This again.”

Sam doesn’t move. “This again.”

Grant’s eyes narrow. “I’m here to speak with Wes.”

Sam’s gaze stays steady. “He didn’t consent.”

Grant’s smile tightens. “Consent. That’s adorable.”

Something cold settles in my chest.

Noel makes a low sound in his throat, like a dog about to bite.

I step forward before Noel commits a felony in my bakery.

My voice comes out steady in a way that surprises me. “Grant.”

Grant’s smile widens. “Wes. There he is.”

I keep my face flat. “You wrote about me.”

Grant spreads his hands. “I wrote about a charming town and its charming characters.”

I feel my teeth want to show. I clamp down on it. Neutral.

“You implied things,” I say. “You invited speculation.”

Grant shrugs. “People speculate. It drives clicks.”

Sam’s voice is calm. “Leave.”

Grant ignores him, eyes on me. “I could also write a follow-up. A correction. If you’d like. A nice quote from you, perhaps.”

My stomach turns.

He wants me to dance.

He wants me to validate the story he stole.

I breathe in, slow.

“No,” I say.

Grant blinks. “No?”

“No,” I repeat. “You don’t get my words.”

Grant’s smile falters for half a second.

Sam’s posture doesn’t change, but the air shifts. Like a door closing.

Grant’s eyes flick to Sam, irritated. “You’re very protective.”

Sam’s voice is flat. “Leave.”

Grant’s jaw tightens. “You can’t stop the internet.”

I stare at him. “No. But I can stop you from being in my bakery.”

Grant looks offended, like I’ve broken the script.

Then, because he can’t resist being cruel, he adds lightly, “People love a mystery. Especially when it involves small-town romance.”

My chest tightens, but I don’t flinch.

I just say, calm as ice, “Get out.”

Noel steps up behind me, voice sweet and lethal. “Grant, darling. Go away.”

Grant looks between us, realizes the room has turned against him, and his smile goes brittle.

He lifts his hands. “Fine. Keep your secrets. But don’t be surprised when the town fills in the blanks.”

He pivots toward the door.

Sam doesn’t move until Grant is right in front of him, then says quietly, “If you contact Wes again, it won’t be friendly.”

Grant pauses, eyes narrowing. “Is that a threat, Officer.”

Sam’s voice stays calm. “It’s a boundary.”

Grant huffs a laugh and leaves.

The bell chimes.

The door shuts.

And the bakery doesn’t collapse.

Noel exhales shakily. “I hate him.”

I swallow. “Me too.”

Sam turns to me, gaze steady. “Still okay.”

My hands are shaking, but my chest isn’t on fire the way it used to be.

Because this time, I didn’t freeze.

I didn’t disappear.

I held the line.

“Yes,” I say, rough. “I’m okay.”

Sam nods once like he’s proud without making it a performance.

Noel points at me, eyes bright. “You kicked him out.”

I glare. “Don’t.”

Noel clamps his mouth shut and does a tiny silent dance anyway.

Sam’s mouth tips slightly.

I hate that my chest warms.

I hate more that it feels… earned.

Not because I earned being treated well.

Because I earned my own control back.

And for the first time, that feels like something I can build a life on.

Not a headline.

Not a rumor.

A choice.

A line.

A hand I can take when I decide.

So, naturally, I’m terrified.

But I stay.

And that’s new.

**[STOP WRITING]**



## Chapter 15: Handprint

I let go of Sam’s hand and keep walking like I didn’t just do something that would’ve made Old Me spontaneously combust.

The candlelight river keeps moving. People murmur softly. Someone laughs up ahead. The world pretends it’s gentle and harmless, like it hasn’t spent my whole life proving otherwise.

My palm still remembers his.

Sam stays close, not touching, not pressing, just… there. Like he understands the rule: if you make it a thing, it becomes a thing. And then the town owns it.

We finish the walk. People blow out candles. Noel cries a little because Noel treats shared oxygen like a spiritual experience.

Eben stands in the snow looking like he wants to arrest sentimentality.

The crowd disperses.

And somehow, no one stops me.

No one corners me with a question. No one asks if the baker and the officer are “together” like it’s their business. No one reaches for their phone like I’m content.

My nervous system doesn’t know what to do with that.

I walk back toward the bakery, boots crunching, shoulders tight, heart still hammering.

Sam keeps pace beside me, still not touching.

Halfway there, he says quietly, “You did that.”

I stare straight ahead. “Did what.”

Sam’s voice stays low. “Chose it.”

My throat tightens, hot and stupid. “It was one second.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “One second is still a choice.”

I hate him for saying it like it’s not terrifying.

I mutter, “Don’t make it inspirational.”

Sam’s voice is calm. “Okay.”

I point at him without looking. “Stop.”

“No.”

I lock it behind us.

Sam nods. “Okay.”

I glare. “You’re enjoying that word too much.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Yes.”

“I meant to,” I correct immediately, because I’m not a liar. “I didn’t mean to… do it where it mattered.”

Sam’s gaze holds mine. “It mattered because you chose it.”

My throat tightens. I look away, annoyed at the sting behind my eyes.

“I hate that it mattered,” I mutter.

Sam’s voice stays steady. “Why.”

“Because,” I say, rough, “if it matters, then the next part matters. And then the part after that. And suddenly I’m… visible.”

Sam nods once, like he’s heard this a thousand times already from the way my body flinches. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

Silence stretches.

Then Sam says quietly, “Do you want to talk about the article.”

My stomach drops.

I swallow hard. “No.”

Sam nods. “Okay.”

My chest tightens. “But we have to.”

Sam’s gaze is calm. “We do. When you’re ready.”

I hate that he gives me time. Time makes me responsible for my own courage.

I take a slow breath.

“Tomorrow,” I say. “We deal with it tomorrow.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he agrees.

Sam’s eyes warm. “Good.”

I swallow. “I also didn’t hate… the hand.”

Sam goes still, careful. “Okay.”

Sam’s mouth tips, faint. “I’m not.”

He is. The liar.

I exhale. “Still okay.”

Sam’s brows lift slightly, warmed. “Yes.”

And in that stupid moment, with tea and warm light and the ovens humming like they’re eavesdropping, I realize something awful:

I’m starting to trust that “yes.”

Which is, frankly, terrible for my brand.

---

## Chapter 16: The Plan

Morning comes with snow and consequences.

Noel arrives at 6:30 a.m. like a man who has not slept and has instead been possessed by civic rage.

He slams his phone on the counter. “We are not letting this stand.”

I don’t look at it. “Noel.”

Noel points at the screen anyway. “He updated it! He added a line about ‘holiday romance blooming under twinkle lights’!”

I go cold.

Sam steps in from the doorway, on duty, calm but sharp. “What’s the plan.”

Noel blinks at him like he’s never seen an adult in the room before. “Plan?”

Sam’s gaze stays steady. “We said neutral. No fuel. But neutral doesn’t mean passive.”

Noel inhales. “Yes. Correct. I am being neutral.”

Noel is not being neutral. Noel is vibrating.

Sam turns to me. “You want control.”

I swallow hard. “Yes.”

Sam nods. “Okay. Then you decide what we do today.”

My chest tightens. The urge to run is immediate. Because deciding means committing. Committing means being seen.

But I also feel last night in my palm, like proof I can survive a choice.

I breathe in, slow.

“We don’t talk about it,” I say. “Not with customers. Not with the town. We keep the bakery normal.”

Noel looks personally offended. “But—”

Sam’s voice cuts in, calm. “Okay. That’s step one.”

I continue, because if I stop, I’ll fall apart. “Step two: we document everything. Screenshots. Dates. Whatever.”

Sam nods. “Already started.”

Noel’s eyes widen. “Oh.”

I swallow. “Step three…”

My throat tightens, because step three is the part that feels like swallowing glass.

“If Grant shows up,” I say, voice rough, “I want him out. Quietly.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

Noel opens his mouth, probably to offer violence.

I look at him. “Noel.”

Noel clamps his lips shut.

Progress. Barely.

Sam’s gaze stays on mine. “Anything else.”

I hesitate.

Because there’s a fourth step that scares me more than Grant.

The town.

Their curiosity. Their whispers. Their “just asking.”

The way attention can turn into ownership if you let it.

I exhale slowly. “If someone brings it up… we redirect. We keep it boring.”

Sam nods. “Neutral.”

Noel makes a strangled noise. “But we could also bury it with a better story.”

I stiffen. “Noel.”

Noel raises both hands. “Not about you. Not about Sam. About the festival. About the charities. About the community. I can flood the internet with cinnamon roll content.”

I stare at him.

Noel’s eyes are wide and earnest. “Let him write his weird little article. We drown it in wholesome.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “That’s… actually neutral.”

Noel beams, vindicated. “THANK YOU.”

I stare at Sam. “Is it neutral.”

Sam’s gaze stays steady. “It’s not a reaction. It’s a replacement.”

My chest tightens.

I hate that it sounds smart.

I exhale. “Fine. Flood the internet with cinnamon roll content. But if you mention me, I will end you.”

Noel nods fast. “No names. No hints. Just buns.”

I pinch the bridge of my nose. “That sentence shouldn’t exist.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

---

The bakery opens.

Customers come.

And the miracle continues: most people act normal.

A few linger too long. A few eyes flick to Sam, then to me, then away. But no one says anything outright.

Until, of course, someone does.

A middle-aged guy in a puffy coat steps up to the counter, smirking. “So, Wes. The article, huh.”

My stomach drops.

Old Wes rises like a demon.

But Sam’s presence is at my side, steady and quiet. A reminder: neutral doesn’t mean helpless.

I take a slow breath.

“I don’t discuss my private life at work,” I say flatly.

The guy blinks, surprised by the firmness.

Then Sam adds, calm and polite, “What can we get you.”

The guy flushes, mutters, “Uh. Two cinnamon rolls.”

I ring him up. Hand him the bag. End of story.

My hands shake under the counter, but my face stays still.

The guy leaves.

No spectacle.

No spiral.

Sam glances at me, quick check. “Still okay.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

And it’s true.

Because I’m holding the line.

---

## Chapter 17: Grant Holloway Gets Bored

At 11:40 a.m., the bell chimes.

Grant Holloway strolls in like he owns the air.

He’s wearing that expensive coat again, hair perfect, smile sharp.

My stomach drops so hard I feel it in my knees.

Noel is in the corner frosting something aggressively, jaw set.

Sam is by the door immediately, posture calm but unmistakably blocking.

Grant’s gaze flicks to Sam, then to me, then back, like he’s assessing where the leverage is.

“Officer,” Grant says smoothly. “Didn’t expect you here.”

Sam’s voice is flat. “Leave.”

Grant laughs softly. “This again.”

Sam doesn’t move. “This again.”

Grant’s eyes narrow. “I’m here to speak with Wes.”

Sam’s gaze stays steady. “He didn’t consent.”

Grant’s smile tightens. “Consent. That’s adorable.”

Something cold settles in my chest.

Noel makes a low sound in his throat, like a dog about to bite.

I step forward before Noel commits a felony in my bakery.

My voice comes out steady in a way that surprises me. “Grant.”

Grant’s smile widens. “Wes. There he is.”

I keep my face flat. “You wrote about me.”

Grant spreads his hands. “I wrote about a charming town and its charming characters.”

I feel my teeth want to show. I clamp down on it. Neutral.

“You implied things,” I say. “You invited speculation.”

Grant shrugs. “People speculate. It drives clicks.”

Sam’s voice is calm. “Leave.”

Grant ignores him, eyes on me. “I could also write a follow-up. A correction. If you’d like. A nice quote from you, perhaps.”

My stomach turns.

He wants me to dance.

He wants me to validate the story he stole.

I breathe in, slow.

“No,” I say.

Grant blinks. “No?”

“No,” I repeat. “You don’t get my words.”

Grant’s smile falters for half a second.

Sam’s posture doesn’t change, but the air shifts. Like a door closing.

Grant’s eyes flick to Sam, irritated. “You’re very protective.”

Sam’s voice is flat. “Leave.”

Grant’s jaw tightens. “You can’t stop the internet.”

I stare at him. “No. But I can stop you from being in my bakery.”

Grant looks offended, like I’ve broken the script.

Then, because he can’t resist being cruel, he adds lightly, “People love a mystery. Especially when it involves small-town romance.”

My chest tightens, but I don’t flinch.

I just say, calm as ice, “Get out.”

Noel steps up behind me, voice sweet and lethal. “Grant, darling. Go away.”

Grant looks between us, realizes the room has turned against him, and his smile goes brittle.

He lifts his hands. “Fine. Keep your secrets. But don’t be surprised when the town fills in the blanks.”

He pivots toward the door.

Sam doesn’t move until Grant is right in front of him, then says quietly, “If you contact Wes again, it won’t be friendly.”

Grant pauses, eyes narrowing. “Is that a threat, Officer.”

Sam’s voice stays calm. “It’s a boundary.”

Grant huffs a laugh and leaves.

The bell chimes.

The door shuts.

And the bakery doesn’t collapse.

Noel exhales shakily. “I hate him.”

I swallow. “Me too.”

Sam turns to me, gaze steady. “Still okay.”

My hands are shaking, but my chest isn’t on fire the way it used to be.

Because this time, I didn’t freeze.

I didn’t disappear.

I held the line.

“Yes,” I say, rough. “I’m okay.”

Sam nods once like he’s proud without making it a performance.

Noel points at me, eyes bright. “You kicked him out.”

I glare. “Don’t.”

Noel clamps his mouth shut and does a tiny silent dance anyway.

Sam’s mouth tips slightly.

I hate that my chest warms.

I hate more that it feels… earned.

Not because I earned being treated well.

Because I earned my own control back.

And for the first time, that feels like something I can build a life on.

Not a headline.

Not a rumor.

A choice.

A line.

A hand I can take when I decide.

So, naturally, I’m terrified.

But I stay.

And that’s new.

**[STOP WRITING]**




## Chapter 15: Handprint

I let go of Sam’s hand and keep walking like I didn’t just do something that would’ve made Old Me spontaneously combust.

The candlelight river keeps moving. People murmur softly. Someone laughs up ahead. The world pretends it’s gentle and harmless, like it hasn’t spent my whole life proving otherwise.

My palm still remembers his.

Sam stays close, not touching, not pressing, just… there. Like he understands the rule: if you make it a thing, it becomes a thing. And then the town owns it.

We finish the walk. People blow out candles. Noel cries a little because Noel treats shared oxygen like a spiritual experience.

Eben stands in the snow looking like he wants to arrest sentimentality.

The crowd disperses.

And somehow, no one stops me.

No one corners me with a question. No one asks if the baker and the officer are “together” like it’s their business. No one reaches for their phone like I’m content.

My nervous system doesn’t know what to do with that.

I walk back toward the bakery, boots crunching, shoulders tight, heart still hammering.

Sam keeps pace beside me, still not touching.

Halfway there, he says quietly, “You did that.”

I stare straight ahead. “Did what.”

Sam’s voice stays low. “Chose it.”

My throat tightens, hot and stupid. “It was one second.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “One second is still a choice.”

I hate him for saying it like it’s not terrifying.

I mutter, “Don’t make it inspirational.”

Sam’s voice is calm. “Okay.”

I point at him without looking. “Stop.”

“No.”

We reach Candle & Crumb. I unlock the door and let us in, warmth hitting my face like relief.

I lock it behind us.

The quiet of the bakery settles around my shoulders.

Sam takes off his coat. Hangs it neatly. The routine of him here makes my chest ache in a way I don’t want to interrogate.

I clear my throat. “Tea.”

Sam nods. “Okay.”

I glare. “You’re enjoying that word too much.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Yes.”

I make tea because it gives my hands something to do besides shake.

We end up in the back again, mugs steaming, the world outside muffled.

I stare at my cup for a long time, then finally mutter, “I didn’t mean to.”

Sam’s brows lift slightly. “You didn’t mean to hold my hand.”

“I meant to,” I correct immediately, because I’m not a liar. “I didn’t mean to… do it where it mattered.”

Sam’s gaze holds mine. “It mattered because you chose it.”

My throat tightens. I look away, annoyed at the sting behind my eyes.

“I hate that it mattered,” I mutter.

Sam’s voice stays steady. “Why.”

“Because,” I say, rough, “if it matters, then the next part matters. And then the part after that. And suddenly I’m… visible.”

Sam nods once, like he’s heard this a thousand times already from the way my body flinches. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

Silence stretches.

Then Sam says quietly, “Do you want to talk about the article.”

My stomach drops.

I swallow hard. “No.”

Sam nods. “Okay.”

My chest tightens. “But we have to.”

Sam’s gaze is calm. “We do. When you’re ready.”

I hate that he gives me time. Time makes me responsible for my own courage.

I take a slow breath.

“Tomorrow,” I say. “We deal with it tomorrow.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he agrees.

I sip tea and stare at nothing for a minute, then hear myself say, “I didn’t hate tonight.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Good.”

I swallow. “I also didn’t hate… the hand.”

Sam goes still, careful. “Okay.”

I glare reflexively. “Don’t you dare make a face.”

Sam’s mouth tips, faint. “I’m not.”

He is. The liar.

I exhale. “Still okay.”

Sam’s brows lift slightly, warmed. “Yes.”

And in that stupid moment, with tea and warm light and the ovens humming like they’re eavesdropping, I realize something awful:

I’m starting to trust that “yes.”

Which is, frankly, terrible for my brand.

---

## Chapter 16: The Plan

Morning comes with snow and consequences.

Noel arrives at 6:30 a.m. like a man who has not slept and has instead been possessed by civic rage.

He slams his phone on the counter. “We are not letting this stand.”

I don’t look at it. “Noel.”

Noel points at the screen anyway. “He updated it! He added a line about ‘holiday romance blooming under twinkle lights’!”

I go cold.

Sam steps in from the doorway, on duty, calm but sharp. “What’s the plan.”

Noel blinks at him like he’s never seen an adult in the room before. “Plan?”

Sam’s gaze stays steady. “We said neutral. No fuel. But neutral doesn’t mean passive.”

Noel inhales. “Yes. Correct. I am being neutral.”

Noel is not being neutral. Noel is vibrating.

Sam turns to me. “You want control.”

I swallow hard. “Yes.”

Sam nods. “Okay. Then you decide what we do today.”

My chest tightens. The urge to run is immediate. Because deciding means committing. Committing means being seen.

But I also feel last night in my palm, like proof I can survive a choice.

I breathe in, slow.

“We don’t talk about it,” I say. “Not with customers. Not with the town. We keep the bakery normal.”

Noel looks personally offended. “But—”

Sam’s voice cuts in, calm. “Okay. That’s step one.”

I continue, because if I stop, I’ll fall apart. “Step two: we document everything. Screenshots. Dates. Whatever.”

Sam nods. “Already started.”

Noel’s eyes widen. “Oh.”

I swallow. “Step three…”

My throat tightens, because step three is the part that feels like swallowing glass.

“If Grant shows up,” I say, voice rough, “I want him out. Quietly.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

Noel opens his mouth, probably to offer violence.

I look at him. “Noel.”

Noel clamps his lips shut.

Progress. Barely.

Sam’s gaze stays on mine. “Anything else.”

I hesitate.

Because there’s a fourth step that scares me more than Grant.

The town.

Their curiosity. Their whispers. Their “just asking.”

The way attention can turn into ownership if you let it.

I exhale slowly. “If someone brings it up… we redirect. We keep it boring.”

Sam nods. “Neutral.”

Noel makes a strangled noise. “But we could also bury it with a better story.”

I stiffen. “Noel.”

Noel raises both hands. “Not about you. Not about Sam. About the festival. About the charities. About the community. I can flood the internet with cinnamon roll content.”

I stare at him.

Noel’s eyes are wide and earnest. “Let him write his weird little article. We drown it in wholesome.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “That’s… actually neutral.”

Noel beams, vindicated. “THANK YOU.”

I stare at Sam. “Is it neutral.”

Sam’s gaze stays steady. “It’s not a reaction. It’s a replacement.”

My chest tightens.

I hate that it sounds smart.

I exhale. “Fine. Flood the internet with cinnamon roll content. But if you mention me, I will end you.”

Noel nods fast. “No names. No hints. Just buns.”

I pinch the bridge of my nose. “That sentence shouldn’t exist.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

---

The bakery opens.

Customers come.

And the miracle continues: most people act normal.

A few linger too long. A few eyes flick to Sam, then to me, then away. But no one says anything outright.

Until, of course, someone does.

A middle-aged guy in a puffy coat steps up to the counter, smirking. “So, Wes. The article, huh.”

My stomach drops.

Old Wes rises like a demon.

But Sam’s presence is at my side, steady and quiet. A reminder: neutral doesn’t mean helpless.

I take a slow breath.

“I don’t discuss my private life at work,” I say flatly.

The guy blinks, surprised by the firmness.

Then Sam adds, calm and polite, “What can we get you.”

The guy flushes, mutters, “Uh. Two cinnamon rolls.”

I ring him up. Hand him the bag. End of story.

My hands shake under the counter, but my face stays still.

The guy leaves.

No spectacle.

No spiral.

Sam glances at me, quick check. “Still okay.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

And it’s true.

Because I’m holding the line.

---

## Chapter 17: Grant Holloway Gets Bored

At 11:40 a.m., the bell chimes.

Grant Holloway strolls in like he owns the air.

He’s wearing that expensive coat again, hair perfect, smile sharp.

My stomach drops so hard I feel it in my knees.

Noel is in the corner frosting something aggressively, jaw set.

Sam is by the door immediately, posture calm but unmistakably blocking.

Grant’s gaze flicks to Sam, then to me, then back, like he’s assessing where the leverage is.

“Officer,” Grant says smoothly. “Didn’t expect you here.”

Sam’s voice is flat. “Leave.”

Grant laughs softly. “This again.”

Sam doesn’t move. “This again.”

Grant’s eyes narrow. “I’m here to speak with Wes.”

Sam’s gaze stays steady. “He didn’t consent.”

Grant’s smile tightens. “Consent. That’s adorable.”

Something cold settles in my chest.

Noel makes a low sound in his throat, like a dog about to bite.

I step forward before Noel commits a felony in my bakery.

My voice comes out steady in a way that surprises me. “Grant.”

Grant’s smile widens. “Wes. There he is.”

I keep my face flat. “You wrote about me.”

Grant spreads his hands. “I wrote about a charming town and its charming characters.”

I feel my teeth want to show. I clamp down on it. Neutral.

“You implied things,” I say. “You invited speculation.”

Grant shrugs. “People speculate. It drives clicks.”

Sam’s voice is calm. “Leave.”

Grant ignores him, eyes on me. “I could also write a follow-up. A correction. If you’d like. A nice quote from you, perhaps.”

My stomach turns.

He wants me to dance.

He wants me to validate the story he stole.

I breathe in, slow.

“No,” I say.

Grant blinks. “No?”

“No,” I repeat. “You don’t get my words.”

Grant’s smile falters for half a second.

Sam’s posture doesn’t change, but the air shifts. Like a door closing.

Grant’s eyes flick to Sam, irritated. “You’re very protective.”

Sam’s voice is flat. “Leave.”

Grant’s jaw tightens. “You can’t stop the internet.”

I stare at him. “No. But I can stop you from being in my bakery.”

Grant looks offended, like I’ve broken the script.

Then, because he can’t resist being cruel, he adds lightly, “People love a mystery. Especially when it involves small-town romance.”

My chest tightens, but I don’t flinch.

I just say, calm as ice, “Get out.”

Noel steps up behind me, voice sweet and lethal. “Grant, darling. Go away.”

Grant looks between us, realizes the room has turned against him, and his smile goes brittle.

He lifts his hands. “Fine. Keep your secrets. But don’t be surprised when the town fills in the blanks.”

He pivots toward the door.

Sam doesn’t move until Grant is right in front of him, then says quietly, “If you contact Wes again, it won’t be friendly.”

Grant pauses, eyes narrowing. “Is that a threat, Officer.”

Sam’s voice stays calm. “It’s a boundary.”

Grant huffs a laugh and leaves.

The bell chimes.

The door shuts.

And the bakery doesn’t collapse.

Noel exhales shakily. “I hate him.”

I swallow. “Me too.”

Sam turns to me, gaze steady. “Still okay.”

My hands are shaking, but my chest isn’t on fire the way it used to be.

Because this time, I didn’t freeze.

I didn’t disappear.

I held the line.

“Yes,” I say, rough. “I’m okay.”

Sam nods once like he’s proud without making it a performance.

I glare. “Don’t.”

Noel clamps his mouth shut and does a tiny silent dance anyway.

Sam’s mouth tips slightly.
