Not because I earned being treated well.

Because I earned my own control back.

And for the first time, that feels like something I can build a life on.

Not a headline.

Not a rumor.

A choice.

A line.

A hand I can take when I decide.

So, naturally, I’m terrified.

But I stay.

And that’s new.

**[STOP WRITING]**




## Chapter 18: Aftershock

For a full minute after Grant leaves, the bakery is too quiet.

Not peaceful quiet. The kind of quiet where your body is waiting for the second hit.

Noel is still behind me, breathing like he just ran a marathon fueled by rage and peppermint.

Sam stands by the door, shoulders squared, calm like the world didn’t just try to take something from me again.

My hands are shaking, but my voice is steady, which feels like witchcraft.

I clear my throat. “Okay. Back to work.”

Noel stares at me like I just levitated. “Wes.”

I shoot him a warning look. “Noel.”

Noel clamps his mouth shut so hard I can see the restraint vibrating in his cheeks.

Sam’s gaze flicks to me. Quick check. No pressure. Just presence.

“Still okay,” he murmurs.

I swallow. “Yes.”

And I mean it.

Because Grant walked in expecting to own the narrative, and I told him no. Out loud. In my own bakery.

That should feel like a tiny victory. It feels like a tectonic plate shifting.

The bell chimes again.

A customer walks in, stamps snow off their boots, and says, “Morning! Smells amazing.”

Normal.

I cling to normal like it’s a railing on an ice rink.

I greet customers. I ring up cinnamon rolls. I keep my face neutral and my voice flat and my boundaries sharp.

Noel stays in the corner, aggressively posting photos of baked goods. I can see him typing with fury.

**NOEL’S PHONE (visible from across the room):**
“Just buns. Just buns. JUST BUNS.”

Sam stays nearby, “on patrol,” which is code for “I’m not leaving you alone with your nervous system today.”

I hate that it helps.

By noon, the town has done what towns do: decided Grant Holloway is trash.

Not loudly. Not with a mob. With the kind of passive, relentless social correction only a small town can deliver.

Mrs. Alden comes in and places her order like always, then pauses and says, very calmly, “That man was rude.”

I blink. “Yes.”

Mrs. Alden nods once, satisfied. “Good.”

Then she adds, voice dropping like she’s sharing state secrets, “If he returns, I will bite him.”

I stare. “Mrs. Alden.”

She smiles sweetly. “I’m old. What are they going to do.”

She leaves with her pastries and her implied violence.

Noel watches her go, eyes shining. “The elders have spoken.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “Okay.”

I glare at him on reflex. “Stop.”

“No.”

The day keeps moving. The line comes and goes. The gossip tries to poke its head up and we keep it boring until it dies.

Neutral. Redirect. Cinnamon rolls.

And somehow, by closing time, the headline feels… smaller.

Still there. Still annoying. Still a violation.

But not a trap.

I lock the door, flip the sign, and exhale like I’ve been holding my breath since dawn.

Noel slides off his stool and says, reverent, “You were terrifying today.”

I mutter, “Good.”

Noel points at me. “Like… hot terrifying.”

I go still.

Sam’s voice cuts in, calm and deadly. “Noel.”

Noel claps a hand over his mouth. “Sorry. Boundaries. I’m learning. I’m leaving.”

He grabs his coat and bolts before I can throw a whisk at him.

Now it’s just me and Sam in the warm bakery light again.

The quiet returns.

But this time it doesn’t feel like a second hit.

It feels like space.

Sam takes off his gloves. “You held the line.”

I stare at the counter, throat tight. “I didn’t fall apart.”

Sam’s gaze stays steady. “No.”

I swallow. “That’s new.”

Sam nods. “Yeah.”

My chest aches. “I don’t know what to do with it.”

Sam’s voice stays low. “Keep it.”

I laugh once, sharp and disbelieving. “Keep it.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

And the ridiculous thing is: my face wants to soften at that.

Which means I need to change the subject before I become a Hallmark movie.

“Soup,” I mutter.

Sam’s eyes warm. “Okay.”

I point at him. “You are insufferable.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I groan and head upstairs.

---

## Chapter 19: The Quiet Choice

My apartment smells like onions within ten minutes because soup is what I do when I don’t know what else to do.

Sam sits at the table while I chop, calm and present, like he belongs in the background of my life now.

Which is a problem.

I throw carrots into the pot with more force than necessary.

Sam watches me for a beat. “You’re angry.”

“I’m always angry,” I mutter.

Sam’s voice stays neutral. “Different angry.”

I exhale hard and admit it because lying would make this worse. “I hate that he tried to turn me into a story.”

Sam nods once. “I know.”

“I hate that people want to consume it,” I add, voice rough. “Like… like my life is entertainment.”

Sam’s gaze holds mine. “It’s not.”

I swallow. “But it feels like it is.”

Sam doesn’t rush in. Doesn’t talk over me. He just waits, like he always does, for the point under the point.

I keep chopping because my hands need violence.

Then I say it, low. “I hate that it makes me want to hide.”

Sam’s voice is steady. “Wanting to hide doesn’t mean you’re weak.”

My throat tightens.

I stare at the knife, then set it down carefully because my hands are shaking again.

Sam doesn’t move closer yet. He asks, “Still okay.”

I swallow. “No.”

Sam nods. “Okay.”

I glare with no bite. “Stop.”

“No.”

The soup simmers.

Silence stretches.

Then Sam says softly, “We can make a new plan.”

My chest tightens. “What kind.”

Sam’s gaze stays calm. “For the town. For Christmas. For… us.”

The last word lands like a heavy ornament.

I swallow hard. “That’s too big.”

Sam nods once. “Okay. Then small.”

I exhale, shaky. “Small.”

Sam leans forward a fraction. “You tell me what you want the town to see.”

I stare at him.

Because the answer isn’t “nothing,” not anymore.

I’ve learned that “nothing” is a cage. It’s safe, sure. It’s also lonely.

And I’m tired of lonely.

I swallow, throat tight. “I want… normal.”

Sam’s brows lift slightly. “Normal.”

“Not rumors,” I say. “Not headlines. Not Noel’s mouth. Just… normal.”

Sam nods. “Okay. What does normal look like.”

My chest aches. “You being around. People seeing you in the bakery. You walking me home sometimes. You existing in my space without it becoming… a spectacle.”

Sam’s gaze warms, careful. “And if they speculate.”

I swallow. “We don’t feed it.”

Sam nods. “Neutral.”

I exhale. “Neutral.”

Sam’s voice goes softer. “And what do you want me to do when someone pushes.”

I stare at him. “The same thing you did today.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he agrees.

The soup is done. I ladle it into bowls and sit across from him, hands wrapped around warm ceramic like I’m trying to borrow steadiness.

We eat quietly.

Then, because I’m apparently incapable of letting peace exist without testing it, I say, low, “Christmas Eve is in two days.”

Sam’s gaze flicks up. “Yeah.”

The fear spikes in my chest, immediate. “Noel will plan something.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Yes.”

“And the town will be… out,” I say, voice tight. “Candles. Tree. Photos. All of it.”

Sam nods, steady. “Yes.”

I swallow hard. “I don’t want to spend it bracing.”

Sam’s gaze warms. “Then we choose one thing. One small thing. On your terms.”

My throat tightens.

I hate how much I want that.

I whisper, “Like what.”

Sam pauses, then says carefully, “You decide. But… maybe we walk to the tree together. No hiding. No performance. Just walking.”

My chest tightens painfully.

That’s visible. That’s the town.

That’s the exact thing my body has always treated like danger.

But I think of the rink. Two fingers. Not dying.

I think of the candle walk. One full hand for one beat. Still not dying.

I think of today. Grant leaving. Me holding the line. Still not dying.

I exhale, shaky. “Walking.”

Sam nods. “Just walking.”

I swallow hard, then nod once. “Okay.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “That okay counts.”

I roll my eyes because it’s safer than crying. “Stop.”

“No.”

---

## Chapter 20: Christmas Eve

Christmas Eve hits Tinsel Town like a glitter bomb.

The square is packed. The big tree is lit. The air smells like cocoa and pine and money people spend on nostalgia.

Noel is everywhere at once, wearing a scarf that could double as a flag, rallying volunteers and directing traffic like he’s running a small nation.

Eben stands near the tree, arms crossed, radiating a silent threat to anyone attempting joy incorrectly.

Hugh is in a coat that looks like it was made from velvet curtains and self-regard.

And me?

I’m at the bakery until seven, doing what I do best: feeding people so they’ll stop talking for two seconds.

Sam is “on patrol” again, drifting in and out, steadying the edges of the night without making it obvious.

At 7:15, I lock the door, flip the sign, and just… stand there for a moment with my hand on the key.

The square is loud outside. Full of light. Full of eyes.

My stomach tightens.

Sam appears at the bottom of the stairs, calm as ever. He doesn’t rush me. He just looks up and waits.

Choice.

I swallow hard and walk down.

When I reach him, he says quietly, “Still okay.”

My chest is tight, but my feet are moving, which is new.

“Yes,” I say.

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he agrees.

We start walking toward Evergreen Square.

Not touching.

Not yet.

Just side by side, matching pace, like it’s normal. Like I’m not about to bolt.

People pass us. A few nod at Sam. A few glance at me.

No one stops us.

Noel sees us from across the street, eyes going wide like he’s about to scream.

Eben barks, “NOEL.”

Noel clamps his mouth shut so fast I’m surprised he doesn’t sprain something.

We reach the edge of the square.

The tree lights glitter.

Phones are out, but they’re aimed at the tree, at the performers, at the snow machine spraying fake flakes like the sky wasn’t already doing that.

Sam leans slightly closer, voice low. “Do you want the tether.”

My heart pounds.

I stare at his hand, gloved, offered without demand.

My whole body wants to run.

My whole body also remembers how it felt not to fall.

I inhale.

“Two fingers,” I whisper.

Sam’s mouth tips, barely. “Okay.”

I slide two fingers into his palm.

He closes his hand gently around them, careful, like this is fragile and sacred and entirely mine to revoke.

We walk into the square like that.

Two fingers.

Not a declaration. Not a headline.

Just a choice.

A couple people glance over. A couple smile. No one says anything.

The world doesn’t end.

My chest unlocks a fraction.

Sam murmurs, “Still okay.”

I swallow hard, eyes stinging for no good reason I’m willing to admit.

“Yes,” I whisper. “I’m okay.”

Sam’s grip stays light. Respectful. Steady.

We stop near the tree, listening to someone singing carols on a little stage. Noel is wiping his eyes dramatically. Eben looks like he’s considering violence against the concept of harmony.

I stand there with two fingers in Sam’s hand, and the town is bright and loud and full of eyes, and somehow it doesn’t feel like a cage.

It feels like air.

And for the first time, I understand something I didn’t want to understand:

Being seen doesn’t have to mean being taken.

Not if you choose what you give.

Not if you keep your hand on the line.

Sam leans in slightly, voice low. “Merry Christmas, Wes.”

My throat tightens.

I hate that my voice breaks when I answer.

“Merry Christmas,” I manage.

Sam’s hand squeezes once, gentle, not claiming.

Then he lets go like he’s honoring the moment for what it is: mine.

We stand under the lights.

And I don’t run.

I just… stay.

**[STOP WRITING]**



## Chapter 21: The Tree Doesn’t Bite

We stand under the lights for a long minute, listening to carolers who are trying their best and succeeding about half the time.

My breath fogs. The air smells like pine and cocoa and other people’s optimism.

Sam doesn’t touch me again. He doesn’t even angle his body like he’s trying to claim space. He just stays near, steady, like a wall I can lean on without anyone noticing.

Which is the point.

Which is why my chest still feels too tight.

Noel spots us again and does a full-body tremor of excitement. His eyes are wet. He’s going to make this weird.

Eben appears beside him like a shadow made of disapproval.

“Noel,” Eben says flatly.

Noel clamps his mouth shut and nods so hard his scarf flutters. He does not approach. He does not speak.

I’m genuinely impressed.

Sam leans in slightly, voice low. “You want to leave now or stay.”

Choice.

My stomach flips.

I look at the tree. The lights. The crowd. The fact that I’m here and I’m not dying.

I exhale slowly. “Stay. Five minutes.”

Sam nods. “Okay. Five.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he says, and it’s warm enough to make my throat tighten again.

We stay.

And the miracle is: nothing happens.

No one corners me. No one asks questions. No one drags my life into their mouth like it’s candy.

A few people smile when they see Sam near me. A few glance at me like I’m part of the town instead of a rumor in an article.

It’s… fine.

It’s more than fine.

It’s survivable.

Then a little girl with a pink hat and a candle cup trips near us and almost spills wax on her mitten.

I react before I can think, steadying the cup with a light touch.

She looks up at me, bright-eyed. “Thanks!”

Her dad nods at me. “Appreciate it.”

No subtext. No commentary. Just… normal.

My chest loosens another fraction.

Sam’s voice is low. “Still okay.”

I swallow. “Yeah.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “Okay.”

I glare weakly. “Stop.”

“No.”

Five minutes become ten without me noticing.

That’s the scariest part.

---

## Chapter 22: Noel Tries Not to Be Noel

Eventually, Noel can’t take it anymore.

He approaches like someone trying not to spook a wild animal.

“I’m being normal,” Noel whispers.

Eben appears behind him like a warning label.

Noel holds up his hands. “I’m not saying anything. I’m just… here. Casually.”

I stare at him. “Noel.”

Noel nods fast. “Yes. Boundaries. I have them. I’m learning them. I’m respecting them.”

Eben’s gaze flicks to me, then back to Noel. “Speak less.”

Noel presses a hand to his heart. “This is oppression.”

I exhale through my nose, but it almost turns into a laugh, and that’s unacceptable.

Noel’s eyes flick between me and Sam, soft for once. “I’m glad you came.”

My chest tightens, suspicious. “Don’t.”

Noel nods. “Okay. I won’t make it a thing.”

He pauses, then adds very carefully, “If anyone gives you trouble because of that stupid article… they answer to me.”

I blink.

Noel, the chaos gremlin, looks genuinely angry on my behalf.

My throat tightens.

I force out, rough, “Thanks.”

Noel’s eyes go shiny again, immediately. “Okay, I’m leaving before I cry.”

He turns and flees into the crowd.

Eben watches him go, then looks at me.

Eben’s face is still blank, but his voice is quieter. “You did good.”

I freeze.

Eben does not do praise. Eben does not do warmth. Eben barely does oxygen.

I swallow hard. “It was ten minutes.”

Eben nods once. “Still good.”

Then he walks away like he didn’t just drop a brick of validation on my nervous system.

Sam glances at me, the faintest curve in his mouth. “Eben approved.”

I stare at him. “Don’t you dare.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Okay.”

I point at him. “Stop.”

“No.”

---

## Chapter 23: Upstairs

We leave the square when my body finally starts to shake from cold and adrenaline.

No drama. No big exit.

Just two people walking away from the lights.

Halfway down the street, Sam says quietly, “You chose it.”

I grit my teeth. “You’re going to say that every time.”

Sam’s voice is calm. “Yes.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

We get back to Candle & Crumb. I unlock the door. We step into warmth.

And the second the lock clicks behind us, the last of the public tension snaps.

Not in a panic way.

In a release.

My shoulders drop. My breath comes easier. My skin feels like it’s been buzzing for hours and is finally allowed to land.

Sam stays still, watching me like he’s waiting for me to set the pace.

Choice again.

I hate how much I’m starting to need that.

I swallow hard and say it, low. “Upstairs.”

Sam’s gaze warms. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he agrees.

We go up.

My apartment is dim, warm, quiet. A little string of lights Noel insisted I hang in the window twinkles like it’s trying to be smug.

I take off my coat and toss it over a chair. Sam does the same, neat and controlled.

I turn to face him and realize my hands are trembling.

Sam notices immediately. He doesn’t move closer. He just asks, soft, “Still okay.”

I exhale, shaky. “Yes. I’m just… full.”

Sam nods, understanding without making it poetry. “Yeah.”

I stare at him for a beat, then whisper, “I held your hand.”

Sam’s eyes soften. “You did.”

I swallow hard. “In public.”

Sam’s voice is low. “Yeah.”

My throat tightens. “And you didn’t… squeeze. You didn’t… make it into anything.”

Sam’s gaze holds mine. “It was yours.”

My chest aches in a way that hurts.

I step closer, slow. He doesn’t move. He waits.

I stop just inside his space and whisper, “I want you to kiss me.”

Sam’s eyes flicker, careful. “Okay. Where.”

I blink. “Where?”

Sam’s voice is steady. “Do you want it gentle. Do you want it hungry. Do you want it quick.”

My throat tightens, heat pooling low in my belly. The fact that he asks makes my whole body unclench.

I swallow. “Hungry. But… slow.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

He kisses me like a promise he’s still checking in on. Warm, firm, unhurried. His hands hover at my waist, waiting.

I nod slightly.

He touches me, light at first, then steadier when I lean into it.

My whole body feels like it’s been braced for years and is finally allowed to soften.

I pull back just enough to breathe and mutter, “This is… stupid.”

Sam’s brows lift. “What is.”

“Feeling good,” I snap quietly. “It’s suspicious.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

He kisses me again, and the sarcasm drains out of me like it never stood a chance.

We make it to the couch. Then the floor. Then the bed, because gravity is rude.

It’s not frantic. It’s not performative.

It’s just… chosen.

Every time he checks, I answer.

Every time I tense, he slows.

When I finally break, it’s into his shoulder, quiet and shaking, like my body doesn’t know how to accept warmth without apology.

Sam’s hand rubs slow circles along my back, grounding. “Still okay,” he murmurs.

I swallow hard, voice wrecked. “Yes.”

And for once, the yes doesn’t feel like bravery.

It feels like truth.

---

## Chapter 24: Christmas Morning

I wake up to sunlight and silence.

For one horrifying second, my brain doesn’t know why there’s a body beside me.

Then I remember.

Sam is asleep on his back, hair mussed, expression peaceful in a way that looks wrong on him because he’s usually so controlled.

My chest tightens.

I stare at him like he might vanish if I blink.

He doesn’t.

He shifts slightly, eyes opening, locking on mine immediately like he’s been trained to wake up alert.

Then his gaze softens when he recognizes me.

“Merry Christmas,” he says, voice rough with sleep.

My throat tightens so hard it hurts.

I stare at him, then mutter, “Don’t.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I exhale. “Stop.”

“No.”

I roll onto my back and stare at the ceiling like it’s safer than looking at the man I just let into my bed and my life.

Silence stretches.

Then Sam says quietly, “Do you regret it.”

I swallow.

My instincts want to say yes, because yes is armor. Yes is control. Yes means I don’t have to risk wanting again.

But my body is warm. My apartment is quiet. My palm still remembers his.

I breathe in.

“No,” I whisper. “I don’t.”

Sam’s voice is low. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he agrees.

I stare at the ceiling, then finally turn my head and look at him again.

He’s watching me like I’m something fragile he refuses to treat as fragile.

I whisper, “I don’t know how to do this.”

Sam’s gaze stays steady. “We do it your way.”

My chest tightens. “My way is… terrible.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

I exhale, then say the thing that scares me most because it’s simple and it’s honest.

“Stay,” I whisper.

Sam doesn’t hesitate. Doesn’t make it a win. Doesn’t make it a vow he’ll weaponize later.

He just nods once. “Okay.”

And the okay lands like an anchor.

---

Downstairs, the bakery is still asleep. The ovens are cold. The world is quiet for once.

We make coffee together in my tiny kitchen like it’s normal.

Sam stands close, not touching, then brushes his fingers against mine when he reaches for a mug.

Not asking now. Just… domestic accident.

My chest tightens anyway.

I glance up at him.

He pauses, catches himself, and asks softly, “Still okay.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Okay.”

I roll my eyes and sip coffee to hide the fact that I’m smiling.

Outside, the town is still glowing under snow.

Inside, I’m not bracing.

Not fully.

Not today.

And that’s enough.

For now.

**[STOP WRITING]**



## Chapter 25: Open Sign

Christmas morning is supposed to be sacred and quiet and full of family traditions.

In Tinsel Town, it’s also apparently a contact sport.

My phone buzzes at 8:07 a.m.

**NOEL:** ARE YOU ALIVE
**NOEL:** I AM BRINGING MUFFINS
**NOEL:** DO NOT PRETEND YOU’RE NOT HOME
**NOEL:** I CAN SEE YOUR WINDOW LIGHTS FROM THE SQUARE

I stare at the screen like it’s a threat.

Sam sips coffee across from me, watching my face. “Noel.”

“Yes,” I mutter.

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

“Stop.”

“No.”

The buzzer downstairs goes off five minutes later.

Noel doesn’t use doors. Noel announces himself.

I exhale and stand. “Do not let him upstairs.”

Sam’s brows lift. “He’s your friend.”

“He’s a hazard,” I snap.

Sam’s eyes warm. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

I go downstairs and unlock the bakery door.

Noel barrels in with a giant box and cheeks pink from cold, wearing a Santa hat like it’s welded to his personality.

“MERRY CHRISTMAS,” Noel stage-whispers, because he’s incapable of a normal volume.

“Quiet,” I hiss.

Noel freezes, then whispers even louder, “SORRY.”

He holds up the box. “Muffins.”

I stare. “Why.”

Noel looks offended. “Because it’s Christmas. And because I assumed you’d be open anyway because you’re allergic to rest.”

I open my mouth to argue.

Then I realize he’s right and it makes me furious.

Noel’s eyes flick past me, up the stairs. “Is he here.”

“Noel.”

Noel presses a hand to his chest. “Boundaries. I’m not asking about your private life. I’m asking about… his physical location.”

I narrow my eyes. “He’s upstairs.”

Noel’s whole face lights up like a child who’s been told Santa is real.

I point at him. “You will not go upstairs.”

Noel nods rapidly. “I will not go upstairs. I am a normal person. I respect privacy.”

Eben’s voice behind him says, “Liar.”

Noel whips around. “EBEN.”

Eben steps in from the cold, scarf tight, expression grim as ever, holding a small paper bag like it contains contraband.

I blink. “Why are you here.”

Eben shrugs once. “Noel said muffins.”

Noel lifts the box proudly. “Muffins.”

Eben holds up the bag. “I brought coffee.”

I stare at him like he’s committed an act of kindness in public and might be arrested.

Eben’s eyes narrow. “Do not react.”

I swallow. “Okay.”

Eben nods once like that’s acceptable.

Noel leans in, whispering, “We are having a *friend Christmas*. In your bakery. This is not negotiable.”

“I didn’t agree,” I mutter.

Noel smiles brightly. “Yes you did.”

I hate him.

I also… don’t.

Because the bakery is warm. The lights are soft. The world outside is loud. And somehow, these idiots have decided this is where they want to be.

I exhale through my nose. “Fine. But quiet.”

Noel grins. “Quiet.”

He immediately fails at being quiet.

---

## Chapter 26: Friend Christmas

Sam comes downstairs a few minutes later, hair still slightly damp from a quick shower, hoodie under his coat, looking like he slept in my bed and is trying not to make it a thing.

Noel sees him and makes a sound like a kettle.

Eben’s head snaps toward Noel.

“Noel,” Eben warns.

Noel clamps his mouth shut so hard it’s impressive, then whispers, “Hi.”

Sam nods politely. “Hi.”

Noel trembles with restraint.

Eben sets the coffee on the counter and looks at Sam, then at me, then away like eye contact might cause feelings.

“I’m here for food,” Eben says flatly. “Not… whatever this is.”

Noel whispers, “Love.”

Eben’s stare could curdle milk. “Stop.”

Noel stops.

We end up at the little table in the back with muffins and coffee and the kind of quiet that feels earned.

Noel tells a story about accidentally volunteering himself to dress up as Santa for the kids’ breakfast at the inn.

“I did not realize the suit would be… sweaty,” Noel says, solemn.

Eben says, “It’s a suit.”

Noel nods gravely. “It’s a swamp.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly.

I catch it, and my chest tightens because it’s stupidly domestic to be sitting here with Sam while Noel talks nonsense and Eben looks like he’s suffering through community.

Noel pulls out his phone and shoves it toward me. “Okay. Important. The cinnamon roll content strategy is working.”

I narrow my eyes. “What did you post.”

Noel beams. “Only buns. No names. No hints. Just buns.”

He scrolls. It’s photos of pastries. Festive behind-the-scenes shots. The bakery oven. The town tree in the background.

A caption reads: **“CANDLE & CRUMB: BAKING THROUGH THE SEASON.”**

Noel looks up, proud. “See? Harmless.”

I squint. “That’s dangerously competent.”

Noel gasps. “I can be competent.”

Eben mutters, “Rare.”

Noel ignores him and scrolls again. “Also, people are roasting Grant.”

I tense.

Noel quickly adds, “Not because of you. Mostly because he’s annoying and nobody likes being marketed at.”

Eben nods once. “He’s a parasite.”

Noel whispers, delighted, “Eben said parasite.”

Eben’s eyes narrow. “Stop being happy.”

Noel tries. Fails.

Sam looks at me, quiet check. “Still okay.”

I swallow. The headline still exists. The violation still happened. But the bakery is warm. The room is small. The people are… on my side.

“Yes,” I say. “I’m okay.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he agrees.

Noel presses his hands together like a little villain. “Now. Gifts.”

I go cold. “No.”

Noel’s eyes widen. “Yes.”

Eben says, “No.”

Noel looks at Eben. “Yes.”

Eben’s face stays blank. “I will leave.”

Noel slams a small gift bag down on the table. “No one leaves.”

Eben stares at the bag like it’s a bomb.

Sam’s mouth tips. “Noel.”

Noel lifts both hands. “Small gifts. Tiny. Symbolic. Not emotional. I am normal.”

He pushes the gift bag toward me.

I stare at it, suspicious. “What is it.”

Noel whispers, “Open it.”

I don’t like being told what to do.

Which means I open it immediately.

Inside is a small enamel pin: a tiny cinnamon roll with a little crown on it.

I blink.

Noel says softly, “For your apron. Because you are the king of stubborn.”

My throat tightens.

I hate it.

I also… really don’t hate it.

I force out, rough, “Thanks.”

Noel’s eyes shine. “Okay.”

Eben makes a sound and slides a small box toward me without looking at me. “Here.”

I blink at him. “Eben.”

Eben’s jaw tightens. “Open it.”

I open it.

Inside: a simple keychain, heavy metal, engraved with the bakery logo Noel must’ve designed. Under it: **CANDLE & CRUMB.**

No flourish. No joke. Just… solid.

Eben’s voice is flat. “So you stop losing your keys.”

My chest tightens.

I swallow. “I don’t lose my keys.”

Eben stares. “You do.”

Noel whispers, “He noticed.”

Eben says, “Stop talking.”

I close the box carefully. “Thank you.”

Eben nods once like he’s accepted the transaction and is now emotionally bankrupt again.

Noel turns to Sam, shoving another bag at him. “Open.”

Sam opens it.

It’s a knitted scarf. Dark green. Simple. Actually nice.

Noel beams. “I made it.”

Eben mutters, “Lies.”

Noel points at Eben. “My aunt made it. But I *commissioned* it with love.”

Sam’s gaze warms. “Thank you.”

Noel trembles with happiness, then turns to me with another bag.

I stare. “No.”

Noel whispers, “Yes.”

I take it reluctantly.

Inside is a small notebook, leather-bound, with a simple pressed-foil design of pine branches.

On the first page, Noel has written: **“PLANS THAT AREN’T PRISONS.”**

My throat tightens so hard it hurts.

Noel says quietly, “So you can write the things you want. The boundaries. The plans. The… whatever.”

I stare at the notebook like it might explode.

Then I force out, low, “That’s… actually useful.”

Noel grins, relieved. “Yes. Usefulness. I can do it.”

Sam looks at me, eyes steady.

I can feel it in the air: the possibility of gratitude turning into something worse, something softer.

So I do what I always do when I’m close to being emotionally honest.

I get up. “I’m making more coffee.”

Noel whispers, reverent, “He is overwhelmed.”

Eben says, “Stop narrating.”

Noel whispers, “Never.”

---

## Chapter 27: The Smallest Future

Later, Noel and Eben leave. Noel hugs me without asking, which earns him a sharp look and a quick step back, and he immediately apologizes with frantic sincerity.

“Sorry,” Noel blurts. “Habit. I’m learning.”

I nod once. “Okay.”

Noel smiles weakly. “That okay counts?”

I glare. “Don’t start.”

Noel laughs, then flees into the snow.

Eben pauses at the door and looks at me like he’s deciding whether to speak.

Then he says, low and blunt, “You handled it.”

I swallow. “Thanks.”

Eben nods once and leaves, coat snapping behind him.

The bakery quiets again.

Sam and I stand near the counter, the ovens cold, the lights soft.

I run my thumb over the notebook’s cover like I’m grounding myself.

Sam watches me for a beat. “You want to write the plan.”

I swallow. “Not today.”

Sam nods. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

I exhale, then admit, low, “I didn’t think Christmas could feel… like that.”

Sam’s gaze warms. “Like what.”

“Not like a test,” I mutter.

Sam nods once. “Good.”

I swallow, throat tight. “Grant didn’t win.”

Sam’s voice is calm. “No.”

“He didn’t get to make it mine,” I say.

Sam’s gaze holds mine. “You didn’t let him.”

My chest aches.

I whisper, “I held your hand.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “Yeah.”

I swallow. “And no one died.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “No.”

I stare at him, then finally say the thing I’ve been circling for days like it’s a trap.

“I want this,” I whisper. “But I want it… my way.”

Sam’s expression softens, serious and steady. “Okay. Tell me what your way is.”

Choice again.

I inhale.

“My way is slow,” I say. “My way is consent. My way is boring in public. My way is no photos. My way is… you don’t get to surprise me.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

I swallow. “And my way is… if I say stop, you stop.”

Sam’s voice is immediate. “Always.”

My chest tightens, warm and terrified.

I whisper, “Okay.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “That okay counts.”

I roll my eyes because I refuse to cry in my bakery. “You’re ruining the word.”

Sam’s gaze warms. “Okay.”

“Stop.”

“No.”

We stand there in the soft Christmas light, the town quiet outside for once, and the future doesn’t feel like a cliff.

It feels like a line I can draw and keep.

And for the first time in my life, the idea of being seen doesn’t feel like a sentence.

It feels like a choice.

**[STOP WRITING]**



## Chapter 28: The Notebook Lies in Wait

Christmas afternoon is the kind of quiet that feels illegal in this town.

No festival announcements. No carolers ambushing strangers. No Noel sprinting past my window like a glitter-coated cryptid.

Just snow, soft light, and the bakery downstairs sitting dark and still.

I sit at my kitchen table with the notebook Noel gave me.

I glare at it like it personally offended me.

Sam sits across from me, mug in hand, watching without hovering. He’s learned the difference between “support” and “pressure,” which is both rare and annoyingly attractive.

I tap the notebook. “I hate this.”

Sam’s brows lift slightly. “Writing.”

“Planning,” I correct. “Putting things into words makes them real.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

I exhale, then flip it open.

The first page stares back at me:

**PLANS THAT AREN’T PRISONS.**

Noel’s handwriting is dramatic, which is on-brand and unforgivable.

I pick up a pen.

My hand shakes slightly.

Sam doesn’t speak.

So I write anyway.

### RULES (WES)

1. No photos. Not of me. Not of us.
2. No public labels. Not yet.
3. No surprises. Ask first.
4. If I say stop, you stop. Immediately.
5. Neutral response to gossip. No feeding.
6. Public touch: my choice. Always.
7. Home is safe. Bakery is safe.

I stare at the list, throat tight, like I’ve just written a spell.

Sam’s voice is low. “Can I add one.”

My pulse spikes.

Choice again.

I swallow. “Yes.”

Sam leans forward slightly, careful. “Add: ‘If you panic, you don’t have to explain. You just say it. And we leave.’”

My throat tightens.

I write it down before I can overthink it.

8. If I panic, I don’t have to explain. I say it. We leave.

I set the pen down.

My chest aches, warm and horrible.

Sam’s gaze holds mine. “Still okay.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he agrees.

And then, because the universe hates peace, my phone buzzes again.

This time it’s not Grant.

It’s Noel.

**NOEL:** DAY NINE EVENT HAS BEEN RESCHEDULED
**NOEL:** BECAUSE I AM A RESPONSIBLE ADULT
**NOEL:** IT IS NOW “COZY MOVIE NIGHT” AT THE INN
**NOEL:** YOU CAN COME AND SIT IN THE DARK AND NO ONE WILL LOOK AT YOU
**NOEL:** I HAVE CONTROLLED THE LIGHTING

I stare at the messages, deadpan.

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “Controlled the lighting.”

“He thinks he’s a stage director,” I mutter.

Sam’s gaze stays warm. “Do you want to go.”

My stomach flips.

Movie night. Dark. Quiet. Public, but not bright. That’s… almost tolerable.

I exhale. “For one hour.”

Sam nods. “Okay. One.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he agrees.

I glare at him. “Stop.”

“No.”

---

## Chapter 29: Movie Night and the Art of Not Being Perceived

The inn’s lounge looks like Noel broke in after hours and turned it into a winter-themed nest.

Blankets stacked like offerings. Candles everywhere, but the electric kind, because Eben apparently threatened arson if Noel used real flame again. A projector aimed at a white sheet hung across the far wall.

It’s cozy in a way that makes me suspicious.

Noel greets us at the door wearing glasses he does not need, holding a clipboard he definitely does not need.

“Welcome,” Noel whispers, like we’re in a library.

Eben stands behind him like a bouncer in a holiday sweater, arms crossed.

Noel points at the room. “Low light. Back row seating. Minimal eye contact. I have done everything you asked.”

I blink. “I asked for none of this.”

Noel nods solemnly. “And yet.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly.

I glare at him. “Don’t.”

Noel ushers us toward the back, where he has arranged two chairs slightly tucked behind a decorative plant.

A plant.

Noel has hidden me behind foliage.

I hate that it works.

We sit.

The movie starts, some cheesy Christmas romance with bad acting and fake snow. Everyone laughs at the corny parts, sighs at the dramatic parts, and shushes Hugh when he starts narrating.

Hugh gets shushed by Eben. Hugh lives.

Miracles happen daily here.

Sam sits beside me, close but not touching. Present without pressure. I can feel the warmth of him through fabric and it makes my skin prickle.

Halfway through, Noel brings cocoa around like a waiter in a fever dream.

He sets a mug in my hand and whispers, “No marshmallows. Too messy.”

“Thank you,” I whisper back, and immediately regret it because I’ve just rewarded him.

Noel’s eyes shine. He retreats before he cries.

Sam leans in slightly, voice low. “Still okay.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

And it’s true.

Because it’s dark. Because no one is staring. Because I’m not bracing for a camera flash.

And because, for once, I’m just… in a room with people without feeling like prey.

Toward the end of the movie, something quietly changes.

Not with the town.

With me.

Because my hand, resting on my leg, creeps closer to Sam’s.

I don’t even realize I’m doing it until my knuckles brush his.

Sam goes still.

He doesn’t move.

He waits.

Asks without words.

Choice.

My heart pounds.

Then I do the small thing, the safe thing.

I hook my pinky around his.

Just a pinky.

Not a declaration. Not a headline.

Sam’s pinky curls around mine, gentle and steady.

My chest tightens, warm and terrifying.

He doesn’t squeeze. Doesn’t tug. Doesn’t turn it into anything.

He just… holds.

And the stupid part is: nobody notices.

The movie ends. People clap ironically. Noel leaps up and announces, “WAS THAT NOT A MASTERPIECE OF EMOTIONAL TRUTH.”

Eben says, “No.”

Noel beams anyway.

We leave with the crowd, blending into winter coats and muffled laughter.

Outside, under the inn’s porch lights, Sam says quietly, “Pinky.”

I stare straight ahead. “Don’t.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I exhale. “Stop.”

“No.”

We walk back through the snow, and my pinky still feels like it has a pulse of its own.

Which is absurd.

But so am I, apparently.

---

## Chapter 30: Day Ten

Day Ten is “Snowed-In Market Day.”

Meaning the town has collectively decided to set up little booths in the square and sell crafts, cocoa, homemade candles, and other things people buy when they need to feel like life has meaning.

I am not selling anything.

I am not speaking to anyone.

I am not participating.

Noel texts at 9:02 a.m.

**NOEL:** YOU ARE PARTICIPATING
**NOEL:** YOUR BOOTH IS “BAKERY BOXES FOR CHARITY”
**NOEL:** PEOPLE BUY A BOX OF PASTRIES AND IT FUNDS THE FOOD BANK
**NOEL:** YOU DO NOT HAVE TO SPEAK
**NOEL:** I HAVE PRE-PRINTED SIGNS

I stare at the message.

Sam, sipping coffee, watches my face. “He’s doing it again.”

I mutter, “He’s doing it in a way that’s hard to argue with.”

Sam’s eyes warm. “Do you want to do it.”

My stomach flips.

Public. But controlled. For charity. No talking. Signs.

And the truth is: I like feeding people. It’s the one way I can be in the world without feeling exposed.

I exhale. “Yes. But… no photos.”

Sam nods. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he agrees.

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

We set up the booth.

Noel has gone full production mode: warm lights, tasteful signage, a cash box, a little chalkboard that says **“PAY WHAT YOU WANT. FEED SOMEONE ELSE TOO.”**

I don’t have to talk much. People come up, smile, pay, leave with pastry boxes like they’re holding tiny miracles.

A few try to ask about the article.

I keep it boring.

“I don’t discuss my private life,” I say, and hand them a box.

Sam, nearby, redirects with calm efficiency.

Noel floats around managing everything like a glittery logistics demon.

Eben appears once, buys two boxes, and disappears again like a social ghost leaving evidence.

And then something happens I didn’t plan for.

A teen steps up, hands shoved in his pockets, face red from cold.

He points at the sign. “This really goes to the food bank.”

I nod. “Yes.”

He hesitates, then quietly says, “My mom uses it sometimes.”

My throat tightens.

He fumbles cash onto the table, too much, then tries to pull away like he’s embarrassed.

I stop him gently. “That’s too much.”

He shrugs, eyes down. “It’s fine.”

I swallow hard.

Then I do the only thing I know how to do when words are dangerous.

I slide the biggest box toward him. Extra pastries. No announcement. No pity. Just… more.

He looks up, startled.

I keep my voice flat. “Merry Christmas.”

His face changes. Softens.

“Thanks,” he mutters, and leaves fast.

My chest aches.

Sam’s voice is low beside me. “That was good.”

I swallow. “It was nothing.”

Sam’s gaze stays steady. “It was something.”

I exhale, shaky. “Stop making it meaningful.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

---

## Chapter 31: Day Eleven

Day Eleven is supposed to be “Midnight Sleigh Ride.”

Noel is obsessed with it. Something about “old-fashioned romance” and “twinkle-light intimacy.”

I hate sleighs. I hate horses. I hate being trapped in a moving vehicle with other humans.

Noel knows this.

So Noel texts me:

**NOEL:** YOUR SLEIGH IS PRIVATE
**NOEL:** IT IS JUST YOU AND SAM
**NOEL:** I HAVE MADE THIS A BOUNDARY-COMPLIANT EVENT
**NOEL:** YOU’RE WELCOME

I stare at the message, deadpan.

Sam reads over my shoulder and says, calm, “Private sleigh.”

I mutter, “He is unwell.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

And the worst part is… I want it.

Not because I’m suddenly a romantic.

Because it’s contained. Dark. Quiet. Cold air. No crowd.

And because it’s with Sam, and I’m starting to understand that being with him doesn’t make me smaller.

It makes me steadier.

So I say it, low. “We can do it.”

Sam’s gaze warms. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he agrees.

---

## Chapter 32: Sleigh

The sleigh is parked at the edge of town near the tree farm, lanterns glowing softly, snow blanketing the ground like the world’s quietest threat.

The driver is an older man with a beard like a snowdrift who looks like he’s seen everything.

He looks at us, then pointedly looks away like he doesn’t care.

I love him.

We climb in. Blankets. Warmth. The horse snorts like it’s judging us.

The sleigh starts moving, bells ringing softly.

The town lights fade behind us. The trees close in. The night goes quiet in the way that makes my whole body loosen.

Sam sits close, not touching at first.

Then, after a minute, he asks quietly, “Tether.”

My heart pounds.

I stare at his gloved hand.

Choice.

I exhale slowly, then slip two fingers into his palm.

Sam’s hand closes gently, warm and steady even through leather.

We ride like that under the stars, the sleigh gliding, the world quiet.

Sam leans in slightly, voice low. “You did something tonight.”

I stare straight ahead. “What.”

Sam’s gaze stays steady. “You chose comfort.”

My throat tightens.

I mutter, “Don’t make it inspirational.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

The horse snorts again, like it approves.

The driver hums an off-key carol under his breath.

The sleigh keeps moving through the dark.

And for the first time in my life, Christmas feels less like a spotlight and more like a blanket.

When we return to town, the square is quiet, most people asleep, lights still twinkling like the town refuses to stop performing even when no one is watching.

Sam walks me back to the bakery.

At the door, he pauses, gaze steady. “Still okay.”

My chest is tight in a different way now. Warm. Full.

“Yes,” I say. “I’m okay.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he agrees.

Then he kisses me, brief and careful in the snow, like a promise that doesn’t demand anything.

I kiss him back, then pull away first, because control still matters.

Sam doesn’t complain.

He just says, low, “Goodnight.”

I whisper, “Goodnight.”

I go inside, lock the door, lean against it, and stare into the quiet.

My fingers still remember his.

And the terrifying part is: I don’t want to forget.

**[STOP WRITING]**



## Chapter 33: Day Twelve, Twelfth Night, and Other Threats

Day Twelve arrives with the kind of crisp, bright cold that makes everything look cleaner than it is.

The town schedule board in Evergreen Square reads:

**TWELFTH NIGHT: THE STARLIGHT SOCIAL**
**Dress warm. Dress festive. Dress like you’re not terrified of feelings.**
(Last line absolutely added by Noel.)

My phone buzzes at 9:11 a.m.

**NOEL:** FINAL EVENT
**NOEL:** LOW LIGHT
**NOEL:** LIVE JAZZ TRIO (THEY ARE TAME)
**NOEL:** NO PHOTOS (I SWEAR ON MY SCARF COLLECTION)
**NOEL:** YOU CAN HIDE BEHIND A PLANT AGAIN IF YOU WANT

I stare at it.

Sam, standing at my sink rinsing mugs like he’s been doing it forever, glances over. “Plant again.”

I mutter, “He thinks foliage is a privacy policy.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

“Stop.”

“No.”

I look at the schedule again.

Twelfth Night means people will be out. The inn will be full. The town will be… itself. Warm and nosy and relentlessly hopeful.

My stomach flips.

Sam turns slightly, voice low. “You don’t have to go.”

I swallow. “I know.”

He waits. Doesn’t fill the space with encouragement. Doesn’t do the “you can do it” speech. Just waits like my choices matter even when they scare me.

I exhale slowly.

“I want to go,” I admit. “But I want… an exit.”

Sam nods once. “Okay. We make an exit plan.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he agrees.

I glare at him. “Stop.”

“No.”

I grab Noel’s notebook off the table and flip to a fresh page.

### TWELFTH NIGHT PLAN

* Arrive late
* Stay 45 minutes
* Stand near a wall
* No photos
* Two-finger tether allowed
* If panic: leave. No explanation.

I underline **leave** twice, like that’s going to stop my nervous system from being dramatic.

Sam reads it, then adds one line in neat handwriting beneath mine:

* If someone tries to corner you: I intervene. You don’t have to.

My throat tightens.

I stare at his handwriting like it’s evidence of something I’m not ready to name.

I swallow. “Okay.”

Sam’s gaze warms. “That okay counts.”

I roll my eyes hard enough to injure my soul. “You’re going to say it until I die.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

“Stop.”

“No.”

---

## Chapter 34: The Starlight Social

The inn has been transformed into Noel’s idea of elegant.

Which is to say: twinkle lights, pine garlands, candles that are definitely electric because Eben has veto power now, and a small jazz trio tucked in the corner playing something soft and nostalgic.

The whole room smells like mulled cider and expensive perfume and other people’s ability to enjoy life.

Noel sees us enter and does a visible inhale like he’s about to scream.

Eben appears behind him instantly.

“Noel,” Eben says.

Noel clamps his mouth shut, eyes shining, and just gestures wildly toward the back wall where Noel has, no joke, set up “quiet seating.”

There are chairs. In shadow. Next to a large decorative plant.

I stare at it.

Sam’s mouth tips. “He’s consistent.”

I mutter, “He’s insane.”

We take the seats.

It’s dim enough that no one’s really looking at us. People are focused on the music, the drinks, the sparkle of it all. Conversations bubble like soft static.

I can breathe.

For the first ten minutes, I just… exist in a room.

And the room doesn’t eat me.

Sam sits beside me, close but not touching. His knee is near mine, warm through fabric, and my body keeps wanting to lean like it’s learned something it shouldn’t.

He glances at me, low. “Still okay.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he says, and it’s so steady it makes my chest ache again.

I hate how often my chest aches lately. It’s like my body is developing emotions. Disgusting.

---

### The unavoidable part

A woman I vaguely recognize from the gingerbread contest drifts by, drink in hand, eyes scanning.

She spots me. Her face brightens.

“Oh! Wes,” she says, too warmly. “Glad to see you out.”

My stomach tightens.

Neutral, neutral, neutral.

“Hi,” I say, flat and polite.

She leans in slightly, like she’s about to say the thing.

Sam shifts, smooth as a door closing, and says calmly, “Evening.”

The woman’s eyes flick to Sam. Then back to me. She smiles wider like she thinks she’s discovered a secret.

“So,” she says, voice dropping, “the article was…”

I feel my shoulders start to rise.

Sam cuts in, gentle but firm. “We’re not discussing it.”

The woman blinks. “I’m just saying, it was—”

Sam’s voice stays even. “Enjoy the party.”

Not angry. Not threatening. Just… a line.

The woman flushes, mutters, “Right, of course,” and glides away.

My hands are shaking.

Sam doesn’t touch me. He just leans in, low. “You don’t have to fight it.”

I swallow. “I didn’t.”

Sam’s gaze warms. “No. You stayed.”

My throat tightens.

“Stop making it meaningful,” I whisper.

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

“Stop.”

“No.”

---

## Chapter 35: Dance

The jazz trio shifts into something slow.

Couples drift to the small cleared space near the center of the room. Some people sway like they’ve rehearsed being soft.

Noel notices and looks like he’s about to implode.

Eben’s presence clamps down on him like gravity.

Noel doesn’t move. He just watches with the intensity of a man witnessing prophecy.

Sam glances at the dance floor, then back at me.

He doesn’t ask right away. He lets the question exist in the air first, like he’s giving my nerves time to vote.

Then, quietly: “Do you want to dance.”

My stomach flips.

Public. Visible. Not hidden behind a plant.

My first instinct is a clean, immediate no.

But my body also remembers the sleigh. The rink. The candle walk. The market booth.

Small steps.

Chosen.

I swallow hard. “In the dark.”

Sam’s brows lift slightly. “We can stand near the edge.”

I exhale. “No spinning. No… show.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he says, and his voice is so calm it almost makes me brave.

We stand.

We move to the edge of the dance floor, where the light is lower and people aren’t facing us directly.

Sam holds out his hand, palm up.

He waits.

Choice.

My heart pounds.

I place my hand in his.

Full hand this time. Not two fingers.

Sam’s other hand settles lightly at my side, not pressing, just there. His touch asks permission even when it doesn’t.

I exhale, shaky.

Sam’s voice is low in my ear. “Still okay.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

He starts moving, slow, guiding me into the rhythm.

It’s not performance dancing. It’s barely dancing at all. More like swaying, like we’re trying to convince the world this is casual.

But my body knows the truth.

My chest is tight. Warm. Alive.

I keep my gaze angled down, avoiding faces, but I can feel people around us and none of them are reacting like we’re scandal.

A couple people glance and smile and go back to their drinks.

No one claps. No one points. No one owns it.

And the realization hits hard: the town isn’t the cage.

The cage was always inside me.

Sam murmurs, “You’re doing it.”

I grit my teeth. “Don’t.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “Okay.”

We sway through one song.

Then another starts.

Sam leans in. “Do you want to stop.”

Choice.

I swallow.

I should stop. I planned forty-five minutes. I planned a wall. I planned minimal exposure.

But I’m warm and steady and not falling apart.

I whisper, “One more.”

Sam nods. “Okay. One more.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he agrees.

We sway through the second song.

Near the end, I finally lift my gaze, just enough to meet his eyes.

Sam is watching me like I matter.

Not like a project. Not like a fragile thing. Just… me.

My throat tightens.

I whisper, “Don’t look at me like that.”

Sam’s brows lift slightly. “Like what.”

“Like you’re… staying,” I mutter.

Sam’s voice is steady. “I am.”

My chest aches so sharply I almost pull away.

Instead, I do the most reckless thing I’ve done all week.

I press my forehead briefly to his shoulder.

One second.

Then I pull back like it didn’t happen.

Sam doesn’t comment. Doesn’t trap me with it. He just keeps swaying, steadying the world around my shaking edges.

The song ends.

I exhale hard.

Sam leans in, low. “Do you want to leave.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

We slip out quietly, coats on, no announcements.

Noel spots us leaving and puts both hands over his mouth to prevent a cheer.

Eben nods once at us like an approving bouncer.

We leave.

And the night doesn’t follow us.

---

## Chapter 36: Outside the Inn

Snow falls lightly as we step onto the porch.

The air is cold enough to sting, which helps. Cold is honest. Cold doesn’t pretend to want anything from you.

Sam stands beside me, hands in his coat pockets.

I breathe in, slow.

I made it.

I didn’t run.

I didn’t snap.

I didn’t vanish.

I danced. In public. Like a person.

The realization makes my stomach flip again.

Sam’s voice is quiet. “You did what you planned.”

I huff. “I danced.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Yes.”

I stare at the streetlights. “That was… too much.”

Sam nods. “Okay.”

I turn sharply. “But I also… didn’t hate it.”

Sam’s gaze warms. “Good.”

I swallow hard. “I don’t know what that means.”

Sam’s expression stays steady. “It means you’re allowed to want.”

My throat tightens.

I look away fast because the word *want* is dangerous.

Then I force it out anyway, low and raw: “I want you.”

Sam doesn’t move. He doesn’t grab. He doesn’t rush.

He just asks, soft: “Still okay.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

He steps closer, slow, and kisses me under the porch light. Brief. Careful. Warm.

No audience. No spectacle.

Just the kind of touch that doesn’t take anything I didn’t offer.

I kiss him back, then pull away first, because control still matters.

Sam lets me.

Of course he does.

I exhale, shaky laugh. “This is ridiculous.”

Sam’s mouth tips. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

We walk back to the bakery together, snow catching in our hair.

And for once, the future doesn’t look like a trap.

It looks like a line I can keep drawing.

One choice at a time.

One hand at a time.




## Chapter 37: The Morning After the Plant

We get back to Candle & Crumb and the whole building feels like it exhales with me.

Not because it’s magic. Because it’s mine. Because I know every creak in the stairs, every draft around the window frame, every place the floorboards complain.

Sam hangs his coat the same way he always does now. Quiet. Neat. Like he’s not trying to take up space he hasn’t been given.

I set my keys down and stare at them for a second like I’m expecting them to start screaming.

Sam’s voice is low. “Still okay.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he says, like he’s carved the word into his bones.

I go upstairs without saying much because my brain is full of the dance. Full hand. My forehead against his shoulder. Me leaving first because I still need control like oxygen.

Sam follows without pushing, like he’s learned the pace of my fear.

Inside my apartment, the quiet hits again. It should be relief.

Instead, it’s the aftershock.

Because the public part is over, and now my brain wants to replay everything that could’ve gone wrong.

“What if someone saw.”
“What if someone posts.”
“What if Grant writes another update.”
“What if I did too much.”

I pace once, then stop because pacing makes it worse.

Sam watches me, steady.

“You were good,” he says.

I laugh once, sharp. “That’s not helpful.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “Okay.”

I point. “Stop.”

“No.”

I turn away, then blurt, because apparently I’ve decided honesty is my new hobby. “I danced.”

Sam’s gaze warms. “Yes.”

“In public,” I add, like I’m confessing a crime.

Sam nods once. “Yes.”

I swallow hard. “And I didn’t die.”

Sam’s eyes soften. “No.”

My chest tightens. “I don’t know what that means.”

Sam steps closer, slow, like he’s approaching a wild animal that might bolt. “It means you can be seen and still be safe.”

My throat tightens.

I hate that the words land.

I whisper, rough, “I don’t trust it.”

Sam’s voice is steady. “You don’t have to trust the whole world. Just the plan.”

I stare at him.

Then I nod once, because nodding is easier than speaking when my throat feels like it’s full of glass.

Sam’s gaze stays on mine. “Do you want quiet.”

I exhale. “Yes.”

Sam nods. “Okay.”

He doesn’t move to touch me. He just sits on the couch, back straight, hands relaxed, waiting like a man who understands that quiet is sometimes intimacy.

I sit too, leaving a careful gap between us that I hate needing.

The gap shrinks on its own. My shoulder brushes his.

He still doesn’t move.

Choice.

I lean in until my head rests against his shoulder.

One second.

Two.

Sam’s hand hovers near my back, then he asks, almost too soft to hear. “Touch.”

My throat tightens.

I whisper, “Yes.”

His hand settles on my shoulder, warm and steady through fabric.

And my body finally stops bracing like it’s waiting for impact.

---

## Chapter 38: The Boring Truth

In the morning, the town wakes up like it always does: hungry, nosy, and convinced it’s entitled to a story.

I wake up like I always do: tense, alert, and ready to fight a headline.

Sam is already awake. Of course he is.

He looks over when I sit up, voice low. “Still okay.”

I blink at him, then exhale. “Yes.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter automatically, then stop.

Because I just said it like it’s mine now.

Sam’s mouth tips slightly, warm. “It counts.”

I groan and roll out of bed.

Downstairs, the bakery smells like cold flour and yesterday’s sugar, which is comforting in the way only routine is.

I unlock the door. Flip the sign.

OPEN.

And the second the bell chimes, the world returns.

The first few customers are regulars. They order normally. They smile normally. They pretend they don’t know anything.

Then, of course, someone tries it.

A tourist in a bright coat steps up, phone already half-raised in that “I’m just casually holding this” way.

I feel the old heat rise in my chest.

Sam is near the front, “on patrol,” calm and sharp.

The tourist smiles. “Hey, you’re the baker from that article.”

I keep my face flat. “I’m the baker.”

The tourist laughs like I’m being cute. “Right, but like… the *story.*”

I feel my teeth sharpen.

Then I remember the notebook. The plan. The line.

I take a slow breath. “There is no story.”

The tourist blinks. “But it said—”

Sam’s voice cuts in, calm and final. “No photos.”

The tourist’s smile wobbles. “I wasn’t—”

Sam doesn’t raise his voice. He doesn’t threaten. He just holds the boundary like it’s weather. “No photos.”

The tourist lowers his phone, flustered. “Okay. Sorry.”

I ring him up. Hand him a bag. End of interaction.

The tourist leaves.

The bakery keeps breathing.

I didn’t explode.

I didn’t collapse.

I stayed boring.

It’s ridiculous how powerful boring can be.

Sam glances at me, quick check. “Still okay.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he agrees.

And I hate that I’m starting to believe it.

---

## Chapter 39: The Line in the Snow

That evening, after the last tray is cleaned and the door is locked, I sit at the back table with the notebook open.

Sam stands by the sink, rinsing a mug like he’s trying to be useful without being in the way.

I tap the pen against the paper, then write:

### WHAT I WANT (WES)

* A life that isn’t a rumor.
* A relationship that isn’t public property.
* To stop flinching when someone looks at me.
* To choose touch without bracing.

I stare at the last line like it’s too honest.

Sam’s voice comes softly from the sink. “You can.”

I swallow. “I did. Twice.”

Sam’s mouth tips slightly. “More than twice.”

I glare. “Don’t count.”

Sam’s voice stays calm. “Okay.”

I exhale, then add another section.

### WHAT I CAN OFFER (WES)

* Slow.
* Honest.
* Private.
* Consistent.
* Trying.

My throat tightens on the last word.

Trying sounds small.

Trying is also everything.

Sam sits across from me without asking, careful about the chair, careful about distance.

He nods toward the notebook. “Can I read.”

Choice again.

I swallow. “Yes.”

He reads quietly. No commentary. No smugness. No “good job.”

When he looks up, his eyes are steady and warm. “This is enough.”

My chest tightens. “It doesn’t feel like enough.”

Sam’s voice is calm. “It is. Because it’s real.”

I stare at him, then whisper the thing that scares me because it’s not clever. It’s not armored. It’s simple.

“What do you want.”

Sam doesn’t hesitate. “You. And your terms. And time.”

My throat tightens.

“And,” he adds, careful, “I want to be allowed to exist in your life without you having to earn it every day.”

My stomach flips.

I whisper, rough, “I don’t know how to stop earning.”

Sam’s gaze holds mine. “Then start by letting yourself keep what you already did. The dance. The hand. The line.”

I swallow hard.

Outside, snow falls quietly.

Inside, the world is warm.

I look down at the notebook and write one last line under everything:

* I don’t have to disappear to be safe.

My hand shakes as I write it.

Then I set the pen down like I’ve finished a battle.

Sam’s voice is low. “Still okay.”

I breathe in.

This time, the answer doesn’t feel like bravery.

It feels like a decision.

“Yes,” I say. “I’m okay.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter, but there’s less bite in it now.

“It counts,” he says, and reaches across the table, palm up.

He waits.

Choice.

I slide my fingers into his, full hand this time, and hold on.

Not because I’m trapped.

Because I want to.

And the line in the snow holds.



## Chapter 40: The Hand That Stays

Sam’s hand in mine makes the bakery feel smaller in the best way. Like the room isn’t full of traps. Like the air isn’t waiting to turn.

I don’t squeeze hard. I don’t cling. I just… hold.

Sam doesn’t move like he’s trying to turn this into a moment. He doesn’t smile like he’s won. He just sits there, steady, letting my choice be what it is.

After a minute, I whisper, rough, “This is new.”

Sam’s thumb brushes once over my knuckles, barely there. “Yeah.”

My chest tightens anyway. I look down at our hands, then mutter, “Don’t get used to me being… like this.”

Sam’s voice stays calm. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

He doesn’t say “I love it.” He doesn’t say “I always knew you’d soften.” He doesn’t say any of the things that would make my skin crawl and my walls slam back up.

He just stays.

Which is, honestly, the most terrifying form of kindness.

I let go first, because I still need to. Because control is part of how I breathe.

Sam doesn’t react. Doesn’t chase the contact. Doesn’t punish me with distance.

He just nods toward the notebook. “Do you want to add anything else.”

I swallow. “Yeah.”

I pick up the pen and write one more line, under the rules.

9. If I let you in, don’t sprint.

My hand shakes as I finish it, like my body doesn’t trust me with my own truth.

Sam reads it and nods once. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he says, and the quiet warmth of it makes me want to throw the notebook out the window.

Instead, I close it gently, like it’s something fragile I’m learning how to keep.

---

## Chapter 41: The Corrective

The next morning, the update hits.

Not from Grant.

From the site that published him.

Noel slams his phone on the counter like he’s presenting evidence in court. “LOOK.”

I stare. “Volume.”

Noel whispers louder. “SORRY. LOOK.”

I take the phone.

At the top of the article, there’s a new editor’s note:

**Editor’s note: We have removed language that speculated on private individuals’ relationships. Our coverage will focus on public events and community programs.**

My stomach drops, but not in the panic way. In the *relief is suspicious* way.

Sam, near the door, watches my face. “They edited it.”

I swallow. “Why.”

Noel grins like a maniac. “Because Eben knows a lawyer.”

Eben’s voice from behind Noel is flat. “I know someone who knows someone.”

I blink. “Eben.”

Eben avoids eye contact. “He crossed a line.”

Noel whispers, reverent, “Eben has a soft core.”

Eben’s stare is lethal. “Stop breathing.”

Noel beams anyway.

I stare at the editor’s note again. My throat tightens.

Sam’s voice goes low. “Still okay.”

I swallow, and it surprises me that my answer comes out steady. “Yes.”

Because it’s not a full win. It doesn’t erase what happened. But it’s the world, for once, admitting that I’m not public property.

That matters.

And I hate how much it matters.

Noel whispers, “Grant is so mad.”

Eben says, “Good.”

I hand the phone back like it burns.

“Okay,” I mutter.

Sam’s mouth tips. “That okay counts.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

---

## Chapter 42: The Town’s Version of Quiet

The rest of the week after Twelfth Night is the town exhaling.

The big events are over. The tourists thin out. The schedule board comes down. The square stays lit anyway because Noel refuses to accept darkness.

Business stays steady, but the edge of gossip dulls. People stop trying to test the boundary.

A couple customers still smile too knowingly at Sam when he steps in to grab coffee.

Sam stays boring. I stay boring.

It works.

One afternoon, Mrs. Alden comes in, orders her usual, then pauses and says, very casually, “I saw you dancing.”

My stomach drops.

I brace.

Mrs. Alden continues, still casual. “You looked happy.”

My chest tightens so hard it hurts.

I keep my face flat. “I was… not unhappy.”

Mrs. Alden nods like that’s the correct amount of honesty for a man like me. “Good.”

Then she adds, like she’s discussing the weather, “And that writer fellow can go chew ice.”

I blink.

Mrs. Alden smiles sweetly, takes her bag, and leaves.

No drama. No lecture. No demand for a story.

Just… a tiny piece of protection from someone who has absolutely decided I’m hers to defend in the way a town defends its own.

My hands shake under the counter anyway.

Sam catches my eye, calm. “Still okay.”

I swallow. “Yes.”

Sam nods. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he agrees.

And I realize something quietly awful: being cared about by a community doesn’t always feel like a spotlight.

Sometimes it feels like a wall at your back.

---

## Chapter 43: The First Ordinary Night

The first truly ordinary night after the festival feels wrong.

No sleigh. No candle walk. No market booth. No Noel scheduling “emotional milestones” like he’s running a lab.

Just snow outside and the bakery quiet inside.

I lock up, head upstairs, and find Sam already there, sitting on my couch with his boots off, reading a paperback like he lives here.

My chest tightens.

He looks up. “Hey.”

“Hey,” I mutter.

Sam closes the book without snapping it shut like it’s a declaration. “How was today.”

I shrug, then realize that’s not enough anymore. “Quiet. Better.”

Sam nods once. “Good.”

I hover near the doorway like I’m not sure I’m allowed to enter my own room when someone else is in it.

Sam watches me for a beat, then asks softly, “Do you want space.”

Choice.

I exhale. “No.”

Sam nods. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter.

“It counts,” he says.

I step closer, slow, then sit beside him with a careful inch of distance that I hate.

Sam doesn’t close it. He waits.

My shoulder drifts into his. Not on purpose. Not not on purpose either.

He stays still.

I mutter, “I don’t know what we are.”

Sam’s voice is steady. “We’re whatever you can hold.”

My throat tightens.

I hate that answer. I love that answer.

I whisper, “I can hold… this.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

I glare. “Stop.”

“No.”

I lean my head against his shoulder and let my eyes close.

It’s quiet.

It’s safe.

And the most ridiculous part is: nobody had to earn it tonight. Not me. Not him. It’s just… here.

---

## Chapter 44: New Year’s, Small and Stubborn

New Year’s Eve in Tinsel Town is technically an “event,” but it’s not the festival kind. It’s smaller. Local. People who know each other and don’t need to perform as hard.

Noel still tries, obviously, but Eben keeps him on a short leash.

I stay open late for the “midnight cocoa crowd,” which is mostly parents and night-shift workers and people who don’t drink but still want to mark the year changing.

Sam is there, in plain clothes, sitting at the back table with coffee, reading.

And that’s the thing.

He’s not “on patrol.”

He’s not hovering.

He’s just… there.

A couple people nod at him. A couple nod at me. No one makes it weird. When someone starts to, Sam’s gaze goes flat and it dies in their throat.

At 11:58, Noel bursts in with Hugh and Eben like an invading force.

Noel has a party popper. Hugh has champagne. Eben has the dead-eyed resignation of a man who agreed to this under duress.

Noel stage-whispers, “COUNTDOWN IN THE BAKERY.”

I stare. “No.”

Noel grins. “Yes.”

Eben says, “No.”

Noel grins wider. “Yes.”

The little crowd in the bakery starts counting anyway, because humans love being told what to do in groups.

Ten.

Nine.

Eight.

I stand behind the counter, arms crossed, pretending I’m not participating.

Sam stands a few feet away, watching me like he’s ready to leave if it becomes too much.

Five.

Four.

Three.

My chest tightens because here comes the part where people hug and kiss and get loud and public.

Two.

One.

Cheering.

Noel yells, “HAPPY NEW YEAR,” like he’s trying to summon fireworks indoors.

Hugh kisses the air dramatically. Eben looks like he wants to die.

And Sam?

Sam doesn’t reach for me.

He just looks at me, steady, and asks quietly, “Tether.”

Choice.

In the middle of my bakery. With people around. With noise and light and the year changing.

I swallow hard.

Then, because I’m tired of being ruled by fear, I step out from behind the counter and take his hand.

Full hand.

Not two fingers.

Not pinky.

Full.

The room doesn’t explode.

No one screams.

Noel, of course, sees it and goes rigid with delight, but Eben clamps him down with a hand on his shoulder.

Noel doesn’t say a word.

I’m almost proud of him.

Sam’s thumb brushes my knuckles once, gentle.

He doesn’t kiss me. Doesn’t turn it into a performance.

He just holds my hand while the bakery cheers at midnight.

And for the first time, the new year feels like something I’m walking into instead of surviving.

Sam leans in, low. “Still okay.”

My throat tightens.

“Yeah,” I whisper. “I’m okay.”

Sam nods once. “Okay.”

“That okay counts,” I mutter, but I’m smiling when I say it.

“It counts,” he replies.

And the warmth in my chest doesn’t feel like a trap.

It feels like mine.

**[STOP WRITING]**
